To the Reader

Reader, this is an honest book. It warns you right at the door that I wrote it for private, family reasons and nothing more. I gave no thought to being useful to you, or to winning any glory for myself — my powers aren't up to a project like that. I wrote it for the particular benefit of my relatives and friends, so that when they've lost me (which must happen soon), they can recover here some traces of my character and my moods, and so keep their knowledge of me more whole and more alive.

If I'd been out to court the world's favor, I would have dressed myself up in borrowed beauty. Instead, I want to be seen here in my simple, natural, everyday manner, without strain or artifice — because it is myself I'm painting. My faults will show up here as they really are, along with my native shape, as far as public decency allows. If I had lived among those nations that are said to dwell still under the sweet liberty of nature's first laws, I assure you I would very gladly have painted myself entire, and entirely naked.

So, reader: I am myself the matter of my book. There's no reason you should spend your free time on a subject so frivolous and so empty.

Therefore farewell.

Montaigne, this first of March, 1580.

Book I

Of Idleness

You've seen what happens to fields left fallow. If the soil is rich, it erupts with a hundred thousand kinds of weeds and useless wild growth; to get any honest work out of it, you have to break it up and sow it with the seeds you actually want. And women's bodies, they say, can sometimes produce on their own mere shapeless lumps of flesh, but for a true and natural birth they have to be sown with a different seed. Minds are the same. Unless you give a mind some definite subject to rein it in and hold it steady, it tumbles off into a thousand wild excursions, roving endlessly here and there through the vague fields of imagination — like light reflected off trembling water in a bronze bowl, as Virgil describes it, thrown by the sun or the shining moon, flickering across every surface and darting up to strike the highest ceiling.

And in that restless agitation, there is no piece of nonsense too empty for an idle mind to fill with a thousand fantasies and chimeras — "like a sick man's dreams, conjuring empty phantoms," as Horace says. A mind without a fixed aim loses itself — because, as they say, to be everywhere is to be nowhere.

Not long ago I retired to my own house, resolved, as far as I possibly could, to stay clear of all business and spend in privacy and rest the little time I have left to live. I thought the kindest favor I could do my mind was to leave it in complete leisure to entertain and amuse itself — which I hoped it could manage easily by now, having grown more settled and mature with time. But I find that "leisure always breeds a restless mind," as Lucan says. Quite the contrary of settling down, my mind has bolted like a horse that's thrown its rider, running a far wilder course on its own than any horseman would ever have put it to. It births so many chimeras and fantastic monsters, one on top of another, without order or purpose, that — the better to study their strangeness and absurdity at my leisure — I have begun to set them down in writing. These are my attempts: my essais, as we say in French. And I'm hoping, in time, to make my mind ashamed of itself.

Of Liars

There is no man alive with less business talking about memory than me. I can find hardly a trace of one in myself, and I doubt there's another in the world so spectacularly unreliable as mine. All my other faculties are ordinary and average; but in this one I think I'm so rare and singular that I deserve to be famous for it.

Besides the natural inconvenience I suffer from it — and considering how necessary memory is, Plato was right to call it a great and powerful goddess — there's this: where I come from, when people want to say a man has no sense, they say he has no memory. So when I complain about the defect of mine, they correct me and refuse to believe me, as if I were accusing myself of being a fool. They can't see the difference between memory and understanding — which makes my case worse. But they do me wrong. Experience shows us daily, if anything, the opposite: that an excellent memory tends to come paired with shaky judgment.

They do me another wrong too. I am better at friendship than at anything else, and yet the very words that describe my infirmity make me out to be ungrateful. They question my heart on the evidence of my memory, and turn a natural defect into a defect of conscience. "He forgot this request," someone says, "or that promise. He doesn't remember his friends anymore. He forgot to say this, or do that, or keep quiet about the other thing, for my sake." True enough, I forget plenty. But neglect something a friend has actually entrusted to me? Never. It should be enough that I feel the misery and inconvenience of the thing, without being branded with malice on top of it — a vice that couldn't be more foreign to my temperament.

Still, I get a few comforts out of my infirmity. First, it's a flaw that has helped me correct a worse one that would easily have grown on me: ambition. A failing memory is unbearable in anyone who takes up public business. Also — as nature shows us in similar cases — she has strengthened my other faculties in proportion as she left this one bare. Otherwise I'd have been content to let my mind and judgment rest lazily on other people's reports, without ever putting them to work on their own strength, if other men's ideas and opinions had always been at my fingertips by courtesy of memory. And it keeps me from being talkative — for the warehouse of memory is always better stocked than the warehouse of invention. If my memory had stayed faithful to me, I'd have deafened every friend I have with chatter by now, since the subjects themselves would keep rousing what little gift I have for handling and arranging them, warming me up and pulling me along. That would have been a pity. I've watched it happen to some of my closest friends. Their memories hand them the whole subject, entire and fully present, so they start their story too far back and load it with so many useless details that if the story is good, they smother the good in it — and if it isn't, you're left cursing either the strength of their memory or the weakness of their judgment. It's hard to close off a story and cut it short once you're rolling; nothing shows the strength of a horse like a clean, sudden stop. Even speakers who stay on point — I see them wanting to stop and not managing it. While they hunt around for a graceful exit line, they keep rambling on, dragging through trivialities like men staggering on weak legs. Old men are the most dangerous company of all: they remember the past but forget how many times they've told you about it. I've watched stories from the mouth of a very great lord — genuinely entertaining stories — become unbearable by being repeated a hundred times to the same audience.

The second comfort is that I'm slower to remember the injuries people do me. As one ancient put it, I'd need an official register of insults — or a prompter, like Darius, who, so he wouldn't forget the offense the Athenians had given him, had a page repeat in his ear three times every time he sat down to dinner: "Sir, remember the Athenians." And then, too, the places I revisit and the books I reread always smile at me with a fresh novelty.

It's said with good reason that anyone without a sound memory should never take up the trade of lying. I know the grammarians draw a line between telling an untruth and telling a lie. To tell an untruth, they say, is to say something false that we ourselves believe is true; but to lie — by the definition of the Latin word our French comes from — is to say something we know in our conscience to be untrue. It's only this second kind of liar I'm talking about here. These people either invent their falsehoods wholesale, or they disguise and alter a true story until it ends up a lie.

When they disguise and alter a story, telling it differently each time to suit themselves, it's very hard for them to escape getting caught sooner or later. The true version of the thing took possession of the memory first — it got lodged there by way of real knowledge — so it keeps presenting itself to the imagination and shoulders out the falsehood, which can never get such firm and settled footing. The circumstances of the true version keep running through the mind and make them forget the fake ones, which have no parentage but their own invention.

When they invent the whole thing from scratch, there's no true impression to collide with the lie, so they would seem to be in less danger of tripping. But even so, an invention is a hollow body with nothing to grip; it slips easily out of any memory that isn't very sure of itself. I've had some amusing proof of this, at the expense of those professionals who shape everything they say to fit the business at hand — or to please whichever great man they're talking to. The circumstances they're so willing to enslave their word and conscience to keep changing, so their story has to change with them: they tell one man a thing is this, another that it's that, painting the same fact different colors for different audiences. And if those men ever compare notes and discover the trick, what becomes of the whole fine art? Besides, they trip themselves up, ridiculously, all the time — for what memory could possibly be big enough to hold all the different shapes they've forged for one and the same subject? I've known plenty of men in my day who envied the reputation this kind of cleverness brings. What they don't see is that once you have the reputation, the trick no longer works.

In plain truth, lying is an accursed vice. We are men, and hold together as men, only by our word. If we really saw the horror and the weight of lying, we would hunt it down with fire and sword — and more justly than we hunt other crimes. I notice that parents routinely punish their children, and not very sensibly, for innocent little mistakes, tormenting them over thoughtless pranks that leave no mark and carry no consequence. Meanwhile lying — and a step below it, stubbornness — are the faults that ought to be fought from the moment they appear and at every stage of growth, because they grow up right along with the child. Once a tongue gets the knack of lying, you can't imagine how nearly impossible it is to break it of the habit. That's how it happens that otherwise honest men end up enslaved to this vice. I have a perfectly decent lad as my tailor, and I have never once caught him telling the truth — not even when it would have worked in his favor.

If falsehood, like truth, had only one face, we'd be on better terms with it: we could simply take the opposite of whatever the liar said as certain. But the reverse of truth has a hundred thousand faces, and an open field without edge or limit. The Pythagoreans make good finite and certain, and evil infinite and uncertain. There are a thousand ways to miss the target; there's only one way to hit it. For my own part, I hold this vice in such horror that I'm not sure I could bring my conscience to save me even from obvious, extreme danger by telling one brazen, deliberate lie.

One of the old church fathers says that a dog we know is better company than a man whose language we don't understand — so true is it, as Pliny adds, that a foreigner who shares no tongue with us hardly counts as a fellow man. And how much less sociable than silence is false speech?

King Francis I used to boast that he had used exactly this weapon to corner Francesco Taverna, ambassador of Francesco Sforza, Duke of Milan, and a man famous in his day for his skill in talking. Taverna had been sent to make his master's excuses to His Majesty over an affair of very great consequence, which was this. The King, wanting to keep some channel into Italy — from which he had recently been driven out — and especially into the duchy of Milan, had decided to keep a gentleman of his own at the Duke's court: an ambassador in fact, but in appearance a private person, pretending to be there on his own personal business. For the Duke depended far more on the Emperor — especially just then, while negotiating a marriage with the Emperor's niece, daughter of the King of Denmark and now the dowager of Lorraine — and couldn't be seen dealing and conferring with us without serious damage to himself. A Milanese gentleman named Merveille, one of the King's own equerries, was judged right for the job, and was sent off with secret credentials and the instructions of an ambassador, along with letters of recommendation to the Duke about his own private affairs, to mask and color the business. But he stayed at that court so long that the Emperor eventually got wind of his real role there — which, as we suppose, is what caused what followed. On the pretext of a murder charge, the Duke had Merveille's trial dispatched in two days, and his head struck off at night, in prison.

Messire Francesco arrived with a long, counterfeit account of the affair all prepared — for the King had appealed to every prince in Christendom, and to the Duke himself, demanding satisfaction — and was given his audience at the morning council. To support his case, he laid out several plausible defenses of the deed: his master had never taken Merveille for anything but a private gentleman and his own subject, there purely on his own business; he denied absolutely ever having heard that the man belonged to the King's household, or that the King even knew him — let alone took him for an ambassador. The King, in his turn, pressed him with objections and questions, challenged him from every side, and finally cornered him with this: why, then, was the execution carried out at night, and as if by stealth? At which the poor flustered ambassador, trying to wriggle out gracefully, answered that the Duke, out of respect for His Majesty, would have been very sorry to see such an execution performed in daylight. You can guess the dressing-down he got when he came home, for tripping so clumsily in front of a prince with as fine a nose as King Francis.

Pope Julius II sent an ambassador to the King of England to stir him up against King Francis. The ambassador had his audience, and the King, before giving an answer, dwelt on the difficulties he would face in mounting a force big enough to attack so powerful a king, and gave some reasons for them. The ambassador, very unwisely, replied that he had considered the same difficulties himself, and had said as much to the Pope. From this remark — so squarely opposed to the whole point of his errand, which was to push England straight into war — the King of England drew the first hint of what he afterward found to be true: that this ambassador, in his own heart, leaned toward the French side. The King advised the Pope; the ambassador's estate was confiscated when he got home, and he came very close to losing his head.

To Philosophize Is to Learn to Die

Cicero says that to philosophize is nothing else than to prepare ourselves for death. And he has a point. Study and contemplation draw the soul partway out of us, set it working apart from the body — which is a kind of apprenticeship to death, a first resemblance of it. Or else it is because all the wisdom and reasoning in the world comes down, in the end, to one lesson: do not be afraid to die.

Honestly, either reason is mocking us, or it can have no other aim than our contentment. Its whole business is to make us live well and, as Scripture says, at our ease. Every opinion in the world agrees on this much: pleasure is our goal. The schools take different roads to it, but that is where they are all headed. Otherwise we would show them the door at once — who listens to a man who proposes misery and suffering as the destination?

On this point the squabbles of the philosophical sects are just words. Let us skip past such clever trifles, as Seneca says. There is more stubbornness and point-scoring in them than suits so sacred a profession. But whatever role a man takes on, he always mixes some of himself into the performance.

Let the philosophers say what they like: even in virtue, the thing we are all aiming at is pleasure. I enjoy rattling their ears with that word — they gag on it so. And if it means some supreme delight and contentment, that delight belongs to virtue more than to any other helper. This pleasure, being livelier, more sinewy, more robust, more vigorous, is only the more seriously pleasurable for it. We ought to have given virtue the name of pleasure — the kinder, gentler, more natural name — instead of the name for force we hung on it.

As for the lower sort of pleasure, if it deserved that lovely name at all, it should have had to win it in open competition, not hold it by privilege. I find it less free of troubles and crossings than virtue itself. Besides being momentary, fluid, and fragile, it has its vigils, its fasts, its labors, its sweat and its blood. It comes with so many kinds of sharp, wounding passions, and drags such a heavy boredom behind it, that it equals the harshest penance.

And we are wrong to think those discomforts act as spur and seasoning to its sweetness, the way one contrary quickens another in nature — and then to turn around and say, when we come to virtue, that the same difficulties make her grim, austere, and out of reach. In virtue, far better than in indulgence, they ennoble, sharpen, and heighten the divine and perfect pleasure she wins for us. A man who weighs her cost against her fruit makes himself unworthy of her; he understands neither the blessing nor how to use it. Those who keep preaching that the pursuit of virtue is steep and laborious, but the enjoyment of her delightful — what are they telling us, except that she is never any fun? What human effort ever arrives at the enjoyment? The most perfect men have had to settle for aspiring to her, approaching without ever possessing. But they are mistaken, because in every pleasure we know, the pursuit itself is pleasant. The attempt takes its flavor from the thing it reaches for; it is a good part of the result, the same flesh and blood as the result. The happiness that glitters in virtue shines through all her approaches and avenues, from the first gate to the farthest wall.

Now, of all the gifts virtue gives us, contempt for death is one of the greatest. It is what furnishes human life with a soft and easy tranquility, what gives us a pure and friendly taste for living — and without it every other pleasure goes dark. That is why all the rules of philosophy meet and agree on this one article. The philosophers do also teach us, with one voice, to despise pain, poverty, and the other accidents human life is exposed to, but not with the same urgency. Partly because those accidents are not so inevitable — most of mankind goes through life without ever tasting poverty, and some without sickness or sorrow, like Xenophilus the musician, who lived a hundred and six years in perfect, uninterrupted health. And partly because, at the very worst, death can cut all the other troubles short whenever we please. But death itself is inevitable. As Horace says, we are all herded the same way; everyone's lot tumbles from the urn sooner or later, and puts us aboard the boat for everlasting exile.

So if death frightens us, it is a perpetual torment, and one that nothing relieves. There is no direction it cannot come from. We can keep twisting our heads this way and that, as in enemy country, but it hangs over us always — like the stone over Tantalus, as Cicero says.

Our courts often send condemned criminals back to be executed at the scene of the crime. Lead them past fine houses on the way; lay on the best dinner you can. Sicilian delicacies will not coax out their flavor, says Horace, and no song of birds and harps will bring back sleep. Do you think the condemned can enjoy any of it? Do you think that with the journey's end standing in plain sight, the taste of all those treats would not curdle in their mouths? He counts the days, says Claudian; he measures his life by the length of the road, and is tortured by the blow to come.

The end of our race is death. It is the necessary target of our course, and if it terrifies us, how can we take a single step without trembling? The remedy ordinary people use is simply not to think about it. But what brutish stupidity can supply blindness that thick? That is bridling the donkey by the tail — setting a man, as Lucretius says, to plan his steps walking backwards. No wonder he keeps falling into the ditch.

People are frightened by the mere mention of death; most cross themselves at the word, as if it were the name of the devil. And because making a will has to do with dying, no one can be persuaded to pick up the pen until the doctor has passed final sentence on him — and then, between the pain and the terror, God knows what state of mind he is in to do it.

The Romans, because that poor syllable death grated so harshly on their ears and sounded so ominous, found a way to soften it and spin it out. Instead of saying "he is dead," they said "he has lived," or "he has ceased to live." As long as life got a mention, even life that was over, it carried some note of consolation. From them we borrowed our expression "the late Monsieur So-and-so."

Maybe, as the saying goes, the time we have lived is worth our money. I was born between eleven and noon on the last day of February, 1533, counting the year as we do now, from the first of January. It is just two weeks since I turned thirty-nine, and I reckon to live at least as long again. Meanwhile, to fret about a thing so far off would be folly. But then — the young and the old leave life on exactly the same terms. No one exits it any differently than if he had just walked in. And there is no man so old and decrepit that, with Methuselah in mind, he does not figure he has another twenty good years in him.

You fool — who has guaranteed you the term of your life? You are leaning on doctors' tales. Look at facts and experience instead. By the ordinary run of things, you have been living a long while now on special favor. You have already outlasted the usual span of life. Count your acquaintances if you doubt it: how many died before reaching your age, against how many got there? Even among the men who made their lives famous, take a list, and I will wager you find more who died before thirty-five than after. It is reasonable, and pious too, to take our example from the humanity of Jesus Christ himself — and he ended his life at thirty-three. The greatest of merely mortal men, Alexander, died at the same age.

And how many different ways death has of catching us off guard! As Horace says, no man can guess, hour to hour, what danger to dodge. Set aside fevers and pleurisies: who would ever have imagined that a duke of Brittany would be crushed to death in a crowd, as one was at the entry of Pope Clement, my neighbor, into Lyons? Have you not seen one of our own kings killed at a tournament? And did not one of his ancestors die of being jostled by a hog? Aeschylus, warned that a falling house would kill him, took every precaution — for all the good it did, since an eagle dropped a tortoise on his head out of the open sky. Another man choked on a grape seed. An emperor died of a scratch from a comb while having his hair done; Aemilius Lepidus from stumbling over his own threshold; Aufidius from bumping against the door on his way into the council chamber. And between the very thighs of women: Cornelius Gallus the praetor; Tigillinus, captain of the watch at Rome; Ludovico, son of Guido di Gonzaga, Marquis of Mantua; and — a worse example still — Speusippus, a philosopher of Plato's school, and one of our Popes. Poor judge Bebius granted one party in a lawsuit eight days' adjournment — and in the meantime death adjourned him; his own stay of life had run out. Caius Julius, a physician, was anointing a patient's eyes when death closed his own. And if I may bring in an example from my own blood: my brother, Captain Saint-Martin, twenty-three years old and already well proven in courage, was playing a match of tennis when the ball struck him a little above the right ear. There was no sign of wound or bruise; he did not even sit down to rest. But five or six hours later he was dead of the stroke that blow had caused.

With examples this frequent and this ordinary passing before our eyes every day, how can a man possibly shake off the thought of death, or stop feeling that it has us by the throat at every moment?

What does it matter, you will say, how it comes, so long as a man does not torment himself waiting for it? I am of that mind myself. If there were any way at all to duck the blow — even by crawling under a calfskin — I am not the man who would be ashamed of the trick. All I want is to pass my time at ease, and whatever recreations get me there, I take hold of, however inglorious and unexemplary you please. As Horace says, I would rather seem crazy and lazy, so long as my faults please me or at least escape my notice, than be wise and snarling about it.

But it is folly to think you can get there by that road. They come, they go, they trot, they dance — and of death, not a word. All very fine. Yet when it does come — to them, to their wives, their children, their friends — surprising them unguarded and unprepared, then what torments, what cries, what madness and despair! Have you ever seen anything so crushed, so changed, so confounded? We have to provide for it earlier than that. And this brutish carelessness, even if it could lodge in the head of a man of sense (which I think utterly impossible), sells us its merchandise far too dear. If death were an enemy we could dodge, I would advise borrowing the weapons of cowardice. But it is not. It catches the runaway and the coward as surely as the honest man standing his ground — it hounds the fleeing soldier too, as Horace says, and has no mercy on the soft knees and timid back of unwarlike youth. And no armor is tempered well enough to protect us: let a man hide, wary, under iron and brass, says Propertius, and death will still pull his head out of its shell.

So let us learn to stand our ground and fight him. And to begin stripping him of his greatest advantage over us, let us take exactly the opposite road from the usual one. Let us rob death of his strangeness. Let us keep him company, get used to him, have nothing more often in our minds than death. At every moment let our imagination picture him in all his shapes. When a horse stumbles, when a tile falls, at the smallest prick of a pin, let us say it at once — "Well, and what if that had been death itself?" — and stiffen and steady ourselves on the spot. In the middle of our feasting and fun, let us keep the refrain of our frail condition playing, never letting pleasure carry us so far away that we forget how many ways this jollity of ours is death's target, and how many fists he shakes at it. The Egyptians did just that: at the height of their feasts, with the good cheer at its best, they had the dried skeleton of a man carried into the room as a reminder to the guests. Believe each day that dawns to be your last, says Horace, and the unhoped-for hour will be all the more welcome when it comes.

Where death waits for us is uncertain; let us wait for him everywhere. To think about death in advance is to think about freedom in advance. He who has learned to die has unlearned to be a slave. There is nothing evil in life for the man who has fully grasped that losing life is not an evil. Knowing how to die frees us from all subjection and constraint. When the miserable king of Macedon, his prisoner, sent to beg Paulus Aemilius not to parade him in his triumph, Paulus answered: "Let him make that request to himself."

In truth, in everything, if nature does not lend a hand, it is very hard for art and effort to get anywhere. By nature I am not melancholy, but I am a brooder, and there is nothing I have entertained myself with longer than thoughts of death — even in the most reckless season of my life, when my green youth, as Catullus has it, was running riot in its pleasant spring. Among the ladies, in the thick of the games, more than one onlooker probably thought I was chewing on some jealousy, or the uncertainty of some hope. In fact I was remembering some man or other who, a few days before, had come home from a party just like this one — his head as full of idleness, love, and good times as mine was then — and been carried off by a burning fever. For all I knew, the same fate was hanging at my ear. Soon the present will have been, says Lucretius, never to be called back.

Yet that thought wrinkled my forehead no more than any other. It is impossible not to feel the sting of such imaginings at first. But handle them, turn them over, come back to them, and in time they grow so familiar they are no trouble at all. Otherwise I, for one, would live in perpetual fright and frenzy — for no man ever trusted his life less, no man ever counted less on its length. Health, which I have so far enjoyed strong, vigorous, and rarely interrupted, does not stretch my hopes of living, and sickness does not shrink them. Every minute I feel myself slipping away. And one thought runs through my head constantly: whatever can be done tomorrow can be done today. Hazards and dangers, in truth, do little or nothing to hurry our end. If we consider the thousands of other threats still hanging over our heads, beyond the one that looks most pressing, we will find that healthy or sick, at sea or by the fireside, in battle or idle at home, we are all exactly as close to it as each other. No man is frailer than another, says Seneca, and no man surer of his tomorrow.

For anything I still have to do before I die, the longest stretch of leisure would look too short — even if the whole business would take an hour. Someone leafing through my notebooks the other day found a memo about something I wanted done after my death. I told him, and it was the plain truth, that though I had been only a league from home, healthy and cheerful, I had hurried to write it down on the spot, because I could not be sure of living until I got back. Since I am forever brooding over my own thoughts and settling them within myself, I am at every hour about as ready as I am ever going to be; and death, whenever he comes, can bring nothing with him that I have not expected for a long time. We should always be booted and spurred, as far as we can manage, and ready to go — and above all take care, when the time comes, to have no business left with anyone but ourselves. Why, asks Horace, do we tease so short a life with so many big projects? We will find work enough there without adding more.

One man complains, more than of death itself, that it cuts him off from a glorious victory. Another, that he must move out before marrying off his daughter or finishing his children's education. One mourns the loss of his wife's company, another his son's, as the chief comfort of his existence. For my part, I am — thank God — at this moment in such a state that I am ready to move out whenever it pleases Him, without regret for anything at all. I am loosening every worldly tie; my goodbyes are already half said to everyone but myself. No man ever prepared to quit the world more cleanly and completely, or shook himself looser from every claim it had on him, than I expect to do. The deadest deaths are the best:

"Wretch that I am," the dying man cries, "one disastrous day has stripped me of every prize life had to offer." And the builder laments: "My works stand unfinished — the great walls' proud towers left unmade."

We should plan nothing that takes so long to finish — or at least, nothing we burn so hotly to see completed. We are born for action. As the poet says, "when I die, let me be caught in the middle of my work."

I want a man to be doing things — to stretch out the business of living as far as he possibly can. And let death find me planting my cabbages, not caring a whit about death and even less about my unfinished garden. I watched a man die who, at his last gasp, complained of nothing so bitterly as that destiny was cutting the thread of the history he was writing — he had only gotten as far as our fifteenth or sixteenth king. What such men never add is that once you are dead, you will no longer want any of those things.

We need to rid ourselves of these common, harmful moods. This is why men first put burial grounds next to churches and in the busiest parts of town — to accustom ordinary people, women and children included, says Lycurgus, not to flinch at the sight of a corpse, and so that the constant spectacle of bones, graves, and funerals would keep reminding us of our fragile condition. The ancients went further: there was once a custom of livening up banquets with bloodshed — mixing the meal with the grim spectacle of armed men fighting to the death, some of them falling across the very cups and splashing the tables with blood.

The Egyptians, after their feasts, used to have a great image of death carried in before the company, while a man cried out: "Drink and be merry, for when you are dead you will look like this." In the same spirit, I keep death not only in my imagination but continually in my mouth. There is nothing I am so curious about, nothing I love learning more, than how men die — their words, their faces, their bearing — and no passages in history I read more closely. It shows in how I crowd my pages with examples; I have a particular fondness for the subject. If I were in the business of making books, I would compile an annotated register of the various deaths of men. He who would teach men to die would teach them to live. Dicaearchus did write one with a title like that, but he wrote it to another, less useful purpose.

Someone may object that the pain and terror of dying so far outrun anything we can imagine that the best fencer in the world will be out of his game when it comes to the real thrust. Let them say what they like: thinking it through in advance is still, beyond any doubt, an enormous advantage. And is it nothing to get even that far — to the threshold itself — without trembling, without fever? Besides, Nature herself lends us a hand and gives us heart. If the death is sudden and violent, we have no time to fear it. If it is slow, then I notice that as I sink deeper into a sickness, I naturally begin to feel a certain weariness and disdain for life. I find this resolution to die much harder to swallow when I am in good health than when I am burning with fever. The less grip I have on life's comforts, as I begin to lose the use and pleasure of them, the less terrifying death looks. Which gives me hope that the farther I travel from the one and the nearer I come to the other, the more easily I will make the trade. Caesar said that things often look bigger at a distance than up close, and I have found the same: in full health, I dreaded sickness far more than I ever felt it once it arrived. The vigor and cheerfulness I live in now make the opposite condition look so out of proportion to this one that in imagination I inflate every misery to half again its true size, and dread it as far heavier than it turns out to be when it actually lands on me. I hope to find death the same.

Just watch how, through the ordinary changes and declines we suffer daily, nature hides from us the feel of our own decay. What does an old man have left of his youth and his better days? As the line goes, "alas — how small a share of life is left to the old!" When a worn-out old soldier of Caesar's guard came up to him to ask permission to kill himself, Caesar looked at his withered body and broken walk and answered, cheerfully: "So you think you're still alive, do you?"

If a man fell into old age all at once, I do not believe human nature could endure the change. But nature leads us by the hand, down a gentle, almost imperceptible slope, step by step, until she has eased us into that miserable state and made us at home in it. We feel no jolt when our youth dies in us — though that is, in truth, a harder death than the final death of a languishing body, the death of old age. The leap from a weary existence to no existence is not nearly so great as the leap from a sweet, flourishing life to a painful and troublesome one.

A body bent and bowed has less strength to carry a load; so does a soul. We have to raise her up firm and straight against the power of this adversary. As long as she fears death, she can never be at rest. But once she has steadied herself, she can boast of something almost beyond the human condition: that anxiety, anguish, fear — even the smallest disquiet — cannot live in her. No tyrant's glare can shake her settled calm; neither can the storm-wind that rules the wild Adriatic, nor the mighty hand of thundering Jove.

She has become sovereign over her own passions and appetites, mistress of necessity, shame, poverty, and every other injury of fortune. So let those of us who can, seize this advantage. This is the true and sovereign liberty here on earth — the one that arms us to defy violence and injustice and to laugh at prisons and chains. "I will keep you in shackles and chains," says the master, "under a savage jailer." — "A god will set me free the moment I choose." That god, I think, is death. Death is the finish line of all things.

Our religion itself has no surer human foundation than contempt for life. Reason alone invites us to it — for why fear losing a thing which, once lost, cannot be missed? And since so many kinds of death threaten us, isn't it worse to fear them all forever than to suffer one of them once? What does it matter when it comes, since it cannot be avoided? When someone told Socrates, "The thirty tyrants have sentenced you to death," he answered: "And nature, them."

What nonsense, to torment ourselves over the one step that delivers us from all torment! As our birth brought us the birth of all things, our death will bring the death of all things. So it is just as foolish to weep that we will not be alive a hundred years from now as to weep that we were not alive a hundred years ago. Death is the beginning of another life. We wept like this once before; entering this life cost us just as dearly; we stripped off our old veil then, too, when we came in.

Nothing that happens only once can be a grievance. Is it reasonable to spend so long fearing a thing so quickly over? Long life and short life are made all one by death — there is no long or short for things that no longer exist. Aristotle says there are little creatures on the banks of the river Hypanis that live only a single day. The one that dies at eight in the morning dies in its youth; the one that dies at five in the evening dies of decrepit old age. Which of us wouldn't laugh to see that flicker of a lifespan weighed out in happiness and misery? Yet our own — measured against eternity, or even against the duration of mountains, rivers, stars, trees, and some animals — is every bit as laughable.

But nature compels us to it. "Leave this world," she says, "as you entered it. The same passage you made from death to life, without suffering and without fear, make again from life to death. Your death is one piece of the order of the universe; it is a piece of the life of the world. Mortals live by lending life to one another, and like runners in a relay, they hand on the torch of life.

"Shall I unmake this beautiful arrangement of things for you? Death is the condition of your creation; death is a part of you; in running from it, you run from yourselves. This very being you enjoy is divided equally between life and death. The day of your birth was your first step toward the grave. The first hour that gave you life took an hour of it away. Being born, you began dying; the end hangs from the beginning.

"All the time you live, you steal from life; you live at life's own expense. The perpetual work of your life is to build death. You are in death the whole time you are in life — for when you are no longer in life, you are after death. Or, if you like it better this way: you are dead after life, but dying all the while you live — and death handles the dying far more roughly than the dead, more sharply, closer to the quick.

"If you have made your profit of life, you have had your fill of it. Go your way satisfied. Why not leave life the way a well-fed guest leaves a feast? But if you have not known how to use it — if it did you no good — what do you care about losing it? Why would you want to keep it longer? Why ask for more days, only to spend them as badly and lose them all over again?

"Life in itself is neither good nor evil. It is the stage where good and evil play, depending on what you make of it. And if you have lived one day, you have seen everything. One day is equal and alike to all days. There is no other light, no other darkness. This very sun, this moon, these stars, this whole arrangement of things — it is the same your ancestors enjoyed, and the same that will entertain your great-grandchildren. Your fathers saw nothing different; your descendants will see nothing new.

"And at the very worst, the whole cast and plot of my comedy plays itself out in a single year. If you have watched my four seasons turn, you have seen the infancy, the youth, the manhood, and the old age of the world. Then the year has played its part, and it knows no other trick but to begin again. It will always be exactly this. We circle in the same round, forever confined to it; the year turns endlessly in its own footprints. I have no plans to invent new entertainments for you. There is nothing more I can devise to please you — it is the same show, over and over.

"Make room for others, as others made room for you. Equality is the soul of fairness. Who can complain of being included in a destiny that includes everyone? Live as long as you like; you will not shave one moment off the time you must spend dead. It is all for nothing: you will be every bit as long in that condition you fear so much as if you had died in your nurse's arms. Outlast as many centuries as you please — death will still be eternal.

"And yet I will put you in a condition where you have nothing left to complain about. Don't you know that when you are dead there will be no other you, still alive, to stand at your grave and mourn you lying in it? You will not even miss the life you are so worried about now. No one, then, longs for himself or for living; no regret for ourselves touches us at all. Death is less to be feared than nothing — if anything could be less than nothing. It is much less to you, if less is possible, than what you can see to be nothing at all.

"Death does not concern you, dead or alive. Alive, because you are; dead, because you are no more.

"And no one dies before his hour. The time you leave behind you was no more yours than the time that passed before you were born — it concerns you just as little. Look back: that whole eternity of time before you existed was nothing to you. Wherever your life ends, it is complete there. The value of living lies not in the length of days but in the use you make of them. A man may have lived long and yet lived little. Use time while you have it. Whether you have lived enough depends on your will, not on your count of years. Did you imagine you would never arrive at the place you were walking toward without rest? There is no road without an end. And if company eases the way, isn't the whole world traveling the very same road? All things, when your life is over, will follow you.

"Doesn't everything move in the same dance you dance? Is there anything that does not grow old along with you? A thousand men, a thousand animals, a thousand other creatures die in the very instant that you die. No night has ever followed day, no dawn has ever followed night, that did not hear, mingled with the wails of infants, the cries of grief that keep company with death and dark funerals.

"Why shrink back, if there is no way to retreat? You have seen plenty of men who were glad to die, since dying delivered them from great miseries. But have you ever seen one man who was sorry he had died? It is pure foolishness to condemn a thing you have never experienced — not in your own person, nor in anyone else's.

"Why do you complain of me, and of destiny? Do we do you any wrong? Should you govern us, or should we govern you? Your age may not be finished, but your life is. A small man is as much a man as a giant; neither men nor their lives are measured by the yard. Chiron refused immortality when his father Saturn — the god of time and duration himself — told him what its conditions were. Honestly consider how much more unbearable and painful an everlasting life would be to man than the life I have given him. If you did not have death, you would curse me eternally for keeping it from you. I have deliberately mixed a little bitterness into it, to keep you, once you saw how convenient it is, from embracing it too greedily and too rashly. And to settle you in the moderation I ask — neither sick of living nor in a hurry to die — I have tempered both of them between sweetness and pain.

"I taught Thales, the most eminent of your sages, that living and dying are matters of indifference. So when someone asked him, 'Why don't you die, then?' he answered, very wisely: 'Because it makes no difference.'

"Water, earth, air, fire, and the other parts of this structure of mine are no more the instruments of your life than they are the instruments of your death. Why fear your last day? It carries you no closer to your death than any day before it did. The last step does not cause the weariness — it only declares it. Every day travels toward death; the last one arrives."

These are the good lessons of our mother Nature.

I have often wondered why the face of death, whether we meet it in ourselves or in others, looks so much less dreadful to us in war than it does at home in our own houses — otherwise an army would be a parade of doctors and whimpering invalids — and why, since death is everywhere the same, peasants and plain working people meet it with so much more composure than everyone else. I honestly believe it is the terrible faces and trappings we dress it in that frighten us more than the thing itself: a whole new way of living; the cries of mothers, wives, and children; the visits of stunned and grieving friends; a crowd of pale, blubbering servants; a darkened room ringed with burning candles; our bedside besieged by doctors and preachers — in short, nothing but horror and ghostliness all around us. We are shrouded and buried before we are dead. Children are frightened even of their best friends when they see them in a mask, and so are we. The mask must be pulled off things as well as off people. Once it is removed, we will find underneath only the very same death that a servant or a poor chambermaid died the other day, without any fear and without any fuss. Happy the death that leaves us no time to arrange such ceremonies.

Of the Power of the Imagination

"Fortis imaginatio generat casum," say the schoolmen — a strong imagination creates the event itself.

I am one of the people most susceptible to the power of imagination. Everyone gets jostled by it; some get knocked flat. It cuts right through me, and since I haven't the strength to resist it, my whole strategy is to avoid it. I could live on nothing but cheerful, healthy company. The mere sight of another man's suffering causes me real physical pain, and I often find I've borrowed someone else's sensations for my own. A man coughing nonstop in my presence sets my own lungs and throat itching. I'm more reluctant to visit the sick I love and feel bound to than the sick I don't care about and barely look at — because I catch the disease I'm worrying over and take it home with me. So it doesn't surprise me at all that imagination hands out fevers, and sometimes death, to people who give it too much room and entertain it too willingly.

Simon Thomas was a great physician in his day. I remember running into him once at Toulouse, at the house of a rich old man with bad lungs. Discussing the cure with his patient, Thomas told him that one excellent remedy would be to make my visits so pleasant that I'd come often — and then to fix his eyes on the freshness of my face, and his thoughts on the liveliness and vigor glowing out of my youth, and to fill all his senses with the flourishing age I was in; by which means his constitution might possibly improve. What he forgot to add was that mine, at the same time, might get worse.

Gallus Vibius strained his mind so hard to understand the essence and workings of madness that he wrenched his own judgment out of its seat and could never get it back — and could boast that he'd become a fool by too much wisdom. There are men whose fear beats the executioner to the job: like the prisoner whose blindfold was removed so his pardon could be read to him, and who was found stone dead on the scaffold — killed by nothing but the stroke of his own imagination. We startle, tremble, go pale, and blush under imagination's pushes; lying in bed we feel our bodies shaken by its power, sometimes nearly to the point of dying of it. And boiling youth heats up so far in its sleep that it satisfies its desires in a dream — so that, as Lucretius says, as if the whole act were really completed, young men pour out the flood and stain their bedclothes.

It's nothing new to see horns sprout overnight on a forehead that had none at bedtime — but what happened to Cippus, king of Italy, is still worth remembering. He had spent a day as a delighted spectator at a bullfight, dreamed all night of horns on his head, and by the sheer force of imagination grew real ones there. Passion gave the son of Croesus the voice nature had denied him. Antiochus fell into a fever because the beauty of Stratonice had stamped itself too deep into his soul. Pliny claims he saw Lucius Cossitius, who turned from a woman into a man on her own wedding day. Pontanus and others report the same metamorphosis happening in Italy in more recent times — and through the intense longing of the person concerned and of his mother, as Ovid has it, "Iphis the boy paid the vows he had made as a girl."

I myself, passing through Vitry-le-François, saw a man whom the Bishop of Soissons had confirmed under the name Germain, but whom everyone in town had known as a girl named Mary until the age of twenty-two. He was, when I saw him, heavily bearded, old, and unmarried. He told us that as he was straining in a jump, his male organs emerged — and to this day the girls of that town have a song warning each other not to take big strides, or they'll turn into men like Mary Germain. It's no great wonder if this kind of accident happens often. If imagination has any power in such matters, it is so constantly and so fiercely bent on this one subject that — rather than have it relapse again and again into the same thought and the same violence of desire — it might be simpler to give these young women the thing they long for, once and for all.

Some attribute the scars of King Dagobert and of Saint Francis to the force of imagination. It is said to lift bodies right off the ground, sometimes. Celsus tells of a priest whose soul would be carried off into such ecstasy that his body lay a long while without sense or breath. Saint Augustine mentions another man who, at the sound of any sorrowful or mournful cries, would immediately fall into a faint, so far out of himself that it was useless to call him, shout in his ears, pinch him, or burn him until he came back on his own. Then he'd say he had heard the voices as if from far away, and had felt the pinching and the burning. And to prove this was no stubborn act of defiance against his own senses, the man had, all that while, neither pulse nor breath.

It's very likely that visions, enchantments, and all extraordinary effects of that kind owe their credit mainly to the power of imagination working on ordinary, more pliable souls — minds whose belief is so easily captured that they think they see what they do not see.

And I'm still not satisfied that those famous "knots" — the wedding charms our whole age is so obsessed with that people talk of almost nothing else, the spells supposed to tie up a bridegroom and keep him from consummating his marriage — are anything more than the voluntary workings of apprehension and fear. I know this by experience, in the case of a particular friend of mine, a man I can vouch for as I would for myself, who could not possibly fall under any suspicion of weakness in this department, and just as little of being bewitched. He had heard a companion of his tell the story of an extraordinary failure that had overtaken him at the worst possible moment. Later, finding himself in the same situation, the horror of that story suddenly seized his imagination so violently that he met the same fate. And from then on, the miserable memory of his disaster kept running through his mind and tyrannizing over him, and he was prone to relapse into the same misfortune.

He found a remedy for this fantasy in another fantasy: by frankly confessing the weakness in advance, declaring it to the woman before anything began, the agitation of his soul was somewhat relieved — since the failure was now expected, the pressure on him weighed less. Later, at times when he was in no such fear, when his thoughts were free and disentangled and his body in its true and natural state — being then at leisure to have the part examined, so to speak, and the fact communicated to the other party — he was completely cured of the trouble. Once a man has acquitted himself properly with a woman a single time, he is never again in danger of failing with her, except from some genuine and excusable weakness.

This disaster, in fact, is only to be feared in encounters where the soul is strained beyond measure with desire or with reverence — and especially when the opportunity comes unexpected and urgent. There's no way for a man to defend himself from a surprise like that; it throws him entirely out of order. I have known men who protected themselves from the mishap by arriving half-satisfied from elsewhere, on purpose to take the edge off their ardor; and others who, having grown old, find themselves less impotent for being less able; and one man who was cured by a friend's assurance that he carried a counter-charm guaranteed to protect him from enchantments. That story is not a bad one, so you shall have it.

A count of a very great family, with whom I was close, married a beautiful lady who had previously been courted by a man who was present at the wedding. All the count's friends were worried — especially an old lady, his kinswoman, who was presiding over the festivities and hosting them in her own house. She suspected the rival would try foul play with these sorceries, and she told me her fear. I told her to leave it to me.

I happened to have with me a flat plate of gold engraved with celestial figures, a charm against sunstroke and headaches — to work, it had to sit exactly on the seam of the skull, held in place by a ribbon tied under the chin. A piece of nonsense first cousin to the very nonsense we're discussing. Jacques Pelletier, who was living in my house, had given it to me as a great rarity, and it struck me I could put it to some use. I privately warned the count that he might well meet the same bad luck other bridegrooms had met, especially with a certain person in the house who would be delighted to do him that kind of favor — but he should go to bed boldly. I would do him the office of a friend, and would not, at need, spare a miracle that lay in my power, provided he swore on his honor to keep it absolutely secret. He was simply to give me an agreed sign, when they came in during the night to bring him his wedding posset — the custom here is to carry the bridegroom a hot drink in the middle of his wedding night — if things had gone badly.

His ears had been so battered, and his mind so thoroughly worked over by the eternal chatter about this business, that when the moment came he really did find himself tied up by the trouble of his imagination, and gave me the sign. I whispered to him to get up, as if to chase us all out of the room, and — in a joking way — to snatch my nightgown off my shoulders (we were nearly the same height), throw it over his own, and keep it on until he'd done what I told him. Which was this: when we had all left, he was to withdraw as if to relieve himself, repeat certain words three times and perform certain motions three times, and at each of the three, tie the ribbon I'd put into his hand around his waist, setting the medal that hung from it — figures arranged just so — exactly over his kidneys. Then, having on the third time cinched the ribbon so well that it could neither come loose nor slip, he was to go back to his business with full confidence — and not forget to spread my gown over the bed so that it covered them both.

These monkey tricks are the heart of the whole effect. Our fancy, once seduced, believes that means so strange must surely flow from some secret science: their very emptiness gives them weight and authority. In short, it is certain my figures proved themselves more venereal than solar — more active than protective. It was a sudden whim, mixed with a little curiosity, that drew me into an act so contrary to my nature; for I am an enemy of all subtle and counterfeit dealings and hate every kind of trickery, even in sport, even to someone's advantage. The act may not be vicious, but the manner of it is.

Amasis, king of Egypt, married Laodice, a very beautiful Greek girl, and though he was perfectly capable everywhere else, found himself quite another man with his wife — he could not enjoy her at all. He was so enraged that he threatened to kill her, suspecting witchcraft. As usual in matters that live in the fancy, she steered him toward religion; he made his vows to Venus, and found himself divinely restored the very first night after his offerings and sacrifices.

Now women are wrong to greet us with that disdainful, coy, scowling face that puts out our fire even as it lights our desire. The daughter-in-law of Pythagoras said that a woman who goes to bed with a man should take off her modesty with her petticoat, and put it back on with the same. The soul of the attacker, rattled by alarm after alarm, easily loses the power to perform. And whoever imagination has once played this trick on, and humiliated with the shame of it — and she plays it only at first acquaintance, because then a man is most ardent and eager, and most afraid of failing at this first account he gives of himself — having begun badly, he falls into such fever and spite over the accident that the trouble tends to stay with him on every occasion after.

Married people, with all their time before them, should never force the thing or even attempt it unless they find themselves fully ready. It is less shameful to fail to christen the wedding sheets — when a man feels himself all agitation and trembling — and to wait for another, more private, more composed opportunity, than to make himself permanently miserable over having stumbled and been routed in the first assault. Until possession is fairly taken, a man who knows himself subject to this weakness should feel his way gradually, with small trials and light advances, rather than stubbornly stake everything on conquering, once and for all, his own mutinous and unwilling faculties. Those who know their member to be naturally obedient need only take care to out-maneuver their fantasies.

We have good reason to remark on the ungovernable liberty of this member: so inconveniently insistent when we have no use for it, and so inconveniently failing when we need it most; contesting authority with the will so imperiously, refusing every solicitation of hand and mind with such proud obstinacy. And yet — though his rebellion is universally complained of, and the evidence used to condemn him — if he had hired me to plead his case, I might well bring his fellow members under suspicion of having framed him, out of pure envy of the importance and sweetness of his particular office: of having conspired to arm the whole world against him by maliciously charging him alone with a crime they all commit.

Because consider: is there one part of our bodies that doesn't regularly refuse to work when the will commands, and work away when the will forbids? Every one of them has passions of its own that wake it or put it to sleep without asking our leave. How often do the involuntary movements of our face betray the thoughts we meant to keep secret, and expose us to the people in the room? The same cause that animates this member also animates — without our knowledge — the lungs, the pulse, and the heart; the sight of a pleasing object spreads a flame through us, a small fever, before we know it's happening. And is it only these veins and these muscles that rise and fall without the consent not just of our will, but of our very knowledge? We don't order our hair to stand on end or our skin to shiver with fear or desire. The hands often wander where we haven't sent them. The tongue seizes up, and the voice freezes, at its own hour. Even when we have nothing to eat and would gladly forbid it, the appetite for food stirs up the parts subject to it, exactly like that other appetite — and abandons us, just as unseasonably, whenever it sees fit.

The vessels that serve to empty the belly have their own dilations and contractions, beyond and without our consent, just like those that empty the bladder. And as for the case Saint Augustine cites to defend the prerogative of the will — he saw a man who could command his rear end to produce as many discharges as he pleased — his commentator Vives caps it with a contemporary example of a man who could break wind in tune. None of which proves that organ purely obedient; for is anything usually more tumultuous or more indiscreet? And let me add one I know myself, so rude and unruly that for forty years it has marched its master toward one continuous, unbroken eruption, and looks likely to keep at it till he dies of it. I could wish I knew only from books how often a man's belly, denied a single puff of escape, brings him to the very edge of an agonizing death; and that the emperor who gave us liberty to break wind anywhere had also given us the power to do it.

But our will, on whose behalf this whole indictment is brought — with how much better cause might we charge her with mutiny and sedition, with irregularity and disobedience? Does she always will what we would have her will? Doesn't she often will exactly what we forbid — and to our obvious harm? Does she let herself be governed by the conclusions of our reason any more than the rest do?

To finish: I would ask, on behalf of the gentleman my client, that the court consider this. In the matter at hand, his cause is inseparably and indistinguishably joined with an accessory — and yet he alone is prosecuted, on arguments and charges that cannot possibly be brought against her; since her business, though it is sometimes to invite inopportunely, is never to refuse, and her invitations are, moreover, tacit and quiet ones. The malice and injustice of his accusers is therefore plain to see. But however the lawyers and judges may quarrel, nature will go on her own way in the meantime — and she would have done no more than justice if she had granted this member some special privilege: author of the only immortal work of mortals. A divine work, according to Socrates; and love, the desire for immortality, itself an immortal spirit.

One man, perhaps, by just such an effect of imagination, leaves his scrofula — the king's evil — behind him here, while his companion, coming after, carries his back into Spain. And this is why, in such matters, the mind has to be prepared in advance for the thing that's to be done. Why do physicians work so hard, beforehand, on their patients' credulity, with so many false promises of a cure, if not so that the effect of imagination can make up for the fraud in their potions? They know very well — one of the masters of their trade has left it in writing — that there are men for whom the mere sight of medicine does the work.

All these fancies have come into my head from a story told me by an apothecary in my late father's household — a blunt Swiss, of a nation not much given to vanity or lying — about a merchant he had long known at Toulouse: a sickly man, much afflicted with kidney stones, who often needed enemas and had his physicians prescribe various kinds, depending on the turns of his disease. When the enema was brought, none of the usual formalities was skipped — he tested it himself, to feel whether it was too hot. There he lay, flat on his front, everything performed with full ceremony — except the injection itself. The apothecary would withdraw, the patient would be settled as if he had truly received it, and the effect was identical to the real thing. And if the physician judged the operation insufficient, he would administer two or three more doses, in exactly the same fashion. My witness swore that — since the man paid as if he'd really had the medicine — the sick man's wife once tried to save money by having plain warm water put in; the effect exposed the trick, that kind proving useless, and they had to return to the original method.

A woman convinced she had swallowed a pin in a piece of bread cried and wailed with an unbearable pain in her throat, where she thought she felt it sticking. But a clever fellow who was brought in, seeing no swelling and nothing wrong from the outside, judged it was only a fancy taken from some crust that had scratched her on the way down. He made her vomit, and secretly tossed a bent pin into the basin. The moment the woman saw it, believing she had thrown it up, she felt her pain vanish. And I myself knew a gentleman who, three or four days after entertaining a large company at his house, bragged as a joke — for there was nothing to it — that he had served them baked cat. One young lady who'd been at the feast took such horror at this that she fell into violent vomiting and fever, and nothing could be done to save her.

Even the animals are subject to the force of imagination as much as we are. Look at dogs, who die of grief for their lost masters, and bark and tremble and twitch in their dreams; horses kick and whinny in their sleep.

Now all of this can be set down to the close kinship of soul and body, trading their fortunes back and forth. But it's quite another thing when imagination works not on one's own body but on someone else's. Just as an infected body passes its sickness to those nearby — as we see with plague, smallpox, and sore eyes, which run through whole households and whole cities, and as Ovid says, "look at sore eyes and your own eyes grow sore; many ills pass from body to body" — so imagination, violently stirred, lets fly an infection that can strike a target outside itself. The ancients held that certain women of Scythia, when roused to fury against a man, could kill him with a look alone. Tortoises and ostriches hatch their eggs just by gazing at them — which suggests their eyes send out some projecting power. And the eyes of witches are said to be aggressive and harmful: "some eye, I know not whose, is bewitching my tender lambs," as Virgil's shepherd complains.

Magicians are not much of an authority with me. But we do see by experience that women stamp the marks of their fancy on the children they carry in the womb — witness the one who gave birth to a Moor; and the girl from near Pisa, presented to the Emperor Charles, King of Bohemia, all rough and covered with hair, whom her mother said she had conceived that way because of a picture of John the Baptist hanging inside her bed-curtains.

It's the same with animals: witness Jacob's sheep, and the hares and partridges that the snow turns white in the mountains. At my own house, a little while ago, a cat was seen watching a bird at the top of a tree; they fixed their eyes on each other for some time, and at last the bird dropped, dead, straight into the cat's claws — either dazzled by the force of its own imagination or drawn by some attracting power in the cat. Those who enjoy field sports have surely heard the story of the falconer who fastened his gaze on a kite in the air and wagered that he could bring it down by the sheer power of his sight — and did, so it was said.

For the tales I borrow, I send the bill to the consciences of those I had them from. The reasonings are my own, and they stand on the proofs of reason, not of experience. Everyone is free to add his own examples; and whoever has none should still believe — considering the number and variety of accidents in this world — that there are plenty of them. If I don't apply them well, let someone else apply them for me. In the subject I'm treating here — our habits and our impulses — testimony and instances, however fabulous, serve as well as true ones, provided they're possible. Whether it really happened or not, at Rome or at Paris, to John or to Peter, it is still some turn the human mind can take, and that is what I'm usefully studying. I see it and I profit from it, in shadow as well as in substance. And among the various readings the histories offer, I pick out the rarest and most memorable for my own use. There are authors whose whole purpose is to relate what has happened. Mine, if I could manage it, would be to relate what can happen. The schools quite reasonably permit you to invent comparisons when you have none at hand. I don't use that license, though; on this point my scruples outdo all historical authority. In the examples I bring in here — of what I've heard, read, done, or said — I have forbidden myself to alter even the smallest, most trivial circumstance. My conscience does not falsify one jot. What my ignorance may do, I cannot answer for.

This is what makes me wonder, sometimes, whether a theologian or a philosopher — men of such exact and tender conscience and prudence — is fit to write history at all. How can they stake their good name on the faith of the crowd? How can they answer for the opinions of men they've never met, or pass off their guesses as solid coin? They would refuse to give sworn testimony before a judge about actions performed under their own eyes, with several witnesses present; and there's no man alive they know so intimately that they'd stand full guarantee for his intentions. For my part, I think it less risky to write about the past than the present — exactly because the writer then only has to account for borrowed truth that everyone knows he took on trust.

Some people urge me to write the events of my own time. They fancy I see them with an eye less blinded by passion than most, and at closer range, because fortune has given me access to the heads of the various factions. What they don't consider is that I would not take the trouble for the glory of Sallust himself — sworn enemy that I am of obligation, of diligence, of perseverance; that nothing is so contrary to my style as extended narrative, when I keep breaking off in my own writing for sheer want of breath; that I have no gift for composition or development worth anything, and am more ignorant than a child of the phrases and even the words used for the most ordinary things. That is why I have undertaken to say only what I can say, and have cut my subject to fit my strength. Were I to take some guide and follow his pace, I might not be able to keep up; and in the freedom of my own liberty I might publish judgments which, on better reflection and by reason's lights, would be illegitimate and punishable.

Plutarch would tell us, of what he has handed down, that the work of making his examples true and accurate belongs to others; but that they are useful to posterity, and presented with a luster that lights our way to virtue — that is his own work. In an old story, unlike a dose of medicine, there's no great danger in whether it happened this way or that.

Of Custom, and Not Easily Changing an Accepted Law

Whoever invented the story of the village woman and the calf understood the power of custom. She got into the habit of carrying a young calf in her arms, and kept it up every day as the animal grew — until, by sheer custom, she could carry it as a full-grown ox. For custom truly is a violent and treacherous schoolmistress. Little by little, slyly and unnoticed, she slips in the foot of her authority. Then, once time has helped her settle and fix it with that gentle, humble beginning, she pulls off the mask and shows a furious, tyrannical face — and by then we no longer have the courage, or even the strength, to lift our eyes against her. At every turn we catch her forcing and violating the rules of nature. As Pliny says, custom is the most effective master of all things.

I would point to Plato's cave in his Republic, and to the doctors, who so often surrender the reasoning of their own art to custom's authority. And to that king who trained his stomach, by habit, to live on poison; and the girl Albertus tells of, who lived on spiders. In that new world of the Indies, great nations were found, in very different climates, who ate spiders too — kept stocks of them, raised them for the table, along with grasshoppers, mice, lizards, and bats. In a time when such delicacies were scarce, a toad sold for six crowns. They cook all this and serve it up with a choice of sauces. Other peoples were found there for whom our diet, and the meat we eat, were poisonous and deadly.

The power of custom is enormous, as Cicero says: hunters will spend whole nights in the snow, or let the mountain sun roast them; boxers, battered by the leaded glove, never let out a groan.

These strange examples will not seem so strange if we consider what we experience every day: how much custom numbs our senses. We don't even need the stories about the people who live by the cataracts of the Nile, or what philosophers believe about the music of the spheres — that the heavenly bodies, being solid and smooth, touch and rub against one another as they turn, and can't help producing a marvelous harmony, whose changes and cadences drive the rounds and dances of the stars; and yet the ears of every creature down here, deafened and dulled like the Egyptians' by the constant noise, cannot perceive it, however great it is. Blacksmiths, millers, pewterers, forgemen, and armorers could never live inside the perpetual racket of their own trades if it struck their ears with the violence it strikes ours.

My perfumed jacket pleases my own nose at first; after I've worn it three days running, only the bystanders enjoy it. Stranger still, custom can fix its impression on our senses even across long gaps and interruptions — ask anyone who lives near church towers and the frequent noise of the bells. I sleep at home in a tower where a great bell rings out the Ave Maria every morning and evening. The noise shakes the tower itself, and at first it seemed unbearable to me; now I'm so used to it that I hear it without the least annoyance, and often without waking at all.

Plato once scolded a boy for playing at nuts. "You're scolding me for a very small thing," said the boy. "Custom," Plato replied, "is no small thing."

I find that our worst vices take their first bent in our most tender infancy, and that the heart of our education is in the nurse's hands. Mothers are delighted to watch a child wring a chicken's neck, or amuse itself by hurting a dog or a cat. And there are wise fathers in this world who take it as a fine mark of a martial spirit when they hear a son insult a poor peasant or a servant who doesn't dare answer back, or see him lord it over them — and a great sign of cleverness when he cheats a playmate with some piece of malicious trickery. Yet these are the true seeds and roots of cruelty, tyranny, and treason. They sprout there, and afterward shoot up vigorously and grow to monstrous size, cultivated by custom.

It is a very dangerous mistake to excuse these ugly inclinations because the child is young and the stakes are small. First, it is nature speaking — and her voice is all the more honest, her inward thoughts all the more undisguised, while she is weak and young. Second, the ugliness of cheating does not depend on the difference between gold coins and pins. I'd rather draw the conclusion the other way: since he cheats at pins, why wouldn't he cheat at coins? — instead of saying, as people do, "it's only pins; he would never do it for money." Children should be carefully taught to hate vices for their own substance. The natural deformity of those vices should be shown to them so plainly that they not only avoid them in their actions but loathe them in their hearts — so that the very thought of them, under whatever mask, is hateful.

I know this much about myself. Because I was brought up from childhood to deal plainly and straight, and took an aversion to every kind of trickery even in my childish games (and note this: the games of children are not games; they should be judged as their most serious actions), there is no game so small in which I don't feel — from inside, naturally, without study or effort — an extreme aversion to cheating. I shuffle and cut and make as much clatter with the cards, and keep as strict an account of farthings, as if they were gold doubloons. Winning or losing against my wife and daughter is the same to me as when I play in earnest with others, for serious money. At all times and in all places, my own eyes are enough to keep watch on my fingers. No one watches me more closely, and there is no one whose opinion I respect more.

The other day I saw, at my own house, a little fellow from Nantes, born without arms, who has trained his feet so well to do the work his hands should have done that they've half forgotten their natural job. In fact, he calls them his hands. He cuts things with them, loads and fires a pistol, threads a needle, sews, writes, takes off his hat, combs his hair, plays cards and dice — all with as much dexterity as anyone with the usual, more suitable limbs to help him. The money I gave him — he earns his living by showing off these feats — he took in his foot, the way we take it in our hand. I've seen another, still a boy, who flourished a two-handed sword and, if I may put it this way, handled a halberd with nothing but the movements of his neck and shoulders, having no hands; tossed them into the air and caught them again, threw a dagger, and cracked a whip as well as any coachman in France.

But custom shows her effects far more plainly in the strange impressions she stamps on our minds, where she meets less resistance. What can't she impose on our judgments and beliefs?
