Is there any opinion so fantastic — leaving aside the gross impostures of religions, which have so strangely besotted so many great nations and so many intelligent men; for that lies beyond the reach of human reason, and any error is more excusable in those who haven't been granted, by divine bounty, an extraordinary light from above — is there any opinion so extravagant, I say, that custom hasn't planted it and established it as law in whatever part of the world she pleased to exercise her power? So that old exclamation hits the mark exactly: as Cicero says, isn't it a shame for a natural philosopher — an observer and hunter of nature — to go looking for the testimony of truth in minds soaked through with custom?

I believe no fancy so absurd or ridiculous can enter the human imagination that it doesn't meet with the example of some public practice somewhere — and consequently get a footing and backing from our reason. There are peoples where the fashion is to turn your back on the man you greet, and never look at the person you mean to honor. There is a place where, whenever the king spits, the greatest ladies of his court hold out their hands to receive it; and another nation where the most eminent persons around him stoop to take up his excrement in a linen cloth.

Let me steal a little room here for a story. A French gentleman always blew his nose with his fingers — a thing very much against our fashion — and in defending himself for it (he was a man famous for his repartee), he asked me what privilege that filthy excretion enjoyed, that we should keep a fine handkerchief ready to receive it, and then — even better — fold it up carefully and carry it around in our pockets all day. Surely, he said, that was far more nauseating than seeing it thrown away, as we throw away everything else the body discards. I found he wasn't entirely without a point; and after I'd been in his company a while, his slovenly habit had grown familiar to me — though we make a face at it when we hear it reported of another country.

Miracles are miracles according to our ignorance of nature, not according to nature's essence. Being continually accustomed to a thing blinds the eye of our judgment. Barbarians are no more a wonder to us than we are to them, and with no more reason — as everyone would admit if, after touring those remote examples, we could settle down to reflect on our own and compare the two fairly. Human reason is a dye soaked almost equally into all our opinions and manners, whatever form they take — infinite in matter, infinite in diversity.

But back to my point. There are peoples where no one but his wife and children speaks to the king except through a tube. In one and the same nation, the virgins display openly the parts that modesty should tell them to hide, while the married women carefully cover and conceal them. A custom somewhere else is a kind of cousin to that one: there, chastity matters only in marriage, for unmarried women may give themselves to as many men as they please, and if they become pregnant, they may lawfully take medicine, in the sight of everyone, to destroy the child.

And in another place, if a tradesman marries, all the wedding guests of his own condition lie with the bride before he does — and the more of them there are, the greater her honor, and the higher the opinion of her capacity and strength. If an officer marries, it's the same; the same with a laborer or a man of low condition — except then it falls to the lord of the place to perform that office. And yet a severe fidelity is strictly required during the marriage afterward.

There are places where brothels of young men are kept for the pleasure of women; where the wives go to war alongside their husbands, and take their share not only of the dangers of battle but of the honors of command. Places where they wear rings not only through their noses, lips, cheeks, and toes, but heavy links of gold thrust through their nipples and buttocks; where at dinner they wipe their fingers on their thighs, their genitals, and the soles of their feet.

Places where children don't inherit — brothers and nephews do; and elsewhere nephews only, except in the succession of the prince. Where, to maintain their system of common property, sovereign magistrates take universal charge of the farming and distribute the produce according to each person's need. Where they mourn the death of children and feast at the death of old men. Where ten or twelve sleep in one bed, men and their wives together. Where women whose husbands die violent deaths may marry again, and the others may not. Where the condition of women is held in such contempt that they kill their female infants and buy wives from their neighbors to supply the need. Where husbands may repudiate their wives without showing any cause, but wives cannot leave their husbands for any cause whatever. Where husbands may sell their wives if they prove barren. Where they boil the bodies of their dead, then pound them to a pulp, mix it with their wine, and drink it. Where the most coveted burial is to be eaten by dogs — elsewhere, by birds.

Where they believe the souls of the blessed live in perfect freedom, in delightful fields stocked with every delicacy, and that it is these souls, repeating the words we utter, that we call Echo. Where they fight in the water and shoot their arrows with deadly accuracy while swimming. Where, as a sign of submission, they hunch their shoulders up and hang their heads down — and take off their shoes when they enter the king's palace. Where the eunuchs who guard the sacred women have their lips and noses cut off besides, so that no one can love them. Where the priests put out their own eyes, the better to know their demons and receive their oracles.

Where every man makes himself a god of whatever he likes best — the hunter of a lion or a fox, the fisherman of some fish — with idols for every human action and passion; and there the sun, the moon, and the earth are the chief deities, and the way to swear an oath is to touch the ground while looking up at the sky. Where both meat and fish are eaten raw. Where the greatest oath is to swear by the name of some dead person of good reputation, laying a hand on his tomb. Where the new year's gift the king sends each year to the princes who are his vassals is fire: when it is brought, all the old fire is put out, and the neighboring peoples are bound to come fetch the new fire, each for themselves, on pain of high treason. Where, when the king retires from governing to give himself wholly to devotion (which happens often), his next successor is obliged to do the same, and the right to the kingdom passes to the third in line. Where they vary the form of government as affairs seem to demand: depose the king when they think fit, set up certain elders to govern in his place, and sometimes hand it over to the common people. Where men and women alike are both circumcised and baptized. Where the soldier who, in one battle or several, has managed to present seven enemy heads to the king is made a noble. Where they live in that rare and unsociable belief that the soul is mortal. Where the women give birth without pain or fear. Where the women wear copper plates on both legs; and if a louse bites them, they are bound, as a point of courage, to bite it back; and they dare not marry until they have first offered their virginity to the king, if he cares to accept it.

Where the ordinary greeting is to touch a finger to the ground and then point it up toward the sky. Where the men carry loads on their heads and the women on their shoulders. Where the women make water standing and the men squatting. Where they send their blood as a token of friendship, and offer incense, as to gods, to the men they wish to honor. Where kindred may not marry — not just to the fourth degree, but to any degree however remote. Where children nurse for four years, and often twelve — and in that same place it is held fatal to give a child the breast on its first day of life. Where correcting the boys belongs especially to the fathers, and the girls to the mothers — the punishment being to hang them by the heels in the smoke. Where they circumcise the women. Where they eat every kind of herb, with no scruple but the badness of the smell. Where everything stands open — the finest houses, furnished in the richest style, without doors, windows, trunks, or chests to lock — and a thief there is punished double what he would be anywhere else. Where they crack lice with their teeth like monkeys, and find it horrible to see one killed with a fingernail. Where in all their lives they never cut their hair or pare their nails; and in another place, they pare the nails of the right hand only, letting the left grow for ornament and show. Where they let the hair on the right side grow as long as it will and shave the other side; and in the neighboring provinces, some let their hair grow long in front and some behind, shaving the rest close.

Where parents rent out their children to their guests, and husbands their wives. Where a man may get his own mother with child, and fathers make use of their own daughters or sons, without scandal. Where, at their solemn feasts, they lend their children to one another, with no regard for nearness of blood. In one place men feed on human flesh; in another, it is counted a pious duty for a man to kill his father at a certain age. Elsewhere, fathers decide the fates of their children while they are still in their mothers' wombs — these to be kept and carefully raised, those to be abandoned or done away with. Elsewhere the old husbands lend their wives to young men; and in another place wives are held in common, without offense; and in one place in particular, the women count it a mark of honor to wear as many gaily fringed tassels on the hem of their dress as they have had lovers.

Moreover, hasn't custom made a whole republic of women, by themselves? Hasn't she put weapons in their hands, raised them armies, and made them fight battles? And doesn't she teach the most ignorant common folk, by her bare precept, lessons that all the philosophy in the world could never beat into the heads of the wisest men? For we know of entire nations where death was not only despised but welcomed with the greatest celebration; where children of seven let themselves be whipped to death without changing expression; where riches were in such contempt that the poorest citizen would not have stooped to pick up a purse of gold crowns. And we know of regions rich in every kind of provision where, all the same, the most ordinary diet — and the one they liked best — was nothing but bread, cress, and water. And didn't custom work that miracle in Chios, that in seven hundred years no maid or wife was ever known to commit an act against her honor?

To sum up: in my opinion, there is nothing custom does not do, or cannot do; and Pindar had every reason to call her the ruler of the world.

The man who was seen beating his father answered, when reproached for it, that it was the custom of his family: his father had beaten his grandfather the same way, his grandfather his great-grandfather — "and this boy," he said, pointing to his son, "will beat me when he comes to my age." And the father whom his son was dragging and hauling through the streets ordered him to stop at a certain door, because he himself had dragged his own father only that far — that being the fixed limit of the hereditary outrage the sons of that family traditionally practiced on the fathers. It is as much by custom as by infirmity, says Aristotle, that women tear their hair, bite their nails, and eat coal and earth — and more by custom than by nature that men abuse one another's bodies.

The laws of conscience, which we claim are born of nature, are born of custom. Each of us holds an inward reverence for the opinions and manners approved and received among his own people; he cannot break from them without great reluctance, or conform to them without applauding himself. In times past, when the people of Crete wanted to curse someone, they prayed the gods to entangle him in some bad custom.

But the principal effect of custom's power is to seize and grip us so completely that it is hardly in us to wrestle free of her grasp, or to come back to ourselves enough to consider and weigh what she commands. The truth is, because we drink her in with our mother's milk, and because the face of the world presents itself in this posture to our first sight, we seem born on the condition of following this track. And the common notions we find in repute all around us, poured into our minds with our fathers' seed, appear to be the universal and natural ones. From which it comes to pass that whatever is off the hinges of custom is believed to be off the hinges of reason — how unreasonably, for the most part, God knows.

If everyone who heard a good maxim did what those of us who study ourselves have learned to do — stopped at once to ask how it touches his own private case — he would discover that it was less a fine saying than a sharp lash across the everyday stupidity of his own judgment. But people take the precepts and warnings of truth as addressed to the public at large, never to themselves. Instead of applying them to their own conduct, they file them away in memory — ignorantly, and to no profit at all. But back to the empire of custom.

People raised in liberty, who have answered to no authority but their own will, look on every other form of government as monstrous and against nature. People used to monarchy do exactly the same. And whatever chance fortune gives them to change — even when they have just escaped, at enormous cost, from one master who weighed on them — they run, at the same cost, to set up another. They cannot manage to hate subjection itself.

It is custom's doing that everyone is content with the spot where nature planted him. The Highlanders of Scotland do not pine for Touraine, and the Scythians do not pine for Thessaly. When Darius asked certain Greeks what it would take to get them to adopt the Indian custom of eating their dead fathers — for that was the Indian practice, in the belief that the noblest tomb they could give them was inside their own bodies — the Greeks answered that nothing on earth could hire them to do it. But when he then tried to persuade the Indians to give up their custom and burn their fathers' bodies in the Greek manner, they were even more horrified at the suggestion. Everyone does the same, for habit hides from us the true face of things. As Lucretius says, nothing is at first so grand or so marvelous that, little by little, we do not come to wonder at it less.

I once took it on myself to defend one of our own practices — one accepted with absolute authority for many leagues around — and not content, as people usually are, to prop it up with law and precedent, I dug down to its origin. I found the foundation so weak that I, whose job was to convince others, very nearly talked myself out of it. This is the recipe Plato relies on, in his Laws, to cure the unnatural loves of his age — and he rates it sovereign: that public opinion condemn them, that the poets and every other kind of writer tell horrible stories about them. By the power of that recipe, the most beautiful daughters no longer tempt their fathers' lust, nor the handsomest brothers their sisters' desire; the mere fables of Thyestes, Oedipus, and Macareus, sung into the tender brains of children, have planted the wholesome belief there. Chastity is, in truth, a great and shining virtue, and its usefulness is well enough known. But to argue for it from nature, at its true value, is exactly as hard as it is easy to argue for it from custom, laws, and precepts. The fundamental, universal reasons are dark and difficult to dig out, and our masters either skim over them or, not daring to touch them at all, dive straight into the shelter of custom, where they puff themselves up and crow to their hearts' content. Yet those who refuse to be drawn from the original source commit a still greater error and land in wild opinions — witness Chrysippus, who scattered through so many of his writings how little he made of incestuous unions, however close the relation.

Whoever would shake himself free of custom's violent prejudice will find a good many things accepted with total, unquestioning conviction that have no support at all except the hoary head and wrinkled face of ancient usage. But once the mask is off and things are referred to the verdict of truth and reason, he will feel his judgment turned completely upside down — and yet set back on much surer footing. For example, I will ask him: what could be stranger than to see a whole people bound to obey laws they have never understood? Tied, in everything domestic — marriages, gifts, wills, sales, purchases — to rules they cannot possibly know, since the rules are neither written nor published in their own language, so that they must buy both the interpretation and the use of them? This is not the clever advice Isocrates gave his king — to make his subjects' trade and business free, open, and profitable, and their quarrels and lawsuits costly, loaded with heavy fees and penalties. It is something far more monstrous: putting reason itself up for sale, and turning the laws into merchandise. I count myself lucky that, as our historians tell it, the first man to stand up to Charlemagne when he tried to impose Latin and imperial laws on us was a Gascon gentleman — a countryman of mine.

And what could be more savage than a nation where, by lawful custom, the office of judge is bought and sold, where verdicts are paid for in cash, and where justice may be legitimately denied to anyone who cannot afford it? Where this trade stands in such repute that it creates, alongside the three ancient estates of church, nobility, and people, a fourth estate of wrangling lawyers — who, holding the laws in their hands and sovereign power over men's lives and fortunes, form a body apart from the nobility. From which it follows that we have double laws — the laws of honor and the laws of justice — flatly opposed on many points. The nobles condemn a man who swallows an insult as harshly as the courts condemn a man who avenges one. By the code of arms, whoever puts up with an affront is stripped of rank and honor; by the civil law, whoever takes revenge to clear his name incurs the death penalty. Go to court to repair an injury to your honor, and you disgrace yourself; refuse to go, and the law censures and punishes you. Of these two so different systems, both answering to one sovereign, the one is in charge of peace, the other of war; one takes the profit, the other the honor; one the wisdom, the other the virtue; one the word, the other the deed; one justice, the other valor; one reason, the other force; one wears the long robe, the other the short — and so they split the world between them.

As for indifferent things, like clothes — anyone wishing to call them back to their true purpose, which is the service and comfort of the body, and on which their original grace and fitness depend, will find plenty to work with. For the most fantastical inventions imaginable, I give you, among others, our flat caps; that long tail of velvet hanging from our women's heads with its particolored trimmings; and that vain and useless model of a member which we cannot decently so much as name — and which we nevertheless parade in public. Still, none of these considerations will persuade a man of understanding to break with the common fashion. On the contrary, it seems to me that all singular, private fashions are marks of folly and vain affectation rather than of sound reason, and that a wise man ought, inwardly, to withdraw his soul from the crowd and keep it free there, with full power to judge things as they are — but outwardly, to follow the fashion of his time completely. Public society has no claim on our thoughts; but everything else — our actions, our labors, our fortunes, our very lives — we are to lend and surrender to its service and to common opinion. So did that good and great Socrates, who refused to save his life by disobeying the magistrate, even a thoroughly wicked and unjust magistrate. For it is the rule of rules, the general law of laws, that everyone observe those of the place where he is. As the Greek line has it, it is good to obey the laws of one's country.

And now to another point. It is very doubtful whether any change in an accepted law, whatever it may be, can bring a benefit as manifest as the danger and trouble of altering it. A government is a structure of many different parts and members, joined and fitted together so tightly that you cannot stir a single brick or stone without the whole body feeling it. The lawgiver of the Thurians ordained that whoever proposed to abolish an old law or establish a new one should present himself to the people with a halter around his neck — so that if the innovation was not approved by everyone, he could be hanged on the spot. And the lawgiver of Sparta spent his life extracting from his citizens a faithful promise that none of his laws would be broken. The Spartan magistrate who so roughly cut the two strings Phrynis had added to the lyre never stopped to ask whether the addition improved the harmony or made the instrument fuller and more complete. It was enough to condemn the invention that it was a novelty — an alteration of the old way. That is also the meaning of the old rusty sword carried before the magistracy of Marseilles.

For my own part, I have a great distaste for novelty, whatever face or pretense it wears, and I have reason — I have been an eyewitness of the great evils it produces. The evils that have pressed on us for so many years are not all its doing; but one may fairly say it has accidentally fathered and bred the mischief and ruin that have come since, even the mischief done without it and against it. It is innovation, chiefly, that we must accuse for these disorders. Alas, as Ovid says — I suffer wounds made by my own weapons.

Those who give the first shake to a state are nearly always the first to be swallowed in its ruin. The fruits of public upheaval are rarely enjoyed by the man who set it in motion; he beats and stirs the water for another man's net. The unity and weave of this monarchy, this grand edifice, having been ripped and torn in its old age by this thing called innovation, has lain open ever since — a rent giving easy entrance to every injury. Royal majesty comes down with difficulty from the summit to the middle; from the middle to the bottom, it falls and tumbles headlong. But if the inventors do the greater mischief, the imitators are the more vicious — throwing themselves into examples whose horror and punishment they have felt themselves. And if there is any degree of honor in doing harm, the latecomers must concede to the first movers the glory of the invention and the courage of the first attempt. Every kind of new disorder draws, from that primal and ever-flowing spring, its examples and precedents for unsettling our government. In the very laws made to remedy the first evil, we can read the beginnings and the pretexts of every sort of wicked enterprise. What Thucydides said of the civil wars of his time is happening to us: to flatter the public vices, men christened them with new and milder names, sweetening and disguising their true titles — and all this, if you please, to reform our conscience and our belief. "Fine words, truly," as Terence says. But the fairest pretext for innovation is of very dangerous consequence; as Livy puts it, no change from the ancient ways deserves the benefit of the doubt.

And to speak my mind freely: it argues a strange self-love and a vast presumption to prize one's own opinions so highly that the public peace must be overthrown to establish them — to usher in so many certain evils, and so dreadful a corruption of manners, as civil war and political upheaval always bring in their train, and to usher them into the bowels of one's own country. Is there worse housekeeping than to set up so many certain, known vices against errors that are merely contested and debatable? Are there worse kinds of vice than those committed against a man's own conscience and the natural light of his own reason? The Roman Senate, in its dispute with the people over the administration of religion, was bold enough to pay them off with this evasion: that such matters concerned the gods more than the Senate, and the gods themselves would see that their rites were not profaned. It matches the oracle's answer to the people of Delphi who, fearing a Persian invasion in the Median war, asked Apollo what to do with the holy treasure of his temple — hide it, or carry it elsewhere? He answered that they should move nothing, and look after themselves; he was sufficient to take care of what belonged to him.

The Christian religion bears every mark of the highest usefulness and justice, but none more plainly than its strict command to all alike: obey the civil magistrate; uphold and defend the laws. And what a wonderful example divine wisdom has left us of this — that to accomplish the salvation of mankind, and to conduct its glorious victory over death and sin, it would do so no other way than at the mercy of our ordinary forms of justice, submitting the progress and outcome of so high and so saving a work to the blindness and injustice of our customs and usages, sacrificing the innocent blood of so many of the elect, and the long loss of so many years, to the ripening of that priceless fruit. There is a vast difference between the man who follows the forms and laws of his country and the man who undertakes to regulate and change them. The first pleads simplicity, obedience, and example in his excuse; whatever he does cannot be charged to malice — at worst, it is misfortune. For who is there, asks Cicero, whom antiquity cannot move, attested and sealed by the fairest monuments? And as Isocrates says, falling short is nearer to moderation than overshooting. The other man is playing a far riskier game. Whoever takes it on himself to choose and to alter usurps the authority of judging, and had better look hard at what he is doing — had better be sure he sees clearly the defect in what he would abolish and the virtue in what he would introduce.

This very ordinary consideration is what settled me in my station and kept even my most extravagant, unruly youth under the rein: not to load my shoulders with so great a weight as to make myself answerable for a science of that importance — not to dare, in this, what in my better and riper judgment I would not dare in the easiest matters I had been taught, where rashness of judgment costs nothing. It seems to me deeply unjust to try to subject public, established customs and institutions to the weakness and instability of a private and particular fancy — for private reason has only a private jurisdiction — and to attempt against divine law what no government would tolerate against the civil laws. Human reason has far more dealings with civil laws, and yet even they are sovereignly judged by their own proper judges; the utmost skill can do no more than expound and extend the law and custom received — never twist it, never innovate upon it. If divine providence has sometimes gone beyond the rules to which it has necessarily bound us, that is no license for us to do the same. Those are masterstrokes of the divine hand — not for us to imitate, but to admire: extraordinary examples, marks of express and particular purpose, of the order of miracles, set before us as displays of its almighty power, equally beyond our rules and our strength. It would be folly and impiety to try to reproduce them. We are not to follow them, but to contemplate them with the deepest reverence — acts of God's own person, and not for us. Cotta puts it very aptly: in matters of religion, he says, I follow the high priests Coruncanius, Scipio, and Scaevola — not Zeno, Cleanthes, or Chrysippus.

God knows, in our present quarrel — this civil war, where there are a hundred articles to strike out and put in, great and weighty ones — how many men can truly boast of having exactly and thoroughly weighed the grounds and reasons of the two parties? It is a number, if it makes a number at all, that would hardly trouble our peace. But all the rest — where are they headed? Under what flags do they march, in what quarter do they camp? Their remedies work like other weak and badly applied medicines: the humors they meant to purge they have only heated and aggravated by the struggle, and left in us still. The potion was too weak to purge, but strong enough to weaken us. It does not work; we keep it in our bodies; and all we reap from the operation is internal cramps and aches.

So it is, nevertheless, that Fortune — who keeps her authority above anything we can do or say — sometimes confronts us with a necessity so urgent that the laws simply must give a little ground. And when you are resisting the spread of an innovation that forces its way in by violence, holding yourself everywhere and in everything within bounds and rules — against men who have the power, men for whom anything is lawful that advances their design, who recognize no law or rule except what serves their purpose — that is a dangerous obligation and an intolerable inequality. For as Seneca says, putting faith in the treacherous opens the door to harm.

The ordinary discipline of a healthy state makes no provision for these extraordinary accidents. It presupposes a body that stands firm in its principal members and functions, and a common consent to obedience and observance. Proceeding lawfully is a cold, heavy, constrained business, and no match for proceeding headstrong and unbridled. To this day it is thrown in the teeth of those two great men, Octavius and Cato — in the civil wars of Sulla and of Caesar — that they would rather let their country suffer the last extremities than rescue their fellow citizens at the expense of its laws, or be guilty of any innovation. For in truth, in those final necessities where there is no other remedy, it would perhaps be wiser to stoop and yield a little, to take the blow, than to resist with no possibility of doing good and so give violence the occasion to trample everything underfoot. Better to make the laws do what they can, when they cannot do what they would. So did the Spartan king who suspended them for twenty-four hours; so did the man who, for once, shifted a day in the calendar; so did Alexander the Great, who made a second May out of the month of June. The Spartans themselves, those most religious observers of their country's laws, found themselves pinched by one of their own edicts, which expressly forbade choosing the same man twice as admiral — while their affairs absolutely required that Lysander take that command again. So they made one Aratus admiral; true — but Lysander went along as general of the navy. By the same subtlety, one of their ambassadors, sent to Athens to obtain the repeal of a certain decree, was told by Pericles that it was forbidden to take down the tablet on which a law had once been posted. He advised Pericles simply to turn it around — for that was not forbidden. And Plutarch praises Philopoemen for this: that being born to command, he knew how to command not only according to the laws, but to command the laws themselves, when the public necessity required it.

Of the Education of Children

To Madame Diane de Foix, Comtesse de Gurson

I have never met a father who would disown his son, however stunted or misshapen the boy turned out. That doesn't mean the father can't see the defects — unless he's completely drunk on paternal affection, he sees them perfectly well. But the boy is still his.

It's the same with me. I see better than anyone that what I write here amounts to the daydreams of a man who only nibbled the outer crust of learning in his youth, and kept nothing but a vague, shapeless picture of it. A little of everything and nothing of the whole — the French way. I know, in general terms, that medicine exists, and law; that mathematics has four branches; and roughly what each of these is aiming at. Maybe I even know, in a general way, what the sciences claim to do for our lives. But to dig deeper than that — to have worn out my brains studying Aristotle, the reigning monarch of modern learning, or to have devoted myself seriously to any single branch of knowledge — I have never done it. There is no art whose basic outlines I could sketch. The lowest schoolboy in the lowest form can call himself more learned than I am; I couldn't even quiz him on his first lesson. If I'm ever forced to try, I have to save face by asking him some broad, clumsy questions about general things — testing his natural intelligence with a lesson as foreign to him as his lessons are to me.

I have never settled down seriously with any book of solid learning except Plutarch and Seneca. And with those, like the Danaides with their leaking jars, I am forever filling up and forever draining out. Some of it drips onto this paper; almost none of it stays in me. History is my real hunting ground when it comes to reading. Poetry too, which I love with a special tenderness — because, as Cleanthes said, just as a voice forced through the narrow tube of a trumpet comes out sharper and stronger, so a thought packed into the harmony of verse springs out harder and hits the mind with a cleaner, more pleasing blow.

As for the natural faculties I have — and these essays are exactly that, an attempt to test them — I feel them bending under the load. My imagination and judgment only grope along in the dark, tripping and stumbling. And when I have gone as far as I can go, I am never satisfied; I can still see more country ahead of me, vast and blurred, wrapped in clouds I cannot get through. I write about whatever comes into my head, using nothing but my own natural equipment. So when I happen — as I often do — to stumble in some good author across the very subjects I've tried to handle (as I did just recently in Plutarch, on the power of imagination), and I see how weak and limp I look next to those better writers, so heavy, so flat — I pity myself, or despise myself, take your pick.

Still, I take comfort in this: my opinions often have the honor and good luck of matching theirs. I'm walking the same road, even if I'm a long way behind, and at least I can say, "Yes — that's exactly it." I also have one ability not everyone is blessed with: I can see the enormous distance between them and me. And despite that, I let my own inventions run their course, low and feeble as they are, without patching or plastering over the flaws the comparison exposes. Frankly, you need a strong back to keep pace with those people.

The reckless scribblers of our age do the opposite. Into their laborious nothings they paste whole passages and pages of ancient authors, hoping the borrowed brilliance will dress up their own work. It does the reverse. The contrast is so glaring that their own writing looks more sallow and deformed beside it — they lose far more than they gain.

The philosophers Chrysippus and Epicurus took the two opposite extremes here. Chrysippus stuffed his books not just with other authors' sayings but with entire works — in one of them, the whole Medea of Euripides — which led Apollodorus to remark that if you removed everything that wasn't his, you'd be left with blank paper. Epicurus, on the other hand, left behind three hundred volumes without a single quotation in any of them.

The other day I had a little adventure of this kind. I was reading a French book, dozing my way through a long stretch of words so dull, so insipid, so empty of wit or common sense that they were French words and nothing more. After this long, tedious slog I suddenly hit a passage that was lofty, rich, soaring up to the clouds. If the slope up to it had been gradual, or the climb gentle, there might have been some excuse. But it was a sheer cliff, so completely cut off from everything around it that the first six words launched me into another world — and from up there I could see how deep and low the valley was that I'd come from. I have never had the heart to go back down into it since. If I decked out one of my own pieces with rich plunder like that, it would only spotlight the dullness of everything else I'd written.

Criticizing in others a fault I commit myself seems no more unreasonable to me than condemning — as I often do — other people's faults when I find them in me. Faults should be called out wherever they live; no sanctuary. I know perfectly well how boldly I keep trying to rise to the level of what I steal, to make my own style march alongside it — even with the rash hope of fooling my reader's eye so they can't tell the difference. But when I manage it, it's as much by careful placement as by any invention or strength of my own. Besides, I don't take on the whole army of these champions, or even fight any one of them hand to hand. I engage them in quick raids and light skirmishes. I don't grapple; I test their strength and never push as far as I pretend to. If I could actually hold my own with them, I'd be doing well — because I only ever attack them where they are strongest.

But to cover yourself head to toe in another man's armor, as I've seen some do, so that not even your fingertips show; to build a whole work out of old inventions patched together here and there with your own rubbish (easy enough for anyone with a bit of schooling, on an ordinary subject), and then to try to hide the theft and pass it off as your own — that is first of all dishonest and small-minded. Having nothing of their own worth a reputation, these people try to buy one with goods they have no title to. And second, it's ridiculous: they settle for the ignorant applause of the crowd, won by a pitiful con, while cheapening themselves in the eyes of intelligent readers — who turn up their noses at all this borrowed plaster, and whose praise is the only praise worth having. For my part, there is nothing I would not rather do than that. I only say so much about others to better explain myself.

None of this is aimed at the writers of centos — the patchwork poems — who announce themselves as exactly that. I have known some very clever ones in my time, particularly one writing under the name Capilupus, not to mention the ancients. These are genuinely intelligent men who show it both in that form and in others — Lipsius, for instance, in the learned and laborious weave of his Politics.

However that may be, and however trifling these foolish efforts of mine are, I never meant to hide them — any more than I'd hide a portrait of myself, bald and graying, in which the painter has given you not some ideal face but my own. These are my personal opinions and fancies. I offer them as what I believe, not as what anyone else ought to believe. My only aim here is to reveal myself — and tomorrow's self may be a different one, if some new lesson happens to change me. I have no authority to be believed, and I don't want any; I'm too aware of how badly educated I am to go instructing anyone else.

Now — someone who had read the previous chapter told me at my house the other day that I should have said more about the education of children.

Well, madam, if I had any competence on this subject, I couldn't put it to better use than as a gift to the little man who will soon make his happy arrival (you are too generous to begin with anything but a boy). Having played such a part in arranging your marriage, I have a certain personal stake in the greatness and prosperity of everything that comes of it. Besides, you have had first claim on my service for so long that I'm bound to wish honor and advantage on everything that concerns you. But truthfully, all I understand about the matter is this: the greatest and hardest problem in human knowledge is the raising of children.

It's like farming. The work before planting, and the planting itself, is sure, plain, and well understood. But once the thing planted comes to life — that's when the real work begins: more skill, more care, far more difficulty in cultivating it and bringing it to perfection. So with people. Getting children is easy; once they're born, then comes the trouble, the worry, the care of training them up properly. At that tender age the signs of their inclinations are so faint, the promises so unreliable, that it's nearly impossible to build any solid judgment on them. Look at Cimon, look at Themistocles, and a thousand others who completely defied the expectations people had of them. Bear cubs and puppies show their natural temperament right away. But people, the moment they grow up, take on habits, sign up for opinions, conform to laws and customs, and easily alter — or at least disguise — their real disposition. And yet nature's bent is hard to force. The result is that, having picked the wrong road, we often spend enormous effort and years of a child's life training him for things he is by his nature completely unsuited for. Even so, in this difficulty, my opinion is clear: aim them at the best and most rewarding studies, and don't fuss too much over those faint childhood omens — to which Plato, in his Republic, gives far too much weight, I think.

Madam, learning is a great ornament and a marvelously useful tool, especially for people raised to your level of fortune. In truth it can't do its proper work in mean and lowly hands. It is far readier to lend its strength to conducting a war, governing a people, or negotiating the friendship of princes and foreign nations than to constructing a syllogism, arguing a lawsuit, or prescribing a dose of pills. So, madam — confident that you won't leave this essential piece out of your children's education, you who have tasted its sweetness yourself and come from a learned line (we still have the writings of the old Counts of Foix, the ancestors of both you and your husband the Count, and your uncle, Monsieur de Candale, brings out new ones every day, extending your family's claim on this quality for ages to come) — I will presume to offer your ladyship one particular notion of my own, contrary to the usual method. It is all I can contribute to your service in this business.

The duties of the tutor you choose for your son — and the whole success of his education hangs on that choice — involve many other important parts that I won't touch, since I have nothing worthwhile to add to the standard advice. And on the point where I do presume to advise, he should follow me only as far as it seems sensible to him.

So: for a boy of good family who pursues learning not for profit (an aim too low to deserve the grace and favor of the Muses, and one that puts a man in service and debt to others), and not so much for show as for his own inner use — to furnish and enrich himself within, wanting to turn out an accomplished gentleman rather than a mere scholar — for such a boy, I would have his people take care to choose him a tutor with a well-made head rather than a well-filled one. Look for both, certainly; but of the two, value character and judgment above raw learning. And let this man do his job by a new method.

Tutors ordinarily never stop thundering in our ears, as if they were pouring knowledge into a funnel, while our job is simply to repeat back what we've been told. I'd have the tutor correct this from the start. According to the capacity of the mind in his hands, he should put it through its paces — letting the boy taste things for himself, choose and discern for himself; sometimes clearing the path for him, sometimes letting him clear it alone. I don't want the tutor doing all the inventing and all the talking; I want him to listen to his pupil talk in turn. Socrates, and Arcesilaus after him, had their students speak first and spoke to them afterward. As Cicero says, the authority of the teacher is very often an obstacle to those who want to learn.

It's good to make the boy trot out in front, like a young horse, so the tutor can judge his gait and figure out how much to slow his own pace to match the pupil's strength. Get that proportion wrong and we ruin everything. Knowing how to hit it, and stay within it, is one of the hardest things I know; it takes a lofty and very well-tempered soul to stoop to a child's pace and guide it. I walk more firmly and surely uphill than down.

Those who follow our usual practice — one lesson, one pace, for a roomful of boys with completely different minds and abilities — are profoundly mistaken. No wonder that out of a whole crowd of pupils, barely two or three get anything worthwhile from their schooling.

Let the master demand an account not just of the bare words of the lesson but of its meaning and substance; and let him judge the boy's progress not by the testimony of his memory but by the testimony of his life. Make him take what he has just learned and turn it into a hundred different shapes, fit it to a hundred different subjects, to see whether he has truly grasped it and made it his own — pacing the instruction by Plato's method of teaching. Spitting food back up exactly as you swallowed it is proof of rawness and indigestion. The stomach hasn't done its work unless it changes the form and condition of what it was given to digest.

Our minds work only on credit, tied and bound to the lead of someone else's fancy, captive to the authority of someone else's lesson. We've been so broken to the harness that we have no free stride of our own. Our vigor and liberty are gone. As Seneca says, they never come out of wardship.

I was privately taken, in Pisa, to meet a worthy man — but such an Aristotelian that his standing thesis was this: the touchstone and measure of all sound thinking and all truth is conformity to Aristotle's teaching; outside of that, everything is emptiness and chimera, since Aristotle saw everything and said everything. That position, interpreted a bit too broadly and unfairly, once landed him in serious and lasting danger from the Inquisition in Rome.

Let the tutor make the boy sift everything he reads, and lodge nothing in his head on bare authority or on trust. Aristotle's principles should be no more sacred to him than those of Epicurus or the Stoics. Set the whole range of opinions in front of him: he will choose if he can — and if he can't, he'll remain in doubt. As Dante says, "doubting pleases me no less than knowing."

If he embraces the opinions of Xenophon and Plato through his own reasoning, they will no longer be theirs; they will be his. Whoever follows another follows nothing. He finds nothing — he isn't even looking for anything. As Seneca says, we are under no king; let each man claim himself.

Let him at least know that he knows. He should absorb the spirit of these thinkers, not memorize their precepts. And let him cheerfully forget where he got it all, so long as he knows how to make it his own. Truth and reason belong to everyone; they're no more the property of the first man who said them than of the last man to repeat them. It is no more "according to Plato" than according to me, since he and I see and understand it the same way. Bees raid the flowers here and there, but afterward they make honey of it, and the honey is all their own — no longer thyme or marjoram. In the same way, the boy will transform and blend his borrowings into a work that is entirely his: namely, his judgment. His education, his labor, his study aim at nothing but forming that.

He owes no one a list of his sources; he owes only what he has made of them. Plunderers and borrowers parade their houses and their purchases — not how they got the money. You never see the fees and kickbacks of a lawyer; you see the alliances he builds, the honors won for his family. No one publishes his income; everyone publishes what he's acquired.

The whole profit of our study is to become better and wiser. It is the understanding, Epicharmus said, that sees and hears; the understanding that turns everything to use, that arranges everything, that acts and rules and reigns. Everything else is blind, deaf, and soulless. And we make the understanding timid and servile by never letting it do anything on its own. Who ever asked his pupil what he thinks of rhetoric or grammar, or of this or that sentence of Cicero? Our masters jam these things into our memories fully feathered, like oracles where the letters and syllables are the very substance of the matter. Knowing by heart is not knowing; it is only holding on to what you've handed your memory for safekeeping. What a man truly knows, he can deploy at will, without checking with the author or fumbling through the pages of his book.

A purely bookish learning is a poor, paltry learning. It may serve as decoration, but it is no foundation to build on — as Plato says when he calls constancy, faith, and sincerity the true philosophy, and the other sciences, aimed at other ends, mere face paint. I'd love to see Paluel or Pompey, those two famous dancers of my day, teach us capers just by performing them while we sat still in our seats — which is exactly how these men propose to train the understanding without ever setting it in motion. Or to see us learn to ride, handle a pike, play the lute, or sing without the bother of practice — just as they want to make us judge well and speak well without ever exercising us in judging or speaking.

In this kind of education, everything that crosses our path is textbook enough: a page's prank, a servant's blunder, a joke at the table — each one a fresh subject.

For the same reason, mixing with people is enormously useful, and so is travel in foreign countries. Not to come home — as most of our young gentlemen do — with a report on the exact circumference of the Pantheon, or the richness of Signora Livia's petticoats, or how much longer and broader Nero's face is on some ruined statue than on some old medal. Come home instead able to describe the temperaments, the manners, the customs, and the laws of the nations you visited — and to have whetted and sharpened your wits by rubbing them against other people's. I would send a boy abroad very young, and first of all — to kill two birds with one stone — to those neighboring nations whose language is furthest from our own, because if the tongue isn't trained to it early, it grows too stiff to bend.

It's also the general opinion that a child should not be raised in his mother's lap. Mothers are too tender. Their natural affection makes even the most sensible of them so overfond that they can't bring themselves to correct their children's faults, or to let them be toughened by hardship and risk, as they ought to be. A mother cannot bear to see her boy come home dusty and sweating from his exercise, or drink something cold when he's hot, or climb onto an unruly horse, or face a rough fencer with a foil in his hand, or so much as fire a gun. But there's no help for it: whoever wants to raise a boy to be worth anything as a man must spare him nothing while he's young, and must regularly break the rules of medicine. As Horace says, let him live under the open sky, and in the thick of danger.

It isn't enough to toughen his soul; you must toughen his muscles too. The soul is crushed if the body doesn't help carry the load — it's too much work to manage both jobs alone. I know to my own cost how mine groans under the burden, saddled as it is with a body so delicate and so quick to complain, forever leaning its whole weight on her. And I often notice in my reading that our masters pass off as greatness of soul and strength of mind what is really toughness of skin and hardness of bone. I have seen men, women, and children born with constitutions so hard and insensible that a sound beating means less to them than a flick of the finger would mean to me — people who won't cry out, flinch, or shrink under a serious thrashing. When wrestlers imitate the philosophers in endurance, it's strength of sinew, not stoutness of heart. Now, being trained to work hard is being trained to bear pain: labor, as Cicero says, hardens us against suffering.

A boy must be broken in to the toil and roughness of exercise — trained up to bear dislocations, colic, cautery, even prison and the rack. For by bad luck he may come to the worst of these; the way the world goes, such things fall on the good as well as the bad. We have the proof in front of us: in our present civil war, whoever draws his sword against the laws threatens the honestest men with the whip and the noose.

There is this too: at home, the tutor's authority — which ought to be absolute over the boy in his charge — is constantly checked and undermined by the presence of the parents. Add to that the deference the whole household pays the boy as the master's son, and his awareness of the estate and the greatness he stands to inherit, and you have, in my opinion, no small handicaps at that tender age.

And yet even in this conversing with men that I spoke of just now, I've noticed a vice: instead of gathering observations from others, we make it our whole business to put ourselves on display. We're more concerned with showing off our own merchandise than with stocking up on new goods. Silence and modesty are very useful qualities in conversation. So train the boy to be sparing with his knowledge once he has it — a careful manager of it — and not to object to or correct every silly remark or ridiculous story told in his presence. It is rude and unbecoming to carp at everything that doesn't suit our own taste. Let him be content with correcting himself, and not seem to condemn in others everything he wouldn't do himself, or to set himself against common custom. As Seneca says, one can be wise without showing off, wise without arousing envy.

Let him avoid those overbearing airs of authority, and that childish ambition of wanting to seem more polished and accomplished than he is — by which carriage he will only expose himself — and of treating every chance to interrupt and correct as too precious to pass up, hoping to come away with a reputation for being something special. Just as only great poets get to take poetic license, only great and illustrious souls get to set themselves above custom. If Socrates and Aristippus sometimes acted against the manners of their time, Cicero warns, no one should think he has the same permission: they earned that liberty by great and divine merits.

Teach him not to enter an argument or a debate except against an opponent worthy of him — and even then, not to use every little trick that might happen to serve, but only the arguments that serve him best. Teach him to be choosy in selecting his reasons, to despise irrelevance, and therefore to prize brevity. Above all, teach him to surrender and submit to truth the moment he spots it, whether it appears in his opponent's argument or in his own second thoughts. He's not auditioning for a professor's chair, where one performs a set racket of words and syllogisms; he is bound to no argument except as his own judgment approves it. Nor is arguing a trade where the freedom to recant and change your mind on reflection gets sold for cash. As Cicero says, no necessity compels him to defend everything that has been prescribed and ordered.

If his tutor shares my temperament, he will shape the boy's will to be a very good and loyal subject to his prince, deeply attached to him and brave in his quarrel — but he will also cool in him any desire to be tied to that service by anything beyond public duty. Setting aside the other costs to the liberty every honest man ought to keep, a judgment bought and mortgaged by personal obligations is either blinded and less free to do its work, or stained with ingratitude and indiscretion. A man who is purely a courtier can have neither the power nor the will to speak or think anything but well of a master who, out of so many millions of other subjects, picked him by hand to feed and advance. That favor, and the profit flowing from it, cannot help corrupting his freedom and dazzling him — and not without some show of reason. You can hear it: these people speak a different language from everyone else in the nation, and not much of what they say in that courtly dialect is to be believed.

Let his conscience and his virtue shine through his speech, with reason as their only guide. Make him understand that admitting an error he has discovered in his own argument — even if no one but himself has noticed it — is an act of judgment and honesty, which are the main things he is after. Obstinacy and contentiousness are common qualities, most at home in small souls. To revise and correct yourself, to abandon a bad position at the very height and heat of the argument — these are rare, great, philosophical qualities.

Advise him, when he's in company, to keep his eyes and ears in every corner. I find that the seats of greatest honor are usually grabbed by the men with the least in them, and that great fortune and great ability rarely travel together. I've been at tables where, while the people at the head of the room were discussing the beauty of the tapestries or the flavor of the wine, plenty of fine things said at the lower end were lost and thrown away. Let him sound out the talent of every man — a peasant, a bricklayer, a passing stranger. Something can be learned from each of them in his own line, and something from their talk will turn out useful one day or another. Even the folly and foolishness of others will teach him something: by noticing everyone's graces and manners, he'll build a taste for the good ones and a contempt for the bad.

Plant in his mind an honest curiosity to inquire into everything. Whatever is singular or rare near wherever he happens to be, let him go and see it — a fine house, a noble fountain, an eminent man, the field of an ancient battle, the routes of Caesar and Charlemagne. Let him learn, as Propertius has it, which land lies locked in frost, which crumbles with heat, which wind sets fairest for Italy. Let him look into the manners, the revenues, and the alliances of princes — things very pleasant to learn and very useful to know.

In this conversing with men, I mean also — and chiefly — those who live only in the records of history. Through books he will keep company with the great and heroic souls of the best ages. It's an idle and empty study for those who make it so by going at it carelessly; but for those who do it with care and attention, it's a study of priceless fruit and value — the only study, Plato tells us, that the Spartans kept for themselves. What profit won't he reap, where the business of men is concerned, from reading the Lives of Plutarch? But let my tutor remember what his teaching is really aimed at, and stamp on his pupil's memory not so much the date of the fall of Carthage as the characters of Hannibal and Scipio; not so much where Marcellus died as why it was unworthy of his duty that he died there. Let him teach him not so much the narrative of history as how to judge it.

History, in my opinion, is the subject we each take to with the most different measures. I have read a hundred things in Livy that another man hasn't read, or hasn't noticed; and Plutarch read a hundred more there than I could ever find — more, perhaps, than the author ever put in. For some it's purely a grammar exercise; for others it's the very anatomy of philosophy, by which we get into the most hidden parts of our nature. There are in Plutarch many long discussions richly worth careful reading, for in that kind of writing he is, to my mind, the greatest master of all. But there are a thousand other matters he has only brushed against — where he merely points his finger to show which way we may go if we like, or contents himself with one sharp hit at the very nerve of the question, leaving us to grope out the rest. Take the place where he says that the people of Asia served a single master because they could not pronounce one syllable: No. That remark may well have given La Boétie the matter and the occasion for his "Voluntary Servitude." Even watching Plutarch pick out some slight action in a man's life, or a single word that hardly seems worth that much, is a whole discourse in itself. It's our loss that men of understanding are so immoderately fond of brevity; their reputation gains by it, no doubt, but in the meantime we are the poorer. Plutarch would rather we praised his judgment than his knowledge; he would rather leave us hungry to read more than stuffed with what we've already read. He knew very well that you can say too much even on the best subject — and that Alexandridas was right to rebuke the man who made excellent but endless speeches to the Spartan magistrates: "Stranger, you say what you should say, but not the way you should say it." People with thin, spare bodies pad themselves out with clothes; people thin on matter pad themselves out with words.

The human understanding is wonderfully brightened by daily conversation with men, for otherwise we sit squeezed and heaped up inside ourselves, with our sight reaching no farther than the end of our own nose. When someone asked Socrates where he was from, he did not answer "Athens" but "the world." He, whose imagination was fuller and wider than ours, embraced the whole world as his city and extended his acquaintance, his society, and his affection to all mankind — not like us, who look no farther than our feet. When the vines of my village are nipped by frost, my parish priest concludes at once that the wrath of God has gone out against the entire human race, and that the cannibals must already have caught the pip. Seeing our civil wars, who doesn't cry out that the machinery of the world is coming apart and the Day of Judgment is at hand — never considering that far worse has been seen before, and that meanwhile, in a thousand other parts of the earth, people are having a perfectly lovely time? For my part, considering the license and impunity that always ride along with such upheavals, I'm amazed the wars are so moderate, and that there isn't more mischief done. To the man with hailstones rattling about his ears, the whole hemisphere seems one storm and tempest. It's like the ridiculous Savoyard who said, very gravely, that if that simpleton of a French king had only managed his fortune properly, he might in time have risen to be steward of the household to the Duke of Savoy. The fellow's shallow imagination simply could not conceive of anything greater than a Duke of Savoy. And in truth we are all in this error without noticing it — an error of great weight and great harm. But whoever can call up before his mind's eye, as in a painting, the great image of our mother Nature in her full majesty and luster; whoever reads in her face her endless, constant variety; whoever finds himself in that picture — and not just himself but a whole kingdom — as no more than the prick of a fine pencil point against the whole: that man alone sizes things at their true scale and grandeur.

This great world — which some multiply further, as species under one genus — is the mirror in which we must look to know ourselves from the right angle. In short, this is the book I would have my young gentleman study with the most attention. So many temperaments, so many sects, so many judgments, opinions, laws, and customs teach us to judge our own sanely, and train our understanding to recognize its imperfection and its natural weakness — which is no small lesson. So many upheavals of states and kingdoms, so many turns and revolutions of public fortune, teach us not to make any great marvel of our own. So many great names, so many famous victories and conquests drowned and swallowed in oblivion, make ridiculous our hope of immortalizing our name by capturing ten cavalrymen or some henhouse known to history only because it fell. The pride and pomp of so many foreign courts, the swollen majesty of so many grandeurs, steady our eyes to bear the luster of our own without blinking. So many millions of men buried before us encourage us not to be afraid of going to find such good company in the other world. And so on with everything else. Pythagoras used to say that our life is like the great crowded gathering at the Olympic games: some exercise their bodies to carry off the glory of the prize; others bring merchandise to sell for profit; and some — by no means the worst sort — come for no other advantage than to watch, to consider how and why everything is done, and to be spectators of other men's lives, the better to judge and steer their own.

To these examples may fitly be joined all the most profitable lessons of philosophy, which all human actions ought to take as their rule. A pupil shall be taught — as Persius puts it — what it is right to wish for; what use coined money really has; how much we owe to our country and our dear ones; what God commanded us to be, and what post we hold in the human scheme; what we are, and to what purpose we were born. He shall be taught what it is to know and what it is to be ignorant; what the aim of study ought to be; what valor, temperance, and justice are; the difference between ambition and greed, servitude and obedience, license and liberty; by what marks a man may recognize true, solid contentment; how far death, pain, and disgrace really deserve our fear; and, as Virgil says, how to shun each hardship — or how to bear it.

He shall be taught by what secret springs we move, and what drives our shifting moods and indecisions. For it seems to me that the first ideas his understanding should be steeped in ought to be the ones that regulate his conduct and his sense — that teach him to know himself, and how to die well and how to live well. Among the liberal arts, let's begin with the art that makes us free. They all serve life in some measure, as everything else does in some measure too; but let's choose the one that serves it directly and by profession. If we could once confine the duties of human life within their just and natural limits, we'd find that most of the sciences in circulation are of no use to us — and that even in the useful ones there are useless stretches and hollows we'd do better to leave alone, keeping to Socrates' direction and limiting our studies to what has real and honest utility. Dare to be wise, says Horace — begin! The man who keeps putting off the hour of living well is like the bumpkin waiting for the river to flow past before he crosses: but the river flows, and will roll on flowing, to the end of time.

It's great foolishness to teach our children what influence Pisces has, or fierce Leo, or Capricorn washed by the western sea — the science of the stars and the motion of the eighth sphere — before teaching them the science of themselves. What do I care, says Anacreon, for the Pleiades or the stars of Taurus? Anaximenes wrote to Pythagoras: "Why should I trouble myself hunting out the secrets of the stars, with death or slavery always before my eyes?" — for the kings of Persia were just then preparing to invade his country. Everyone ought to say the same: "Assaulted as I am by ambition, greed, rashness, and superstition, with so many other enemies of life inside me, shall I sit and ponder the wobbles of the world?"

After he has been taught what will make him wiser and better, then you may entertain him with the elements of logic, physics, geometry, and rhetoric; and whichever science he then leans to, his judgment being already formed and fit to choose, he will quickly master. His instruction should come sometimes by conversation, sometimes by reading. Sometimes his tutor will put the author himself into his hands — whichever he judges right for him — and sometimes only the marrow and substance of it. And if the tutor isn't bookish enough himself to find all the fine passages the books hold for his purpose, some man of learning can be attached to him, to supply on every occasion the provisions he needs and pass them along to the pupil. Who can doubt that this way of teaching is easier and more natural than the method of Gaza, where the precepts are so tangled and harsh, and the words so empty, so fleshless and meaningless, that there is nothing to take hold of — nothing that quickens and lifts the mind — whereas here the mind has something to bite on and digest? This fruit is not only far fairer beyond comparison; it also ripens far earlier.

It's a thousand pities that matters stand as they do in our age: that philosophy, even among intelligent men, has become an empty and fantastic name, a thing of no use and no value either in opinion or in fact. I think the cause is those quibbles and petty sophistries that have camped in all the approaches to her. People are badly wrong to paint her for children as inaccessible, with a frowning, grim, terrifying face. Who put that false mask on her — that pale, haggard, ghostly look? There is nothing gayer, brisker, merrier — I almost said friskier. She preaches nothing but holiday and good cheer; a sad, anxious face is proof she doesn't live in that house. When the grammarian Demetrius found a knot of philosophers sitting and chatting together in the temple at Delphi, he said to them, "Either I'm much mistaken, or by your cheerful, pleasant faces you're in no very deep discussion." To which one of them, Heracleon of Megara, replied: "Knitting your brows belongs to people puzzling over whether some verb doubles its letter, or hunting the derivation of comparatives and superlatives. Philosophical talk delights and cheers those who have it; it never sours them or casts them down." As Juvenal says, you can read the hidden torments of a mind in a sick body — and read its joys too: the face takes either look from the mind.

The soul that houses philosophy ought to be so healthy that it makes the body healthy too. Her peace and contentment should shine all the way to the surface; her ease should mold the outward bearing into her own shape, arming it with a graceful confidence, a lively and cheerful carriage, a calm and contented face. The most unmistakable sign of wisdom is constant cheerfulness; her state, like everything above the moon, is always clear and serene. It is Baroco and Baralipton — those barbarous passwords of school logic — that leave their disciples so grubby and smoke-stained, not she; they don't know her except by hearsay. What! She is the one who calms the storms and tempests of the soul, who teaches famine and fever to laugh and sing — and not by some imaginary celestial machinery, but by natural, visible reasons. She has virtue for her goal; and virtue is not, as the schoolmen claim, perched on the summit of a steep, rugged, unclimbable cliff. Those who have actually approached her find just the opposite: she lives on a fair, fertile, flowering plain, from which she sees everything spread below her; and anyone can reach her who knows the way, by shady, green, sweetly blooming avenues, down a slope as gentle and smooth as the curve of the sky. It's because they have never visited this supreme virtue — beautiful, triumphant, loving, as delicious as she is courageous, the sworn and implacable enemy of anxiety, sorrow, fear, and constraint, with nature for her guide and fortune and pleasure for her companions — that they have gone and invented, out of their own weakness, this ridiculous image of her: sad, quarrelsome, sulky, threatening, scowling. They've set her on a rock, off by herself among thorns and brambles, and made of her a hobgoblin to frighten people.

But the tutor I have in mind — one who knows his real duty is to fill his pupil with affection for virtue, not just reverence toward it — will be able to explain that the poets have always played to the popular mood, and to make the boy see that the gods placed far more toil and sweat on the road to Venus's chamber than on the road to Minerva's. And when the lad's own feelings begin to stir, the tutor will set before him, as a mistress worth wanting, a Bradamante or an Angelica — a natural, active, generous beauty, vigorous rather than soft, the kind dressed like a heroic youth in a glittering helmet, instead of one tricked out in curls and ribbons like a flirt. He'll then count his own taste truly brave and manly when he chooses exactly the opposite of what that soft shepherd of Phrygia chose.

Such a tutor will teach him this new lesson: that the height and value of true virtue lie in the ease, usefulness, and pleasure of practicing it. Virtue is so far from being difficult that boys can master it as well as men, the simple as well as the clever. Her instrument is order, not force. Socrates, her first favorite, deliberately set aside all strain so as to slip into the natural ease of her path. She is the nursing mother of all human pleasures. By making them just, she makes them pure and lasting. By moderating them, she keeps them fresh and keeps our appetite alive. By cutting off the ones she refuses, she sharpens our taste for the ones she allows — and like a kind and generous mother, she allows in abundance everything nature asks for, right up to satisfaction if not exhaustion. Unless we want to claim that the rule which stops the drinker before he's drunk, the glutton before he's sick, and the lecher before he's caught the pox is somehow pleasure's enemy.

If ordinary good fortune fails her, virtue does without it and builds another fortune of her own — one that doesn't wobble and slip like the first. She can be rich, powerful, and learned, and she knows how to lie on soft, perfumed beds. She loves life; she loves beauty, glory, and health. But her own special office is to know how to use these good things with judgment, and how to lose them without despair — an office far more noble than burdensome, and without it the whole course of a life runs unnatural, turbulent, and deformed. That is where the monsters on the rocks and cliffs really belong: in the picture of a life without her.

If this pupil turns out to have so contrary a nature that he'd rather hear some idle yarn than the true account of a noble expedition or a wise conversation; if, when the drumbeat fires up his companions' young blood, he drifts off after another drummer calling him to a street dance or a bear-baiting; if he wouldn't honestly find it sweeter and finer to come back dusty, sweaty, and victorious from a battle than to come back from tennis or a dance with the prize — then I see no remedy except for his tutor to apprentice him early to a pastry cook in some good town, even if he's the son of a duke. This follows Plato's rule: that children should be placed in life not according to their father's wealth or rank, but according to the capacities of their own souls.

Since philosophy is the thing that teaches us how to live, and since childhood needs that lesson as much as every other age, why don't we give it to children early? The clay is moist and soft, as Persius says — now, right now, is the time to hurry and shape it on the fast-turning wheel. Instead, we begin teaching people how to live when life is nearly over. A hundred students have caught the pox before they ever get to Aristotle's lecture on temperance. Cicero said that even if he lived two men's lifetimes, he would never find leisure to study the lyric poets — and I find our quibbling academics more deplorably useless still. The boy we're raising has even less time to spare: he owes only the first fifteen or sixteen years of his life to education; the rest belongs to action. So let's spend that short time on what's necessary. Away with the thorny subtleties of logic-chopping — they're abuses, things that will never improve a single life. Take the plain discourses of philosophy instead. Learn first how to choose them rightly, then how to apply them. They're easier to understand than one of Boccaccio's stories. A child fresh from his nurse can take them in more readily than he can learn to read and write. Philosophy has lessons fit for childhood just as it has lessons fit for feeble old age.

I'm with Plutarch on this: Aristotle did not spend his time troubling his great pupil with the knack of building syllogisms or the elements of geometry, but with planting in him good principles about courage, boldness, greatness of spirit, temperance, and contempt for fear. With that ammunition, Aristotle sent him out — still a boy, with no more than thirty thousand foot soldiers, four thousand horsemen, and forty-two thousand crowns — to conquer the empire of the whole earth. As for the other arts and sciences, Plutarch says Alexander praised their excellence and charm, and held them in great honor, but was never so swept away as to want to practice them himself. Young men and old, as Persius says, draw from philosophy a settled aim for the mind, and provisions for miserable gray hairs.

Epicurus, at the start of his letter to Meniceus, says that the youngest should not refuse to philosophize, and the oldest should not grow weary of it. Whoever says otherwise seems to imply that the season for living happily either hasn't arrived yet or is already past. For all that, I would not have this pupil of ours imprisoned and made a slave to his book, nor handed over to the gloom and bad temper of some sour-natured pedant.

I would not have his spirit cowed and broken by putting him to the rack, as some do — tormenting him fourteen or fifteen hours a day and turning him into a pack-horse. Nor, if he turns out to have a solitary, melancholy temperament and an excessive appetite for his books, would I think it wise to feed that mood in him; it makes a boy unfit for ordinary human company and pulls him away from better occupations. How many men have I seen in my time made stupid by an immoderate thirst for knowledge! Carneades was so drunk on it that he couldn't find time to comb his hair or trim his nails. Nor would I have the boy's generous manners spoiled by the rudeness and barbarism of someone else's. There used to be a proverb about French intelligence: early to bloom, quick to fade. And it's true — nothing is more charming and promising than the children of France, but they routinely cheat the hopes people form of them, and grown to manhood they show nothing remarkable at all. I have heard sensible people say that it's these colleges of ours, the ones we send our young people to — and of which we have far too many — that turn them into such animals.

But for our young gentleman, a study, a garden, the table, his bed, solitude, company, morning and evening — every hour will be the same to him, and every place a classroom. For philosophy, the shaper of judgment and character, will be his principal lesson, and she has the privilege of having a hand in everything. The orator Isocrates, asked at a banquet to speak about his art, gave an answer the whole company rightly applauded: "This is no time for what I can do; and what it is time for, I cannot do." Delivering orations and staging debates in a company gathered to laugh and eat well would have been badly out of tune — and the same could be said of all the other sciences. But philosophy, at least the part of her that deals with man and his offices and duties, is different: all the wise have agreed that her conversation is so sweet she should be welcome at every game and every dinner party.
