When Plato invited her to his banquet, we see how gently and gracefully she entertained the company, fitting herself to the time and place even while speaking of the loftiest things. As Horace says, she profits poor and rich alike — and neglected, she hurts young and old alike.

By this method of teaching, my young pupil will be far better occupied than his schoolmates at the college. But just as the steps we take pacing in a gallery, though three times as many, tire us less than the same steps on a fixed journey, so our lesson — happening as if by accident, with no set time or place, woven into everything we do — will sink in without his even noticing. Even his games and exercises will become a solid part of his study: running, wrestling, music, dancing, hunting, riding, fencing. I want his outward bearing, his manners, and the carriage of his body trained at the same time as his mind. It is not a soul we are educating; it is not a body. It is a man, and we must not split him in two. As Plato says, we shouldn't fashion one without the other, but drive them together like two horses harnessed to the same coach. And listening to him, doesn't he actually seem to give more time and care to exercising the body, trusting that the mind gets her workout at the same time?

For the rest, this education should be conducted with a severe gentleness — the very opposite of our pedants' practice. Instead of coaxing children toward learning by apt and kindly ways, our teachers present them with nothing but rods and canes, horror and cruelty. Away with this violence! Away with this compulsion! Nothing, I'm convinced, so dulls and degrades a well-born nature. If you want your boy to dread shame and punishment, don't harden him to them. Harden him instead to heat and cold, to wind and sun, to the dangers he ought to hold cheap. Wean him from all softness and fussiness in his clothes and his bed, his food and his drink. Get him used to everything, so he doesn't grow into a pretty Sir Paris, a carpet-knight, but a sinewy, hardy, vigorous young man. I have been of this opinion from childhood to the age I've reached now, and I hold it still.

But among other things, the discipline of most of our colleges has always disgusted me. They might have done less damage by erring on the indulgent side. A college is a genuine jail for captive youth. The boys are made debauched by being punished for debauchery before they've committed any. Just drop in while lessons are in session: you'll hear nothing but the shrieks of boys under torture and the thundering of schoolmasters drunk with rage. What a charming way to give tender, timid souls a love of their book — a furious face and a rod in the hand! A cursed and ruinous way of proceeding. Besides, as Quintilian rightly observed, this tyrannical authority carries dangerous consequences with it — especially our style of whipping. How much more fitting it would be to see their classrooms strewn with green leaves and fresh flowers than with the bloody stumps of birch and willow! If it were left to me, I would paint the schoolhouse with pictures of Joy and Gladness, with Flora and the Graces, as the philosopher Speusippus did his. Where children's profit is, let their pleasure be there too. Food that's good for a child should be sweetened with sugar; food that's bad for him, soured with gall.

It is marvelous to see how much care Plato takes, in his Laws, over the gaiety and amusements of the young people of his city — how long and often he dwells on their races, sports, songs, leaps, and dances, whose ordering and patronage, he says, antiquity entrusted to the gods themselves: to Apollo, Minerva, and the Muses. He goes on at length with endless rules for physical exercise. About book-learning he says very little, and seems to recommend poetry in particular only for the music's sake.

Every oddity in our manners and habits should be avoided as unfit for living among other people. Who wouldn't be astonished by a constitution like that of Demophoon, steward to Alexander the Great, who sweated in the shade and shivered in the sun? I've seen men flee the smell of a ripe apple faster than they'd flee gunfire; others terrified of a mouse; others who vomit at the sight of cream, or nearly faint when a feather bed is being shaken out. Germanicus could endure neither the sight nor the crowing of a rooster. There may, I grant, be some hidden cause and natural aversion in such cases; but in my opinion a man could conquer it if he took it in hand early. Training has worked so well on me — though not without some effort, I admit — that except for beer, my appetite adapts itself to any food you like.

Young bodies are supple, so that is the age to bend and shape them to every fashion and custom. Provided a young man can keep his appetite and his will within proper bounds, then in God's name make him fit for all nations and all companies — even, if need be, for excess and disorder, where the customs of the place call for it. Let him be able to do everything, and love to do nothing but what is good. The philosophers themselves don't excuse Callisthenes for losing the favor of his master Alexander the Great by refusing to match him cup for cup. Let the lad laugh, play, and carouse with his prince. Even in revelry, I'd want him to outdo the rest of the company in stamina and skill — so that if he refrains from doing wrong, it's never for lack of strength or knowledge, but purely for lack of will. As Seneca says, there is a vast difference between not wanting to sin and not knowing how.

I once thought I was paying a compliment to a certain lord — a man as free of these excesses as any in France — by asking him, in front of very good company, how many times in his life he had gotten drunk in Germany while there on the King's business. He took it exactly as intended, answered "Three times," and told us the whole story of his sprees. I know men who, lacking this talent, have been at a serious disadvantage negotiating with that nation. And I have often marveled at the wonderful constitution of Alcibiades, who could shift himself into such different styles without any damage to his health — now outdoing the Persians in pomp and luxury, now the Spartans in austerity and thrift; as reformed in Sparta as he was self-indulgent in Ionia. As Horace says, every shade of life, every station and circumstance, suited Aristippus. That's the kind of man I would make of my pupil — one, as Horace also says, who wears his patched cloak with patience, and would wear a changed fortune just as well, playing either part with equal grace.

These are my lessons. The one who puts them into practice will profit more than the one who merely has them read to him and only knows them. If you see him, you hear him; if you hear him, you see him. God forbid, says someone in Plato, that philosophizing should mean nothing more than reading a great many books and learning the arts. As Cicero says, the masters of that fullest of all arts — the discipline of living well — pursued it with their lives rather than their reading.

Leo, prince of the Phliasians, asked Heraclides Ponticus what art or science he professed. "I know neither art nor science," he answered, "but I am a philosopher." Someone sneered at Diogenes that an ignorant man had no business dabbling in philosophy. "Then I dabble in it," he replied, "with all the more reason." And when Hegesias begged him to read a certain book aloud, Diogenes said: "You amuse me. You choose real, natural figs over painted ones — so why not choose exercises that are real and natural over written ones?"

Our boy will not so much recite his lesson as practice it. He will repeat it in his actions. We shall see whether there is prudence in his undertakings, whether there is sincerity and justice in his conduct, grace and judgment in his speech; whether there is constancy in his sickness, modesty in his fun, temperance in his pleasures, order in his household, and indifference at his table — whether what he eats and drinks is meat or fish, wine or water. He should be a man, as Cicero says, who treats his learning not as a display of knowledge but as the law of his life — who obeys himself and abides by his own rules.

The conduct of our lives is the true mirror of our doctrine. Zeuxidamus, asked why the Spartans did not write down their rules of valor and give them to their young men to read, answered that it was because they wanted to accustom them to deeds, not entertain them with words. Compare such a man, after fifteen or sixteen years of that schooling, with one of our college Latinists, who has thrown the same years away learning nothing but how to talk. The world is nothing but babble, and I have hardly ever met a man who didn't say too much rather than too little. Yet half our lives are embezzled this way: we're kept four or five years just learning words and stitching them into clauses; as many again shaping them into a long discourse, neatly divided into four or five parts; and another five years, at least, learning how to blend and interweave them in some subtle, intricate fashion. Let's leave all that to the people who make a profession of it.

Going to Orleans one day, I met, on the plain this side of Cléry, two pedants traveling toward Bordeaux, walking about fifty paces apart; and a good way behind them I spotted a troop of horsemen with a gentleman at their head — the late Comte de la Rochefoucauld. One of my servants asked the first of these masters of arts who the gentleman was who was coming along behind him. The pedant, who hadn't seen the riders and assumed his companion was meant, answered comically: "He is not a gentleman; he is a grammarian, and I am a logician." Now we, who are trying to breed exactly the opposite — not a grammarian or a logician but a gentleman — can leave these two to squander their leisure. Our business lies elsewhere.

Let our pupil only be well stocked with matter, and the words will follow all too readily; if they won't come on their own, he'll drag them after him. I've heard people excuse themselves on the grounds that they can't express what they mean — claiming their heads are full of fine things they simply can't deliver for lack of eloquence. That is a dodge, nothing more. Want to know what I think? Those fine things are shadows of half-formed notions they can't make sense of inside themselves, and so can't bring out. They don't yet understand what they're trying to say. Watch them hem and stammer at the moment of delivery and you'll see their struggle isn't with the birth at all — it's still back at the conception. They are only licking their shapeless embryo into form. For my part I hold — and Socrates insists on it — that whoever has a clear, living idea in his mind will get it out in one language or another, and if he's mute, in signs. As Horace says, once the thing is grasped, the words follow willingly. Another writer put it just as poetically in prose: when things take possession of the mind, says Seneca, the words come courting. And Cicero again: the things themselves seize the words to express them.

Our pupil may know nothing of ablatives, conjunctives, substantives, or grammar — but then, neither does his footman, or a fishwife on the Petit Pont, and they'll give you a bellyful of talk if you'll listen, and probably trip over their language as little as the finest masters of arts in France. He knows no rhetoric, nor how to butter up "the courteous reader" in a preface — and he doesn't care to. The truth is, all that pretty paintwork is wiped out in a moment by the shine of one plain, blunt truth. Those flourishes serve only to amuse the crowd, which can't digest more solid and nourishing food — as Aper demonstrates plainly in Tacitus.

The ambassadors of Samos came to Cleomenes, king of Sparta, armed with a long, elegant oration meant to stir him to war against the tyrant Polycrates. He heard their whole performance with great gravity and patience, and then gave them this answer: "As for your opening, I don't remember it — nor, therefore, the middle; and as for your conclusion, I will not do it." A very pretty answer, it seems to me, and a pack of learned orators left beached on the sand. And what about that other man? The Athenians were choosing between two architects for a great public building. The first, a slick, self-satisfied fellow, presented himself with a long rehearsed speech on the project and swayed the crowd in his favor. The other settled it in a few words: "Athenians, what this man says, I will do." When Cicero was at the full blaze of an eloquent harangue, many of the listeners were struck with admiration — but Cato only laughed and said, "We have an entertaining consul."

Coming before or coming after, a good maxim or a thing well said is always in season. If it doesn't quite fit what went before or what follows, it is still good in itself. I am not one of those who think good rhyme makes a good poem. Let the poet stretch a short syllable or clip a long one if he likes — what does it matter? If the invention is there, if wit and judgment have done their work well, I'll say: here is a good poet, but a bad rhymer.

Horace describes a writer of this kind: a man with a fine nose for things but rough at building verses. Take such a work, Horace says, and strip away the rhythm and meter — put the first word last and the last word first — and you will still find the scattered limbs of a poet. The pieces will be beautiful all by themselves. That is what Menander meant when a friend scolded him: the day was approaching when he had promised a comedy, and he hadn't even started it. "It's finished and ready," Menander said. "All but the verses." Having worked out the subject and arranged the scenes in his head, he counted the rest as nothing.

Since Ronsard and Du Bellay raised the reputation of our French poetry, every little dabbler, as far as I can see, puffs up his words as grandly and rolls out his cadences almost as harmoniously as they do. More sound than sense, as Seneca says. For the common run of writers, there have never been so many poetasters as there are now. They find it easy enough to copy the rhymes; what they fall infinitely short of copying is the rich descriptions of the one master and the delicate invention of the other.

But what will become of our young gentleman if someone attacks him with the sophistic subtlety of a syllogism? "A Westphalia ham makes a man drink; drinking quenches thirst; therefore a Westphalia ham quenches thirst." Why, let him laugh at it. Laughing is wiser than trying to answer it. Or let him borrow that pleasant dodge of Aristippus: "Why should I bother to untie it, when it gives me this much trouble tied?" When someone tried this dialectical juggling on Cleanthes, Chrysippus cut him short: "Save those toys for playing with children, and stop distracting the serious thoughts of a grown man with such foolery." If these absurd subtleties — twisted and prickly sophisms, Cicero calls them — are designed to plant a falsehood in him, they are dangerous. But if they amount to nothing more than making him laugh, I don't see why a man needs to be fortified against them.

Some writers are so ridiculous they will go a mile out of their way to chase down a fine word — the kind, as Quintilian says, who don't fit words to their subject, but go hunting for subjects to fit their words. And as Seneca puts it, the kind who are lured by some pretty-sounding phrase into writing about something they never meant to touch. For my own part, I would rather drag in a good sentence by the head and shoulders to serve my purpose than bend my purpose to go hunting for a sentence. Words exist to serve; they should follow a man's meaning — and let Gascon step in where French won't do the job. I want the matter to be so compelling, so completely possessing the imagination of the listener, that he has no attention left over for the words.

The way of speaking I love is natural and plain, the same on paper as in the mouth — a sinewy, muscular way of expressing yourself, short and pithy; not so much elegant and polished as prompt and forceful. The speech with real savor, says an old epitaph, is the speech that strikes home. Rather hard than tiresome; free of affectation; irregular, loose-jointed, and bold, where every piece stands as a body of its own. Not the style of a pedant, a preacher, or a lawyer, but rather a soldier's style — which is what Suetonius calls Julius Caesar's, though I confess I can't see why he calls it that.

I have always been ready to imitate that careless dress you still see among the young men of our day — cloak slung over one shoulder, cap tilted to one side, a stocking sagging — which expresses a kind of proud disdain for foreign frills and a contempt for the artificial. But I find that same carelessness even better employed in a way of speaking. All affectation is ungraceful in a courtier, especially in our French gaiety and freedom; and in a monarchy every gentleman ought to be shaped to the court's model. So an easy, natural negligence wears well. I no more like a fabric where you can see every knot and seam than a fine figure so thin you can count the bones and veins. Language in the service of truth, says Seneca, should be plain and unstudied; and again: who speaks with elaborate care, unless he wants to sound affected?

Eloquence that draws all the attention to itself cheats the very subject it was meant to advance. Just as it is a ridiculous vanity in dress to make ourselves conspicuous by some private, peculiar fashion, so in language the hunt for novel phrases and unheard-of words comes from a childish, schoolmasterish ambition. Let me be bound to use no other words than the ones spoken in the markets of Paris! Aristophanes the grammarian completely missed the point when he criticized Epicurus for his plain way of speaking, since the whole aim of his oratory was simply to be understood.

The imitation of words is so easy that a whole people picks it up at once; the imitation of inventing, and of applying words fitly, comes much more slowly. Most readers, finding two robes alike, mistakenly believe the bodies inside are alike too. But force and sinew cannot be borrowed; only the gloss and the outward finery — the words and delivery — can. Most of the people I talk with speak the same language I am writing here; whether they think the same thoughts, I couldn't say.

The Athenians, Plato says, cultivate fullness and elegance of speech, the Spartans brevity, and the Cretans fertility of thought rather than fertility of language — and the Cretans have it right. Zeno used to say he had two sorts of students: one sort curious to learn things — these were his favorites — and the other caring for nothing but words. Not that fine speaking isn't a good and admirable quality. But it is not so excellent or so necessary as some make it out to be, and it scandalizes me that we should spend our whole lives on nothing else. I would first want to understand my own language, and the language of my neighbors, with whom I do most of my business and most of my talking.

Greek and Latin are great ornaments, no doubt, and of great use. But we buy them too dear. I will tell you here of one way to get them cheaper — a way tested on my own person — and whoever likes may use it. My late father made the most careful inquiry a man could make, among men of the greatest learning and judgment, after an exact method of education. They warned him about the drawback of the usual practice: they had him believe that the tedious years we spend learning languages that cost the ancients nothing were the sole reason we could never reach the grandeur of soul and the perfection of knowledge of the old Greeks and Romans. I don't believe that's the only reason. Still, the expedient my father hit upon was this. In my infancy, before I could speak at all, he put me in the care of a German — a man who later died a famous physician in France — who knew not a word of our language and was fluent and expert in Latin. This man, whom my father had brought from his own country and kept on a great salary for this one purpose, had me constantly in his hands. Two others of lesser learning were joined to him, to attend me and relieve him; and these spoke to me in no language but Latin.

As for the rest of the household, it was an unbreakable rule that neither my father himself, nor my mother, nor manservant, nor chambermaid would speak anything in my presence except whatever scraps of Latin each had managed to learn in order to chatter with me. It is hard to imagine how much the whole family got out of this. My father and mother picked up enough Latin to understand it perfectly well, and to speak it as much as ordinary needs required; so did the servants who were most about me. In short, we Latined it at such a rate that the language spilled over into the neighboring villages, where to this day you can find Latin names for craftsmen and their tools, planted there by custom.

As for me, I was more than six years old before I understood French or Périgordin any better than Arabic. And without art, book, grammar, or rule — without the whip, and without the cost of a single tear — I had learned by then to speak Latin as pure as my master's own, for I had no other language to mix in with it. If, for instance, they wanted to give me a written exercise in the college manner, where other boys received it in French, mine had to be given in bad Latin, for me to turn into good. And Nicolas Grouchy, who wrote a book on Roman assemblies; Guillaume Guerente, who wrote a commentary on Aristotle; George Buchanan, that great Scottish poet; and Marc Antoine Muret, whom both France and Italy acknowledged as the best orator of his time — all of them my private tutors — often told me afterward that as a small child I had that language so fluent and ready that they were afraid to strike up a conversation with me. Buchanan in particular, whom I later saw in the suite of the late Marshal de Brissac, told me he was planning to write a treatise on education and meant to take his model from mine; he was then tutor to that Comte de Brissac who later proved so valiant and gallant a gentleman.

As for Greek, of which I have barely a smattering, my father planned to have me taught that too by a device — but a new one, by way of sport, tossing declensions back and forth like those games of counters by which people learn geometry and arithmetic. For among the other advice he had received, he had been told to make me relish learning and duty by my own free will and desire, and to bring up my soul in complete liberty and delight, without severity or constraint. And he observed this to the point of superstition, if I may put it that way. Some people hold that it troubles the tender brains of children to be jerked suddenly and violently out of sleep in the morning — sleep being a thing they are sunk in far more deeply than we are — so my father had me wakened every day by the sound of a musical instrument, and was never without a musician for the purpose. This one example will let you judge the rest, and it is enough by itself to commend both the prudence and the affection of so good a father — who is in no way to blame if he harvested no fruit answering to so exquisite a cultivation.

Two things were the cause of that. First, a sterile and unsuitable soil. Though I had a strong, healthy constitution and a disposition gentle and tractable enough, I was at the same time so heavy, slack, and drowsy that they couldn't pull me out of my idleness even to play. What I saw, I saw clearly enough; and underneath that sluggish temperament I nursed a bold imagination, and opinions beyond my years. But my mind was slow, and would move no faster than it was led; my understanding tardy, my invention limp; and on top of everything, an incredible lack of memory. No wonder nothing much could be extracted from all that. And second: like those people who, impatient with a long and steady cure, will try every prescription and remedy going, the good man — terribly afraid of failing in a thing he had set his whole heart on — finally let himself be overruled by the common opinion, which always trails after its leader like a flight of cranes. He fell in with the custom of the day, no longer having around him the men from Italy who had given him his first model of education, and sent me at six years old to the College of Guienne, then the best and most flourishing in France. And there, nothing could be added to the care he took — choosing me the ablest private tutors, securing every other advantage, reserving several special arrangements contrary to the usual college practice. But for all these precautions, it was a college still. My Latin promptly went bad, and since then, through disuse, I have lost all command of it. All that this novel education did for me was to vault me, on arrival, into the upper forms. By thirteen, when I left the college, I had finished my whole course, as they call it — and in truth with nothing to show for it that I can honestly brag of now.

My first taste for books came from the pleasure of reading the fables of Ovid's Metamorphoses. At about seven or eight years old I gave up every other amusement to read them — partly because that language was my own mother tongue, and it was the easiest book I knew, and by its subject the best suited to my age. As for Lancelot of the Lake, Amadis of Gaul, Huon of Bordeaux, and that whole heap of trash that children love best, I had never so much as heard their names — and to this day I don't know what's in them — so strict was the discipline I was raised under. But Ovid was enough to make me neglect my assigned lessons. And here I had the enormous luck of dealing with an understanding tutor, who knew how to look the other way, discreetly, at this and other truancies of the same kind. By that means I ran through Virgil's Aeneid, then Terence, then Plautus, then some Italian comedies, drawn on by the sweetness of the subject. If he had been fool enough to break me of this diversion, I truly believe I would have carried nothing away from school but a hatred of books, as almost all our young gentlemen do. He managed the business cleverly — pretending to see nothing, and sharpening my appetite by letting me devour those books only in the time I could steal from my regular studies. For the chief qualities my father wanted from the men he had entrusted with my education were good nature and good humor; and to tell the truth, my character had no vices in it but sloth and want of mettle. The danger was not that I would do ill, but that I would do nothing. Nobody predicted I would turn out wicked — only useless. They foresaw idleness, not malice.

And so it has turned out. The complaints I hear about myself run like this: "He is idle; cold in the duties of friendship and kinship, and in public duties; too private; too disdainful." Even the most hostile do not say, "Why did he take that? Why didn't he pay this man?" — but, "Why won't he part with anything? Why doesn't he give?" I would take it as a favor if people expected from me no extra works of generosity beyond those. But they are unjust: they demand from me what I do not owe far more rigorously than they demand from others what they do owe. In condemning me to it ahead of time, they wipe out the pleasure of the act and rob me of the gratitude that would be its proper reward — whereas any good deed from my hands ought to count for more, precisely because I have never received any. I can dispose the more freely of my fortune the more it is mine, and of myself the more I am my own. All the same, if I were any good at publishing my own actions, I could perhaps answer these reproaches very well — and could give certain people to understand that they are not so much offended that I do too little, as that I could do a great deal more than I do.

Yet for all this heavy disposition of mine, my mind, when it withdrew into itself, was not without strong movements, and sound, clear judgments about whatever objects it could reach — and it could digest them alone, without any help. And among other things, I genuinely believe it would have been completely impossible to make it submit to violence and force. Shall I tell you about one faculty of my youth? I had great confidence of face, and flexibility of voice and gesture, in throwing myself into whatever part I undertook to act. Before I had well entered my twelfth year — as Virgil puts it — I was playing the leading roles in the Latin tragedies of Buchanan, Guerente, and Muret, which were staged with great dignity in our College of Guienne. Our principal, Andreas Goveanus, was in this, as in every other part of his office, beyond comparison the best in France; and I was reckoned one of the best actors.

It is an exercise I see nothing wrong with in young people of good family. I have since watched our princes, following the example of some of the ancients, perform in person, handsomely and creditably; in Greece it was even allowed to persons of quality to make a profession of it. Livy tells how a secret was entrusted to Aristo the tragedian — a man of good family and fortune, neither of which was tarnished by his profession, since nothing of the kind was held a disgrace among the Greeks.

I have always called it impertinence to condemn these entertainments, and injustice to bar comedians worth seeing from our good towns, grudging the people that public pleasure. Well-governed states take care to assemble their citizens not only for the solemn duties of devotion but for sports and spectacles too. Fellowship and friendship grow from it. And besides, what diversion could be more orderly and regular than one performed in full view of everyone, often in the presence of the magistrate himself? For my part, I think it reasonable that a prince should sometimes treat his people at his own expense, out of a kind of fatherly goodness and affection; and that in populous cities there should be theaters built for such entertainments — if only to draw people away from worse and private doings.

To come back to my subject: there is nothing like coaxing the appetite and the affections. Otherwise all you produce is so many donkeys loaded down with books. By dint of the whip, you hand them their pocketful of learning to carry. But to do the job properly, learning shouldn't merely be lodged with them; it should be married to them.

Of Friendship

Watching a painter who works for me, I had a mind to imitate his method. He picks out the fairest spot, the middle of each wall, and puts there a picture labored over with all his skill; and the empty space around it he fills with grotesques — odd, fantastic figures with no charm except their variety and the extravagance of their shapes. And honestly, what are these things I scribble but grotesques and monstrous bodies, patched together from assorted parts, with no definite shape, and no order, sequence, or proportion except by accident? As Horace says, a lovely woman ends, below the waist, in a fish.

In this second part I keep pace with my painter well enough; but I fall far short of him in the first and better part, because my skill doesn't stretch to attempting a rich, polished picture, finished according to the rules of art. So I decided to borrow one from Étienne de La Boétie — one that will honor and adorn all the rest of this work. I mean a discourse he called Voluntary Servitude, though those who never knew him have since renamed it, aptly enough, Against One. He wrote it in his youth, as an exercise, in honor of liberty against tyrants. It has long since passed through the hands of men of learning and judgment, and won high and deserved praise, for it is finely written, and as full as a thing can be.

And yet one can fairly say it falls far short of what he could do. If, in the riper age when I had the good luck to know him, he had taken up a plan like mine — to put his thoughts in writing — we would have seen many rare things, things that would have come very near rivaling the best of the ancients. In natural gifts especially, I know no one comparable to him. But he left nothing behind except that treatise — and even that by chance; I believe he never saw it again after it first left his hands — and some observations on that Edict of January which our civil wars have made famous, which may yet find their place elsewhere. That was all I could recover of his remains — I, to whom, with so loving a remembrance, on his deathbed, he willed his library and his papers — besides the little volume of his works that I have since had printed.

And I owe this treatise a particular debt, because it was the occasion of our first acquaintance. It was shown to me long before I ever saw him, and gave me my first knowledge of his name; and so it set going the friendship which, for as long as God was pleased to leave us together, we kept up so perfect, so inviolate, and so entire that you will hardly read of its like — and among the men of our own day there is no sign or trace of any such thing in practice. So much has to come together to build it that it is much if fortune manages it once in three centuries.

There is nothing nature seems to have made us for so much as society; and Aristotle says the good lawgivers took more care of friendship than of justice. Now the highest point of its perfection is this: that it exist for its own sake. Generally, all the attachments that pleasure, profit, or public or private need creates and feeds are so much the less beautiful and noble — so much the less friendships — to the degree that they mix into friendship some other cause and aim and reward than friendship itself. Nor do the four ancient kinds — natural, social, hospitable, erotic — make up a true and perfect friendship, whether taken separately or together.

That of children for parents is really respect. Friendship feeds on a communication that cannot exist between them, the gap being too great; and it might even offend against the duties of nature. Not all a father's secret thoughts are fit to share with his children, for fear of breeding an unbecoming familiarity; and the warnings and corrections, which are among the chief offices of friendship, cannot properly be performed by son toward father. There have been nations where custom had the children kill their fathers, and others where the fathers killed their children, to keep one from standing in the other's way in life; and naturally what the one expects depends on the other's ruin. There have been great philosophers who made nothing of this natural tie. Aristippus, for one: when they pressed him about the affection he owed his children, seeing they had come out of him, he started spitting, and said that this had come out of him too — that we also breed worms and lice. And there was that other man, whom Plutarch tried to reconcile with his brother, who said: "I don't value him the more for having come out of the same hole."

Brother is, in truth, a fine name and full of affection — which is why he and I made it our name for each other. But the tangling of interests, the dividing of estates, the fact that one's wealth must be the other's poverty — these strangely loosen and weaken the brotherly tie. Brothers pushing their fortunes and advancement along the same path can hardly help jostling and blocking each other at every step. Besides, why should the harmony of character, mind, and inclination that breeds true and perfect friendship be found in these relations? Father and son may be of entirely opposite temperaments, and brothers too. He is my son, he is my brother — but he is hot-tempered, ill-natured, or a fool. And then, the more these are friendships that law and natural obligation lay upon us, the less there is in them of our own choice and free will; and our free will produces nothing more promptly and properly its own than affection and friendship. Not that I haven't tasted, in my own person, everything that kind of bond can give. I had the best and most indulgent father that ever was, even into his extreme old age, and he came of a family famous and exemplary for generations in the matter of brotherly concord — he was himself, as Horace says of himself, known for a father's love toward his brothers.

Nor can we bring in here the love we bear to women, though it is an act of our own choice, or set it in this rank. Its fire, I confess — and the goddess who mixes a sweet bitterness into love is no stranger to me, as Catullus admits — is more active, more scorching, more sharp. But it is a rash and fickle flame, wavering and inconstant, a fever that comes and goes in fits, and that holds us only by one corner. The fire of friendship is general and universal, but temperate and even — a constant, settled warmth, all gentleness and smoothness, with nothing harsh or stinging in it. What's more, love is nothing but a frantic desire for what flees from us. It is Ariosto's hunter, who chases the hare through heat and cold, over mountain and shore, prizes it nothing once he sees it caught, and only runs hard after the thing that runs away. As soon as love crosses into the territory of friendship — that is, into an agreement of wills — it droops and goes out. Enjoyment destroys it, because its end is bodily, and bodies get their fill. Friendship, on the contrary, is enjoyed in exact proportion as it is desired. It rises, grows, and feeds only on enjoyment, because it is spiritual, and the soul grows finer with use. Under this perfect friendship those fleeting affections found a place in me too, in my younger years — to say nothing of him, who confesses more than enough of them in his verses. So I had both passions in me, but always so that I could tell them apart; and they were never in any degree of comparison — the first holding its course in so lofty and proud a flight that it looked down with disdain at the other beating along far below it.

As for marriage: besides being a bargain where only the entrance is free — its continuance is forced and compelled, hanging on something other than our will — and a bargain ordinarily struck for other ends, it comes with a thousand foreign tangles to unravel, enough to snap the thread and trouble the course of a living affection. Friendship has no business or traffic with anything but itself. Add to this, to tell the truth, that the ordinary capacity of women is not up to the kind of converse and communication that feeds this sacred bond; nor do their souls seem firm enough to bear the strain of so hard and lasting a knot. And certainly, if it were not for that — if there could be such a free and willing relationship in which not only the souls had this entire enjoyment, but the bodies also shared in the union, so that the whole person was engaged — the friendship would no doubt be fuller and more complete. But there is no example yet of that sex arriving at it, and by the common consent of the ancient schools it is excluded.

And that other Greek licence is justly abhorred by our morals. Besides, since their practice required so necessary a disparity of age and difference of office between the lovers, it answered no better than the love of women to the perfect union and harmony we are asking for here. For what is this love of friendship, Cicero asks — why does no one love an ugly young man, or a handsome old one? Even the picture the Academy paints of it will not, I think, contradict me when I say this: that the first frenzy, breathed by the son of Venus into the lover's heart at the sight of the flower and prime of a budding youth — a frenzy to which they allow all the insolent and passionate excesses that an immoderate ardor can produce — was founded simply on outward beauty, the false image of bodily generation. It could not be founded on the soul, which was still hidden from sight, only just springing up, too young to show what it would become. If that frenzy seized a base spirit, the means of his courtship were rich presents, favor and advancement to office, and other such trash — which they by no means approve. If it fell on a nobler soul, the means were noble in proportion: instruction in philosophy; precepts to revere religion, to obey the laws, to die for the good of one's country; examples of valor, prudence, and justice — the lover laboring to make himself acceptable by the grace and beauty of his soul, that of his body being long since faded, and hoping by this fellowship of mind to establish a firmer and more lasting bond.

When this courtship came to its effect in due season — for what they do not require in the lover, namely leisure and discretion in his pursuit, they strictly require in the one loved, since he has to judge an inner beauty, hard to know and slow to discover — then there was born in the beloved the desire of a spiritual conception, by the mediation of a spiritual beauty. In him, this was the principal thing; the bodily part was accidental and secondary — the exact reverse of the lover. For this reason they rank the beloved higher, and maintain that the gods do too; and they greatly blame the poet Aeschylus for giving the lover's part, in the loves of Achilles and Patroclus, to Achilles, who was in the first beardless flower of his youth and the handsomest of all the Greeks. After this general communion was established, the sovereign and worthiest part presiding and performing its proper offices, they say great benefit came of it, both to private men and to the public; that it made the strength of the countries where it was received, and was the chief safeguard of liberty and justice — witness the wholesome loves of Harmodius and Aristogiton. So they called it sacred and divine, and held that nothing was its enemy but the violence of tyrants and the baseness of the common people. In the end, all that can be granted to the Academy is that this was a love which finished in friendship — which agrees well enough with the Stoic definition of love, as Cicero reports it: love is the attempt to form a friendship out of the appearance of beauty.

I come back to my own description, which is juster and truer: only those friendships deserve the name, as Cicero says, that are fortified and confirmed by mature judgment and length of time.

For the rest, what we commonly call friends and friendships are nothing but acquaintances and familiarities, knit by some chance or convenience, by means of which our souls hold a little conversation. But in the friendship I am speaking of, the souls mix and blend into each other so completely that they wear away the seam that joined them, and cannot find it again. If you pressed me to say why I loved him, I feel it could only be expressed by answering: Because it was he; because it was I.

There is, beyond all my reasoning and beyond everything I can say in particular, some inexplicable and fated force that brokered this union. We were seeking each other before we ever met, on the strength of what we heard of each other — which worked on our affections more than, in reason, mere reports should. I think it was some ordinance of heaven. We embraced each other in our names. And at our first meeting, which happened by chance at a great town feast, we found ourselves so taken with each other, so well known to each other, so bound to each other, that from then on nothing was as near to us as each other. He wrote an excellent Latin satire, since published, in which he excuses and explains the haste of our understanding, so quickly grown to its perfection. Having so little time to last, and having begun so late — for we were both grown men, and he some years the older — it had no time to lose, and no reason to pattern itself on those slow, regular friendships that need so many precautions of long preliminary acquaintance.

This friendship has no model but itself, and can be compared only to itself. It was not one special consideration, nor two, nor three, nor four, nor a thousand. It was some quintessence of the whole mixture which, having seized my entire will, led it to plunge and lose itself in his; which, having seized his entire will, led it to plunge and lose itself in mine, with an equal hunger and an equal striving. I say lose, in all truth, for we kept back nothing for ourselves — nothing that was either his or mine.

When Laelius, in the presence of the Roman consuls — who, after condemning Tiberius Gracchus, were prosecuting everyone who had been close to him — came to ask Caius Blosius, Gracchus's dearest friend, how much he would have done for him, Blosius answered: "Everything." "What, everything?" said Laelius. "And what if he had commanded you to set fire to our temples?" "He would never have commanded me that," Blosius replied. "But what if he had?" Laelius pressed. "I would have obeyed him," said the other. If he was as perfect a friend to Gracchus as the histories report, he had no need to provoke the consuls with that last bold confession; though he might well have stood firm in the assurance he had of Gracchus's will. But those who condemn his answer as seditious do not really understand the mystery, and fail to presuppose what was true: that he held Gracchus's will in his sleeve, both by the power of a friend and by his perfect knowledge of the man. They were friends more than citizens — friends more than friends or enemies of their country, or friends of ambition and upheaval. Having given themselves absolutely to each other, each held absolutely the reins of the other's inclination. Suppose this team driven by virtue and guided by reason — and without that it cannot be harnessed at all — and Blosius's answer is exactly what it ought to have been. If their actions ever flew out of the handle, then by my measure of friendship they were neither friends to each other nor friends to themselves.

Besides, that answer sounds no worse than mine would, if someone asked me, "If your will commanded you to kill your daughter, would you kill her?" and I answered yes. That implies no consent to the deed, because I have no shadow of doubt about my own will — and just as little about the will of such a friend. All the eloquence in the world has no power to dislodge me from the certainty I have of his intentions and judgments. Not one action of his could be put before me — whatever face it wore — without my finding its motive at first sight. Our souls pulled so exactly together, they regarded each other with so ardent an affection, and with the same affection laid open to each other the very bottom of our hearts, that I not only knew his soul as well as my own, but in any business of mine I would sooner have trusted myself to him than to myself.

Let no one set those other, common friendships in the same rank as this. I know them as well as the next man, and the most perfect of their kind too; but I advise against mixing up their rules — you would be badly deceived. In those other friendships you must walk with the bridle in your hand, with prudence and caution; the knot is not tied so tight that there is no cause to mistrust it. "Love him," said Chilo, "as if you must one day hate him; and hate him as if you must one day love him." This precept, which would be an abomination in the sovereign and ruling friendship I am describing, is perfectly sound in the practice of ordinary and customary ones — to which you may also apply the saying Aristotle had so often in his mouth: "O my friends, there is no friend."

In this noble commerce, the services and benefits that feed other friendships do not even deserve to be counted. The reason is the complete fusion of our wills. For just as the kindness I have for myself gets no increase from the help I give myself in time of need — whatever the Stoics say — and just as I feel no gratitude to myself for any service I do myself, so the union of such friends, being truly perfect, makes them lose the very feeling of such duties, and hate and banish from between them these words of division and difference: benefit, obligation, gratitude, request, thanks, and the rest. Everything being actually common between them — wills, thoughts, judgments, goods, wives, children, honor, and life — and their agreement being that of one soul in two bodies, by Aristotle's very apt definition, they can neither lend nor give anything to each other. That is why the lawmakers, to honor marriage with some imagined resemblance to this divine bond, forbid gifts between husband and wife: to infer by it that everything should belong to both of them, and that they have nothing to divide or share out.

In the friendship I am speaking of, if one could give to the other, it is the one receiving the kindness who would oblige his friend. For since each of them, above everything, is looking for the chance to do the other good, the one who supplies the matter and the occasion is the generous one — giving his friend the satisfaction of doing for him the very thing he most desires. When the philosopher Diogenes was short of money, he used to say he asked his friends to give it back, not to give it.

And to show how this works in practice, here is a singular old example. Eudamidas of Corinth had two friends, Charixenus of Sicyon and Areteus of Corinth. Coming to die a poor man, his two friends being rich, he made his will like this: "I bequeath to Areteus the keeping of my mother, to support and provide for her in her old age. To Charixenus I bequeath the marrying of my daughter, and the giving of as large a dowry as he can manage. And in case one of them should die, I substitute the survivor in his place." Those who first saw this will had a good laugh at it. But the heirs, once they were told, accepted it with extraordinary satisfaction; and when Charixenus died five days later, and both charges fell to Areteus alone, he kept the old woman with great care and tenderness, and of the five talents he had in his estate, he gave two and a half in marriage with his own only daughter, and two and a half in marriage with the daughter of Eudamidas — and held both weddings on the same day.

This example is very full — except for one objection: the plurality of friends. For the perfect friendship I am speaking of is indivisible. Each gives himself so entirely to his friend that he has nothing left to share elsewhere. On the contrary, he is sorry he is not double, triple, quadruple — sorry he does not have several souls and several wills, so as to confer them all on this one object. Common friendships can be divided up: you can love the beauty of this man, the easy temper of that one, the generosity of a third, a father's affection in a fourth, a brother's in a fifth, and so on. But this friendship that possesses the whole soul, and rules there with absolute sovereignty, cannot possibly be doubled. If two friends called for your help at the same moment, which would you run to? If they asked contrary services of you, how could you serve both? If one trusted to your silence a thing it mattered for the other to know, how would you get clear? A single, ruling friendship dissolves every other obligation. The secret I have sworn to reveal to no other, I can impart without perjury to him who is not another: he is myself. It is a great enough miracle for a man to double himself; those who talk of tripling do not know the height of the thing. Nothing is extreme that has its match. And whoever supposes that of two friends I love one as much as the other, and that they love each other and love me just as I love them, multiplies into a brotherhood the most single and unified thing in the world — the thing of which even one example is the hardest in the world to find.

The rest of that story fits very well with what I was saying. For Eudamidas grants it to his friends, as a kindness and a favor, to be of use to him in his need. He leaves them heirs to his own generosity, which consists in putting into their hands the means of doing him good. And without doubt the force of friendship shows far more richly in his act than in Areteus's. In short, these are effects beyond the imagining of anyone who has not tasted them. They make me honor beyond measure the answer of that young soldier to Cyrus, who asked him how much he would take for a horse that had just won him the prize in a race — whether he would trade it for a kingdom: "No indeed, Sire. But I would gladly let him go to gain a true friend by it, if I could find a man worthy of such an alliance." He did not speak badly when he said "if I could find." You can easily enough find men fit for a superficial acquaintance. But in this kind, where a man deals from the very bottom of his heart and holds nothing back, you can be sure that every spring of the works must be perfectly true and perfectly sure.

In partnerships that hold only by one end, we need only guard against the flaws that touch that particular end. It cannot matter to me what religion my doctor or my lawyer holds; that consideration has nothing in common with the services of friendship they owe me. And in the household dealings between me and my servants, I take the same line. I hardly ask whether a footman is chaste; I ask whether he is diligent. A gambling muleteer troubles me less than a weak one, a foul-mouthed cook less than an ignorant one. I do not set up to tell the world what it should do — plenty of others meddle with that — only to give an account of what I do in it. As Terence has it: this is my way; as for you, do as you find needful.

At table I prefer the witty to the wise; in bed, beauty before goodness; in conversation, the best talker, sincere or not. And so with everything else. There was a man caught astride a stick, playing horses with his children, who asked the one who surprised him to say nothing of it until he was a father himself — judging that the fondness which would then be born in his own soul would make him a fair judge of the sight. So I, too, could wish I were speaking only to people who have experienced what I am telling. But knowing how far such a friendship lies from common practice, and how rare it is, I do not expect to meet a single good judge of it. For even the discourses antiquity has left us on the subject seem flat and weak to me beside the feeling I have of it. And in this point the facts outdo the very precepts of philosophy. As Horace says, while I have my senses, there is nothing I would set against an agreeable friend.

Old Menander called that man happy who had managed to meet even the shadow of a friend. He certainly had reason to say so, especially if he spoke from experience. For in truth, if I compare all the rest of my life — though by God's grace I have passed it pleasantly enough, comfortably, and, except for the loss of such a friend, free of any heavy affliction, in great tranquility of mind, content with my natural and original advantages without hunting after others — if I compare the whole of it, I say, with the four years I was given to enjoy the sweet company and society of that excellent man, it is nothing but smoke, nothing but a dark and tedious night.

Since the day I lost him — a day forever bitter to me, forever honored, for so, you gods, you willed it, as Virgil says — I have only dragged out a languishing life. Even the pleasures that offer themselves to me, instead of consoling me, double my grief at his loss. We went halves in everything; it seems to me now that by outliving him I am stealing his share. I had resolved, as Terence's character says, that it was not right for me to enjoy any pleasure while the partner of all my pleasures was gone.

I was already so accustomed to be always two, everywhere and in everything, that it seems to me now I am no more than half of myself. If an untimely blow carried off that half of my soul, as Horace asks, why do I linger here, the other half — not so dear now, and not whole? The same day was the ruin of us both.

There is no action or thought of mine in which I do not miss him — as I know he would have missed me. For just as he surpassed me by infinite degrees in virtue and every other ability, so he did in the duty of friendship. What shame or measure can there be, Horace asks, in mourning a head so dear? And Catullus's lament for his brother says it for me: O brother, torn from me in my misery — with you all our joys have gone, the joys your sweet love fed while you lived. Dying, brother, you shattered all my happiness; my whole soul is buried with you; since your death I have driven from my mind these studies and every delight of the mind. Shall I never speak with you, never hear you speak again? Never see you again, brother dearer than life itself? At least I will love you always.

But let us hear this boy of sixteen speak for a while.

Because I have found that this work has since been brought out, and to a bad end, by men who aim to disturb and change the state of our government without troubling themselves whether they will improve it — and because they have mixed it in among writings of their own making — I have changed my mind about placing it here. And so that the author's memory may take no harm with those who could not know his opinions and his conduct at close hand, I tell them that he treated this subject in his boyhood, only by way of exercise, as a common theme worked over in a thousand books. I have no doubt he believed what he wrote, for he was conscientious enough not to lie even in jest. And I know besides that, had the choice been his, he would rather have been born in Venice than in Sarlat — and with reason. But he had another maxim sovereignly imprinted on his soul: to obey, and most religiously to submit to, the laws under which he was born. There never was a better citizen, or one who cared more for his country's quiet, or a sharper enemy of the commotions and innovations of his time. He would far sooner have used his talents to put out those civil fires than to give them anything to feed on. His mind was cast in the mold of better ages than ours.

So, in exchange for that serious work, I will substitute another from the same hand, written in the same season of his life — but of a gayer and more playful air.

Of Cannibals

When King Pyrrhus invaded Italy and got his first good look at the army the Romans had sent against him, he said: "I don't know what kind of barbarians these are" — the Greeks called all foreigners barbarians — "but there is nothing barbarous about the way this army is arranged." The Greeks said much the same about the army Flaminius brought into their country, and Philip, looking down from a hill at the order and layout of the Roman camp in his kingdom, said it too. Which shows how careful we ought to be about swallowing common opinion. We should judge things by the eye of reason, not by popular report.

For a long time I had a man living in my house who had spent ten or twelve years in the New World — the one discovered in our own century, in the region where Villegaignon landed and which he named Antarctic France. The discovery of so vast a country seems to me worth thinking about seriously. And I cannot promise that another one won't turn up tomorrow, since so many men wiser than we are have been wrong about this already. I am afraid our eyes are bigger than our stomachs — that we have more curiosity than capacity. We grab at everything and catch nothing but wind.

Plato has Solon tell a story he heard from the priests of Saïs in Egypt: that long ago, before the Flood, there was a great island called Atlantis, sitting right at the mouth of the Straits of Gibraltar, larger than Africa and Asia put together. Its kings owned not just the island but a stretch of the mainland too — across Africa as far as Egypt, and into Europe as far as Tuscany — and they set out to swallow Asia as well, and to subdue every nation bordering the Mediterranean all the way to the Black Sea. They overran Spain, Gaul, and Italy, and pushed into Greece, where the Athenians stopped them. Some time after, the Athenians, the invaders, and the island itself were all swallowed by the Flood.

It is quite likely that this tremendous rush of water made strange changes in the dwelling places of the earth. They say it was the sea that split Sicily from Italy — "these lands, once a single country, were torn apart by violence and vast ruin," as Virgil has it — and Cyprus from Syria, and the island of Negropont from the mainland of Boeotia; and that elsewhere it joined lands that had been separate, filling the channels between them with sand and mud. "What was long a sterile marsh, where oars dipped, now feeds neighboring cities and feels the weight of the plough," says Horace.

But there is no real chance that this island was the New World we have just discovered. Atlantis practically touched Spain, and it would take an unbelievable flood to shove it back more than twelve hundred leagues. Besides, our modern navigators have nearly established that the New World is no island at all but solid mainland — joined to the East Indies on one side and to the lands under the two poles on the other, or, if it is cut off from them, cut off by a strait so narrow that it hardly earns the name of island on that account.

It seems that in this great body of the world, as in our own, there are two kinds of motion: one natural, the other feverish. When I consider what my own river, the Dordogne, has done in my lifetime to the right bank as it flows down — how much ground it has gained in twenty years, undermining the foundations of house after house — I can see this is no ordinary disturbance. If it had always moved at this pace, or were to keep it up, the face of the world would be turned upside down. But rivers change their moods: sometimes they wear at one bank, sometimes the other, and sometimes they keep quietly to their channel. I am not talking about sudden floods, whose causes everyone understands. In Médoc, along the seashore, my brother the Sieur d'Arsac watches an estate of his lie buried under the sand the sea vomits up ahead of itself. The rooftops of some buildings can still be seen; his rents and farmland have turned into miserably thin pasture. The locals say the sea has been driving at them so hard in recent years that they have lost four leagues of land. These sands are her advance scouts — and we can see great dunes of moving sand marching half a league ahead of her, conquering ground.

The other ancient testimony that people try to attach to this discovery is in Aristotle — at least if that little book of Unheard-of Wonders is really his. He tells us there that certain Carthaginians, having struck out across the Atlantic beyond the Straits of Gibraltar and sailed a very long time, finally discovered a great fertile island, all covered with woods and watered by broad, deep rivers, far from any mainland. They — and others after them, drawn by the goodness and richness of the soil — went there with their wives and children and started a colony. But the senate of Carthage, seeing its population draining away little by little, issued an express ban, on pain of death, against anyone else going, and drove out the new settlers — fearing, it is said, that in time they would multiply enough to supplant Carthage herself and ruin the state. But Aristotle's tale fits our new lands no better than Plato's.

This man of mine was a plain, simple fellow — which makes him the more likely to tell the truth. Your refined sort of man is far more curious, true, and notices much more; but then he glosses it. To give weight to what he says and win you over, he cannot resist improving the story a little. Such men never show you things as they are; they show you things as they appeared to them, or as they would like them to appear to you. And to earn a reputation for judgment and make their account more convincing, they happily pad out the facts with inventions of their own. What we need is either a man of scrupulous honesty, or one so simple that he lacks the raw material to build false stories and make them look plausible — a man with nothing to gain from a lie. Mine was that man. And besides, he brought to my house, at various times, several sailors and merchants who had made the same voyage. So I am content with his information, without troubling to ask what the cosmographers say about it.

What we need are topographers who would give us exact accounts of the particular places they have actually been. But because a man has had the advantage over us of seeing the Holy Land, he claims the privilege of telling us news about all the rest of the world too. I wish everyone would write what he knows, and only as much as he knows — and not just on this subject but on every subject. A man may have precise knowledge and experience of the nature of one river or one spring, and on everything else know no more than the next fellow; yet to put his little scrap of learning into circulation, he will undertake to write the whole science of nature. From this vice great troubles grow.

Now, to return to my subject: I find that there is nothing barbarous or savage about this nation, from anything I have been told — except that everyone calls barbarism whatever is not his own practice. And indeed we have no other measure of truth and reason than the example and pattern of the opinions and customs of the place we live in. There — always there — is the perfect religion, the perfect government, the perfectly polished way of doing everything.

These people are wild in the same sense that we call fruits wild — wild, the way we call fruits wild that nature produces by herself, in her own ordinary course. When in truth, the ones we ought to call wild are those we have altered by our tricks and twisted out of the common order. In the first, the genuine virtues and properties — the most useful and natural ones — are alive and vigorous; in the second, we have bastardized them, adapting them to the pleasure of our own corrupted taste. And even so, our taste has to admit that several of the fruits of those countries, grown without any cultivation at all, have a flavor and delicacy excellent enough to rival the best of ours.

It is not reasonable that art should win the place of honor over our great and powerful mother, nature. We have so overloaded the beauty and richness of her works with our own inventions and ornaments that we have very nearly smothered her. Yet wherever she shines in her own purity, she puts our vain, frivolous efforts wonderfully to shame. "The ivy grows best where it grows by itself; the arbutus is loveliest in the lonely cave; and birds sing the sweeter for having no training," as Propertius says. With all our straining we cannot even copy the nest of the smallest bird — its structure, its beauty, its convenience — or so much as the web of a poor spider. All things, says Plato, are produced by nature, by fortune, or by art: the greatest and most beautiful by one of the first two, the least and most imperfect by the last.

These nations, then, seem to me barbarous only in this sense: that they have received very little shaping from the human mind, and are still very close to their original simplicity. The laws of nature still govern them, hardly corrupted at all by ours — and in such purity that I am sometimes sorry we did not learn of them earlier, in the days when there were men better able to judge them than we are. I am sorry Lycurgus and Plato never knew them. For it seems to me that what we actually see in these nations surpasses not only all the pictures the poets have painted of the golden age, and all their inventions for imagining a happy condition of man, but the very conception and desire of philosophy itself. The philosophers could never imagine a simplicity as native and pure as the one we see with our own eyes; they could never believe that human society could be held together with so little artifice and human solder.

This is a nation, I would tell Plato, in which there is no kind of commerce; no knowledge of letters; no science of numbers; no word for magistrate or political superiority; no servants; no riches and no poverty; no contracts, no inheritances, no divisions of property; no occupations but easy ones; no respect for any kinship except the common kind; no clothing; no agriculture; no metal; no use of wheat or wine. The very words for lying, treachery, deceit, greed, envy, slander, and forgiveness — unheard of. How far short of this perfection would he find his imaginary Republic? These are "men fresh from the gods," as Seneca says; "these are the ways nature first taught," says Virgil.

For the rest: they live in a country very pleasant and temperate, so that, my witnesses tell me, a sick person is a rare sight. They assure me they never saw a single native palsied, bleary-eyed, toothless, or bent with age. They are settled along the seacoast, walled in on the landward side by great high mountains, with a strip about a hundred leagues wide between. They have a great abundance of fish and meat that bear no resemblance to ours, and they eat them with no preparation beyond plain cooking. The first man who rode a horse there — though he had made friends among them on several earlier voyages — so terrified them with his centaur shape that they shot him dead with arrows before they could recognize him.

Their buildings are very long, big enough to hold two or three hundred people, made of the bark of tall trees set with one end in the ground and leaning together at the top, supporting one another like some of our barns, whose roofs slope all the way down and double as walls. They have wood so hard they cut with it, making their swords of it, and grills for cooking their meat. Their beds are of woven cotton, slung from the roof like our sailors' hammocks, each man with his own — for the wives sleep apart from their husbands. They rise with the sun and eat, immediately, enough for the whole day; that is their only meal. They drink nothing with it — like certain Eastern peoples Suidas mentions, who never drank at meals — but they drink many times through the rest of the day, and sometimes heroically. Their drink is made from a certain root and is the color of our claret; they only drink it lukewarm. It keeps no more than two or three days; it has a slightly sharp, lively taste, goes nothing to the head, is good for the stomach — laxative for newcomers, but a very pleasant drink for anyone used to it. In place of bread they use a certain white substance, like preserved coriander. I have tasted it: sweet, and a little flat.

The whole day is spent in dancing. The young men hunt wild animals with bows and arrows, while some of the women busy themselves warming the drink — that is their chief duty. In the morning, before they begin to eat, one of the old men preaches to the whole household, walking from one end of the building to the other and repeating the same sentence many times until he has finished the circuit — for the houses are a good hundred yards long. He commends only two things to them: courage against their enemies and love for their wives. And he never fails to note, in closing, that it is the wives who keep their drink warm and well seasoned. You can see, in several places — my own house among them — the style of their beds, their ropes, their swords, the wooden bracelets they tie on their wrists for battle, and the long canes, open at one end, whose sound keeps the rhythm of their dances. They shave their whole bodies, and more cleanly than we do, with no razor but one of wood or stone. They believe the soul is immortal, and that those who have deserved well of the gods are lodged in the part of the sky where the sun rises, and the damned in the west.

They have priests and prophets of some sort, who very rarely show themselves to the people, living as they do in the mountains. When one arrives there is a great festival and a solemn gathering of many villages — each longhouse, as I have described it, makes a village, and they stand about a French league apart. The prophet speaks to them in public, exhorting them to virtue and to their duty; but their whole moral science contains just the two articles: resolution in war and affection for their wives. He also prophesies things to come, and the outcomes they should expect from their undertakings, and steers them toward war or away from it. But he had better get it right: if he fails in his prediction, and things fall out otherwise than he foretold, he is cut into a thousand pieces — if they catch him — and condemned as a false prophet. For that reason, a prophet who has once guessed wrong is never seen again.

Divination is a gift of God; that is why abusing it ought to be punished as fraud. Among the Scythians, when the diviners missed the mark, they were laid, bound hand and foot, on carts full of brushwood drawn by oxen, and burned there. Those who handle matters within the reach of ordinary human ability can be excused for doing their best. But these others, who come to us boasting of an extraordinary faculty beyond our understanding — shouldn't they be punished for failing to deliver on their promise, and for the recklessness of their fraud?

They have constant wars with the nations beyond their mountains, further inland — and they go to them naked, with no weapons but bows and wooden swords pointed like our boar-spears. Their steadiness in battle is astonishing, and the fighting never ends without great slaughter, for as to running away — they do not know what it is. Every man brings home as his trophy the head of an enemy he has killed and mounts it over his door. They treat their prisoners well for a long time, giving them every comfort they can think of; then the man who owns a prisoner calls a great assembly of his friends. He ties a rope to one of the prisoner's arms and holds the end of it, standing back out of reach, and gives the other arm to his dearest friend to hold the same way; and the two of them, before the whole assembly, kill him with their swords. That done, they roast him, eat him together, and send portions to absent friends.

They do not do this for food, as the ancient Scythians did, but as the extreme form of revenge. The proof: when they saw that the Portuguese, who were allied with their enemies, used a different sort of execution on those they captured — burying them to the waist, shooting the exposed half full of arrows, and then hanging them — these people of the other world (men who had sown the knowledge of plenty of vices among their neighbors, and were far greater masters of every kind of wickedness than they were) decided that such a revenge must mean something, and must hurt more than their own. So they began to abandon their old method and follow this one.

I am not sorry that we notice the barbarous horror of such an act. I am very sorry that, judging their faults so correctly, we should be so blind to our own. I think there is more barbarity in eating a man alive than in eating him dead; more barbarity in tearing apart, by rack and torture, a body still full of feeling, in roasting it by degrees, in having it bitten and mangled by dogs and pigs — as we have not only read but seen, within living memory, not between old enemies but between neighbors and fellow citizens, and, what is worse, under the banner of piety and religion — than in roasting and eating a man after he is dead.

Chrysippus and Zeno, the two heads of the Stoic school, thought there was no harm in using our dead bodies any way we need to, including for food — as our own ancestors did when, besieged by Caesar in the town of Alesia, they resolved to outlast the famine on the bodies of the old men, the women, and the others who were useless for fighting. "The Gascons, it is said, prolonged their lives on such food," says Juvenal. And our physicians think nothing of using corpses in every sort of way for our health, applying them outside or prescribing them within. But no opinion was ever so unruly as to excuse treachery, disloyalty, tyranny, and cruelty — which are our everyday vices.

So we may well call these people barbarians by the rules of reason, but not by comparison with ourselves, who outdo them in every kind of barbarity. Their warfare is wholly noble and generous, and has as much excuse and beauty as that human disease can have. Among them it rests on one foundation only: rivalry in courage. They are not fighting to conquer new land; what they have is so naturally fruitful that it supplies, without labor or worry, everything they need, in such abundance that they have no reason to enlarge their borders. And they are happy in this too — that they desire only what their natural needs demand; everything beyond that is simply useless to them. Men of the same age call one another brothers; the younger ones are children; the old men are fathers to everyone. The old leave the full possession of their goods to all heirs in common, undivided, with no other title than the one nature gives every creature by bringing it into the world.

If their neighbors cross the mountains to attack them, and win, the victor's prize is glory — the advantage of having proved the better in valor and virtue. They have no other use for the goods of the conquered; they turn around and go home, where they lack nothing they need, and lack nothing of that greatest good of all: knowing how to enjoy their condition happily, and to be content with it. And the others, in their turn, do the same. They ask of their prisoners no ransom but this — an admission of being beaten. But in a whole century you will not find one who would not rather die than make that confession, or yield, by a single word or look, one inch of an unbreakable courage. There is not a man among them who would not rather be killed and eaten than so much as open his mouth to ask to be spared. They treat their prisoners with complete freedom and generosity, so that life will be the dearer to them — and meanwhile entertain them with threats of their coming death, of the torments they will suffer, of the preparations under way, of the chopping of their limbs, and of the feast at which they will be the only dish. All of it for one purpose: to wring some soft or submissive word from them, or to make them want to run — to gain the advantage of having frightened them and bent their constancy. Because, rightly understood, that is the only point in which true victory consists. "There is no victory unless the enemy admits it in his own mind," as Claudian says.

The Hungarians, a very warlike people, never used to press their advantage further than bringing the enemy to surrender. Once they had forced that confession, they let him go unharmed and unransomed — at most making him give his word never to bear arms against them again. Plenty of the advantages we gain over our enemies are borrowed, not truly ours. To have stronger arms and legs is the quality of a porter, not a virtue. Agility is a dead, physical quality. It is a stroke of luck when our enemy stumbles, or has the sun in his eyes. To be a good fencer is a trick of training and skill, which may land on a coward or a nobody. The worth and value of a man lie in his heart and his will; there his true honor lives. Valor is strength not of legs and arms but of courage and soul; it does not lie in the worth of our horse or our weapons, but in our own.
