The man who falls still fierce in his courage — who "fights on his knees if his legs fail him," as Seneca says — the man who, facing certain death, gives up not an ounce of his assurance, who as he dies still throws his enemy a hard and scornful look: that man is beaten not by us but by fortune. He is killed, not conquered. The bravest men are sometimes the unluckiest.

So there are defeats more triumphant than victories. Those four sister victories, the fairest the sun ever looked on — Salamis, Plataea, Mycale, and Sicily — would never dare set all their combined glory against the glory of the annihilation of King Leonidas and his men at the pass of Thermopylae. Who ever ran more gloriously, more ambitiously, toward winning a battle than Captain Ischolas ran toward losing one? Who ever worked more ingeniously to save himself than he did to destroy himself? He was assigned to hold a pass in the Peloponnese against the Arcadians. Considering the terrain and the odds, he saw it was utterly impossible — every man who faced that enemy would stay on that ground forever. On the other hand he judged it unworthy of his own courage, his greatness of spirit, and the Spartan name to fail in his duty. Between these two extremes he took a middle road: the youngest and fittest of his men he saved for the defense and service of their country, and sent home; with the rest, whose loss would matter less, he resolved to hold the pass and make the enemy buy their entry as dearly as he possibly could. So it fell out. Surrounded on every side by the Arcadians, he and his men made a great slaughter of them and were cut to pieces, every one. Is there a trophy raised to victors that was not better earned by these losers? True conquest plays its part in the fight, not in the outcome; the honor of valor is in fighting, not in winning.

But to return to my story. These prisoners, far from showing the smallest weakness under all the terrors heaped on them, do the opposite: through the two or three months they are kept, they wear a cheerful face. They urge their masters to hurry up and put them to the test. They defy them, insult them, taunt them with their cowardice and with the count of battles they have lost to the prisoners' own people. I have a song composed by one of these prisoners, in which he dares them all to come and dine on him, and welcome — for they will be eating, at the same time, their own fathers and grandfathers, whose flesh has fed and nourished his body. "These muscles," he says, "this flesh, these veins — they are yours, poor fools that you are. You do not see that the substance of your ancestors' limbs is still in them. Taste carefully, and you will find the flavor of your own flesh." There is nothing of the barbarian in that invention. Those who paint these people dying show the prisoner spitting in his executioners' faces and making faces at them. And it is certain that to the last breath they never stop defying and taunting them, in word and gesture. Truly, here are real savages by our standards — for either they must be, or we must be. There is an astonishing distance between their ways and ours.

The men there have several wives, and the higher their reputation for courage, the more wives they have. One genuinely remarkable thing about their marriages: the same jealousy our wives use to keep us from the friendship and intimacy of other women, their wives use to win it for them. Caring more for their husbands' honor than for anything else, they take pains to seek out and bring in as many companions as they can — since that is a testimony to the husband's worth. Our wives will cry miracle; it is not. It is a properly matrimonial virtue, and of the highest order. In the Bible, Sarah, and Leah and Rachel, the wives of Jacob, gave their beautiful handmaids to their husbands. Livia furthered the appetites of Augustus, against her own interest. And Stratonice, the wife of King Deiotarus, not only gave a lovely young chambermaid to her husband's bed but carefully raised the children he had by her, and helped them to their father's throne.

And so that no one thinks all this is done out of simple, slavish bondage to custom, under the pressure of ancient habit, without thought or judgment — because their souls are too dull to do anything else — I should show you a few samples of what they are capable of. Besides the war song I quoted, I have another of their songs, a love song, which begins like this: "Stay, adder, stay — so that my sister may copy the pattern of your markings, and make from it a rich ribbon to give my beloved; so shall your beauty and the elegant order of your scales be honored forever above all other serpents." That first phrase, "Stay, adder," is the refrain of the song. Now I have spent enough of my life with poetry to say this: not only is there nothing barbarous in this invention, it is downright Anacreontic. Their language, by the way, is a soft language, pleasant to the ear, with endings rather like Greek.

Three of these people — not knowing how dearly their peace and happiness will one day pay for acquaintance with the corruptions of our side of the world, and that this contact will be their ruin, which I suppose is already well under way; poor men, to let themselves be cheated by the appetite for new things, and to have left the sweetness of their own sky to come and look at ours! — three of them were at Rouen when the late King Charles the Ninth was there. The king talked with them a long while; they were shown our manners, our pomp, the shape of a fine city. Afterward someone asked their opinion — wanted to know what they had found most remarkable in all they had seen. They named three things. I have forgotten the third, and I am sorry for it; but two I still remember.

They said, first, that they found it very strange that so many tall, bearded men, strong and well armed, who stood around the king — they probably meant the Swiss of his guard — should consent to obey a child, instead of choosing one of themselves to command. Second — they have a way of speaking in which they call men halves of one another — they said they had noticed among us men gorged and stuffed with every sort of comfort, while their halves stood begging at their doors, gaunt with hunger and poverty; and they found it strange that these needy halves could endure such injustice without taking the others by the throat or setting fire to their houses.

I talked with one of them a long while; but my interpreter was so bad, and so hampered by his own stupidity from grasping what I meant, that I could get hardly anything worthwhile out of him. When I asked the man what benefit he got from his rank among his own people — for he was a captain, and our sailors called him king — he said: to march first into war. Asked how many men followed him, he pointed to a stretch of ground, meaning as many as that space would hold — perhaps four or five thousand men. Asked whether his authority lapsed when the war ended, he said this much of it remained: when he visited the villages that depended on him, they cut paths for him through the thick of their woods, so that he could travel at his ease.

None of that sounds bad at all — but what of that? They don't wear pants.

Of Solitude

Let's skip the long old debate between the active life and the solitary one. And as for the fine sayings that ambition and greed hide behind — that we are not born for ourselves but for the public — let's appeal boldly to the people actually in public life. Let them put their hands on their hearts and tell us whether titles, offices, and the bustle of the world aren't really pursued for private advantage at public expense. The corrupt methods men use nowadays to climb to the heights their ambition aims at show plainly enough that the ends can't be worth much.

Tell ambition this: she's the one who gives us a taste for solitude in the first place. What does she avoid more than company? What does she crave more than elbow room?

A man can behave well or badly anywhere. But if Bias is right that the greater part of men is the worse part, or the Preacher that there's not one good man in a thousand — good men, as Juvenal says, being scarcer than the gates of Thebes or the mouths of the Nile — then the contagion in a crowd is very dangerous. You must either imitate the vicious or hate them, and both are risky: to grow like them because they are many, or to hate the many because they are unlike you.

Merchants who go to sea are right to be careful that the people who board with them aren't blasphemers or scoundrels — they consider such company unlucky. That's why Bias said cheerfully to some shipmates who were calling on the gods in the middle of a dangerous storm, "Quiet — keep your voices down, so they don't realize you're here with me." And a more striking case: Albuquerque, viceroy of the Indies for King Emmanuel of Portugal, in extreme danger of shipwreck, hoisted a young boy onto his shoulders for one reason only — that in their shared danger the boy's innocence might serve as his protection and recommend him to God's favor, and so get him safely to shore.

It's not that a wise man can't live content anywhere, alone even in the thick of a palace crowd. But given the choice, the philosopher will tell you he'd flee the very sight of the crowd. He'll endure it if he must; left to himself, he'll choose to be alone. He can't feel sufficiently rid of vice while he still has to wrestle with it in other men. Charondas punished as wicked those convicted of keeping wicked company. There is nothing so unsociable and so sociable as man: unsociable by his vice, sociable by his nature. And Antisthenes, I think, gave a poor answer to the man who reproached him for keeping bad company, when he said that physicians live well enough among the sick. For if physicians do help the sick get healthy, they certainly damage their own health through the contagion, the constant sight of disease, and the daily handling of it.

Now the goal, as I take it, is always the same: to live more at leisure and at one's ease. But people don't always take the right road to it. They often think they've quit business altogether when they've only traded one job for another. There's hardly less trouble in governing a private family than a whole kingdom. Wherever the mind is tangled up, it's tangled entirely; and domestic chores are no less bothersome for being less important. Besides, by shaking off the court and the marketplace, we haven't shaken off the chief torments of life. As Horace says, it's reason and prudence that banish care, not a house with a commanding view of the sea. Ambition, greed, indecision, fear, and runaway desires don't stay behind just because we leave the country — black care, as Horace also says, sits right behind the horseman. They follow us into cloisters and philosophy schools. Neither deserts, nor caves, nor hair shirts, nor fasting can shake them off: the fatal arrow stays lodged in our side.

Someone told Socrates that a certain man hadn't been improved at all by his travels. "I believe it," said Socrates, "for he took himself along with him." Why do we go looking for lands warmed by a different sun? What exile from his country ever escaped from himself too?

If a man doesn't first unload himself and his mind of the burden weighing on him, moving around will only make it press harder and sit heavier — the way a ship's cargo is less trouble when it's stowed snug and settled. You do a sick man more harm than good by moving him from place to place; you drive the disease in deeper by jostling it, just as stakes sink deeper and stand firmer the more you work them up and down in the hole where they're meant to stay. So it's not enough to get away from the public; it's not enough to change your ground. A man must get away from the crowd's habits that have taken up residence inside him. He must sequester himself and take possession of himself again. You say you've broken your chains? The dog who finally snaps his chain after a long struggle still drags a good length of it from his neck as he runs. We carry our chains with us. Our liberty isn't absolute; we keep glancing back at what we've left behind, and our imagination is still full of it. Unless the mind is purged, as Lucretius says, what battles and dangers we take on in spite of ourselves — what bitter anxieties, what terrors, what wreckage from pride, lust, anger, luxury, and sloth!

Our disease is in the mind, and the mind cannot escape from itself — the fault, says Horace, lies in a mind that never gets away from itself. So the mind must be called home and shut up inside itself. That is true solitude, and it can be enjoyed even in crowded cities and the courts of kings — though more comfortably apart.

Now, since we're undertaking to live alone and do without company, let's arrange it so that our contentment depends entirely on ourselves. Let's dissolve every tie that binds us to others. Let's win this much from ourselves: to be able to live alone in earnest, and to live at our ease doing it.

Stilpo escaped the burning of his city, where he lost his wife, his children, and everything he owned. Demetrius Poliorcetes, seeing him walk out of so great a ruin with an untroubled face, asked whether he hadn't suffered any loss. "No," he answered — thank God, nothing of his was lost. The philosopher Antisthenes meant the same thing when he said, neatly, that a man should equip himself with goods that float, and can swim out of a shipwreck with their owner. Certainly a wise man never loses anything, as long as he has himself. When the city of Nola was sacked by the barbarians, Paulinus, the bishop there, lost everything he had and was taken prisoner; and he prayed like this: "Lord, keep me from feeling this loss; for Thou knowest they have not yet touched anything that is mine." The riches that made him rich and the goods that made him good were still intact. That is what it means to choose treasures that can't be plundered, and to hide them in a place no one can enter — a place that can be betrayed by no one but ourselves.

A man should have a wife, children, property, and above all health, if he can get them. But we must not fasten our hearts to them so tightly that our happiness depends on them. We must reserve a back shop all our own, entirely free, where we keep our real liberty — our principal retreat and solitude. There our daily conversation should be with ourselves, and so private that no outside business or company gets in. There we should laugh and talk as if we had no wife, no children, no property, no staff, no servants — so that when the day comes that we lose them, it will be nothing new to do without them. We have a soul that can turn in on itself; it can keep itself company. It has the means to attack and to defend, to give and to receive. No need to fear that solitude will leave us slumped in boring emptiness — in solitude, as Tibullus says, be a crowd to yourself.

Virtue is satisfied with herself: no rules, no words, no audience. In our ordinary actions, not one in a thousand actually concerns ourselves. That man you see scrambling up the rubble of that wall, furious, beside himself, with all those gunshots aimed at him; and that other one, covered in scars, pale, faint with hunger, resolved to die rather than open the gates to him — do you think they're there for their own sake? No — most likely for the sake of someone they've never seen, who never gives a thought to their pain and danger, and is wallowing in idleness and pleasure the whole time. And this drooling, bleary-eyed, unwashed fellow you see shuffling out of his study after midnight — do you think he's been digging through books to learn how to be a better, wiser, happier man? No such thing. He'll die at that desk, but he'll teach posterity the meter of Plautus's verses and the correct spelling of a Latin word. Who doesn't willingly trade health, rest, and life itself for reputation and glory — the most useless, frivolous, counterfeit coin in circulation? As if our own death weren't frightening enough, we take on the deaths of our wives, children, and household. As if our own affairs didn't supply enough worry, we take up our neighbors' and friends' affairs too, the better to rack our brains and torment ourselves. Ah, says Terence — can any man let anything become dearer to him than himself?

Solitude seems to me to sit best on those who have already given their most active and flourishing years to the world, after the example of Thales. We have lived enough for others; let us live what little is left for ourselves. Let's bring our thoughts and intentions home, to ourselves and our own ease. Arranging a safe retreat is no small job; it will keep us busy enough without mixing in other projects. Since God gives us time to organize our departure, let's pack up, say our goodbyes early, and pry ourselves loose from those gripping obligations that drag us elsewhere and pull us away from ourselves.

We must untie the knot of our obligations, however strong. We may still love this person or that thing, but we should wed ourselves to nothing but ourselves. That is: let the rest be ours, but not glued so close that it can't be pulled away without flaying us or tearing out a piece of us. The greatest thing in the world is to know how to belong to yourself.

It's time to wean ourselves from society when we can no longer contribute to it. A man who's in no condition to lend should forbid himself to borrow. Our strength is failing; let's call it in and concentrate it on ourselves. Whoever can fold the duties of friendship and company back into himself, let him do it. In this decay of age, which makes a man useless, burdensome, and tiresome to others, let him take care not to become useless, burdensome, and tiresome to himself. Let him indulge and pamper himself, and above all govern himself with such respect for his own reason and conscience that he'd be ashamed to stumble in their presence — for it is rare, as Quintilian says, that a man holds himself in sufficient reverence.

Socrates says that boys should get themselves educated, grown men should practice doing good, and old men should withdraw from all civil and military employment and live at their own discretion, bound to no office. Some temperaments suit these precepts of retirement better than others. People of a soft, slow disposition, with delicate attachments and a will that doesn't readily submit to harness — and I am one, by nature and by conviction — will take to this advice more easily than active, busy souls who embrace everything, sign up for everything, run hot for everything, who offer and give themselves to every occasion. We should use these accidental, external goods as far as they please us, but never make them our main foundation. They aren't one; neither nature nor reason allows it. Why then, against their laws, should we enslave our contentment to a power outside ourselves?

But to leap ahead of fortune's accidents — to strip ourselves in advance of the comforts within our reach, as some have done out of devotion and some philosophers out of reasoning; to wait on yourself hand and foot, to sleep on boards, to put out your own eyes, to throw your wealth in the river, to go hunting for pain — some chasing bliss in the next life through misery in this one, others lying flat on the ground to be safe from falling — all that is virtue run to excess. Let the sturdiest, toughest natures make even their hiding glorious and exemplary. As Horace puts it: when money is short, I praise the safe little life, tough enough on slim rations — but the moment things get richer and easier, I declare that the only wise men, the only ones living well, are those whose money shows in handsome country villas.

A great deal less would do for me. It's enough, while fortune favors me, to prepare for the day she turns; enough, sitting comfortably, to picture trouble coming as far as my imagination will stretch — the way we stage mock combats at jousts and tournaments in the middle of perfect peace. I don't think the philosopher Arcesilaus any less temperate or virtuous for knowing he ate off gold and silver plate when his fortune allowed it; in fact I think better of him than if he had refused what he used with generosity and moderation. I see the true limits of natural necessity. Noticing the poor man begging at my door, often merrier and healthier than I am, I put myself in his place and try to fit my mind to his cut. Running through other examples the same way — imagining death, poverty, contempt, and sickness hard on my heels — I find it easy to resolve not to be terrified of what a lesser man than I accepts with such patience. I can't believe a meaner understanding can do more than a stronger one, or that the effects of reasoning can't reach as high as the effects of habit. And knowing how flimsy these accidental comforts are, I never fail, in the middle of enjoying them, to make it my chief prayer to God that He make me content with myself and with the condition I'm in. I see healthy young men who nevertheless keep a stash of pills in their trunk at home, to take when a cold strikes — which they fear the less because they think the remedy is within reach. That's the way to do it — and further, if you find yourself prone to some more violent disease, stock yourself with the medicines that numb and deaden the part.

The occupation a man picks for this retired life should be neither laborious nor boring; otherwise there's no point in having retired at all. Which occupation depends on each man's taste. Mine doesn't bend at all toward farming, and those who love it should pursue it in moderation — trying, as Horace says, to make circumstances bow to me, not me to circumstances. Farming is otherwise a very servile business, as Sallust calls it — though some branches are more forgivable than the rest, like the care of gardens, which Xenophon attributes to Cyrus. And a middle way can be found between the grubbing, anxious absorption of men who make farming their whole life and study, and the slack, total negligence of others who let everything run wild — like Democritus, whose cattle, as Horace says, ate up his crops and fields while his soaring mind ranged abroad without its body.

But let's hear the advice the younger Pliny gives his friend Caninius Rufus on the subject of solitude: "I advise you, in that full and prosperous retreat of yours, to leave the farm work to your hired hands and devote yourself to the study of letters, so as to extract from them something entirely your own." He means reputation — the same spirit as Cicero, who says he wants to use his retirement from public affairs to win immortal life through his writings. Is your knowing nothing, asks Persius, unless somebody else knows that you know it?

It seems only reasonable, when a man talks of leaving the world, that he should look entirely beyond it. These men do it by halves. True, they set their affairs up for the time when they'll no longer be in the world; but the fruit of their project they still claim to draw from the world after they've left it — a ridiculous contradiction.

The notion of those who seek solitude for devotion's sake, filling their hopes and courage with the certainty of divine promises in the next life, is far more soundly built. They set God before themselves, an object infinite in goodness and power; the soul has room there to satisfy her desires in perfect freedom. Afflictions and sufferings turn to profit, being spent to purchase eternal health and joy; death becomes a thing to wish for, the passage to so perfect a state. The harshness of their rules is quickly softened by habit, and the appetites of the flesh are baffled and beaten down simply by being refused — since nothing keeps them alive but use and exercise. This single end — another, happily immortal life — genuinely deserves that we abandon the comforts and pleasures of this one. And whoever can truly and steadily set his soul ablaze with the fire of that living faith and hope builds himself, in solitude, a life more delicious and luxurious than any other kind of existence.

So neither the goal nor the method of Pliny's advice pleases me; we often jump out of the frying pan into the fire. This business with books is as laborious as any other, and just as much an enemy of health — which ought to be our first consideration. And we shouldn't let ourselves be lulled by the pleasure of it: it's the same pleasure that destroys the frugal man, the miser, the sensualist, and the ambitious man.

The sages warn us often enough to beware the treachery of our appetites, and to tell true, whole pleasures apart from those laced with more pain than they're worth. Most of our pleasures, they say, hug and flatter us only to strangle us, like the thieves the Egyptians called Philistae. If the hangover arrived before the drunkenness, we'd take care not to drink too much; but pleasure, to deceive us, walks in front and hides what's trailing behind her. Books are pleasant — but if poring over them ends up costing us our health and our good humor, the best things we have, let's drop them. I'm one of those who think no fruit they yield can make up for that loss. Like men long worn down by illness who finally surrender to medicine and accept a regimen of living they must never again break, so the man who retires, weary and sick of the common way of life, should design his new one by the rules of reason, and set it up with forethought and reflection. He should have said goodbye to every kind of labor, whatever it promises, and shaken off all the passions that disturb the peace of body and soul — and then choose the road that suits his own temper best: let each man know his own way.

In farming, study, hunting, and every other pursuit, we should go to the outer limit of pleasure, and take care not to push past the point where trouble starts to mix in. We should keep only as much business as is needed to keep us in shape and protect us from the troubles that the other extreme — dull, slack idleness — drags along with it. There are barren, knotty sciences, mostly hammered out for the crowd; leave those to the people still in the world's service. Personally, I care for no books except the pleasant, easy kind that tickle me, or the kind that console me and teach me how to manage my life and my death — strolling silently in the healthy woods, as Horace says, pondering whatever is worthy of a wise and good man.

Wiser men, with strong and vigorous souls, can fashion themselves a rest that is purely spiritual. But I, who have a perfectly ordinary soul, need the help of the body's comforts to hold myself up; and since age has lately robbed me of the pleasures I liked best, I train and sharpen my appetite for the ones that remain and suit this season better. We must hang on with tooth and nail to the use of life's pleasures, which the years wrench out of our hands one after another. Let's pluck the sweets, says Persius — the living is ours; soon enough you'll be ashes, a ghost, a story people tell.

Now as for the goal Pliny and Cicero hold out to us — glory — it's nowhere near my reckoning. Ambition is, of all moods, the most contrary to solitude. Glory and repose cannot lodge in the same house. As far as I can see, these men have only their arms and legs out of the crowd; their soul and intention are stuck in it deeper than ever — does an old man, asks Persius, really gather tidbits for other people's ears? They've stepped back only to get a better running start and charge harder into the crowd. Want to see how short they shoot? Set against them the advice of two philosophers, from two very different schools — one writing to Idomeneus, the other to Lucilius — urging their friends to leave honors and affairs for solitude.

"Until now," they say, "you have lived swimming and floating; come now and die in the harbor. You have given the first part of your life to the light; give what remains to the shade. It's impossible to give up the work unless you also give up the fruit of it; so strip yourself of every concern for name and glory. There's a danger the shine of your past deeds will throw too much light on you and follow you right into your hiding place. Quit, along with the other pleasures, the pleasure that comes from other people's approval. As for your knowledge and talents, don't worry — they won't lose their effect if you yourself are the better for them. Remember the man who was asked why he worked so hard at an art that hardly anyone would ever appreciate. 'A few are enough for me,' he replied; 'one is enough; none at all is enough.' He spoke truly. You and one companion are theater enough for each other — or you for yourself. Let the whole people be as one person to you, and be yourself a whole people to one. It's a shabby ambition to try to wring glory out of one's idleness and seclusion. Do as the hunted animals do, and brush out your tracks at the mouth of your den. Your business is no longer what the world says of you, but what you say to yourself. Withdraw into yourself — but first prepare yourself to receive yourself. It would be madness to trust yourself to your own keeping if you can't govern yourself. There are ways to fail in solitude just as in company. Until you have made yourself into someone you wouldn't dare stumble in front of, and until you feel a certain shame and reverence before yourself — keep honest images ever before the mind, as Cicero says — hold in your imagination Cato, Phocion, and Aristides, in whose presence even fools would hide their faults, and make them auditors of all your intentions. If your intentions go crooked, reverence for those men will set them straight again. They will keep you on the road of being content with yourself, of borrowing nothing from anyone but yourself, of steadying and fixing your soul on definite, bounded thoughts where she can take her pleasure — and, having understood the true goods, which we enjoy in proportion as we understand them, of resting satisfied, with no craving for more life or more name."

That is the counsel of true and natural philosophy — not of a boastful, chattering philosophy like that of the other two.

Of the Inequality Among Us

Plutarch says somewhere that he doesn't find as much difference between one beast and another as he finds between one man and another. He means the inner qualities — the furnishings of the soul. And honestly, I find such a gulf between Epaminondas, as I judge him, and certain men I know — men of good sense, mind you — that I'd happily go Plutarch one better: there is more difference between this man and that man than between that man and a beast. As Terence says, "ah, how far one man can outstrip another!" There are as many degrees of mind as there are yards between here and heaven, and just as countless.

But when it comes to valuing men, here is a strange thing: we are the only creature appraised by what isn't ours. Every other animal is rated on its own qualities. We praise a horse for its strength and the sureness of its feet — as Juvenal says, it's the swift horse we applaud, the one that sets the whole hoarse circus roaring as it wins — not for its fancy harness. We praise a greyhound for its speed, not its collar; a hawk for its wing, not its hood and bells.

Why, then, don't we value a man for what is actually his own? He has a grand household, a beautiful palace, plenty of credit, so many thousand a year in income. All of that is around him. None of it is in him.

You don't buy a cat in a bag. If you're bargaining for a horse, you have its blankets stripped off so you can see it bare and open to your eye — or if it's draped, as horses used to be when presented to princes, only the unimportant parts are covered, so that you won't be distracted by a glossy coat or a broad rump and will look instead at the legs, the eyes, the feet: the parts that do the work. Horace says this was the custom of kings — when they bought horses, they inspected them uncovered, so a handsome head standing on a weak foot wouldn't tempt some gaping buyer.

So why, when you size up a man, do you take him wrapped and bundled in his packaging? He shows you nothing then but the parts that aren't remotely his own, and he hides exactly the parts by which his value can be judged. It's the price of the blade you want, not the scabbard. Strip him, and maybe you wouldn't bid a penny. Judge him by himself, not by his outfit. As one of the ancients put it very neatly: "Do you know why you think he's tall? You're counting the height of his shoes." (That's Seneca.) The pedestal is no part of the statue.

Measure him without his stilts. Make him set aside his income and his titles and present himself in his shirt. Is his body sound and lively, fit for its work? What kind of soul does he have? Is it a fine one, a capable one, fully equipped? Is it rich in what's its own, or only in what it has borrowed? Has luck had a hand in the matter? Can it look drawn swords in the eye without blinking? Does it care whether life leaves by the mouth or by the throat? Is it settled, even, content? That is what needs examining. That is how you measure the enormous differences between man and man.

Is he, as Horace describes, "wise and master of himself, a man whom neither poverty nor death nor chains can frighten; strong enough to tell his appetites no and to look down on honors; complete in himself, smooth and round, so that nothing outside can get a grip on him — a man against whom fortune always charges and always limps away"? A man like that stands five hundred yards above kingdoms and duchies. He is an absolute monarch — of himself, in himself. "The wise man," says Plautus, "shapes his own fortune."

What is left for such a man to want? As Lucretius asks, doesn't nature cry out for nothing more than this — a body free of pain, and a mind enjoying itself, clear of fear and worry?

Now compare him with the general run of mankind: stupid, small-souled, servile, unstable, endlessly tossed about by the storm of one passion after another, hanging on others for everything — and the distance between them is greater than the distance between heaven and earth. Yet such is the blindness of habit that we take little or no notice of it. Meanwhile, put a peasant next to a king, a nobleman next to a vassal, a magistrate next to a private citizen, a rich man next to a poor one, and everybody sees an enormous gap — though they differ, you might say, only in their trousers.

In Thrace the king was set apart from his people in a particularly entertaining way: he had a religion all to himself, a private god — Mercury — whom his subjects weren't allowed to worship, while he, in turn, wouldn't lower himself to theirs, Mars, Bacchus, and Diana. But these are only stage paintings; they make no real difference in the thing itself. Watch actors play a duke or an emperor up on the stage, then go back, a moment later, to being the valets and porters they really are. Just so with the emperor whose pomp dazzles you in public — all those great emeralds set in gold, as Lucretius says, flashing green, and the sea-blue silk robe. Peek behind the curtain and you'll find nothing but an ordinary man, possibly a more contemptible one than the least of his subjects. As Seneca says, one man is happy on the inside; the other's happiness is gold leaf.

Cowardice, indecision, ambition, spite, and envy churn in him as much as in anyone. Horace says it: neither treasure chests nor the consul's bodyguard can clear away the mind's miserable riots, or the cares that flutter around paneled ceilings. And worry and fear grab him by the throat even in the middle of his armies — for as Lucretius says, the fears and gnawing anxieties of men are not scared off by the clash of weapons or the points of spears; they move boldly among kings and the masters of the world, and the glitter of gold doesn't awe them a bit.

Do fevers, gout, and strokes spare him any more than they spare one of us? When old age settles on his shoulders, can the yeomen of his guard lift it off? When the fear of death turns him white, can the gentlemen of his bedchamber talk him out of it? When jealousy or some other obsession is swimming in his head, do our bows and compliments restore his good humor? That canopy stitched with pearls and gold over his bed has no power whatsoever against a bad attack of colic. Burning fevers, as Lucretius says, leave you no sooner when you toss on embroidered tapestry under royal purple than when you lie in a common blanket.

Alexander the Great's flatterers had him convinced he was the son of Jupiter. But one day he was wounded, and watching the blood run from the wound he said, "Well, gentlemen, what do you say now? Isn't this blood crimson, and entirely human? It's hardly the kind Homer has flowing from the wounds of gods." The poet Hermodorus wrote verses in honor of Antigonus calling him the son of the sun. Antigonus said: "The man who empties my chamber pot knows better."

A king is a man, no more — and if he's defective or ill-made from birth, the empire of the whole universe can't fix him. Let the girls fight over him, says Persius; let a rose spring up wherever he steps — what good is any of it if he's a fool? Even pleasure and good luck can't be tasted without vigor and sense. As Terence says, things are worth what the mind of their owner is worth: good to the man who knows how to use them, bad to the man who uses them badly.

Whatever fortune hands you, it takes a palate to taste it. It's enjoying things, not owning them, that makes us happy. It isn't lands, as Horace says, or heaps of bronze and gold that drive fevers from a sick owner's body or cares from his mind; the possessor has to be healthy if he plans to get any good from his pile. To a greedy man or a frightened one, house and estate are what a painting is to a blind man — what a hot compress is to gout.

A dull man's taste is flat and dead. He no more enjoys what he has than a man with a head cold enjoys fine wine — than a horse appreciates its expensive saddle. Plato has it right: health, beauty, strength, riches, and everything else we call goods are exactly as bad for the unjust man as they are good for the just one, and the evils the other way around. Where body and mind are out of order, what use are all the external conveniences? The smallest pinprick, the smallest passion of the soul, is enough to strip the pleasure out of being monarch of the whole world. At the first twinge of gout, what comfort is it to be called Sire and Your Majesty?

Made all of silver, as Tibullus says, made all of gold — doesn't he forget his palaces and his grandeur anyway? If he loses his temper, does being a prince keep him from going red, going pale, and grinding his teeth like a madman? And on the other hand, if he's a capable man of sound nature, kingship adds almost nothing to his happiness. If your belly is well, says Horace, and your sides and your feet, all the wealth of kings can add nothing more. He can see the whole apparatus for what it is: counterfeit and trickery. Maybe he'd even agree with King Seleucus, that a man who knew the real weight of a scepter wouldn't bother to pick one up if he found it lying on the ground — so heavy and painful are the duties of a good king.

It can be no small job to govern others when we find it so hard to govern ourselves. And as for ruling, which looks so attractive: considering how feeble human judgment is, and how hard it is to choose among new and doubtful things, I'm strongly of the opinion that it is far easier and far pleasanter to follow than to lead. It is a great rest for the mind to have only one road to walk and no one to answer for but yourself. As Lucretius says, far better to obey in peace than to crave the reins of power. Add to that Cyrus's saying, that no man is fit to command unless he's worth more than the people he commands.

But King Hiero, in Xenophon, goes further: even in the enjoyment of pleasure itself, he says, kings are worse off than private men, because having everything available on command takes away the sharp delight ordinary people get from it. Love that is too rich and too easy, as Ovid says, turns to boredom, and like sweets on the stomach, it sickens.

Do we imagine the choirboys take any great delight in music? Surfeit makes it tedious to them. Feasts, balls, masquerades, tournaments — these thrill people who see them rarely and long to see them; to someone who attends constantly, the flavor goes flat and stale. Women don't much excite the man who has them whenever he likes. The man who never gives himself time to be thirsty will never know the real pleasure of drinking. Farces and acrobatics delight the audience; to the performers they're a grind. The proof is that princes sometimes amuse themselves by going in disguise, stepping down from their rank for a while to live, briefly, in the poor plain style of the humblest of their people. The rich enjoy a change, as Horace says: a clean simple supper under a poor man's small roof, no tapestries, no purple, has smoothed many an anxious forehead.

Nothing clogs and sickens like abundance. What appetite wouldn't quail at three hundred women all at its mercy, as the Grand Signor has in his seraglio? And what relish for the hunt did that ancestor of his keep for himself who never went hawking without seven thousand falconers?

Beyond all this, I suspect the very glare of greatness brings real trouble into the enjoyment of even the most tempting pleasures. The great stand in too bright a light; they live exposed to every eye. And I don't know why we demand, more than of anyone else, that they hide their faults — since what counts as mere indiscretion in us, the public brands in them as tyranny and contempt for the law. People are inclined to believe that, on top of their natural appetite for vice, princes get an extra thrill out of trampling public decencies. Plato in his Gorgias actually defines a tyrant as one who has license, in a city, to do whatever he pleases. And often, because of that impunity, the open display of a prince's vices does more damage than the vice itself. Every man dreads being watched and pried into; princes are watched down to their gestures, their glances, and their thoughts, the whole populace believing it has the right and title to judge them. Besides, blemishes grow with the eminence and brightness of the place where they sit: a mole or a wart on them shows larger than a gaping wound on other men. That's why the poets imagine Jupiter conducting his love affairs in so many borrowed shapes — of all the escapades they charge him with, there is only one, as I remember, where he appears in his own majesty and grandeur.

But back to Hiero. He also complains of what his royalty cost him in this: he couldn't go abroad and travel freely, being practically a prisoner inside the borders of his own country, and in everything he did he was hemmed in by an importunate crowd. Truthfully, when I see our kings sitting all alone at table, ringed by so many people talking at them and so many strangers staring at them — as they always are — I have often felt more pity than envy.

King Alfonso used to say that in this respect donkeys were better off than kings: their masters let them feed at their ease, a favor kings can't get from their servants. It has never struck me as any great advantage to a man of sense to have twenty people chattering around him while he sits on the toilet. Nor can I see why the services of a man with ten thousand a year, a man who took Casale or held Siena, should be more comfortable or more welcome to him than those of a good valet who knows his job.

The advantages of being a sovereign are mostly imaginary. Every level of fortune contains some little image of kingship. Caesar called all the lords of France who held free rights on their own lands "kinglets" — petty kings. And in truth, apart from the title "Sire," they go a long way toward royalty. Look at the provinces far from court — Brittany, say. Consider the retinue, the vassals, the officers, the duties, the service, the ceremony, the whole state kept up by a lord living away from court in his own house among his own tenants and servants — and watch where his imagination flies. Nothing could be more royal. He hears talk of his actual master once a year, the way one hears of the king of Persia, and acknowledges him only through some distant family tie his secretary keeps filed away in a register somewhere. Our laws sit lightly enough, truth be told: a French gentleman feels the weight of the crown pinch his shoulders maybe twice in his life. Real, effective subjection falls only on those of us who volunteer for it — who stick their necks under the yoke hoping to profit by it in wealth and honors. A man who is content at his own fireside, and can run his household without lawsuits and quarrels with the neighbors, is as free as the Doge of Venice. Servitude binds few men, as Seneca says; many men bind themselves to it.

But what bothers Hiero most is finding himself stripped of all friendship, cut off from all mutual companionship — the thing in which the truest and most perfect enjoyment of human life consists. For what testimony of affection can I squeeze out of a man who owes me, like it or not, everything he's capable of giving? Can I read anything into his humble speech and his bowing and scraping, when those courtesies aren't his to withhold? The honor we receive from people who fear us is not honor. Those respects are paid to my royalty, not to me. As Seneca has it in his tragedy, the greatest advantage of kingship is that the people are forced not only to endure their master's deeds but to praise them.

Don't I see that the bad king and the good one, the hated and the beloved, get exactly the same reverence? My predecessor was served with the same ceremony, and my successor will be. If my subjects do me no harm, that's no proof of affection — why should I take it for one, when they couldn't harm me if they wanted to? No one follows me or obeys me out of any friendship between him and me; friendship can't form where there is so little in common and so little exchange. My height has lifted me clean out of human fellowship: there's too much disparity, too much distance. They follow me out of form and habit — or rather, they follow my fortune, not me, hoping to grow their own. Everything they say to me and do for me is surface paint. Their freedom is curbed on every side by the great power I hold over them, so I see nothing around me that isn't masked and disguised.

The Emperor Julian, being applauded one day by his courtiers for his exact justice, said: "I'd be proud of this praise if it came from people who dared to condemn or criticize the opposite, were I to do it."

All the real advantages princes have, they share with men of middling condition. It's for the gods to ride winged horses and dine on ambrosia. Kings have no other sleep than ours and no other appetite. The steel they arm themselves with is no better tempered than the steel we use. Their crowns keep off neither the rain nor the sun.

Diocletian, who wore as fortunate and revered a crown as any, resigned it to retire into the happiness of private life. Some time later, when public affairs demanded that he take it up again, he answered the men who came to urge him: "You would never try to persuade me to this if you had seen the fine order of the trees I've planted in my orchard, and the beautiful melons I've sown in my garden."

In Anacharsis's opinion, the happiest state would be one in which, all else being equal, rank was measured out according to men's virtues, and disgrace according to their vices.

When King Pyrrhus was preparing his expedition into Italy, his wise counselor Cyneas, wanting to make him feel the vanity of his ambition, asked him: "Well, sire — what is all this great preparation for?" "To make myself master of Italy," the king replied. "And when that's done?" said Cyneas. "Then I'll cross into Gaul and Spain." "And then?" "Then I'll go conquer Africa; and at last, when I've brought the whole world under me, I'll sit down and rest, and live content and at ease."

"Then for God's sake, sire," said Cyneas, "tell me what stops you from being in that condition right now, if you please? Why not settle yourself this instant in the state you're aiming at, and spare yourself all the labor and danger you're putting in between?"

The trouble, as Lucretius says, was that he never learned where acquiring should stop, or how far real pleasure can actually grow.

I will close with an old verse that I find very fit for the purpose — Cornelius Nepos's line: "Every man frames his own fortune."

Of Democritus and Heraclitus

Judgment is a tool that works on any subject, and it pokes its nose into everything. That's why, in these essays of mine, I grab whatever occasion comes along. If it's a subject I don't understand at all, so much the better — I sound the ford from a good distance, and if I find it too deep for my height, I stay on the bank. Knowing you can go no further is itself an act of judgment, and one of the acts it's proudest of.

Sometimes I take an empty, trivial subject and see whether I can find anything in it to build a body out of, something to prop up and support. Sometimes I wander into a noble subject, one that's been worked over by a thousand hands, where nothing new can be said and the road is so beaten on every side that you have no choice but to walk in someone else's footsteps. There, judgment's job is to pick the route that seems best — to decide, out of a thousand paths, that this one or that one is the right choice.

I take the first argument fortune offers me. They're all equally good to me, and I never plan to cover any of them completely — because I never see the whole of anything. Neither do the people who promise so confidently to show it to us. Of the hundred limbs and faces every subject has, I take one. Sometimes I just glance it over; sometimes I lift the skin a little; sometimes I pinch it to the bone. I jab in — not as wide as I can, but as deep as I can — and most of the time I'm tempted to take a subject up by some new light I've found glinting in it.

If I knew myself less well, I might risk handling some topic to the bottom and discover my inability the hard way. Instead I scatter a word here, a word there — samples snipped from various pieces of cloth, strewn around without any design and without committing myself too far. I'm not answerable for them, and not obliged to stick to my subject. I can vary it whenever I like, and surrender to doubt and uncertainty — and to my own ruling method, ignorance.

Every motion reveals us. The same soul of Caesar that showed itself so brilliantly in marshaling the battle of Pharsalia also showed itself in arranging his idle hours and his love affairs. You judge a horse not only by watching it show off its paces, but by its ordinary walk — even by seeing it standing in the stable.

Among the soul's functions, some are low and humble. Whoever does not see her in those small offices as well as the grand ones never sees her whole — and she may show best moving at her plain, everyday pace. The winds of passion catch her most strongly at her highest altitudes. Besides, she throws herself entirely into each subject she touches, handles only one thing at a time — and handles it not according to what it is, but according to what she is.

Things in themselves may have their own weights, measures, and qualities. But once inside us, the soul cuts them to fit her own taste. Death is terrifying to Cicero, desirable to Cato, a matter of indifference to Socrates. Health, conscience, authority, knowledge, riches, beauty, and their opposites — all of them strip off at the door when they enter us, and receive a new robe from the soul, in whatever color she chooses: brown, bright, green, dark; and whatever quality: sharp, sweet, deep, or shallow. Each soul decides for herself, since none of them have agreed on any common standard of rules or procedures — each one is queen in her own realm.

So let us stop blaming the external qualities of things. The reckoning is ours to give. Our good and our ill depend on no one but ourselves. That is where our offerings and our vows belong — not to fortune, who has no power over our character. On the contrary: our character drags fortune along in its train and casts her in its own mold.

So why shouldn't I judge Alexander at the dinner table, ranting and drinking at the prodigious rate he sometimes did? Or over a chess board? What string of his soul does that idle, childish game leave untouched? I hate it and avoid it myself, precisely because it isn't enough of a game — it's too grave and serious a pastime, and I'm ashamed to spend on it an amount of attention that would serve for better things. Alexander didn't rack his brains harder over his glorious expedition into India; another man sweats no harder untangling a problem on which the salvation of mankind depends. Look how this ridiculous game torments the soul, all her faculties summoned for such a trivial cause — and what a fine chance it gives everyone to know himself and judge himself rightly. There is no posture in which I sift myself more thoroughly. What passion doesn't it stir up? Anger, spite, hatred, impatience, and a burning ambition to win — in a contest where the more honorable ambition would be to lose. Because excelling, standing out above the common run in frivolous things, hardly suits a man of honor.

What I say of this example can be said of all the others. Every particle of a man, every occupation, reveals him just as well as any other.

Democritus and Heraclitus were two philosophers. The first, finding the human condition ridiculous and vain, never went out in public except with a mocking, laughing face. Heraclitus, pitying that same condition of ours, wore a perpetually sad expression and tears in his eyes. As Juvenal puts it, the one laughed every time he set foot outside his door; the other wept.

I prefer the first temper — not because laughing is more pleasant than weeping, but because it is more disdainful, and condemns us more than the other does; and it seems to me we can never be despised as much as we deserve. Pity and lamentation imply that the thing lamented has some value. What we laugh at, we declare worthless. I do not think there is so much unhappiness in us as vanity; we are not so much wicked as silly; we are not so full of evil as of emptiness; we are not so miserable as we are cheap.

That's why Diogenes — who passed his time rolling around in his tub and thumbing his nose at the great Alexander, rating us no higher than flies or bladders puffed with air — was a sharper, more penetrating judge, and therefore in my opinion a fairer one, than Timon, the one they called the Man-hater. Because whatever a man hates, he takes to heart. Timon wished us ill; he passionately desired our ruin; he shunned our company as dangerous, the company of wicked and depraved creatures. Diogenes valued us so little that contact with us could neither disturb him nor infect him. He dropped our company not from fear, but from contempt — he figured we were incapable of doing either good or harm.

In the same key was Statilius's answer when Brutus invited him into the conspiracy against Caesar. He agreed the enterprise was just; he just didn't think mankind was worth a wise man's trouble. Which matches the teaching of Hegesias, who said a wise man should do nothing except for himself, since no one else is worth doing anything for; and the saying of Theodorus, that it isn't reasonable for a wise man to risk himself for his country — to endanger wisdom for the sake of fools.

Our condition is as laughable as it is laughed at.

Of Smells

It's been said of certain people — Alexander the Great, for one — that their sweat gave off a sweet smell, thanks to some rare and extraordinary constitution. Plutarch and others went looking for the cause. But the ordinary human body works quite the other way, and the best it can hope for is to smell of nothing at all. Even the sweetness of the purest breath has no greater perfection than to carry no offensive smell whatsoever, like the breath of healthy children. That's what made Plautus say of a woman: she smells sweetest who smells of nothing.

And people who go around in fine exotic perfumes deserve to be suspected of some natural flaw underneath — something they're using all that scent to cover up. To smell good is to stink. As Martial teases a friend: "You laugh at us, Coracinus, for wearing no scent; I'd rather smell of nothing than smell sweet." And elsewhere he says it again: the man who always smells good doesn't smell good.

That said, I'm a great lover of good smells myself, and I detest bad ones — and I catch them at a greater distance, I think, than other men. As Horace puts it, my nose is quicker to sniff out a rank armpit or a festering sore than a keen hound is to find the hidden sow.

Among smells, the simple and natural ones please me most. The ladies should take note, since this is chiefly their department. Even in the depths of barbarism, the Scythian women used to powder and crust their faces and whole bodies after bathing with a certain fragrant drug that grew in their country; when they scrubbed it off to be intimate with their men, they came away perfumed and sleek.

You wouldn't believe how strangely smells of every kind cling to me, how readily my skin drinks them in. Whoever complains that nature gave us no vehicle for carrying smells to the nose has it wrong — smells carry themselves, especially to me. My mustache, which is full, does the job nicely. If I so much as brush it with my gloves or my handkerchief, the smell stays a whole day. It tells on me — it reports where I've been. And the close, luscious, devouring, clinging, melting kisses of youthful passion, back in my wilder days, would leave a sweetness on my lips for hours afterward.

And yet I've always been remarkably untouched by epidemic diseases — the kind caught at a sick man's bedside or bred from bad air — and I've escaped every one of my time, and there have been several, in our cities and in our armies. We read of Socrates that he never once left Athens through all the plagues that ravaged the city, and he alone was never infected.

Doctors could, I believe, get far more use out of odors than they do. I've often noticed that smells change me — they work on my spirits, each according to its nature. Which makes me approve of the old idea that incense and perfume in churches — a practice so ancient and so universal across all nations and religions — was meant to cheer us, to rouse and purify the senses, and so make us fitter for contemplation.

To judge the matter better, I'd love to have tasted the work of those cooks who had the rare art of seasoning exotic scents into the flavor of food. It was noticed especially in the household of the king of Tunis, who in our own day landed at Naples to meet the Emperor Charles. His dishes were stuffed with aromatic drugs at such expense that dressing one peacock and two pheasants in their fashion came to a hundred ducats; and when the carver cut them open, not just the dining hall but every room of his palace and the neighboring streets filled with a sweet vapor that took a long while to fade.

My chief concern in choosing where to stay is always to avoid thick and stinking air. And those beautiful cities, Venice and Paris, lose a good deal of my affection for them — the one by the foul smell of her marshes, the other by the smell of her mud.

Of Age

I can't accept the way we settle, in advance, how long our lives are supposed to last. I notice the wise men cut the figure much shorter than popular opinion does. When the younger Cato's friends tried to stop him from killing himself, he asked them: "Am I now at an age where I can be accused of leaving the world too soon?" He was forty-eight. He considered that a ripe, advanced age, given how few people ever reach it.

As for those who soothe themselves with some notion of "the natural course of things" and promise themselves years beyond that — well, if they could be exempted from the endless accidents we're all naturally exposed to, maybe they'd have a case. But what an idle fantasy it is to expect to die of sheer worn-out strength, the kind of death that comes only at the furthest extreme of old age, and to set the lease of our life by that measure — when it is, of all deaths, the rarest and least often seen. We call that one alone a natural death. As if it were against nature for a man to break his neck in a fall, drown in a shipwreck, or get carried off by pleurisy or plague — as if our ordinary condition didn't expose us to exactly these hazards. Let us stop flattering ourselves with these fine words. Perhaps we ought instead to call natural whatever is general, common, and universal.

Dying of old age is a death that is rare, extraordinary, singular — and therefore less natural than the others, not more. It is the last and furthest kind of dying; the more remote it stands, the less we should count on it. It is, true enough, the boundary stone beyond which we cannot pass, the limit the law of nature has fixed. But it is also a privilege she very rarely grants — to last all the way there. It's a lease she signs only as a special favor, maybe to one person in two or three centuries, and even then she throws in a safe-conduct pass to get him past all the obstacles and ambushes she has scattered along that long road.

So here is my opinion: once we reach forty, we should regard it as an age very few attain. Since men don't usually get that far, it's a sign we are already well along. And once we've passed the ordinary bounds — which are the true measure of life — we shouldn't expect to go much further. Having slipped past so many precipices of death, the same ones we've watched so many other men tumble into, we ought to admit that a run of luck this extraordinary — the luck that has rescued us from all those dangers and kept us alive beyond the usual term — is not likely to hold much longer.

Even our laws keep this error alive. They say a man isn't capable of managing his own estate until he's twenty-five — when he'll be hard pressed to manage his life that long. Augustus shaved five years off the old Roman standard and declared that thirty was old enough to be a judge. Servius Tullius excused knights over forty-seven from the hardships of war; Augustus released them at forty-five. Though sending men to the fireside before fifty-five or sixty strikes me as a bit unreasonable. My own view is that our work and our usefulness should be stretched as far as possible, for the public good. The fault I find is on the other side: they don't put us to work early enough. This same emperor was running the whole world at nineteen — and yet he wanted a man to be thirty before he was fit to settle a dispute about a gutter.

For my part, I believe our souls are as grown up at twenty as they will ever be, as capable then as ever. A soul that hasn't given clear proof of its force by that age never will. Our natural qualities and powers show whatever vigor and beauty they have within that term, or never. As they say in Dauphiné, "if the thorn doesn't prick when it's born, it will hardly ever prick at all."

Of all the great human actions I've ever heard or read of, of whatever kind, in past ages and in our own, more were done before the age of thirty than after — and often in the lives of the very same men. May I not point with confidence to Hannibal, and to his great rival Scipio? They spent the better half of their lives living off the glory they had won in their youth. Great men afterward, yes — compared with everyone else. But not compared with themselves.

As for me personally, I'm certain that since that age both my mind and my body have shrunk rather than grown — retreated rather than advanced. It's possible that in those who use their time well, knowledge and experience keep increasing with the years. But quickness, readiness, steadiness, and the other parts of us that matter far more, and are far more essentially our own — these droop and decay. As Lucretius says, "when the body is shaken by the heavy blows of time and the limbs sag with blunted force, the judgment limps, the tongue and the mind wander."

Sometimes the body surrenders to age first, sometimes the mind. And I've seen plenty of men whose brains went weak before their legs or their stomach did. It's a disease that causes its sufferer little pain and shows obscure symptoms — which makes it all the more dangerous. This is why I complain about our laws: not that they keep us at work too long, but that they set us to work too late. Considering how fragile life is, and how many ordinary, natural rocks it can wreck on, we should not hand over so large a share of it to childhood, idleness, and apprenticeship.

Book II

That Our Actions Are Inconsistent

Those who make it their business to study human behavior never run into anything that stumps them more than fitting our actions together into one consistent picture. Our deeds contradict one another so strangely that it seems impossible they came from the same person. The younger Marius shows up sometimes as a son of Mars, sometimes as a son of Venus. Pope Boniface VIII, they say, came into his office like a fox, ruled in it like a lion, and died like a dog. And who would believe it was Nero — the very portrait of cruelty — who, when the death warrant of a condemned man was brought to him to sign, as the custom was, cried out, "I wish I had never learned to write!" That is how much it pained him to send a man to his death.

History is full of such examples, and every man can supply so many from his own experience that I sometimes wonder at intelligent people taking the trouble to sort these pieces into a matching set — since indecision strikes me as the most common and obvious defect of our nature. Remember the famous line of the comic player Publius: "A plan that cannot be changed is a bad plan."

There is some sense in judging a man by the general run of his life. But given how naturally unstable our habits and opinions are, I've often thought that even the best authors are a little off in stubbornly trying to weave us into one solid, consistent fabric. They settle on a general image of a man and then interpret all his actions to fit it — and whatever they can't bend into uniformity, they write off as play-acting. Augustus has escaped them: there was such an obvious, sudden, and continual variety in everything he did, his whole life long, that even the boldest critics have had to let him slip away whole and unjudged. Constancy is the virtue I find hardest of all to believe in, and inconstancy the easiest. Whoever judges a man in detail, piece by piece, will hit the truth more often.

In all of antiquity it's hard to pick out a dozen men who set their lives on one fixed and certain course — which is the main point of wisdom. To sum it all up in a word, says one of the ancients, and to gather every rule of human life into one: it is to will, and not to will, always the same thing. I won't bother to add, he says, "provided the will is just" — because if it isn't just, it cannot possibly stay the same. I learned long ago that vice is nothing but disorder and lack of measure, and so constancy can never be fastened to it. Demosthenes is supposed to have said that the beginning of all virtue is reflection and deliberation — and its end and perfection, constancy. If we chose our course by reason, we would choose the best one; but nobody has thought of it. As Horace says, "what he sought, he scorns; what he just let go, he chases again. He seethes, and his whole life is out of order."

Our usual practice is to follow the pull of our appetite — left, right, up, down, wherever the breeze of occasion carries us. We never think about what we want until the instant we want it; and we change like that little creature that takes its color from whatever you set it on. What we decided a moment ago we immediately reverse, and then reverse again. It is all swaying and inconsistency. As Horace says, we are wooden puppets, moved by strings in other people's hands.

We do not go; we are carried — like floating things, drifting now gently, now violently, as the current runs calm or rough. As Lucretius asks, don't we see them all, not knowing what they want, forever seeking a change of place, as if that could set down the load? Every day a new whim, and our moods move with the weather — as Cicero has it, "men's minds are like the light that father Jupiter sends across the fruitful earth."

We float among our various leanings. We will nothing freely, nothing absolutely, nothing constantly. If a man had fixed laws and a settled plan of conduct in his head, we would see an evenness shine through his whole life — an order, an unfailing fit of each action to the next. Empedocles spotted the flaw in the people of Agrigentum: they threw themselves into pleasures as if every day were their last, and built as if they were going to live forever. The younger Cato is easy to judge: strike one note and you have the whole instrument — a harmony of perfectly tuned sounds that cannot clash. But with us it's the opposite: every single action needs its own separate judgment. The surest way, in my opinion, is to judge each act by the circumstances closest around it, without digging any further and without drawing any larger conclusion from it.

During the civil disorders of our poor kingdom, I was told that a young woman, close by where I was then staying, had thrown herself out of a window to escape the advances of a soldier quartered in her house. The fall didn't kill her, so she tried again, and would have cut her own throat if she hadn't been stopped — though not before wounding herself badly enough to show she meant it. And then she freely admitted that the soldier had so far pressed her with nothing worse than courtship, persistence, and presents — she was only afraid he would come to force in the end. And all this she said with such a look, such a tone, and her own blood still on her to vouch for her virtue, that she seemed a second Lucretia. Yet I have since learned, on good authority, that both before and after, she drove no such hard bargain. As the host says in Ariosto's tale: be as handsome a man and as fine a gentleman as you please, but don't conclude too much about your lady's unshakable chastity just because you've been turned down. It may only mean the muleteer suits her better.

Antigonus had taken one of his soldiers into high favor for his courage, and ordered his physicians to cure the man of a deep, lingering disease that had tormented him for years. After the cure, Antigonus noticed him going about his fighting far more coolly than before, and asked what had so tamed him. "You did, sir," the soldier answered, "by relieving me of the pains that made my life not worth keeping." A soldier of Lucullus had been stripped of everything by the enemy, and in revenge pulled off a magnificent stroke against them that left him richer than before. Lucullus, who had formed a high opinion of him from that exploit, set about recruiting him for some extremely dangerous mission, with all the handsome speeches and promises he could think of — the kind of words, as Horace says, that might put heart into a coward. "Send some poor soldier who's been plundered," the man answered — for, as Horace has it, "the fellow who has lost his purse will go wherever you like" — and he flatly refused to go.

We read that Mehmed furiously berated Hassan, the commander of his Janissaries, for letting the Hungarians break into his squadrons and for fighting feebly himself. Hassan's only answer was to charge alone, scimitar in hand, into the first body of enemies he met, where he was promptly cut to pieces. That was perhaps not so much vindication as a swing of mood — not natural bravery so much as a flash of wounded pride. The man you saw yesterday so daring and bold — don't be surprised to find him just as great a coward the day after. Anger, necessity, company, wine, or the sound of a trumpet had set his blood up. That is not courage formed and settled by reason; it is courage thrown together by circumstances — and it's no wonder if different circumstances turn it into something else entirely.

These supple shifts and contradictions in us have led some to imagine that we have two souls, and others that two powers attend us, each pulling its own way — one toward good, the other toward ill — since such jerky variety could never, they think, flow from a single source.

As for me, every gust of every accident not only carries me along with it; I also unsettle and trouble myself by the sheer instability of my own posture. Whoever looks closely into himself will hardly ever find himself twice in the same state. I give my soul one face or another, depending on which side I turn her to. If I speak of myself in different ways, it's because I see myself in different ways. Every contradiction can be found in me, in some corner or other, in some form or other: bashful, insolent; chaste, lustful; talkative, silent; hardworking, delicate; clever, dull; gloomy, cheerful; lying, truthful; learned, ignorant; generous, greedy, and wasteful. I find all of it in myself, more or less, depending on which way I turn. And whoever sifts himself to the bottom will find this same flux and discord in himself — even in his own judgment. I have nothing to say about myself that is whole, simple, and solid, without mixture and confusion. Distinguo — "I draw a distinction" — is the most universal clause in my logic.

Though I always mean to speak well of what's good, and to put the better interpretation on things rather than the worse, still our condition is so strange that vice itself often pushes us into doing good — if doing good were judged by anything but intention. So one gallant deed should not prove a man brave. If he were truly brave, he would be brave always, on every occasion. If his courage were a habit and not a single outburst, it would make him equally steady in every emergency: the same alone as in company, the same in the tournament lists as on the battlefield. For say what they will, there is not one courage for the street and another for the field. He would bear an illness in his bed as bravely as a wound in battle, and fear death no more in his own house than in an assault. We would not see the same man charge into a breach with superb confidence and then torment himself over a lost lawsuit or the death of a child. When a man who is a notorious coward stands firm under poverty — when he flinches at the barber's razor and yet runs head-on at enemy swords — the action deserves praise; the man does not.

Many Greeks, says Cicero, cannot bear the sight of an enemy yet endure sickness with courage; the Cimbrians and Celtiberians are exactly the reverse — for, as he adds, nothing can be steady that does not spring from a fixed principle.

No valor was more extreme in its kind than Alexander's; but even his is of one kind only — not full enough at every point, not universal. Incomparable as it is, it has its blemishes. We see him losing his head at every faint suspicion that his captains were plotting against his life, and throwing himself into those investigations with a violence and reckless injustice, and a fear, that overturned his natural reason. The superstition that ran so deep in him carries its own trace of cowardice. And his extravagant remorse over the murder of Clytus is one more mark of how unevenly his courage ran.

Everything we do is a patchwork of separate pieces, and we want to win honor under a false flag. Virtue can only be followed for her own sake; if we sometimes borrow her mask for some other purpose, she promptly snatches it back off our face. Virtue is a vivid, fast dye: once the soul is truly steeped in it, it will not come out except with the cloth itself. So to judge a man rightly, we must follow his track a long way and watch it closely. If constancy does not stand firm there on its own foundation — "if the path of his life has been thought through and laid out in advance," as Cicero puts it — if the variety of events makes him change his pace (his path, I mean; the pace may run faster or slower), then let him go: that man runs wherever the wind blows, as the motto of our Talbot has it.

It's no wonder, says one of the ancients, that chance has such power over us, since we live by chance. Whoever has not aimed his life at some definite goal cannot possibly arrange the individual acts. Whoever does not carry the whole shape in his head cannot fit the pieces together. What good is a supply of paints to a man who doesn't know what he means to paint? No one draws up a definite plan for his life; we decide it only piece by piece. The archer must first know what he is aiming at, and then fit his hand, bow, string, arrow, and movement to the target. Our plans go astray because they have no address and no destination. No wind works for the sailor who has no port in mind.

I can't accept the verdict given in favor of Sophocles, when the reading of one of his tragedies was taken as proof — against his own son's accusation — that he was fit to manage his household affairs. Nor do I think the Parians, sent to straighten out the Milesians, drew a sound conclusion from what they saw. Visiting the island, they took note of the best-tended fields and the best-run country houses, wrote down the owners' names, called an assembly of the citizens, and appointed those farmers the new governors and magistrates — reasoning that men so careful of their private business would be just as careful of the public's.

We are all patchwork, and so shapeless and diverse in composition that every piece plays its own game every moment. And there is as much difference between us and ourselves as between us and other people. "Consider it a great thing," says Seneca, "to play one and the same man."

Since ambition can teach men valor, and temperance, and generosity — yes, and even justice; since greed can take a shop boy raised in shade and idleness and give him the nerve to leave the fireside and trust himself, in a frail boat, to the waves and the anger of Neptune, and can teach him discretion and prudence along the way; since Venus herself puts boldness and resolve into boys still under the schoolmaster's rod, and steels the hearts of tender girls in their mothers' laps — as Tibullus tells it, the girl, with love for her guide, slips past the sleeping guards and steals through the dark, alone, to her young man — given all this, it is not enough for a sound judgment to weigh us simply by our outward actions. It must probe right down to the soul, and discover there what springs set the motion going. But since that is a high and hazardous undertaking, I wish fewer people would attempt it.

A Custom of the Island of Cea

If to philosophize is, as they define it, to doubt, then surely to write at random and play the fool, as I do, must count as doubting too. It is for novices and freshmen to inquire and dispute, and for the chairman to moderate and decide. My chairman is the authority of the divine will, which governs us without contradiction and sits high above these vain human quarrels.
