When Philip forced his way into the Peloponnese, someone told Damidas that the Spartans would suffer badly if they did not hurry to make peace with him. "You poor fellow," Damidas replied, "what can people suffer who do not fear death?" Agis was asked how a man might live free. "By despising death," he said. These sayings, and a thousand others like them, plainly mean something more than just waiting patiently for death to arrive on its own. There are things in life far worse to endure than death itself.

Take the Spartan boy captured by Antigonus and sold as a slave. When his master ordered him to some degrading task, the boy said, "You shall see what you have bought. It would be shameful for me to serve when freedom is so close at hand" — and threw himself from the top of the house. When Antipater threatened the Spartans harshly to force them to accept one of his demands, they answered, "If you threaten us with worse than death, we shall die all the more willingly." And when Philip wrote that he would block all their undertakings: "What — will you also keep us from dying?"

This is the meaning of the old saying: the wise man lives as long as he ought, not as long as he can. The kindest gift nature has given us — the one that strips away every excuse for complaining about our condition — is that she has left the keys of life in our own keeping. She has arranged only one door into life, but a hundred thousand ways out. We may run short of land to live on, but land enough to die on can never be lacking, as Boiocalus answered the Romans. Why do you complain of this world? It does not hold you. If you live in pain, your own cowardice is the cause. To die, nothing is needed but the will to die. As Seneca says, "Death is everywhere — heaven has provided well for that. Anyone can take a man's life; no one can take his death. A thousand roads lead to it."

Nor is death a remedy for one disease only; it is the infallible cure of them all. It is a sure harbor, never to be feared and often to be sought. It comes to the same thing whether a man gives himself his end or waits to receive it, whether he pays before his day or waits until payment falls due. Wherever it comes from, it is still his own. Wherever the thread breaks, there is the end of the spool. The most voluntary death is the finest. Life depends on the will of others; death depends on our own. In nothing should we suit our own inclination so much as in this. Reputation has no stake in such a venture, and it is folly to be swayed by any worry about it. Living is slavery if the liberty of dying is missing.

The ordinary course of medicine is carried out at the expense of life. They torment us with burnings, incisions, and amputations of limbs; they forbid us food and drain our blood. One step further and we are cured indeed, and for good. Why is the jugular vein not as much at our disposal as the median vein? For a desperate disease, a desperate cure. Servius the grammarian, tortured by gout, could think of no better remedy than to apply poison to his legs and kill their feeling. Let them be as gouty as they pleased, so long as they could feel no pain.

God gives us leave enough to go when He reduces us to a state where living is far worse than dying. It is weakness to buckle under infirmities, but it is madness to nurse them. The Stoics say it is living according to nature for a wise man to take his leave of life even at the height of prosperity, if he does it at the right moment; and for a fool to cling to life even in misery, provided he still has most of the things they count as natural goods. Just as I do not break the law against thieves when I pocket my own money and cut my own purse, nor the law against arsonists when I burn my own wood, so I am not subject to the laws against murderers for having taken my own life.

Hegesias said that as the conditions of life depend on our choice, so should the conditions of death. And when Diogenes met the philosopher Speusippus, so swollen with chronic dropsy that he had to be carried in a litter, and Speusippus called out, "I wish you good health" — "No health to you," Diogenes answered, "who are content to live in such a condition." And in fact, not long after, Speusippus, weary of so lingering a state, found a means to die.

But this does not pass without dispute. Many hold that we cannot quit this garrison of the world without the express command of Him who posted us in it; that it belongs to God — who placed us here not for ourselves alone, but for His glory and the service of others — to dismiss us when it pleases Him, and not for us to leave without His permission. We are born not for ourselves only but for our country too, and the laws have a claim on us in their own interest, with a charge of manslaughter ready against us. And if those courts fail to take up the case, we are punished in the next world as deserters of our duty. As Virgil writes, the next sad region of the underworld holds those who, though guiltless, made their own deaths with their own hands, and hating the light, threw their lives away.

There is more constancy in wearing the chain we are bound to than in breaking it, and stronger proof of firmness in Regulus than in Cato. It is rashness and impatience that drive us over these cliffs. No misfortune can make true virtue turn her back; she seeks out hardship, pain, and grief as the very food that nourishes her. The threats of tyrants, the racks and the tortures, only stir her up and give her life — like the oak on Mount Algidus in Horace's lines, which draws fresh vigor from the very axe that lops it. And as Seneca says, "Father, it is no virtue to fear life; virtue is to stand against great misfortunes and neither flinch nor retreat." Or as Martial puts it, "In adversity it is easy to despise death; the braver man is the one who can endure to live wretched."

It is cowardice, not virtue, to crouch in a furrow under a tombstone to dodge the blows of fortune. Virtue never halts or leaves her road, however hard the storm blows. As Horace says, "If the world cracked and fell, the ruins would strike a fearless head." Most of the time, fleeing other troubles drives us straight into this one; indeed, in trying to escape death we often run right into its mouth. "Tell me," asks Martial, "is it not madness to die for fear of dying?" — like those who, terrified of a precipice, throw themselves over it. As Lucan says, the very fear of coming evil has pushed many into extreme danger; the bravest man is the one who can face what he dreads when it confronts him, and can also wait. And Lucretius observes that the dread of death so seizes some men that it makes them hate both life and daylight, and they kill themselves in their misery — forgetting that this very fear is the fountain of all their cares.

Plato, in his Laws, assigns a disgraceful burial to the man who has robbed his nearest and dearest friend — himself — of life and of his appointed course, when not compelled by public judgment, by some grievous and inescapable stroke of fortune, or by unbearable disgrace, but driven merely by cowardice and the weakness of a timid soul. And the opinion that holds life so cheap is absurd, for life is our being; it is all we have. Beings of a nobler and higher nature may look down on ours, but it is against nature for us to despise ourselves and hold ourselves of no account. It is a disease peculiar to man, found in no other creature, to hate and scorn his own existence. And it is a vanity of the same stamp to wish to be something other than what we are. Such a desire can never touch us, since it contradicts and cancels itself. The man who wishes to be turned from a man into an angel does nothing for himself; he would gain nothing by it — for once he no longer exists, who is left to feel the improvement and rejoice in it for him? As Lucretius says, he for whom misery and pain lie in the future must himself exist at the time the ills befall him.

The security, the rest, the freedom from feeling, the escape from the evils of this life that we think we are buying at the price of death — none of it profits us at all. The man who cannot enjoy peace gains nothing by escaping war, and the man who cannot taste rest gains just as little by avoiding trouble.

Among those who hold the first of these two opinions, there has been a great debate about what occasions are sufficient to justify the contemplation of self-slaughter — what they call "a reasonable exit." For though they say men must often die for slight causes, since the things that keep us in life are themselves of no great weight, still there has to be some limit. There are wild and senseless moods that have driven not only single men but whole nations to destroy themselves; I have given examples elsewhere. We read of the virgins of Miletus, who by a frantic compact hanged themselves one after another until the magistrate put a stop to it, decreeing that any woman found hanged should be dragged by the same rope, stark naked, through the city.

When Therykion urged Cleomenes to kill himself because of the wretched state of his affairs — having missed the more honorable death in the battle he had just lost, he should accept this one, second in honor, and not give the conquerors the chance to inflict on him either a shameful death or a shameful life — Cleomenes, with a courage truly Spartan and Stoic, rejected the advice as weak and unmanly. "That remedy," he said, "can never run out, but a man must never use it while an inch of hope remains." It was sometimes constancy and valor to live, he said; he wanted even his death to be of service to his country, and meant to make it an act of honor and virtue. Therykion still thought himself right, and did his own business; Cleomenes did the same afterward, but not until he had first tried fortune's utmost malice.

All the troubles in the world are not weighty enough that a man should die to escape them. Besides, there are so many sudden and unexpected turns in human affairs that it is hard to judge rightly when we have truly reached the end of our hope. As the poet says, even the gladiator beaten in the cruel arena keeps hoping, though the menacing crowd turns its thumb. All things, says the old adage, may be hoped for by a man while he lives. Yes, replies Seneca — but why should that thought always run in a man's head, that fortune can do anything for the living, rather than this one: that fortune has no power over the man who knows how to die?

Josephus found himself in danger so close and so plain — a whole people violently set against him, with no visible means of escape — that even when a friend counseled him in that extremity to kill himself, it served him well to hold on to hope; for fortune turned the event beyond all human expectation, and he came out of it without harm. Whereas Brutus and Cassius, by the haste and rashness with which they killed themselves before the proper time and a just occasion, threw away the last remains of Roman liberty, of which they were the sole protectors. Monsieur d'Enghien, at the battle of Cerisoles, twice tried to run himself through, despairing of the day's fortune — which was going very badly on his side of the field — and by that haste came very near cheating himself of a magnificent victory. I have seen a hundred hares escape from the very teeth of the greyhounds. As Seneca says, some men have outlived their executioners. And as Virgil writes, length of days and the shifting labor of changeful time bring things to a better state; fortune, turning, shows another face, and sets men back on solid ground.

Pliny says there are only three kinds of disease that give a man good title to destroy himself, and the worst of them is a stone in the bladder when the urine is blocked. Seneca says only those diseases that unsettle the workings of the soul over a long time.

And some, to avoid a worse death, have chosen one to their own liking. Democritus, general of the Aetolians, was brought a prisoner to Rome and found means to escape by night; but with his keepers close behind him, rather than be retaken he fell on his own sword and died. Antinous and Theodotus, when the Romans had reduced their city in Epirus to the last extremity, counseled the people to kill themselves together; but the people preferred to surrender, so the two leaders went to find the death they wanted, charging furiously into the enemy, intent on striking and not on parrying.

When the island of Gozo was taken by the Turks some years ago, a Sicilian there, who had two beautiful daughters of marriageable age, killed them both with his own hand, and their mother too, who came running in to save them. That done, he went out into the street with a crossbow and a harquebus, killed with two shots the two Turks nearest his door, then drew his sword and charged in among the rest, where he was instantly surrounded and cut to pieces — delivering his family and himself from slavery and dishonor.

The Jewish women, after circumcising their children, threw them and themselves off a precipice to escape the cruelty of Antigonus. I have been told of a man of rank in one of our prisons whose friends, learning that he would surely be condemned, bribed a priest to tell him — to spare him the disgrace of such a death — that his only means of deliverance was to commend himself to a certain saint, under certain vows, and to fast eight days together without taking any nourishment, whatever weakness or faintness he might feel. He followed the advice, and so destroyed himself without knowing it, never dreaming of death or of any danger in the experiment. Scribonia, advising her nephew Libo to kill himself rather than wait for the stroke of justice, told him that preserving his life only to hand it over within three or four days to the men who would come fetch him to execution was doing other people's business, and that keeping his blood for his enemies to spill was simply serving their malice.

We read in the Bible that when Nicanor, the persecutor of God's law, sent his soldiers to seize the good old man Razis — surnamed, in honor of his virtue, the father of the Jews — the old man, seeing no other remedy, his gates burned down and the enemy ready to lay hands on him, chose to die nobly rather than fall into the power of his wicked adversaries and be butchered by them against the honor of his rank. He stabbed himself with his own sword; but the blow, struck in haste, did not go home, so he ran and threw himself headlong from the top of a wall into the crowd below, which parted and made room, so that he landed squarely on his head. Feeling some life still in him even then, he summoned his courage again, got to his feet all bloody and broken, forced his way through the crowd to a steep rock, and there, through one of his wounds, drew out his own bowels and, tearing them apart with both hands, flung them among his pursuers — calling down divine vengeance on them all the while for their cruelty and injustice.

Of all violences done to the conscience, the one done against a woman's chastity is, in my opinion, the most to be avoided, since a certain pleasure is naturally mixed in it, and for that reason the refusal can never be entirely perfect and whole; the violence seems mingled with some small consent of the party forced. Church history holds several examples of devout women who embraced death to protect themselves from the outrages tyrants prepared against their religion and their honor. Pelagia and Sophronia were both canonized: the first threw herself into a river with her mother and sisters to escape being forced by soldiers, and the second killed herself to avoid being violated by the emperor Maxentius.

It may perhaps stand to our honor in future ages that a learned author of our own day — a Parisian — takes great pains to persuade the ladies of our time to take any course at all rather than enter into the horrible contemplation of such a despair. I am sorry he never heard, to add to his other stories, the saying of a woman at Toulouse who had passed through the hands of some soldiers: "God be praised," she said, "that for once in my life I have had my fill without sin." In truth, these cruelties are quite unworthy of the good French temper, and, God be thanked, our air has been well purged of them since that good advice. It is enough that they say "no" while doing it, following the rule of good Marot — "a sweet no, with a sweet smile, is so very gracious."

History is full everywhere of those who, by a thousand different roads, have traded a painful and weary life for death. Lucius Aruntius killed himself, he said, to flee both the future and the past. Granius Silvanus and Statius Proximus killed themselves after being pardoned by Nero — either scorning to live by the favor of so wicked a man, or unwilling to be put to the trouble of obtaining a second pardon someday, knowing how prone he was to suspect worthy men and believe accusations against them. Spargapises, son of Queen Tomyris and a prisoner of war to Cyrus, used the first kindness Cyrus showed him — ordering him unbound — to kill himself, having wanted no other benefit from liberty than to take revenge on himself for the disgrace of his capture.

Boges, governor of Eion for King Xerxes, besieged by the Athenian army under Cimon, refused the terms offered him — safe return to Asia with all his wealth — unable to bear outliving the loss of a place his master had given him to keep. He defended the city to the last extremity; then, when nothing was left to eat, he threw all the gold and everything else the enemy might take as plunder into the river Strymon, ordered a great pyre lit and the throats of all the women, children, concubines, and servants cut, threw their bodies into the fire, and at last leaped into it himself.

Ninachetuen, a lord of the Indies, on hearing the first whisper of the Portuguese viceroy's plan to strip him, for no stated cause, of his command at Malacca and transfer it to the king of Campar, made this resolution by himself. He had a platform built, longer than it was wide, raised on columns, royally hung with tapestries and strewn with flowers and quantities of perfume. Then, in a robe of cloth of gold thick with jewels of great value, he came out into the street and mounted the steps; at one corner burned a pyre of aromatic wood. Everyone ran to see what these strange preparations meant. Ninachetuen, with a bold but indignant face, declared how much the Portuguese nation owed him, and with what spotless fidelity he had carried out his charge; that having so often shown, sword in hand, on behalf of others, that honor was dearer to him than life, he was not going to abandon that concern in his own case; that since fortune denied him every means of opposing the injury planned against him, his courage at least commanded him to free himself from the feeling of it, and not to serve as a fable for the people or a triumph for men less deserving than himself. Having said this, he leaped into the fire.

Sextilia, wife of Scaurus, and Paxaea, wife of Labeo, to hearten their husbands to escape the dangers closing in on them — dangers in which they had no share but their married love — voluntarily gave up their own lives, to serve them in that final necessity as companion and example. What they did for their husbands, Cocceius Nerva did for his country, with less use though equal devotion: this great jurist, flourishing in health, wealth, reputation, and the emperor's favor, had no other reason to kill himself than pure grief at the miserable state of the Roman republic.

Nothing can be added to the beauty of the death of the wife of Fulvius, a close favorite of Augustus. Augustus, having discovered that Fulvius had leaked an important secret entrusted to him, received him coldly one morning when he came to pay his respects, and frowned on him. Fulvius went home in despair and sorrowfully told his wife that, having fallen into this misfortune, he was resolved to kill himself. "It is only right that you should," she answered flatly, "since you have had so much experience of my loose tongue and never took warning from it. But let me kill myself first." And without another word she ran herself through with a sword.

Vibius Virrius, despairing of the safety of his besieged city and of Roman mercy, told the senate of Capua in its last deliberation, after many arguments to that end, that the noblest way to escape fortune was by their own hands. The enemy would hold them in honor, he said, and Hannibal would feel how many faithful friends he had abandoned. He invited those who approved his advice to a good supper waiting at his house, where after they had eaten well they would drink together of what he had prepared — "a drink," he said, "that will deliver our bodies from torture, our souls from insult, and our eyes and ears from the sight and sound of all the hateful evils the conquered suffer at the hands of cruel and implacable conquerors." He had arranged, he said, for fit persons to throw their bodies onto a funeral pyre before his door as soon as they were dead. Many approved this high resolution; few imitated it. Twenty-seven senators followed him, and after trying to drown the thought of that fatal decision in wine, they ended the feast with the deadly dish. Embracing one another, after they had jointly mourned the misfortune of their country, some went home to their own houses, others stayed to be burned with Vibius on his pyre. And they were all so long in dying — the wine filling their veins and delaying the poison's work — that some of them came within an hour of seeing the enemy inside the walls of Capua, which was taken the next morning, and of suffering the very miseries they had paid so dearly to avoid.

Jubellius Taurea, another citizen of the same country, when the consul Fulvius was returning from the shameful slaughter he had made of two hundred and twenty-five senators, called him back fiercely by name, and when he had made him stop, said, "Give the order for someone to dispatch me too, after the massacre of so many others, so you can boast of having killed a far braver man than yourself." Fulvius dismissed him as a madman — and besides, letters had just arrived from Rome condemning the inhumanity of his executions, which tied his hands. So Jubellius went on: "Since my country is taken, my friends are dead, and I have slain my wife and children with my own hands to save them from the desolation of this ruin — since I am denied the death of my fellow citizens — let me borrow from virtue a vengeance on this hateful life." And drawing a short sword he had hidden on him, he ran it through his own breast and fell backward, dying at the consul's feet.

When Alexander was besieging a city in the Indies, the people inside, finding themselves hard pressed, resolved stoutly to deprive him of the pleasure of his victory, and burned themselves all together along with their city, in spite of his humanity. A new kind of war: the enemy fighting to save them, they fighting to destroy themselves, doing everything to make sure of death that men ordinarily do to make sure of life.

Astapa, a city in Spain, finding its walls and defenses too weak to withstand the Romans, the inhabitants piled all their riches and furniture in the public square, placed all the women and children on top of the heap, stacked wood and kindling around it to catch fire quickly, and left fifty of their young men to carry out what they had resolved. Then they made a desperate sally, and lacking the power to win, got themselves killed to the last man. The fifty, after slaughtering every living soul left in the city and setting fire to the pile, threw themselves into it last of all — ending their noble liberty in a manner without feeling rather than in sorrow and disgrace, and showing the enemy that if fortune had allowed it, they would have had the same courage to snatch victory away as they had to make it dreadful, even fatal: for many of the enemy, lured by the gold melting in those flames, pressed close and were suffocated and burned, unable to retreat through the crowd pushing behind them.

The people of Abydos, pressed by King Philip, took the same resolution; but lacking time, they could not carry it out. The king, horrified by their reckless haste — the treasure and goods they had condemned to the flames having first been seized — drew off his soldiers and granted them three days to kill themselves in, so that they might do it with more order and at greater ease. They filled those three days with blood and slaughter beyond the worst excess of any enemy's cruelty, so that not a single soul was left alive that had the power to destroy itself. There are countless examples of such mass resolutions; they seem the more fierce and cruel as their effect is more universal, yet they are really less so than when carried out singly. What argument and persuasion cannot do with men one by one, they can do with all together — the fever of the group sweeping away individual judgment.

In the reign of Tiberius, the condemned who waited to be executed forfeited their goods and were denied burial rites; those who anticipated the sentence by killing themselves were buried and had liberty to dispose of their estates by will.

But men sometimes covet death in hope of a greater good. "I desire to be with Christ," says St. Paul; and, "who shall deliver me from these bonds?" Cleombrotus of Ambracia, having read Plato's Phaedo, conceived so great a longing for the life to come that, with no other occasion at all, he threw himself into the sea. Which shows how wrongly we call this voluntary dissolution "despair," when it is often the eagerness of hope that inclines us to it — and often a calm and temperate desire, proceeding from a mature and deliberate judgment. Jacques du Chastel, bishop of Soissons, on St. Louis's expedition overseas, seeing the king and the whole army about to return to France leaving the affairs of religion unfinished, resolved to go to paradise instead. Having taken solemn leave of his friends, he charged alone, in full view of everyone, into the enemy's army, where he was promptly cut to pieces.

In a certain kingdom of the newly discovered world, on a day of solemn procession when the idol they worship is drawn through public streets on a chariot of marvelous size, many are seen cutting off pieces of their own flesh to offer it; and a number of others prostrate themselves in its path, letting themselves be crushed and broken under the heavy wheels, to earn a veneration for sanctity after death — which is duly paid them. The bishop's death, sword in hand, has more magnanimity in it and less feeling, the heat of combat absorbing part of the pain.

Some governments have taken it on themselves to regulate the justice and the proper occasions of voluntary death. In former times our city of Marseilles kept a poison prepared from hemlock, at public expense, for anyone who wished to hasten his end — provided he first gave the Six Hundred, their senate, an account of the reasons and motives for his design. It was not lawful to do violence to oneself otherwise than by leave of the magistrates and upon just occasion. The same law was in use elsewhere.

Sextus Pompeius, on his expedition into Asia, stopped at the island of Cea, near Negropont. While he was there — we have it from someone who was with him — a woman of great rank, having explained to her fellow citizens why she was resolved to end her life, invited Pompeius to her death, to make it the more honorable. He accepted; and having long tried in vain, with all the power of his eloquence, which was very great, to turn her from her design, he yielded at last to her will.

She had passed her ninetieth year in a very happy state of both body and mind. Lying now on her bed, better dressed than usual and propped on her elbow, she said: "May the gods, O Sextus Pompeius — and rather those I leave than those I go to seek — reward you for not disdaining to be both the counselor of my life and the witness of my death. For my part, having always felt the kindly face of fortune, I am going, for fear that the desire to live too long may show me the other one, to dismiss by a happy end the remains of my soul, leaving behind two daughters of my body and a legion of grandchildren." Then, after urging her family to live in peace, she divided her property among them, commended her household gods to her eldest daughter, took up the bowl containing the poison with a steady hand, and having made her vows and prayers to Mercury to conduct her to some happy seat in the other world, briskly swallowed the fatal draught. After that, she kept the company informed of its progress — how the cold took hold of the parts of her body one after another — until at last, telling them it had reached her heart and bowels, she called her daughters to do the final office and close her eyes.

Pliny tells of a certain Hyperborean nation where, thanks to the sweet mildness of the air, lives rarely ended except by the voluntary surrender of the inhabitants: weary of living and sated with it, they had the custom, at a very great age, of making good cheer and then throwing themselves into the sea from the top of a certain rock set aside for that service.

Pain, and the fear of a worse death, seem to me the most excusable incitements.

Of Books

I have no doubt that I often end up talking about things that are handled better, and more truly, by the people who actually master them. What you have here is purely a test of my natural abilities, not my acquired ones. Catch me in some ignorance and you've won nothing off me — I'd hardly answer to someone else for writings I don't answer for to myself, or even feel satisfied with. Anyone hunting for knowledge should go fish for it where it lives; there is nothing I claim less. These are fancies of my own, and I'm not trying to explain the world with them — I'm trying to lay myself open. The things themselves may be known to me someday, or may have been once, back when fortune happened to set me down wherever they were being explained. But I've completely forgotten. I may be a man of some reading, but I am a man of no retention.

So I can promise nothing certain, beyond showing you exactly how far the knowledge I currently have happens to reach. Don't pay attention to the matter I write about — pay attention to the shape I give it as I write. Watch, in what I borrow, whether I knew how to choose the right thing to lift or support the idea, which is always my own. Because I make others say for me — not before me, but after me — what I can't say so well myself, whether for want of language or want of sense. I don't count my borrowings; I weigh them. If I'd wanted to make them impressive by quantity, I'd have packed in twice as many. They are almost all from names so famous and so ancient that they seem to introduce themselves, without my help.

In the reasonings, comparisons, and arguments that I transplant into my own soil and mix in with my own, I deliberately hide the author. I do it to put a check on those rash critics who pounce on every kind of writing — especially recent writing, by men still living, and in the common tongue, which lets every man fancy himself qualified to judge, and makes the whole conception and design seem common too. I want them to flick Plutarch's nose thinking it's mine, and burn themselves insulting Seneca in me. I have to hide my weakness under these great reputations. And I will love anyone who can pluck my feathers off me — by sheer clearness of understanding and judgment, by nothing but the force and beauty of the writing itself. Because I, whose memory fails me at every turn when I try to sort the borrowed plumes out by their nation, am still wise enough to know, by the measure of my own abilities, that my soil could never produce the rich flowers I find growing there, and that all the fruit of my own raising isn't worth a single one of them.

For this much, though, I do hold myself accountable: if I trip over myself; if there's vanity or weakness in my writing that I don't sense myself, or can't see even when someone points it out. Plenty of faults escape our eye — but the real infirmity of judgment is failing to see them when somebody else lays them open to us. Knowledge and truth can lodge in us without judgment, and judgment can live in us without them; in fact, confessing your ignorance is one of the finest and surest proofs of judgment that I know.

I have no drill sergeant to put my writings in rank and file except chance. As things come into my head, I pile them up; sometimes they crowd in whole battalions, sometimes single file. I want people to see my natural, ordinary pace, lopsided as it is. I let myself jog along however I happen to go. And anyway, these are not subjects a man is forbidden to be ignorant about, or to discuss casually, at a venture. I'd love to understand things more perfectly, but I won't pay what perfect understanding costs. My plan is to pass what's left of my life gently, not laboriously. There is nothing I am willing to break my head over — not even knowledge, whatever it's worth.

In books, all I look for is the pleasure of an honest pastime; or if I study, the only learning I'm after is the kind that teaches me to know myself, and to die well and live well. As Propertius says, "this is the post my horse must sweat toward."

When I hit difficulties in my reading, I don't sit there chewing my nails over them; after a charge or two, I let them go. If I dug in, I'd only lose myself, and my time with it. My mind is impatient — it has to be satisfied on the first pass. What I don't see at once, staring makes more obscure, not less. I do nothing without gladness; grinding away too stubbornly darkens, dulls, and tires my judgment. My sight blurs and scatters with poring; I have to pull it away and send it back in fresh attempts — exactly the way they teach us to judge the sheen of scarlet cloth: pass the eye lightly across it, then sweep over it again in several quick, repeated glances.

If one book bores me, I take up another — and I only touch them at all in the hours when doing nothing has started to weary me. I don't bother much with new books, because the old ones seem fuller and stronger; nor much with the Greeks, because my judgment can't do its work on a half-understood text.

Among the books that are simply pleasant, of the moderns I find Boccaccio's Decameron, Rabelais, and the Kisses of Johannes Secundus — if that one may be filed under the heading at all — worth the time, for amusement. As for Amadis and that sort of stuff, it couldn't hold even my childhood. And I'll say further — boldly or rashly, take your pick — that this old, heavy soul of mine is no longer tickled by Ariosto, nor even by the worthy Ovid. His easy flow and his inventions, which used to ravish me, hardly interest me now; I can barely sit still for them.

I say my opinion freely of all things — even of things that may well exceed my capacity, and that I don't pretend for a moment fall under my jurisdiction. What my judgment delivers is the measure of my own eyesight, not the measure of the things I'm bold enough to judge. When I find myself put off by Plato's Axiochus — finding it a feeble work, with all due respect to such an author — my judgment doesn't trust itself. It isn't arrogant enough to set itself against the authority of so many famous judgments of antiquity, which it regards as its tutors and masters, and with whom it would rather be wrong. In a case like that, it convicts itself: either it stopped at the outer bark, unable to reach the heart, or it was looking at the thing in some false light. It settles for keeping itself clear of confusion and disorder; as for its own weakness, it owns up to it freely. It believes it's giving a fair reading of what its perceptions show it — but those perceptions are weak and imperfect. Most of Aesop's fables carry several layers of meaning. The interpreters pick out one that happens to fit the story, but for the most part that's only the first face it shows, the surface; there are other layers underneath — more living, more essential, more profound — that they never managed to reach. And that's just how it is with me.

But to get on with the business of this essay: I have always thought that in poetry, Virgil, Lucretius, Catullus, and Horace stand many degrees above the rest — and Virgil above all in his Georgics, which I consider the most accomplished work in all of poetry. Set the Georgics beside the Aeneid, and you can easily see passages in the Aeneid the author would have given one more polish if he'd had the time. The fifth book of the Aeneid strikes me as its most perfect. I also love Lucan, and read him gladly — not so much for his style as for his own worth, and the truth and solidity of his opinions and judgments. As for good Terence, the very refinement and grace of the Latin tongue, I find him wonderful at catching, alive, our manners and the movements of the soul. What we do keeps throwing me back on him at every turn; and I can't read him often enough to stop discovering some new grace and beauty.

People who lived near Virgil's own time complained when anyone compared Lucretius to him. I think the comparison is, in truth, badly unequal — though it's a belief I have a hard time holding steady when I'm caught up in one of Lucretius's superb passages. But if that comparison made them angry, what would they say to the brutish, barbarous stupidity of the people who nowadays compare Virgil to Ariosto? What would Ariosto himself say? "O what a stupid and tasteless age," as Catullus put it.

I think the ancients had even more reason to complain about those who set Plautus beside Terence — a much closer match — than about Lucretius beside Virgil. It counts heavily in Terence's favor that the father of Roman eloquence has him, and him alone of his class, so constantly on his lips, and that the best judge of Roman poets passed the verdict he did on Terence's rival.

It has often struck me that our modern comedy writers — imitating the Italians, who are happy enough at this game — take three or four plots from Plautus or Terence to make one of their own, and cram five or six of Boccaccio's tales into a single comedy. What makes them load up on material like this is distrust of their ability to stand on their own merits. They need something to lean on; lacking stuff of their own to hold our attention, they bring in story to cover the weakness of the writing. With my author it's the opposite: the elegance and perfection of his way of speaking makes us lose our appetite for his plot. His grace and refinement of language hold us everywhere; he runs so clear throughout — "liquid, like the purest stream," as Horace says of him — and so fills the soul with his own beauties that we forget the beauties of his story.

The same observation takes me a step further. I notice that the best of the ancient poets avoided straining for effect — avoided not only the fantastic flights of the Spanish and Petrarchan styles, but even those softer, gentler touches that have decorated all the poetry written since. Yet no sound judgment will hold that against the ancients, or fail to admire, beyond all comparison, the smooth polish, the perpetual sweetness, the flowering beauty of Catullus's epigrams over all the stingers Martial fastens to the tails of his. The reason is the one I just gave — the one Martial gave about himself: "my subject did the work, and spared my wit the labor." The first sort make themselves felt without working themselves up, without getting angry; they have material enough to laugh with everywhere — they don't need to tickle themselves. The second sort need outside help: the less wit, the more body. They get on horseback because they can't stand on their own legs.

It's the same at our balls. The hired men who teach dancing, unable to carry off the bearing and dignity of our nobility, try to recommend themselves with dangerous leaps and other strange tumblers' tricks. And the ladies have an easier time of it in dances full of fancy steps, changes, and quick flurries of motion than in certain stately dances where they have only to walk at a natural pace and carry their ordinary grace and presence. I've likewise seen first-rate clowns, in their everyday clothes and wearing their everyday faces, give us every pleasure their art can give — while their apprentices, not yet up to that pitch of perfection, have to flour their faces, climb into ridiculous costumes, and pull a hundred grotesque expressions to scrape up a laugh.

This idea of mine is nowhere easier to see than in setting the Aeneid against Orlando Furioso. The first you see up on a strong wing, in a high, brave line of flight, always tracking its point. The second flutters and hops from tale to tale as from branch to branch, never trusting its wings except for the shortest hauls, perching at every turn for fear its breath and strength will give out — making, as Virgil says, "only short excursions."

Those, then, in that line, are the authors that please me best.

As for my other reading — the kind that mixes a little more profit with the pleasure, and teaches me how to order my opinions and my conduct — the books that serve me here are Plutarch (now that he's been translated into French) and Seneca. Both have this notable convenience, perfectly suited to my temperament: the knowledge I'm after comes in loose pieces that don't demand long stretches of reading, of which I am incapable. That's how it is with Plutarch's minor works and Seneca's letters, which are the best and most profitable parts of everything they wrote. It costs no great effort to pick one up, and I quit whenever I please, because they have no sequence, no dependence on one another.

These two authors agree in most of their useful and true opinions, and their parallel runs further: fortune sent them into the world in roughly the same century; both were tutors to Roman emperors; both came from foreign lands; both were rich, and both were great men. Their teaching is the cream of philosophy, served plainly and to the point. Plutarch is more uniform and steady; Seneca more various, more in motion. Seneca toils and strains every muscle to arm virtue against weakness, fear, and vicious appetite; Plutarch seems to rate their power lower — he won't alter his pace or even bother to put up his guard. Plutarch's opinions are Platonic, gentle, fitted to civil society; Seneca's are Stoic and Epicurean, further from common use but, to my mind, more useful to a single man and more solid. Seneca seems to bend a little toward the tyranny of the emperors of his day — and only seems, I'm sure, for I take it as certain he is speaking against his own judgment when he condemns the noble killers of Caesar. Plutarch is frank from end to end. Seneca sparkles with sharp touches and sallies; Plutarch with things that warm you and move you more. Plutarch contents you and pays you better. He leads us; the other drives us.

As for Cicero, the works of his most useful to my purpose are the ones that treat of character and the rules of living. But to confess the truth boldly — since once you've jumped the fence of impudence there's no reining in — his way of writing strikes me as tedious, and so does every writing of the same kind. His prefaces, definitions, subdivisions, and etymologies eat up the greater part of his work; whatever life and marrow there is gets smothered under the long windup. If I spend an hour reading him — which is a lot for me — and then try to recall what juice and substance I've extracted, most of the time I find nothing but wind, because he hasn't yet gotten to the arguments that serve his purpose, or the reasons that actually tie the knot I'm looking for.

For me, who only want to become wiser — not more learned, or more eloquent — these Aristotelian arrangements of parts are beside the point. I want a writer to begin with the main proposition. I know perfectly well what death and pleasure are; let no man waste his time dissecting them for me. What I want, from the first stroke, are good solid reasons that teach me how to stand up to their shock — and for that, neither grammatical subtleties nor ingenious weavings of words and syllogisms are the slightest use. I'm for arguments that charge straight into the heart of the strongpoint. Cicero's loiter around the edges of the subject. They're fine for the schools, the bar, and the pulpit, where we have leisure to doze and can wake up a quarter of an hour later in plenty of time to pick up the thread.

That manner of speaking is necessary with judges you mean to win over, right or wrong; with children; with ordinary people, to whom you have to say everything and see what sticks. I don't want an author laboring to get my attention, shouting "Hear ye!" fifty times like a herald. The Romans opened their religious rites with Hoc age — "attend to this" — the way we open ours with Sursum corda, "lift up your hearts." For me those are so many words wasted: I arrive already fully prepared from my own room. I need no appetizer, no invitation, no sauce; I eat the meat raw. All those preparatives, instead of whetting my appetite, tire it out and spoil it.

Will the license of the times excuse a sacrilege? — because I find Plato's dialogues themselves draggy, smothering the matter; and I mourn all the time a man with so many better things to say spent on those long, useless warm-up conversations. My ignorance excuses me here in better style, since I don't know Greek well enough to see the beauty of his language.

In general I choose books that use learning, not books that merely lead up to it. The first two, and Pliny, and others of their kind, have no Hoc age about them: they want readers who come already instructed — or if they do say "attend to this," it's a substantial attending, with a body of its own.

I also delight in reading the Letters to Atticus — not only because they hold so much of the history and affairs of Cicero's times, but far more because in them I discover his own private moods. For I have a singular curiosity, as I've said elsewhere, to pry into the souls of my authors, their natural and genuine opinions. From the works they put on the world's stage you may judge their talent — but not their character, and not the men themselves. I have lamented a thousand times the loss of the treatise Brutus wrote on virtue: it is a fine thing to learn the theory from those who best know the practice. But since the sermon is one thing and the preacher another, I'd as gladly see Brutus in Plutarch as in a book of his own. I would rather know for certain what he said in his tent to a few particular friends the night before a battle than the speech he made to his army the next morning; rather know what he did in his study and his chamber than what he did in the public square and the senate.

As for Cicero the man, I share the common opinion: learning aside, there was no great excellence in him. He was a good citizen, of an affable nature — as fat, easy men like him usually are — but soft-living, in truth, and with a mighty share of vanity and ambition. And I don't know how to excuse him for thinking his poetry fit to publish. Writing bad verses is no great flaw; failing to see how unworthy they were of the glory of his name — that is a flaw. As for his eloquence, it stands beyond all comparison; I believe no one will ever equal it.

The younger Cicero, who resembled his father in nothing but his name, was commanding in Asia when one day he found several strangers at his table — among them Cestius, seated at the lower end, the way men slip into the open tables of the great. Cicero asked one of his servants who the man was, and was told his name. But like a man with his mind elsewhere, who keeps forgetting the answer, he asked again — three or four times. The servant, tired of repeating himself, and wanting to fix the man in his memory by some particular detail, said: "It's that Cestius — the one you were told makes no great account of your father's eloquence next to his own." Cicero, suddenly stung, ordered poor Cestius seized on the spot and had him soundly whipped, in his own presence. A most discourteous host.

Even among those who, all things weighed, judged Cicero's eloquence incomparable, there were some who didn't hesitate to note its faults: his great friend Brutus, for one, who called it a broken and feeble eloquence. The orators nearest his own age also faulted his obsessive care for a certain long cadence at the close of his sentences, and singled out the words esse videatur — "it would seem to be" — which he leans on so often there. For my own part, I prefer a style that comes off shorter and rounder. He does sometimes snap his clauses together briskly enough, but very seldom. One passage I marked myself: "I had rather be old for a shorter time than be old before I am old," as he says in his book on old age.

The historians are the ball that comes to my strong side — pleasant and easy, and at the same time the place where man in general, the creature I hunt the knowledge of, appears more vividly and more entire than anywhere else: the variety and truth of his inward qualities, in bulk and in detail, the diversity of materials he's assembled from, and the accidents that threaten him. Now the writers of lives — since they dwell more on counsels than on events, more on what issues from within than on what happens without — are the historians fittest for me; and that is why, in every way, Plutarch is my man. I am very sorry we don't have a dozen Diogenes Laertiuses, or that his lives of the philosophers aren't longer and better known, for I am every bit as curious about the lives and fortunes of these great teachers of the world as about the variety of their doctrines and opinions.

In this branch of study you have to tumble through all sorts of authors without distinction, old and new, French and foreign, to learn the things they variously treat of. But Caesar, in my opinion, deserves study in a class by himself — not for the history alone, but for the man, he has so much excellence and perfection above all the rest, Sallust included. Truly, I read this author with rather more reverence and respect than we usually grant human writings: now considering the man himself, through his actions and his miraculous greatness; now the purity and inimitable polish of his language, in which he surpassed not only every historian, as Cicero admits, but perhaps Cicero himself. He speaks of his enemies with such fairness that — apart from the false colors he uses to dress up his bad cause, and the filth of his pestilent ambition — I think the only fault you can charge him with is speaking too sparingly of himself. So many great things could not have been done under his command unless his own personal share in them was far larger than he lets on.

I love historians either very simple or excellent. The simple ones, who have nothing of their own to mix in, and whose only business is to gather everything that comes to their notice and record it all faithfully, without choosing or sorting, leave our judgment whole for discerning the truth. Such, among others, is honest Froissart, who marches through his enterprise with so frank a plainness that when he's made a mistake, he isn't ashamed to confess it and correct it on the spot where it's pointed out — and who gives us even the variety of rumors then in circulation, and the different reports that were made to him. His is the naked, unshaped stuff of history, and every reader can profit from it according to his own understanding.

The truly excellent ones have the judgment to pick out what is worth knowing; of two reports, to weigh which is the likelier true; from the condition and temperament of princes, to infer their counsels and put fitting words in their mouths. They have the right to assume the authority of bending our belief to theirs — but certainly that privilege belongs to very few.

The middle sort — and most of them are the middle sort — spoil everything. They insist on chewing our meat for us. They take it on themselves to judge, and so, inevitably, to bend history to their own fancy; for once the judgment tilts to one side, the narrative can't help twisting and writhing to that bias. They undertake to select what's worth knowing, and meanwhile hide from us exactly the word, the private act, that would have taught us most; they leave out as unbelievable the things they don't understand — and some things, perhaps, because they can't render them in good French or Latin. Let them flaunt their eloquence and their cleverness all they like; let them judge to their hearts' content; but let them also leave us something to judge after them. Let them not alter or dilute, by their trimmings and their selections, anything of the substance of the matter, but hand it to us pure and entire, in all its dimensions.

For the most part — and especially in these latter ages — the people picked for this work are picked out of the crowd for the single qualification of speaking well, as if we came to history to learn grammar. And having been hired for that alone, and bringing nothing to market but chatter, they're right not to trouble themselves over anything else. So with a fine jingle of words they cook us up a tidy stew of the reports they gather on street corners. The only good histories are those written by the men who themselves held command in the affairs they describe, or who had a share in conducting them, or who at least have conducted others of the same kind. That's nearly all the Greek and Roman histories: with several eyewitnesses writing on the same subject — in an age when greatness and learning commonly met in the same person — any error that slips in must be a slight one, on a deeply doubtful point. What can you expect from a physician writing about war, or a schoolman writing about the designs of princes?

If you want to see how scrupulous the Romans were on this point, one example will do. Asinius Pollio found, even in Caesar's own histories, things misreported — because Caesar couldn't have his eye on every part of his army at once and had trusted individuals who brought him less than accurate accounts, or because his lieutenants hadn't fully informed him of what they'd done in his absence. Which shows how delicate a thing the hunt for truth is, when you can't trust the report of a battle to the knowledge of the man who commanded it, nor to the soldiers who fought in it, unless — as in a judicial inquiry — the witnesses are confronted and the objections heard on the proof of every smallest incident. Honestly, the knowledge we have of our own affairs is far murkier still. But that has been handled well enough by Bodin, and in line with my own opinion.

To prop up the weakness of my memory a little — so extreme that more than once I've picked up a book as new and unread that I'd carefully gone through a few years before, scribbling it full of my notes — I've adopted a custom of late: at the end of every book I don't intend to read again, I write down the date I finished it and the verdict I'd reached, so it can at least show me again the general air and idea I'd formed of the author while reading him. And I'll copy out some of those annotations here.

Here's what I wrote, some ten years ago, in my Guicciardini (whatever language my books speak to me in, I speak to them in my own): "He is a diligent historiographer, from whom, in my opinion, a man may learn the truth of the affairs of his time as exactly as from any other — and in most of them he was himself an actor, in honorable rank. There's no sign that he ever disguised anything out of hatred, favor, or vanity; the free judgments he passes on the great — particularly on those who advanced him and employed him in posts of trust, like Pope Clement VII — are ample testimony to that. As for the part he thinks himself best at, his digressions and reflections, some are genuinely good, enriched with fine touches; but he loves them too much. Determined to leave nothing unsaid, with a subject so full, so vast, so nearly infinite, he turns flabby, and smacks a little of scholastic prattle. I've also noticed this about him: of all the souls and deeds he judges, all the motives and counsels, he never credits a single one to virtue, religion, or conscience — as if those things had died out of the world entirely. Of every action, however fine it looks on the outside, he refers the cause to some vicious motive or some hope of profit. It is impossible to imagine that among the countless actions he passes judgment on, not one was produced by way of honest reason. No corruption could have infected men so universally that nobody escaped the contagion. Which makes me suspect the bad taste was his own, and that he judged other men by himself."

In my Philippe de Commines there is this: "You will find the language here sweet and agreeable, of a natural simplicity; the narration pure, with the author's good faith showing plainly through it — free of vanity when he speaks of himself, of partiality and envy when he speaks of others; his reflections and exhortations carried more by zeal and truth than by any exquisite skill; and throughout, an authority and gravity that mark him as a man of good stock, brought up in great affairs."

And on the Memoirs of Monsieur du Bellay, this: "It is always a pleasure to read things written by men who have experienced how such things ought to be done; but it can't be denied that in these two lords there is a plain falling-off from the freedom and liberty of writing that shines in the older men of their kind — the Sire de Joinville, companion of Saint Louis; Eginhard, chancellor to Charlemagne; and, nearer our own day, Philippe de Commines. What we have here is less a history than an apology for King Francis against the Emperor Charles V. I won't believe they falsified anything as to the bare facts; but they make a regular practice of twisting the judgment of events, often against all reason, to our advantage, and of leaving out whatever is awkward in their master's life. Witness the affairs of Messieurs de Montmorency and de Biron, which are simply missing — and the very name of Madame d'Estampes is nowhere to be found. Secret doings a historian may conceal; but to pass over in silence what the whole world knows, and things that dragged such great public consequences after them, is an inexcusable defect. In short: whoever wants a complete knowledge of King Francis and the events of his reign should look for it elsewhere, if my advice carries any weight. The only profit to be had from these Memoirs is in the particular accounts of the battles and campaigns these gentlemen took part in personally; in certain words and private acts of the princes of their time; and in the treaties and negotiations handled by the Seigneur de Langey, where there are things worth knowing on every page, and reflections above the common run."

Of Cruelty

I think virtue is something other — something nobler — than the good nature we're born with, the simple inclination toward decency. Souls that are well-made and well-bred follow the same path virtue does, and wear the same face in their actions. But the word virtue implies something greater and more active than letting yourself be led, gently and quietly, by a happy disposition down the road of reason.

A man who shrugs off insults out of natural sweetness does a fine and praiseworthy thing. But a man who is stung to the quick by an offense, who arms himself with reason against the raging appetite for revenge, and who after a hard fight finally masters his own passion — that man does far more. The first acts well; the second acts virtuously. Call one action goodness and the other virtue. The very name of virtue presupposes difficulty and struggle; it cannot exist without an opponent. That may be why we call God good, mighty, generous, and just — but we do not call Him virtuous. His works are natural and cost Him nothing.

Many philosophers have held this view — not only Stoics but Epicureans too. And before anyone objects to my lumping them together: I'm borrowing the common opinion of the Epicureans, which is false, whatever the clever quip of Arcesilaus may suggest. When someone needled him that plenty of students left his school for Epicurus's, but never one came the other way, he answered, "Naturally — you can make capons out of roosters, but never roosters out of capons." In truth the Epicurean sect yields nothing to the Stoic in firmness or in the rigor of its opinions and rules. One Stoic — showing more honesty than those debaters who, to score points against Epicurus, put words in his mouth he never thought, twisting his sentences by grammatical hairsplitting into meanings they knew were foreign to his mind and his morals — declared that he had quit the Epicurean school for this reason among others: he found their road too lofty and too steep. For those who are called lovers of pleasure are in fact lovers of honor and justice, and cultivate and practice every virtue — so says Cicero.

These philosophers say it is not enough to have the soul well-placed, well-tempered, and well-disposed to virtue. It is not enough to have our resolutions and our reasoning fixed beyond fortune's reach. We must go looking for occasions to put them to the test. They would seek out pain, hardship, and contempt on purpose, to wrestle with them and keep the soul in training — for, as Seneca says, virtue grows stronger by being attacked.

This is one reason Epaminondas — who belonged to yet a third school — refused riches that fortune offered him by perfectly lawful means. I have poverty to wrestle with, he said; and he held that extreme to the end of his life. Socrates, it seems to me, set himself an even harder drill: he kept a thoroughly bad-tempered, scolding wife for daily practice, which is fencing with sharpened blades.

Metellus, alone of all the Roman senators, undertook by sheer force of virtue to withstand Saturninus, the tribune of the people, who was determined to ram through an unjust law in favor of the commons. Having thereby incurred the death penalty that Saturninus had decreed for dissenters, Metellus told the men leading him to execution something like this: that doing evil was too easy and too base; that doing good where there was no danger was commonplace; but that doing good where there was danger was the proper work of a man of virtue.

These words of Metellus show exactly what I'm getting at: virtue refuses to keep company with ease. The smooth, gentle, downhill path that a sweet natural disposition travels is not the path of true virtue. Virtue demands a rough and stormy passage. She wants either external difficulties to wrestle with, like Metellus had — fortune delighting to break her stride — or internal ones, supplied by the unruly appetites and imperfections of our condition.

I've come this far at my ease. But here it occurs to me that the soul of Socrates, the most perfect that has ever come to my knowledge, would by this rule deserve very little credit. I cannot detect in that man the slightest stirring of a vicious inclination. I cannot imagine any difficulty or constraint in the course of his virtue. I know his reason was so powerful and so sovereign in him that it would never have allowed a vicious appetite even to sprout. To a virtue as elevated as his, I have nothing to oppose. I seem to see him marching at a victorious, triumphant pace, in state and at his ease, with nothing in his way.

If virtue can only shine by fighting contrary appetites, shall we say she cannot exist without the help of vice — that she owes vice her reputation and her honor? And what would become of that brave and generous Epicurean pleasure, which prides itself on nursing virtue tenderly in its lap, giving her shame, fevers, poverty, death, and tortures for toys to play with? If I assume that perfect virtue shows itself in struggling, in patiently enduring pain, in bearing the worst extremities of gout without being shaken from her seat; if I make trouble and difficulty her necessary diet — then what becomes of a virtue raised so high that she not only despises pain but rejoices in it, that finds the stabbing of a sharp colic positively delightful? For that is the virtue the Epicureans established, and many of them proved it by their actions. So have others, who I find outdid the very rules of their own school.

Witness the younger Cato. When I see him dying, tearing out his own bowels, I am not content to believe merely that his soul was free of trouble and horror. I cannot think he simply held the steadiness the Stoic rules prescribed — calm, unmoved, imperturbable. There was something in that man's virtue, it seems to me, too lively and too fresh to stop there. I believe — I have no doubt — that he felt pleasure and delight in so noble an action, and enjoyed it more than anything else he ever did. He quit life rejoicing that he had found a reason to die, as Cicero says. I believe it so thoroughly that I wonder whether he would have wanted to be deprived of the occasion for so brave a deed. And if the goodness that made him care more for the public's interest than his own didn't hold me back, I could easily slip into the opinion that he was grateful to fortune for putting his virtue to so splendid a trial — grateful to that brigand Caesar for trampling underfoot the ancient liberty of his country. I seem to read in that act a kind of exaltation of soul, an extraordinary and manly thrill of pleasure, as he looked on the nobility and height of his enterprise: fiercer for having resolved on death, as Horace puts it.

Not spurred by any hope of glory, as some have shallowly and feebly concluded — that consideration was too small to touch a heart so generous, so proud, so determined — but for the beauty of the thing in itself, which he, who handled the springs of it, saw far more clearly and in its perfection than we can. Philosophy has obliged me by ruling that so brave an action would have been indecently placed in any life but Cato's: that only his life was entitled to end that way. And yet, following reason, he ordered his son and the senators who were with him to manage their affairs differently. Cato, says Cicero, to whom nature had given incredible dignity, and who had strengthened it by lifelong constancy, never wavering in his resolve, was bound to die rather than look upon the face of a tyrant.

Every death ought to be in proportion to the life that went before it. We do not become someone else just because we are dying. I always interpret a death by the life that preceded it. If anyone tells me of a death that looked strong and steadfast, attached to a feeble life, I conclude it came from some feeble cause, in keeping with the life. The ease of Cato's death, the facility in dying that he had acquired by the vigor of his soul — should that dim the luster of his virtue?

And who, with even a drop of true philosophy in his head, can be content to imagine Socrates merely free of fear and passion under arrest, in chains, condemned? Who does not recognize in him not just firmness and constancy — that was his everyday condition — but some new satisfaction, a playful cheerfulness, in his final words and acts? In that little start of pleasure he felt scratching his leg when his irons were taken off, doesn't he show the same serenity and joy of a soul released from past discomforts and on the point of entering into knowledge of things to come?

Cato will pardon me, if he pleases. His death is more tragic, more drawn-out; but this one is — I don't quite know how — finer. Aristippus, hearing people lament that death, said, "May the gods send me one like it."

In the souls of these two men, and of their imitators (for I very much doubt they ever had equals), one sees so perfect a habit of virtue that it became their very constitution. It is no longer a laborious virtue, no longer reason's precepts, held up by a soul straining at the load — it is the very essence of their soul, its natural and ordinary bearing. They made it so by long practice of philosophy's precepts working on a rich, fine nature. The vicious passions that spring up in us find no entrance into them; the force and vigor of their souls stifle and snuff out unruly desires the moment they begin to stir.

Now I don't think it can be doubted that it is nobler, by a high and godlike resolution, to prevent temptations from being born at all — to be so formed to virtue that the very seeds of vice are rooted out — than to stop their progress by main force; and that this second way, catching ourselves surprised by the first stirrings of passion and arming ourselves to stand firm and beat them back, is itself far more generous than simply being equipped with an easy, affable nature that happens to dislike debauchery and vice. For this third and lowest sort of virtue seems to make a man innocent, but not virtuous; free from doing evil, but not strong enough to do good. Besides, this condition sits so close to imperfection and weakness that I hardly know where to draw the line between them. The very names of goodness and innocence have, for this reason, become almost terms of contempt.

I know very well that several virtues — chastity, sobriety, temperance — can come to a man through personal defects. Steadiness in danger (if it deserves the name), contempt of death, patience in misfortune, can often be found in men simply because they fail to judge such events properly and don't grasp them for what they are. Dullness and stupidity sometimes counterfeit virtuous deeds. I have often seen men praised for what deserved blame.

An Italian lord once said this in my presence, at the expense of his own nation: that the Italians' subtlety and quickness of mind were so great, and they foresaw dangers from so far off, that no one should wonder if in war they often provided for their safety before they had even sighted the peril. We French and the Spaniards, not so cunning, went a step further: we had to see and feel the danger before taking alarm — and even then we couldn't hold steady. But the Germans and the Swiss, heavier and slower, hadn't the wit to look around them even when the blows were landing on their heads. Perhaps he was only joking. Yet it is quite true that raw soldiers rush into danger more recklessly than men who have taken their beatings — knowing well, as Virgil says, how much fresh glory in arms and the sweetness of honor can do in a first battle.

This is why, when we judge a particular action, we must consider the circumstances, and the whole man who performed it, before we give it a name.

Now a word about myself. I have sometimes heard my friends call prudence in me what was merely luck, and credit me with courage and patience where it was only judgment and opinion — giving me one title when another was due, sometimes to my advantage, sometimes not. As for the rest, I am so far from having reached that first and most perfect degree of excellence, where virtue becomes habit, that I've barely given proof of the second. I have not worked very hard at curbing the desires that have pressed me. My virtue is a virtue — or rather an innocence — that is casual and accidental. If I'd been born with a more disorderly temperament, I'm afraid the result would have been shabby, for I have never noticed much steadiness in my soul for resisting passions, even mildly vehement ones. I don't know how to nurse quarrels and conflicts inside my own chest. So I owe myself no great thanks for being free of several vices. If my nature is flawed by only a few minor vices and otherwise sound, as Horace says, it is like faulting moles on a handsome body.

I owe it to my fortune rather than my reason. She had me born of a family famous for integrity, and of a very good father. Whether he passed some of his temperament into me, or whether the examples at home and the good upbringing of my childhood worked on me without my noticing, or whether I was simply born this way — whether the Scales or dread Scorpio presided over my birth hour, as Horace has it, or Capricorn, tyrant of the western sea — I cannot say. But this much is so: I have a natural horror of most vices. The answer of Antisthenes, asked what was the best thing to learn — "to unlearn evil" — seems to point at this. I hold them in horror, I say, with a detestation so natural and so much my own that the same instinct and impression I brought away from my nurse I keep still; no temptation has ever had the power to alter it. Not even my own reasonings, which, straying off the common road in some things, might easily seem to license me for actions that my natural inclination makes me hate.

I will say a monstrous thing, but I will say it anyway: I find my conduct, in many matters, more under control than my opinions, and my appetite less debauched than my reason. Aristippus laid down opinions so bold in favor of pleasure and riches that he set all the philosophers against him. But as for his conduct: when the tyrant Dionysius presented three beautiful women to him and told him to choose, he answered that he would choose all three — Paris got into trouble for preferring one over the other two. But having brought them home, he sent them back untouched. And when his servant, carrying his money behind him on the road, found the load too heavy, Aristippus told him to pour out and throw away whatever was troubling him. Epicurus, whose doctrines were supposedly so irreligious and soft, lived a life laborious and devout. He wrote to a friend that he lived on nothing but biscuit and water, and asked him to send a little cheese to keep on hand for when he wanted to throw a feast.

Must it be true, then, that to be perfectly good we must be so by some hidden, natural, universal property — without law, reason, or example? The excesses I've been mixed up in are not, thank God, of the worst sort. I have condemned them in myself fairly — my judgment was never infected by them; on the contrary, I accuse them more severely in myself than in anyone else. But that is all. Beyond that, I put up too little resistance and let myself lean too easily to the other side of the scale — except that I keep my vices in bounds and stop them from mixing with other vices, which mostly cling together in a cluster if a man isn't careful. I have trimmed mine back and kept them as single and as simple as I could, and I do not carry the wrong any further, as Juvenal says.

As for the Stoics' opinion that the wise man, when he acts, acts by all the virtues at once, though one shows most, according to the nature of the action — the human body might lend them some analogy there, since anger cannot work unless all the humors assist it, even if choler predominates. But if they want to draw the parallel conclusion, that when the wicked man does wickedly, he does it by all the vices at once, I don't believe it, or else I don't understand them, for I find the opposite in practice. These are sharp, airless subtleties that philosophy sometimes amuses itself with. I indulge some vices, but I flee others as thoroughly as any saint could. The Peripatetics likewise reject this all-or-nothing bundling, and Aristotle holds that a prudent and just man can still be intemperate and unrestrained. Socrates admitted to people who spotted a certain inclination to vice in his face that it was indeed his natural bent — but that he had corrected it by discipline. And friends of the philosopher Stilpo said that, born with a weakness for wine and women, he had made himself by study thoroughly abstinent from both.

What good there is in me, I have, on the contrary, by the chance of my birth. I do not hold it from law, precept, or any other schooling. The innocence in me is a simple innocence: little vigor, and no art.

Among all the vices, I cruelly hate cruelty, both by nature and by judgment, as the extreme of all vices. And with such softness that I cannot watch a chicken's neck wrung without distress, and cannot bear to hear a hare crying in my dogs' teeth — even though the chase is a violent pleasure.

Those who have sensuality to fight against like to use this argument, to show that it is altogether vicious and unreasonable: that at its height it masters us so completely that reason has no access. And they cite our own experience in the act of love — that moment, as Lucretius describes it, when the body already has its foretaste of joy and Venus is on the point of finishing her work. They conceive that the pleasure so transports us that our reason cannot do its job while we are in that ecstasy. But I know very well it can be otherwise; a man may sometimes, if he chooses, win this point over himself and turn his soul, even at the critical moment, to other thoughts — though he must bend it to that purpose deliberately. I know the utmost force of this pleasure can be mastered. I have experienced it in myself, and have not found Venus so imperious a goddess as many men, far more virtuous than I am, declare her to be. I do not count it a miracle, as the Queen of Navarre does in one of the tales of her Heptameron (a very pretty book of its kind), nor even a thing of extreme difficulty, to spend whole nights with a long-desired mistress, with every freedom and opportunity a man could want, and still keep one's pledged word to be content with kisses and simple caresses, pressing no further.

I think the pleasure of the hunt would make a better example. There the pleasure is smaller, but the surprise is sharper, leaving reason no time to brace itself for the encounter — when, after a long search, the animal bursts up suddenly where we least expected it. That jolt, and the heat of the shouting, strike us so hard that those who love this lesser chase would find it impossible to wrench their thoughts elsewhere at that instant. And the poets make Diana triumph over Cupid's torch and arrows: in such delights, asks Horace, who does not forget the anxious cares of love?

But back to my point. I am tenderly compassionate of other people's afflictions, and would readily cry for company, if I could cry at all, for any occasion whatever. Nothing tempts my tears but tears — and not only real ones, but tears of every kind, feigned or painted. The dead I scarcely pity; I would sooner envy them. But I deeply pity the dying. Savages who roast and eat the bodies of the dead offend me less than men who torment and persecute the living. I cannot even watch the ordinary executions of justice, however reasonable, with a steady eye.

Someone wanting to give evidence of Julius Caesar's clemency wrote: "He was mild in his revenges. Having forced the surrender of the pirates who had earlier captured and ransomed him, and having sworn to crucify them, he did condemn them to the cross — but only after they had first been strangled. His secretary Philemon, who had tried to poison him, he punished with nothing harsher than plain death." Without naming the Latin author who dares offer it as proof of mercy that a man merely killed those who had wronged him — it's easy to guess he was haunted by the horrid, inhuman examples of cruelty the Roman tyrants had set.

For my part, even in justice itself, anything beyond a simple death looks to me like pure cruelty. Let them kill, but not torture.
