This holds especially for us, who ought to have some care for souls, and dismiss them in a good and tranquil state — which cannot be done when we have shaken them with unbearable torments.

Not long ago, a soldier being held prisoner noticed from his tower that crowds were gathering at the place of execution and carpenters were busy putting up a scaffold. Concluding the preparations were for him, he resolved to kill himself, but could find nothing to do it with except an old rusty cart-nail that fortune put in his way. With it he gave himself two great wounds in the throat; finding these were not enough, he gave himself a third in the belly, and left the nail buried up to the head. The first of his keepers to come in found him like this — still alive, but sunk down and drained by his wounds. To use what time remained before he died, they hurried to read him his sentence. When he heard he was only to be beheaded, he seemed to take new courage, accepted the wine he had refused before, and thanked his judges for the unhoped-for mildness of their sentence. He had resolved to dispatch himself, he said, for fear of some more terrible and unbearable death — the preparations he'd seen had convinced him they meant to torture him with some horrible execution — and he counted himself delivered from death, now that it was changed from the one he had dreaded.

I would advise that these displays of severity, meant to keep the people in line, be practiced on the dead bodies of criminals. To see them denied burial, to see them boiled and cut in quarters, would work on the crowd nearly as much as the pain inflicted on the living — which in effect amounts to little or nothing, as God himself says: they kill the body, and after that there is no more they can do. And the poets dwell with special force on the horror of that picture as something worse than death — alas, cries Cicero's verse, that the half-burnt remains of the king, his bones laid bare, should be dragged foully through the dirt and gore.

I happened to be in Rome one day just as they were executing Catena, a notorious robber. He was strangled without any stir among the spectators; but when they began to cut him in quarters, the hangman could not strike a blow without the people following it with a wailing cry, as if every one of them had lent his own sense of feeling to that miserable carcass. Such inhuman excesses should be spent on the bark, not on the quick. Artaxerxes, in a somewhat similar spirit, softened the old laws of Persia by ordaining that noblemen who had committed a fault, instead of being whipped as they used to be, should be stripped and their clothes whipped in their place; and instead of having their hair torn out, should only have their tall headdress taken off. The Egyptians, devout as they were, thought they satisfied divine justice well enough by sacrificing pigs in image and effigy — a bold invention, to pay God in picture and show for so essential a substance.

I live in a season when unbelievable examples of this vice flourish, through the license of our civil wars. You will find nothing in the ancient histories more extreme than what we witness every day. But it has not in the least reconciled me to it. I could hardly persuade myself, before I saw it with my own eyes, that there existed souls so monstrous that they would commit murder for the sole pleasure of it; that they would hack and chop off other men's limbs; that they would sharpen their wits to invent unheard-of tortures and new kinds of death — without hatred, without profit, with no other end than to enjoy the pleasant spectacle of the gestures and writhings, the pitiful groans and cries, of a man dying in anguish. For this is the farthest point cruelty can reach: that a man should kill a man, not in anger, not in fear, but only to watch, as Seneca says.

For my own part, I cannot without grief see even an innocent animal pursued and killed — a creature with no defense, that has done us no harm. And it often happens that a stag we are hunting, finding himself spent and out of breath, with no escape left, surrenders himself to us, his pursuers, imploring mercy with his tears — bleeding, as Virgil says, and like a creature begging for grace. That has always been a deeply unpleasant sight to me. I hardly ever take an animal alive that I don't turn loose again. Pythagoras used to buy them from fishermen and fowlers to do the same. It was the slaughter of wild beasts, says Ovid, that first warmed man's steel with blood.

Natures that are bloodthirsty toward animals show a native bent toward cruelty. At Rome, once they had accustomed themselves to spectacles of slaughtered animals, they proceeded to slaughtered men — to gladiators. Nature herself, I fear, has stamped in man some instinct toward inhumanity. Nobody takes pleasure in watching animals play with and caress one another; everybody enjoys watching them tear each other to pieces.

And so that no one laughs at this sympathy of mine for animals: theology itself commands us some kindness on their behalf. Considering that one and the same master has lodged us together in this palace for his service, and that they, like us, belong to his household, it has reason to enjoin us some affection and regard for them. Pythagoras borrowed the transmigration of souls from the Egyptians, but it was afterward received by many nations, our Druids among them — souls never die, says Ovid, but leave their old seat and live on, received into new homes. The religion of our ancient Gauls held that souls, being eternal, never stop moving and shifting from one body to another. And they mixed into this notion a thought about divine justice: according to how the soul had behaved while it was in Alexander, they said, God assigned it another body to live in, more or less painful, suited to its condition. He makes them wear the mute chains of brutes, says Claudian — the bloodthirsty he shuts up in bears, robbers in wolves, deceivers in foxes — until, after years and a thousand shapes, purged in Lethe's stream, he restores them at last to human form. If the soul had been valiant, they lodged it in a lion's body; if voluptuous, in a hog's; if cowardly, in a deer's or a hare's; if treacherous, in a fox's — and so on, until, purified by this punishment, it entered the body of some other man. For I myself remember, says Ovid's Pythagoras, that in the days of the Trojan war I was Euphorbus, son of Panthous.

As for that kinship between us and the beasts, I don't put much stock in it — nor in the practice of several nations, among the oldest and noblest, who not only welcomed animals into their society and company but ranked them far above themselves, treating them sometimes as familiars and favorites of their gods, holding them in more than human reverence; others recognized no god and no divinity except them. Beasts were made sacred by the barbarians, says Cicero, for the benefits they conferred. And Juvenal: one region adores the crocodile; another trembles before the ibis, glutted on serpents; here gleams the golden image of the sacred ape; here men venerate a river fish; there whole towns worship a dog.

Even the interpretation Plutarch gives this error — and it is a well-conceived one — does the animals credit. He says it was not the cat or the ox, for instance, that the Egyptians worshipped; rather they worshipped, in those beasts, some image of the divine faculties — in this one, patience and usefulness; in that one, liveliness; or, as with our neighbors the Burgundians and all the Germans, impatience at being shut up — by which they represented liberty, which they loved and adored above every other divine attribute. And so on through the rest. But when, among the more moderate opinions, I meet arguments that try to demonstrate the close resemblance between us and the animals — how large a share they have in our greatest privileges, how plausibly we can be compared — truly, I knock a good deal off our presumption, and I willingly resign that imaginary sovereignty we are supposed to hold over the other creatures.

But suppose all of that were false. There remains, even so, a certain respect, and a general duty of humanity, that binds us not only to animals, which have life and feeling, but even to trees and plants. We owe justice to men, and kindness and gentleness to the other creatures capable of receiving it. There is a certain exchange between them and us, a mutual obligation.

I am not ashamed to confess how childishly tender my nature is on this point: I can hardly refuse my dog when he pesters me to play at the most inconvenient moments. The Turks have alms and hospitals for animals. The Romans made the feeding of geese a public charge, in honor of the vigilance that had saved their Capitol. The Athenians decreed that the mules which had worked on the building of the temple called Hecatompedon should be set free, to graze wherever they pleased, unhindered. The people of Agrigentum made a solemn custom of burying the animals they had loved — horses of rare quality, dogs, useful birds, even those kept only to amuse their children — and the magnificence they showed in everything else appeared particularly in the splendor and number of the monuments raised for this purpose, which stood in their beauty for centuries afterward. The Egyptians buried wolves, bears, crocodiles, dogs, and cats in sacred ground, embalmed their bodies, and wore mourning at their deaths. Cimon gave honorable burial to the mares that had three times won him the prize at the Olympic Games. The elder Xanthippus had his dog buried on a headland by the sea, which has kept its name ever since. And Plutarch says he himself could never quite bring himself, for a small profit, to sell off to the butcher an ox that had served him a long time.

All Things Have Their Season

People who compare Cato the Censor with the younger Cato — the one who killed himself — are comparing two beautiful natures that resemble each other closely. The elder built his reputation in several arenas, and he stands out for his military exploits and his useful public service. But the younger's virtue — and it would be blasphemy to compare anyone to him for sheer vigor — was far more pure and unblemished. For who could clear the Censor of envy and ambition, when he dared to attack the honor of Scipio, a man infinitely beyond him, or anyone else of his time, in goodness and every other excellent quality?

One of the stories they tell about the Censor is that in his extreme old age he threw himself into learning Greek with a greedy appetite, as if quenching a long thirst. To me, that does not say much for his honor. It is, properly speaking, what we call falling into second childhood. All things have their season — even good ones. I can say my Lord's Prayer at the wrong moment: they accused Titus Quintius Flaminius of exactly that, because as general of an army he was seen off praying by himself in the middle of a battle. A battle, mind you, that he won.

The wise man, as Juvenal says, sets a limit even on honest things.

Eudemonidas, watching Xenocrates still bent over his school lectures in old age, asked, "When will this man be wise, if he is still learning?" And when people praised King Ptolemy for drilling himself in arms every day, Philopoemen said, "It is nothing to admire in a king of his age to still be practicing these things. By now he ought to be using them." The young should make their preparations, say the sages, and the old should enjoy them. The greatest vice they find in us is that our desires keep growing young again. We are always beginning to live over.

Our studies and our desires ought to feel their age at some point. Yet here we are, one foot in the grave, and our appetites and projects keep springing up fresh every day. As Horace puts it, you contract for marble to be cut when you are at the edge of the funeral, and build houses forgetful of the tomb.

The longest of my plans does not stretch beyond a year. I think of nothing now but finishing. I am ridding myself of all new hopes and undertakings, taking my last leave of every place I depart from, and letting go, every day, of something I own. As Seneca says, henceforth I will neither lose anything nor gain it — I have more left to pay for the journey than I have journey left. And with Virgil's Dido I can say: I have lived, and finished the course Fortune set before me.

It is, in fact, the only comfort I find in my old age: it kills off in me a number of the cares and desires that have disturbed my life — care about how the world is going, care about riches, grandeur, knowledge, health, myself. There are men learning to speak just when they ought to be learning to be silent forever. A man may always study, but he must not always go to school. What a contemptible thing, an old man at his ABCs!

Different things suit different men, as the poet says; all things are not for all ages.

If we must study, let us study something suited to our present condition, so we can answer like the man who, asked what use study was at his decrepit age, said: "That I may leave better, and more at ease." Such a study was the younger Cato's, when he felt his end approaching and found it in Plato's discourse on the eternity of the soul. Not — let us be clear — that he was not long since furnished with everything needed for such a departure. Of assurance, settled will, and instruction he had more than Plato had in all his writings; his knowledge and his courage were, in this respect, above philosophy. He took up that reading not as a service to his death, but as a man whose sleep was never troubled by a matter of such weight: without choosing it or changing anything, he simply continued his studies along with the other accustomed actions of his life. The night he was denied the praetorship, he spent at play. The night he was to die, he spent reading. Losing his life or losing an office — to him it was all one.

Book III

Of Repentance

Other writers set out to shape mankind. I only describe a man — and a particular one at that, badly enough made, whom I would certainly build quite differently if I had it to do over. But it's done; there's no recalling it.

Now, though the lines of my portrait keep shifting and changing, the likeness holds. The world is one perpetual see-saw. Everything in it moves without rest — the earth, the rocks of the Caucasus, the pyramids of Egypt — both with the general motion and with a motion of their own. Even steadiness is nothing but a slower, more languid swaying. I cannot pin my subject down. He goes staggering and reeling along with a natural drunkenness. I take him as he is at the moment I turn my attention to him. I do not portray being; I portray passing. Not the passage from one age of life to another — or, as people say, from one seven-year stretch to the next — but from day to day, from minute to minute. My history has to be fitted to the hour. I may change presently, not only by accident but by intention. This book is a register of varied and changing events, of ideas that are unsettled and, when it happens so, contradictory — whether because I am then a different self, or because I catch my subjects under different circumstances and from different angles. So yes, I may well contradict myself now and then; but the truth, as Demades said, I never contradict. If my soul could ever get a solid footing, I would stop making attempts and start making decisions. But it is forever in its apprenticeship, forever on trial.

The life I am setting out is an ordinary one, without luster. No matter. You can tie the whole of moral philosophy to a common private life just as well as to one of richer stuff: every man bears the whole form of the human condition.

Authors present themselves to the public by some special, external badge. I am the first ever to do it by my whole being — as Michel de Montaigne, not as a grammarian or a poet or a lawyer. If the world complains that I talk too much about myself, I complain that the world does not so much as think about itself.

But is it reasonable that someone so private in his way of living should presume to make himself publicly known? Is it reasonable to bring before the world — where polish and craftsmanship carry so much credit and authority — the raw, simple products of nature, and a fairly weak nature at that? Isn't writing books without learning and without art like building a wall without stone or brick? The fancies of music are guided by art; mine, by chance.

Still, I have this much going for me, by the rules: no man ever handled a subject he understood better than I understand the one I have taken up — in this field I am the most learned man alive. And second: no man ever went deeper into his material, sifted its parts and connections more finely, or arrived more exactly at the goal he set himself. To finish the job, all I need to bring is fidelity — and it is here, the purest and most sincere fidelity to be found anywhere. I tell the truth, not as much as I would like to, but as much as I dare; and I dare a little more as I grow older, since custom seems to allow old age more freedom to chatter, and more indiscretion in talking about oneself.

What I often see elsewhere cannot happen here — the craftsman and his work contradicting each other. "Can a man whose conversation is so sensible have written such a foolish book?" Or, "How do such learned writings come from a man whose talk is so feeble?" When a man's conversation is ordinary and his writing remarkable, it means his capacity is borrowed, not his own. A learned man is not learned in everything; but a whole man is whole everywhere — even in his ignorance. Here, my book and I walk hand in hand, keeping one pace. In other cases you can praise or blame the work apart from the workman; not here. Whoever touches the one touches the other. Whoever judges the book without knowing the man wrongs himself more than me; whoever does know him gives me all the satisfaction I want. I will be lucky beyond what I deserve if I win just this much public agreement: that intelligent people see I was capable of profiting from learning, if I had had any — and that I deserved a better memory.

Allow me here to repeat what I have said often: that I rarely repent, and that my conscience is content with itself — not the conscience of an angel, not the conscience of a horse, but the conscience of a man. And I always add this clause — not as a formality but as real and honest submission — that I speak as one inquiring and doubting, and refer myself, purely and simply, to the common and accepted beliefs for the final answer. I do not teach; I relate.

There is no vice that is truly a vice which doesn't offend, and which a sound judgment doesn't condemn. Its ugliness and wrongness are so obvious that perhaps the people are right who say vice is mainly bred from stupidity and ignorance — it is that hard to imagine a man knowing it without hating it. Malice sucks up most of its own venom and poisons itself with it. Vice leaves repentance in the soul the way an ulcer sits in the flesh, forever scratching and tearing at itself. Reason wipes out other griefs and sorrows, but it breeds the grief of repentance — which is all the harder to bear because it springs from within, just as the chills and fevers that rise inside us bite sharper than the ones that strike only the skin. I count as vices (each in its own measure) not only the ones that reason and nature condemn, but also the ones that human opinion has made vices — even false and mistaken opinion — once law and custom have given it authority.

Likewise there is no virtue that doesn't gladden a well-born nature. There is a kind of — I don't know what — congratulation in doing well, an inward satisfaction, and a generous boldness that keeps company with a good conscience. A boldly vicious soul may perhaps armor itself with confidence, but it cannot supply itself with this contentment. It is no small satisfaction to feel yourself preserved from the contagion of so rotten an age, and to say to yourself: "Anyone who could see straight into my soul would not find me guilty of anyone's affliction or ruin, or of revenge or envy, or of any public offense against the laws, or of stirring up trouble, or of breaking my word. And though the license of these times permits and even teaches everyone to do it, I have not laid hands on any Frenchman's goods or money; I have lived on my own purse, in war as in peace; and I have never used a man's labor without paying him his wage." These testimonies of conscience are a pleasure; and this natural rejoicing is a great benefit to us — the only payment that can never fail us.

To rest the reward of virtuous deeds on other people's approval is to build on far too uncertain a foundation — especially in an age as corrupt and ignorant as this one, when the good opinion of the crowd is an insult. Whom would you trust to show you what deserves praise? God keep me from being a good man by the descriptions I see people giving every day of themselves in their own honor. As Seneca says, what used to be vices are now customs.

Some friends of mine have at times taken it on themselves to school me and scold me with great frankness — sometimes of their own accord, sometimes at my invitation, as a service which, to a well-made soul, surpasses all the other offices of friendship, not just in usefulness but in kindness. I have always welcomed it with the wide-open arms of courtesy and gratitude. But to speak honestly between us, I have often found so much false measure in their blame and in their praise alike that I would hardly have gone wrong to do wrong, rather than do right by their notions.

Those of us who live private lives, on display to no one but ourselves, need above all to have a standard set up within, by which to test our actions — and, by that standard, sometimes to pat ourselves on the back and sometimes to correct ourselves. I have my own laws and my own court to judge myself, and I appeal to them more than anywhere else. I do restrain my actions out of regard for others; but I extend them only by my own rule. You alone know whether you are cowardly and cruel, or loyal and devout. Others never see you; they only guess at you by uncertain conjectures. They see your art, not your nature. So do not cling to their verdict; cling to your own. As Cicero says, it is your own judgment you must use on yourself; the weight of your own conscience in the discovery of your virtues and vices is immense — take that away, and everything collapses.

But the saying that repentance follows hard on the heels of sin doesn't seem to apply to sin in its full dress, the kind that lodges in us as in its own house. We can disown and retract the vices that take us by surprise, the ones passion sweeps us into. But the vices that long habit has rooted in a strong and deliberate will are not open to being contradicted. Repentance is nothing but a recanting of our will, a turning against our own fancies — and our fancies drive us whichever way they please. Look at this man, whom repentance makes disown his former virtue and self-control; as Horace has it, why didn't I have the mind I have today when I was a boy — or why, now that I have it, can't I have my smooth cheeks back?

It is a rare life that keeps its order even in private. Anyone can play his part in the puppet show and act the honest man on the boards. The real point is to be ruled inside, in your own chest, where everything is permitted to you and everything is hidden. The next step down is to be orderly at home — in your own house, in your own chimney corner, in your everyday doings, for which you answer to no one, and where there is no rehearsal and no act. That is why Bias, describing the excellent state of a household, said: the master must be the same man within it, of his own free virtue and temper, that he is outside it for fear of the law and what people will say. And it was a fine answer Julius Drusus gave the builders who offered, for three thousand crowns, to remodel his house so that his neighbors could no longer see into it as they did: "I will give you six thousand," he said, "to make it so everyone can see into every room." It is recorded to the honor of Agesilaus that on his journeys he always took lodging in temples, so that the people — and the gods themselves — could look in on his most private doings. Many a man has been a miracle to the world in whom his wife and his valet never saw anything even remarkable. Few men have been admired by their own households. No one was ever a prophet, not merely in his own house but in his own country — so says the experience of history. It is the same with trifles; and in the following small example you can see the image of the great ones. In my home country of Gascony they think it's comic to see me in print. The farther from my own doorstep my readers are, the higher my stock. In Guienne I pay the printers; elsewhere they pay me. That is the hope men bank on who hide themselves while alive and present, to get themselves a name when they are dead and gone. I would rather have less of a name and take none but what is owed me while I'm here to collect it. When I leave the world, I waive the rest.

Watch this official, whom the people escort in pomp, with wonder and applause, right to his door: he sheds the pageant with his robe, and falls as low as he was hoisted high. Inside himself, all is tumult and squalor. And even if there were order in there, it would take a quick and finely chosen judgment to detect it in those low, private motions. Add to that the fact that order is a dull and somber virtue. Storming a breach, conducting an embassy, governing a nation — these are glittering deeds. Scolding, laughing, selling, paying, loving, hating; dealing gently and justly with your own household and with yourself; not slacking off, not giving yourself the lie — that is rarer, harder, and less noticed. So whatever people say, retired lives carry duties as stiff and strenuous as other lives do, or stiffer. Private men, Aristotle says, serve virtue at a harder and higher rate than men in office, because we gear ourselves up for the grand occasions more out of glory than out of conscience. The shortest road to glory would be to do for conscience what we now do for glory. Alexander's virtue, on his enormous stage, looks to me a good deal less vigorous than the virtue of Socrates in his lowly, obscure routine. I can easily picture Socrates in Alexander's place; Alexander in Socrates' place, I cannot. Ask the one what he can do, and he answers, "Subdue the world." Ask the other, and he answers, "Live a human life in keeping with its natural condition" — a far more general, weighty, and legitimate science.

The worth of a soul lies not in soaring high but in walking in order. Its greatness is exercised not in greatness but in ordinary things. Just as those who judge us from inside set little store by the shine of our public actions — seeing that these are only threads and jets of clear water spurting from a thick and muddy bottom — so those who judge us by the brave outward show conclude that the inside matches, and cannot believe that faculties as common as their own could live in the same skin as these other faculties that astonish them, so far out of their range. That is why we give demons such monstrous shapes. Who doesn't picture Tamerlane with huge eyebrows, flaring nostrils, a terrible face, and a giant's frame — the size of the image his name has stamped on our imagination? If someone had brought me, back in the day, to meet Erasmus, I could hardly have believed that anything he said to his servant or his landlady was not all adages and maxims. We find it much easier to imagine a craftsman on the toilet, or on his wife, than a great chief justice — so venerable in his bearing and his learning. It seems to us that men like that never stoop from their lofty benches so far as to actually live. And just as vicious souls are sometimes spurred to do good by some outside push, so virtuous souls are spurred to do wrong. They must be judged, then, by their settled state, when they are at home with themselves — if they ever are — or at least when they are nearest to rest and to their native position.

Natural inclinations get help and reinforcement from education, but they are hardly ever changed or overpowered by it. A thousand natures in my own day have slipped off toward virtue or toward vice straight through schooling that pushed the opposite way. As Lucan says, wild beasts caged and grown unused to the woods turn tame, put off their fierce looks, and submit to the rule of man; but let one small taste of blood reach their mouths, and their rage and fury come back — their throats swell at the remembered savor, and their anger barely spares the trembling keeper. You do not root out these original qualities; you cover them up, you hide them. Latin is like a native tongue to me; I understand it better than French. But I haven't spoken it, and have hardly written it, for forty years. Yet in the sudden, extreme emotions I have fallen into two or three times in my life — once when I saw my father, in perfect health, collapse on top of me in a faint — the first words that burst up from the bottom of me have always been Latin: nature surging out and forcing her own expression, against forty years of disuse. And the same thing is told of plenty of others.

Those who in my time have tried to correct the world's morals with new opinions reform the surface vices; the essential ones they leave where they were — when they don't actually swell them. And swelling is the danger: we put off all other good conduct on the strength of these external, cut-rate, showy reforms, and through them we pay off cheaply for the natural vices, the ones bred in the bone, the ones inside. Look a little at your own experience. There is no man who, if he listens to himself, does not discover in himself a form all his own, a ruling form, which fights against his education and against the storm of passions that oppose it. For my part, I rarely feel myself jolted by shocks; I am nearly always found in my place, like a heavy, lumbering body. If I am not at home, I am always close by. My excesses do not carry me very far. There is nothing extreme or strange about the case. And still I have my sound and vigorous moments of turning back.

The true condemnation, the one that hits the common practice of mankind, is this: that their very retirement is full of filth and corruption; that their idea of reform is smudged; that their repentance is nearly as sick and lame as their sin. Some are so glued to vice by natural bent or long habit that they no longer see its ugliness. Others — and I am of this tribe — do feel the weight of vice, but they balance it against the pleasure or some other consideration, and they put up with it and rent themselves out to it at a certain price — viciously, though, and basely. Still, you might imagine a disproportion so extreme that the pleasure could justly excuse the sin, as we say usefulness sometimes does — not only when the pleasure is incidental, outside the sin itself, as in theft, but when it lies in the very exercise of the sin, as with women, where the urge is violent and, they say, sometimes beyond overcoming.

The other day, at Armagnac, on the estate of a kinsman of mine, I met a peasant whom everyone calls the Thief. He gave this account of his life: born a beggar, and concluding that earning his bread by the sweat of his hands would never get him clear of want, he decided to turn thief, and being a strong young man, he plied the trade through his whole youth in perfect safety — for he harvested and gleaned in other men's fields, but far from home, and in such quantities that nobody could imagine one man carrying off so much on his shoulders in a night. And he took care, besides, to spread the damage around evenly, so the loss fell lighter on each individual. He is now old, and rich for a man of his station — thanks to his trade, which he admits to anyone openly. To square his accounts with God, he says he is making daily restitution, through good turns, to the heirs of the people he robbed; and if he doesn't finish (for he can't do it all at once), he will leave the duty to his own heirs, in proportion to the wrong that only he knows he did to each. By this account, true or false, the man regards theft as a dishonest act and hates it — but he hates it less than poverty. He repents of it in itself, simply enough; but insofar as it was counterbalanced and compensated this way, he does not repent. This is not the habit that fuses us into vice and bends our very understanding to its shape; nor is it that whirlwind of passion that swamps and blinds the soul in gusts and hurls us for the moment, judgment and all, into the power of sin.

What I do, I customarily do completely, in one piece and one stride. I rarely have an impulse that hides itself and sneaks past my reason; nearly every move I make is made with the consent of all my faculties, without split or inward mutiny. My judgment gets all the blame or all the praise for it — and the blame it gets once, it keeps forever, for almost since infancy it has been one and the same judgment: the same bent, the same track, the same force. And in the matter of general convictions, I settled in childhood into the spot where I meant to stand.

Some sins are violent, quick, sudden; set those aside. But these other sins, repeated so many times, deliberated, planned — sins of temperament, or sins of profession and trade — I cannot conceive how they could sit so long in one and the same resolve unless the reason and conscience of the man who keeps them is constant in willing them. And the repentance he claims comes over him at some appointed instant — that I find very hard to imagine or believe. I do not follow the Pythagorean idea that men take on a new soul when they approach the images of the gods to receive their oracles — unless the sect meant that the soul must indeed be a foreign one, new, loaned for the occasion, since our own shows so little sign of the purity and cleanliness the office calls for.

These men do exactly the opposite of the Stoic teaching, which tells us, yes, to correct the imperfections and vices we find in ourselves, but never to let them disturb the peace of our souls. These men would have us believe they carry great grief and remorse inside; but of amendment, of correction, of stopping, they show us nothing. Yet there is no cure if the disease is not thrown off. If repentance sat in one pan of the scales, it would outweigh the sin. I know no quality so easy to counterfeit as piety, when a man's life and morals are not made to match it: its essence is hidden and secret; its appearances are easy and showy.

For my own part, I may wish in a general way to be different than I am; I may condemn my whole form and be displeased with it, and beg God to remake me entirely and to pardon my natural weakness. But this I ought not to call repentance, it seems to me — no more than my displeasure at not being an angel or Cato. My actions are in order; they match what I am and match my condition. I can do no better. And repentance does not properly reach the things that are not in our power — regret reaches those. I can imagine numberless natures loftier and better ordered than mine; for all that, I do not improve my own faculties, any more than my arm or my mind grows stronger because I conceive of someone else's being stronger. If imagining and wishing for a nobler way of acting than ours bred repentance for our own, we would have to repent of our most innocent works — since we may rightly suppose that in a more excellent nature they would have been done with greater dignity and perfection, and we would wish ours had been. When I compare the conduct of my youth with the conduct of my old age, I find I have generally kept order in both, by my own lights; that is all my powers of resistance can do. I do not flatter myself: in the same circumstances, I would do the same things again. It is not a spot on me; it is a dye that stains me through and through. I know no repentance that is superficial, halfway, and ceremonial. It must sting me on every side before I will call it by that name; it must grip and wring my bowels as deeply and as universally as God sees into me.

As for business — many fine opportunities have slipped through my fingers for lack of good management. And yet my deliberations were sound enough, given what was in front of me at the time; their method is always to take the easiest and safest course. I find that in my old resolutions I proceeded sensibly, by my own rule, according to the state of the matter proposed; and in the same situation I would do the same a thousand years from now. I don't ask what it looks like now, but what it looked like then, when I was weighing it. The force of any counsel lies in its timing; occasions and circumstances roll and change without end. I have made some heavy mistakes in my life — important ones — not for lack of good judgment but for lack of good luck. There are secret parts in the matters we handle, parts no one can guess — especially in human nature: mute conditions that never show themselves, unknown sometimes even to their owner, which are sprung and startled into life by occasions. If my prudence could not see into them or predict them, I hold no grudge against it; its commission only runs so far. If the outcome beats me — if it favors the side I rejected — there's no help for that. I don't blame myself; I accuse my fortune, not my work. And that is not to be called repentance.

Phocion once gave the Athenians a piece of advice that was not followed; and when the affair went off successfully against his prediction, someone said to him, "Well, Phocion, are you glad things are going so well?" "Very glad," he said, "that this has happened — but I do not repent of advising the other." When friends come to me for advice, I give it freely and clearly, without stalling — the way almost everyone else does — over the chance that the thing may turn out against my opinion and leave me blamed for the counsel. That worries me not at all. They will be in the wrong for having consulted me; I could not decently refuse them the service.

I, for my part, can rarely blame anyone but myself for my blunders and misfortunes, because in practice I rarely ask anyone else's advice — only as a courtesy of form, or where I need information, technical knowledge, or the facts of the matter. In things where I have nothing to supply but judgment, other people's reasons may back mine up, but they seldom turn me around. I listen to them all graciously and patiently; but as far as I can remember, I have never trusted any but my own. To my mind they are only flies and motes that flit about my will. I set little store by my own opinions — but I set just as little by other people's, and fortune pays me out accordingly: I take little advice, and I am asked for little. I am rarely consulted, and even more rarely believed; I know of no undertaking, public or private, that my advice ever restored or improved. Even the people fortune had tied, more or less, to my management have let themselves be run by anyone's counsel sooner than mine. And being a man every bit as jealous of his peace as of his authority, I prefer it that way. By leaving me alone they treat me according to my professed creed, which is to be settled and contained entirely within myself. To me it is a pleasure to be uninvolved in other men's affairs and clear of standing surety for what they do.

About all things past, however they fell out, I have little regret. This thought spares me the pain of it: that's how they were bound to fall out; they sit now in the great revolution of the universe, in the chain of Stoical causes. Your fancy cannot, by wish or imagination, shift one jot of it without overturning the whole order of things, and the past, and the future.

For the rest, I hate that accidental repentance that old age brings in its baggage. The ancient who said he was obliged to his years for weaning him off pleasure held a different opinion from mine. I can never think myself beholden to impotence for any good it may do me. Nor, as Quintilian says, will Providence ever be so hostile to her own work that feebleness should be ranked among the best things. Our appetites are scarce in old age; a deep satiety settles on us after the act. In that I see nothing of conscience. Sourness and weakness stamp us with a drowsy, crabbed, rheumatic virtue. We must not let natural decay carry us so far that it corrupts our judgment along the way. Youth and pleasure never so mastered me, back then, that I failed to see the face of vice in the pleasure; and the distaste the years have brought me does not so master me now that I cannot see the pleasure in the vice. Now that I am no longer in my prime, I judge these things as if I were. As the old poet says, old as I am, unfit now for ladies' love, I still remember what beauty can do.

I, who examine my reason narrowly and strictly, find it the very same it was in my most licentious years — except perhaps weaker and dimmer for having grown old. And I find that whatever pleasure it now refuses me out of regard for my bodily health, it would refuse me no more than it ever did out of regard for the health of my soul. I do not rate it the more valiant for being out of combat. My temptations are so broken and crippled that they are not worth its opposing: I push them off with outstretched hands, nothing more. But set the old concupiscence in front of my reason, and I fear it would have less strength to resist than it once had. I cannot see that, of itself, it judges anything differently now than it did then, or that it has come into any new light. So if there is a convalescence here, it is a bewitched one. A wretched sort of cure, to owe your health to your disease!

It is not for our misfortune to perform that office; it is for the good fortune of our judgment. Blows and afflictions get nothing out of me but curses. They are for people who can't be wakened without a whip. My reason runs far more freely in prosperity. It is much more distracted and put upon digesting pains than digesting pleasures. I see best in clear weather. Health counsels me more cheerfully, and to better effect, than sickness does. I did all that lay in me to reform and regulate myself in the matter of pleasures while I still had the health and vigor to enjoy them. I would be ashamed and resentful if the misery and bad luck of my decrepitude were to be rated above my good, healthy, lively, vigorous years — if men were to take my measure not by what I have been, but by what I have ceased to be.

In my opinion it is living happily, and not — as Antisthenes said — dying happily, that makes human felicity. I have made no effort to fix a philosopher's tail onto the head and body of a dissolute man; I will not have this wretched remainder of me give the lie to the pleasant, sound, and long part of my life. I want to present myself uniform throughout. Were I to live my life over again, I would live it just as I have lived it. I neither complain of the past nor fear the future; and unless I am badly fooled, things have gone inside me about as they have gone outside. One of my chief debts to my fortune is that my bodily life has run its course with each thing in its natural season. I have seen the grass, the flower, and the fruit; now I see the withering — happily, because naturally. I bear my present infirmities the more gently because they came on schedule, and because they make me remember all the more kindly the long happiness of my past life. My wisdom may have been the same size in both seasons; but it performed better, and with better grace, when it was green and gay than now that it is broken down, peevish, and labored.

So I renounce these accidental, painful reformations. God must touch our hearts. Our consciences must amend of themselves, by the strengthening of our reason — not by the failing of our appetites. Pleasure is not, in itself, something pale and colorless, fit to be made out only by dim, bleary eyes.

We ought to love temperance for its own sake, and out of reverence for God, who has commanded it, and chastity too. But what catarrhs reduce us to — what I owe to the favor of my kidney stones — is neither chastity nor temperance. A man cannot brag of despising and resisting pleasure if he cannot see it, if he doesn't know what it is — its graces, its powers, its most winning beauties. I know both sides; I have the right to say it. But it seems to me that in old age our souls suffer more troublesome ailments and defects than in youth. I said so when I was young and got laughed at for my beardless chin; and I say so now that my grey hair gives me some standing. We call our peevish tempers, and our distaste for whatever is in front of us, wisdom. But the truth is we don't so much abandon our vices as trade them — and, in my opinion, for worse ones. Besides a silly, threadbare pride, a tedious itch to talk, prickly and unsociable moods, superstition, and a ridiculous appetite for riches just when we have lost the use of them, I find in old age more envy, more injustice, more malice. It plants more wrinkles in our minds than on our faces; and you will seldom or never see a soul that doesn't, in aging, come to smell sour and musty. The whole man moves together, toward his ripening and toward his rot.

Looking at the wisdom of Socrates, and several details of his condemnation, I would venture to believe he lent himself to it on purpose, in a way, by playing along — at seventy, with the prospect just ahead of feeling the high motions of his mind cramped and his accustomed brightness dimmed.

What strange transformations I watch old age work, every day, in so many of my acquaintances! It is a powerful disease, and it creeps in naturally and invisibly. It takes a vast stock of study, and great precaution, to dodge the defects it loads onto us — or at least to slow their advance. I can feel that, for all my fortifications, it gains on me foot by foot. I hold out as well as I can. But where it will finally bring me, I do not know. Come what may, I am content that the world should know, when I have fallen, from what height I fell.

Of Three Kinds of Company

We should not bolt ourselves so tightly to our own moods and temperaments. The best skill we have is knowing how to apply ourselves to many different things. To keep yourself tied and chained to one single course is to exist, not to live. The finest souls are the ones with the most variety and flexibility in them. There is an admiring report of the elder Cato along these lines — as Livy says, his mind was so adaptable to everything that whatever he happened to be doing, you would swear he had been born to do exactly that.

If I were free to shape myself by my own design, there is no way of being — however graceful — that I would want to be fixed in so firmly that I could not break loose from it. Life is an uneven, irregular, many-shaped motion. To be led around by the nose by yourself, endlessly, so stuck in your own grooves that you cannot turn aside or twist your neck out of the collar — that is not being a friend to yourself, much less a master. It is being a slave.

I say this now, at this point in my life, because I find I cannot easily shake off the nagging of my own soul. It usually cannot entertain itself except with narrow things, and it cannot work except entirely and with all its strength. Give it the lightest subject and it will swell and stretch the thing to the point where its full power is engaged. For that reason, idleness is hard labor for me, and bad for my health. Most minds need outside material to wake them up and get them moving; mine needs it rather to settle down and rest — because, as Seneca says, the vices of leisure have to be shaken off by being busy. The chief and hardest study of my mind is studying itself. Books, for it, are the kind of work that distracts it from that study. The first thoughts that strike it set it bustling, testing its strength in every direction — sometimes flexing its force, sometimes steadying, moderating, and putting itself in order out of a feeling for grace and proportion. It has plenty of its own to work on. Nature has given it, as she gives everyone, enough material of its own to use, and subjects enough for inventing and judging.

Meditation is a powerful and rich study for anyone who can really taste it and apply himself to it. I would rather shape my soul than furnish it. There is no occupation either weaker or stronger — depending on the soul in question — than entertaining your own thoughts. The greatest men make it their whole business: those, as Cicero says, for whom to live is to think. Nature has favored this activity with a special privilege: there is nothing we can do for so long, and no activity we give ourselves to more often or more easily. It is the business of the gods, says Aristotle, and the source of their happiness and of ours.

The main use of reading, for me, is that the variety of its subjects wakes up my reason and exercises my judgment — not my memory. Few conversations can hold me without force and effort. It is true that beauty and elegance of speech win me over as much as the weight and depth of the subject, or more. And since I tend to drift off in every other kind of talk, lending only the rind of my attention, it often happens that in flat, pitiful exchanges — mere chatter — I either give drowsy, meaningless answers, unworthy of a child and ridiculous, or, more foolishly and rudely still, hold an obstinate silence. I have a brooding streak that pulls me into myself, and alongside it a heavy, childish ignorance of many perfectly ordinary things. Between those two qualities, I have earned this much: a man could truthfully tell five or six stories about me as ridiculous as any told about anyone.

But back to my point. This difficult temperament of mine makes me picky about the company I keep — I have to cull and select men for my purpose — and it makes me unfit for ordinary society. We live and do business with people. If their conversation annoys us, if we refuse to lower ourselves to plain and common souls — and the plain and common are often as sound as the finest-spun, and all wisdom is folly that does not adapt itself to the common ignorance — then we must stop meddling in other people's affairs and in our own, because business, public and private alike, gets done with exactly these people. The least forced and most natural motions of the soul are the most beautiful; the best occupations are the least strained. My God, what a good turn wisdom does for those whose desires it cuts to fit their power! There is no more useful knowledge. "According to what a man can" was the favorite saying and motto of Socrates — a motto of great solidity.

We must steer our desires toward the things nearest and easiest to get. Isn't it a foolish humor of mine to cut myself off from a thousand people my fortune has joined me to, people I cannot live without, in order to cling to one or two who are out of my reach — or rather, to a fantastical longing for something I cannot have? My gentle, easy manners, the enemies of all sourness and harshness, have probably kept me clear of envy and hostility; no man ever gave less occasion for being hated — I do not say for being loved. But the coldness of my conversation has, reasonably enough, cost me the goodwill of many people, who can be excused for reading it another and worse way.

I am very capable of forming and keeping rare and exquisite friendships. Since I seize so hungrily on the acquaintances that suit my taste, I throw myself at them with such force that I rarely fail to stick, and to make an impression where I hit — as I have happily proved more than once. In ordinary friendships I am somewhat cold and shy, for I do not move naturally except under full sail. Besides, having been given a taste, in my youth, of one single and perfect friendship, fortune has honestly left me with a kind of distaste for the others, and stamped too deep in my imagination the old saying that man is a companionable animal, but not a herd animal. And I have a natural difficulty about sharing myself by halves, with the hedging and the servile, wary caution required for keeping up many imperfect friendships — and that caution is demanded of us especially in this age, when you cannot talk about the world except dangerously or falsely.

Yet I see perfectly well that a man whose aim is the comforts of life — the essential comforts, I mean — as mine is, should run from these refinements and delicacies of temperament like the plague. What I would praise is a soul with several floors to it: one that knows how to stretch and to slacken; that is at ease wherever fortune carries it; that can talk with a neighbor about his building, his hunting, his lawsuits; that can chat with a carpenter or a gardener with pleasure. I envy those who can get familiar with the humblest of their own staff and make conversation in their manner. And I dislike Plato's advice that we should always speak to our servants, men or women, in the tone of a master, never playful, never familiar. For besides the reasons I have given, it is inhuman and unjust to set such a price on this pitiful accident of fortune; the societies that allow the least distance between masters and servants seem to me the fairest.

Other men study how to raise and elevate their minds; I study how to lower mine and bring it down. It is only faulty when it stretches. As Horace says, you tell us long tales of the line of Aeacus and the battles fought under sacred Troy — but what a cask of good wine costs, who will heat the bath, whose house will shelter me from the cold and when, about that you say nothing.

The Spartans' courage needed moderating — needed the sweet, harmonious sound of flutes to soften it in battle, so they would not throw themselves into recklessness and fury — while every other nation uses harsh, shrill sounds and loud fierce cries to whip its soldiers' courage to the limit. In the same way, it seems to me, and against the usual view, our minds mostly need lead rather than wings — coolness and calm rather than heat and agitation. Above all, in my opinion, it is playing the fool magnificently to put on the grave airs of a lofty intellect among people who are nothing of the kind, and to speak forever by the book — "talking with the point of a fork," as the Italians say of affected speech. You must come down to the level of the people you are with, and sometimes even affect ignorance. Set aside force and subtlety in everyday conversation; keeping things decent and orderly is enough. Crawl along the ground, if that is what they want.

The learned trip over this stone constantly. They are forever parading their pedantic learning and strewing their books over everything. These days they have so stuffed the boudoirs and the ears of the ladies with it that even if the ladies have missed the substance, they have at least kept the words — so that in every discussion, on any subject however plain and ordinary, they speak and write in a new and learned style. As Juvenal says, in this language they pour out their fears, their anger, their joys, their cares, and every secret of their souls — what more? they even make love learnedly. They quote Plato and Aquinas on matters the first passerby could settle just as well. The learning that cannot reach their souls stays sitting on the tongue.

If women of quality will take my advice, they will content themselves with displaying their own natural treasures. They hide and bury their beauties under beauties that are not theirs. It is great folly to snuff out your own light so as to shine by a borrowed one — to be buried, as Seneca says, under art, painted and packaged from head to foot. The reason is that they do not know themselves well enough, or do themselves justice. The world has nothing fairer than they are; it is for them to do honor to the arts, and to put paint itself to shame. What do they need beyond living loved and honored? They have, and know, more than enough for that. All they need do is rouse and warm a little the faculties that are already their own.

When I see them dabbling in rhetoric, law, logic, and similar drugs — so useless and unnecessary for their purposes — I begin to suspect that the men who push these fancies on them do it in order to govern them by that route. What other excuse can I find? It is enough that without any instruction from us they can tune the grace of their eyes to gaiety, severity, or sweetness; season a refusal with sharpness, suspense, or favor; and need no interpreter for the speeches we make in courting them. With that knowledge they hold the whip hand and rule the schoolmasters and the schools.

But if it nevertheless galls them to yield to us in anything at all, and they want their share of books out of curiosity, then poetry is the entertainment suited to them: a wanton, subtle, sly, chattering art, all pleasure and all show, like themselves. They can also draw various profits from history. And in philosophy — the moral part of it — they can pick out the lessons that will teach them to judge our humors and our characters, to defend themselves against our treacheries, to manage the heat of their own desires, to husband their freedom, to stretch out the pleasures of life, and to bear with grace the inconstancy of a lover, the rudeness of a husband, and the nagging of years and wrinkles. That is the most I would allow them in the sciences.

Some particular natures are private and withdrawn. My own native manner is made for communication and apt to put me on display; I am all outside and in plain view, born for company and friendship. The solitude I love and recommend is mainly nothing more than drawing my thoughts and affections back into myself — restraining and reining in not my steps but my cares and desires, shedding all outside worry, and avoiding like death all servitude and obligation: fleeing not so much the crowd of men as the crowd of business. Solitude of place, to tell the truth, rather gives me more room and sets me at large. I throw myself into affairs of state and into the world more readily when I am alone. At the Louvre, in the bustle of the court, I fold up inside my own skin; the crowd presses me back into myself. I am never so wanton, so free, so much my own man in my musings as in places of dignity and ceremonious caution. Our follies do not make me laugh — our wisdom does.

I am no natural enemy of court life; I have spent part of my own life there, and my temperament takes cheerfully to great company — provided it is in doses, and at times of my choosing. But this softness of judgment I have been describing chains me, like it or not, to solitude. Even at home, in a numerous household, in a house that sees plenty of visitors, I see people enough, but rarely the people I delight in talking with. And I reserve there, for myself and for everyone else, an unusual liberty. My house has no ceremony — no ushering, no escorting guests out to their coach, none of the other tiresome rituals our code of courtesy commands (oh, that servile, nagging custom!). Everyone there runs himself by his own method. Whoever wants to may speak his thoughts; I sit silent, musing, shut up in my study, and no guest takes offense.

The men whose society and familiarity I seek out are those we call sincere and able men. The image of these men puts me off all the rest. Theirs is, rightly considered, the rarest of our forms — and a form owed chiefly to nature. The end of this kind of company is simply intimacy, frequenting one another, and conversation: the exercise of souls, with no other profit. In our talk any subject will do for me; I do not care if it has neither weight nor depth — charm and aptness are always there. Everything in it is colored by ripe, steady judgment, and mixed with goodness, openness, gaiety, and friendship. It is not only on questions of kings and state that our minds show their force and beauty; they show it every bit as much in private talk. I know my men even by their silences and their smiles, and discover them better, perhaps, at the table than in the council chamber. Hippomachus said very well that he could spot the good wrestlers just by watching them walk down the street. If learning cares to join our conversation, it will not be turned away — provided it is not magisterial, imperious, and tiresome, as it usually is, but a junior partner, and teachable itself. We are only after passing the time; when we want to be instructed and preached at, we will go find learning on its throne. Let it lower itself to us for the occasion, please; for useful and profitable as it is, I imagine that at a pinch we could manage perfectly well without it and do our business unassisted. A well-born soul, practiced in the company of men, makes herself agreeable on her own. Art is nothing but the catalogue and record of what such souls produce.

The company of beautiful and honorable women is, for me, another sweet kind of association — for, as Cicero says, we too have educated eyes. If the soul has less to enjoy here than in the first kind, the bodily senses, which have a larger share in this one, bring it up to a level close to the other — though not, in my opinion, equal to it. But it is a commerce in which a man must keep a little on his guard, especially a man in whom the body has great power, as in me. I scalded myself there in my youth, and suffered all the torments the poets say fall on men who throw themselves into love without order or judgment. It is true that the whipping has made me wiser since — like Ovid's sailor, whoever escaped the wreck on the Capharean rocks steers wide of those waters forever after.

It is folly to fix all your thoughts on it and to plunge in with furious, reckless passion. But on the other hand, to go into it without love and without desire, like actors, playing a stock part, putting in nothing of your own but the lines — that is to look after your safety, true, but in as cowardly a way as a man who would abandon his honor, his profit, or his pleasure out of fear of danger. For it is certain that the men who set up such an arrangement can expect no fruit from it that could please or satisfy a noble soul. A man must have honestly desired what he honestly expects to enjoy. I say this even when fortune unjustly favors the play-acting — which happens often, because there is not one woman in the world, be she as ugly as the devil, who does not think herself well worth loving, and who does not prefer herself to other women on the strength of her youth, the color of her hair, or her graceful walk. There are no more universally ugly women than universally beautiful ones; the Brahmin girls who have nothing else to recommend them, when the town crier has called the people together for the purpose, go down to the marketplace and display their matrimonial parts to the public, to see whether those, at least, are temptation enough to fetch them a husband. So there is not one woman who will not readily let herself be won by the first vow of devotion a man makes her.

Now from this common, everyday treachery of the men of our time, the result we already see in practice was bound to follow: either the women band together and withdraw among themselves to escape us, or else they take their schooling from the example we set, play their side of the farce as we play ours, and enter the game without passion, care, or love — answerable, as Tacitus says, neither to their own affections nor to anyone else's — believing, with Lysias in Plato, that they can give themselves to us all the more usefully and conveniently the less we love them. And so it goes as in the theater: the audience gets as much pleasure as the actors, or more.

For my part, I no more recognize a Venus without a Cupid than a mother without children: they are things that lend and owe their being to each other. So the cheat bounces back on the cheater. The game costs him next to nothing, but he gets next to nothing out of it. The people who made Venus a goddess noticed that her principal beauty was bodiless and spiritual; but the Venus these men chase is not even human — not even animal. The beasts themselves would not take it so gross and earthbound. We see that imagination and desire heat them before the body does; we see that in both sexes there is choosing and preferring within the herd, and long affectionate attachments among them. Even those whom old age has cut off from the act still tremble, whinny, and twitter with love. We see them before the act full of hope and ardor, and after the body has played its game, still savoring the sweet memory of the pleasure — some strutting with pride afterward, others, tired and satisfied, trumpeting their triumph aloud. An animal that merely needs to relieve the body of a natural necessity does not trouble others with such elaborate preparations. This is no food for a gross, coarse appetite.

Not wanting to be thought better than I am, I will say this here about the sins of my youth. Partly for the danger to health (though I was not careful enough to escape two light mishaps), and even more out of contempt, I rarely went in for paid and mercenary embraces. I wanted the pleasure sharpened by difficulty, by desire, and by a certain kind of glory. I was of the emperor Tiberius's mind, who in his loves was taken as much by modesty and good birth as by any other quality, and of the courtesan Flora's humor, who never lent herself to anyone below a dictator, a consul, or a censor, and relished the rank of her lovers. No doubt pearls and brocade, titles and retinue, add something to it.

Beyond that, I set a high price on intelligence — provided the body passed muster. For to confess the truth, if one or the other of these two attractions had to be missing, I would sooner have given up the intelligence: it has its uses in better things. But in the matter of love — a matter that mainly concerns the senses of sight and touch — something can be done without the graces of the mind; without the graces of the body, nothing. Beauty is the true prerogative of women, so peculiarly their own that ours, though it calls for somewhat different features, is only at its peak when youthful and beardless — a blurred copy of theirs. They say that those who serve the Grand Turk on the strength of their beauty, and they are an infinite number, are dismissed at twenty-two at the latest. Reason, prudence, and the offices of friendship are better found among men; that is why men run the affairs of the world.

These two kinds of company are matters of luck, and depend on other people. The first is troublesome because it is rare; the second withers with age. So they could never have been enough for the business of my life. The company of books, which is the third, is far more certain and far more our own. It concedes every other advantage to the first two, but it has for its own share the constancy and the ease of its service. It walks beside me along my whole road and helps me everywhere. It comforts me in old age and in solitude. It relieves me of the weight of a tedious idleness, and frees me, at any hour, from company I dislike. It blunts the edge of grief, as long as the grief is not extreme and has not taken full possession of my soul. To shake off a troublesome thought, I have only to run to my books: they fix me at once and steal the other thing out of my mind. And they do not mutiny at seeing that I only come to them for lack of other amusements, more real, natural, and alive; they always receive me with the same welcome.

A man may well go on foot, they say, who leads his horse by the bridle. Our James, King of Naples and Sicily — handsome, young, and healthy, yet having himself carried about the country on a stretcher, lying on a shabby mattress, in a worn robe of grey cloth with a cap to match, all the while attended by a royal retinue of litters and led horses of every kind, gentlemen and officers — was practicing an authority still tender and wobbly: the sick man has nothing to complain of who carries his cure in his sleeve. All the benefit I draw from books lies in the experience and practice of that maxim, which is a very true one. In fact, I make scarcely more actual use of them than people who have never opened one. I enjoy them the way misers enjoy their money — in knowing I can enjoy them whenever I please; my mind is satisfied with the right of possession. I never travel without books, in peace or in war, and yet I will pass days, sometimes months, without looking at them. I will read by and by, I tell myself, or tomorrow, or whenever I feel like it — and meanwhile the time slips past and does me no harm. For I cannot tell you how much I rest and settle in the thought that they are there beside me, to give me pleasure whenever I choose; they are always at my call, and I have only to remember what a refreshment they are to my life. It is the best provision I have found for this human journey, and I deeply pity any man of understanding who travels without it. I will accept any other sort of amusement, however slight, precisely because this one can never fail me.

At home I retreat a little more often to my library, from which I oversee all the workings of my household at a glance. It stands over the entrance to my house: from there I look down on my garden, my courtyard, my farmyard, and into almost every part of the building. There I leaf through one book, then another, on all sorts of subjects, without method or plan. One hour I muse; another I jot down or pace back and forth dictating these whimsies of mine that you are reading now. It is on the third floor of a tower. The ground floor is my chapel; the second floor a bedroom with a dressing room and a closet, where I often sleep when I want to be more alone; and above is a great wardrobe. In the old days it was the most useless spot in the house. I pass most of the days of my life there, and most of the hours of each day. I am never there at night. Beside it is a rather handsome study with a fireplace very conveniently placed, and plenty of light; and if I were not more afraid of the trouble than the expense — the trouble that scares me off every project — I could easily add on each side, at the same level, a gallery a hundred paces long and twelve wide, since walls built for some other purpose already stand at the right height. Every place of retreat needs a walk. My thoughts fall asleep if I sit still; my mind will not go unless my legs move it — and everyone who studies without a book is in the same condition.

The shape of my library is round, with no flat wall except what my table and chair require, so the whole curve of the circle offers me all my books at once, ranged on five tiers of shelves all around me. It has three fine, open views, and is sixteen paces across. In winter I am there less constantly, for my house sits on a hill — that is what its name says — and no part of it is more exposed to wind and weather than this. That suits me well enough: the awkward climb gives me exercise and keeps the crowd away. There is my throne. There I try to make my rule absolute, and to withdraw this one corner from all society — conjugal, filial, and civil. Everywhere else my authority is merely verbal, and muddled in substance. The man who has nowhere at home to be by himself, to pay court to himself in private, to hide — that man, in my opinion, is miserable. Ambition pays its servants the fitting wage of keeping them always on display, like a statue in the public square. As Seneca says, a great fortune is a great slavery. They cannot even be private in the privy. I have found nothing in the austere life our monks profess harder than the rule I have seen in some of their orders: perpetual company, with many persons present at everything they do whatsoever. It seems to me far more bearable to be always alone than never to be able to be.

If anyone tells me that using the Muses only as a toy and a pastime is to cheapen them, he does not know, as I do, the worth of the toy, the pleasure, and the pastime. I can hardly keep from adding that every other aim is ridiculous. I live from day to day and — with all due reverence — I live only for myself; my plans go no further. In my youth I studied to show off; later, to make myself a little wiser; now, for my amusement — never for gain. The vain, spendthrift taste I once had for this kind of furniture — books acquired not just to supply my needs but to dress the walls and make a show — I have long since cured myself of entirely.

Books have many charming qualities for those who know how to choose them. But every good has its evil. This pleasure is no purer or cleaner than the others; it has its drawbacks, and weighty ones. The soul gets its exercise there, but the body — whose care I must never neglect either — sits motionless all the while, and grows heavy and dull. I know no excess that does me more harm, or that I should avoid more carefully in these my declining years.

These, then, have been my three favorite and personal occupations. I say nothing of the ones I owe to the world by civil obligation.

Of Coaches

It's easy to show that when the great authors write about causes, they don't use only the ones they believe to be true. They also use the ones they don't believe, as long as those have some beauty and invention in them. They speak truly enough, and usefully enough, if they speak ingeniously. We can never be certain of the master cause, so we pile up a whole heap of them, hoping the real one might happen to be somewhere in the pile. As Lucretius says, "it is not enough to give one cause; we must give many, though only one of them is true."

You ask me where the custom comes from of blessing people who sneeze? Well: we break wind three ways. The wind that escapes from below is too filthy to mention. The wind that comes out of the mouth carries a whiff of gluttony with it. The third is the sneeze, and because it comes from the head and gives no offense, we grant it this polite reception. Don't laugh at the distinction — they say it's Aristotle's.

I think I read in Plutarch — who, of all the authors I know, best mixed art with nature and judgment with learning — that seasickness is caused by fear; he had first worked out some argument proving that fear could produce such an effect. Now, I am very prone to seasickness, and I know perfectly well that this cause has nothing to do with me. I know it not by argument but by hard experience. I'll set aside what I've been told — that animals get seasick too, especially hogs, who have no notion whatsoever that they're in danger. And I'll set aside what an acquaintance told me about himself: that though he's very subject to it, the urge to vomit left him three or four times when he was badly frightened in a violent storm. He was like that ancient who said, "I was too frightened to be sick," as Seneca tells it.

I was never afraid on the water — nor in any other danger, and I've had enough of them in front of my eyes to qualify, if death qualifies — never so afraid, I mean, as to be stunned out of my judgment. Fear springs sometimes from a lack of judgment as much as from a lack of courage. Every danger I've ever been in, I looked at without blinking, with an open, sound, and whole sight. Besides, it takes courage to be afraid. That courage once served me better than anything else: it let me order and manage my retreat so that it was, if not without fear, at least without panic and without astonishment. I was shaken, yes, but not dazed or stupefied.

Great souls go much further than this, and show us retreats that are not just steady and composed but downright lofty. Let me give you the account Alcibiades left of Socrates, his comrade in arms. "I found him," he says, "after the rout of our army, him and Laches, among the last of those retreating. I could study him at my leisure and in safety, because I was on a good horse and he was on foot, as he had fought. First I noticed how much judgment and resolve he showed compared with Laches. Then I noticed the boldness of his walk — no different from his everyday stride — and his gaze, firm and level, weighing and judging everything that passed around him, looking now at one group, now at another, friends and enemies alike. To his friends, that look gave heart; to his enemies, it announced that he would sell his life dearly to anyone who tried to take it. And so they came off safe — because people don't like to attack a man like that. They chase the ones who look frightened."

That is the testimony of a great captain, and it teaches what we see confirmed every day: nothing throws us into danger like a frantic eagerness to get clear of it. As Livy puts it, "where there is least fear, there is usually least danger."

Our people are wrong to say that a man "is afraid of death" when what they mean is that he thinks about it and sees it coming. Foresight is equally useful for whatever concerns us, good or bad. To consider a danger and weigh it is, in a way, the very opposite of being stunned by it.

I do not find myself strong enough to stand against the force of this passion of fear, or of any other violent passion. If it once conquered me and knocked me flat, I would never get up again entirely sound. Whoever made my soul lose her footing would never set her upright after. She probes and re-probes herself too deeply, too much to the quick; she would never let the wound she'd received close and scar over. It's been lucky for me that no illness has yet unhinged her. Every time I'm charged, I meet it with all the resistance and defense I have — which means the first assault that routed me would leave me no way to rally. I have no second line. Wherever the flood breaks through my banks, I lie open, and I drown without remedy. Epicurus says a wise man can never become a fool. I hold the reverse opinion: that whoever has once been a thorough fool will never afterward be thoroughly wise. God sends me cold according to my coat, and passions in proportion to my means of withstanding them. Nature, having left me exposed on one side, has covered me on the other: having disarmed me of strength, she has armed me with insensibility — with a composure that is steady, or, if you prefer, dull.

Now, I cannot long endure — and when I was young I could endure even less — a coach, a litter, or a boat, and I hate every kind of travel but horseback, in town and country alike. But I can bear a litter even less than a coach, and by the same logic I can bear rough water, which frightens people, better than the motion of a calm. At those little jolts the oars give, stealing the vessel out from under us, I feel — I don't know how — my head and my stomach both go wrong; just as I can't sit on a wobbly chair. When the sail or the current carries us evenly along, or we're being towed, that smooth motion doesn't bother me at all. It's interrupted movement that offends me, and worst of all when it's slow. I can't describe it any better than that. The doctors prescribed squeezing and binding my lower belly with a towel to fix this trouble — which I haven't tried, being accustomed to wrestling with my own defects and beating them myself.
