If my memory could serve me, I wouldn't think my time wasted in setting down here the endless variety that history offers on the use of chariots in war — varied by nation and by age, and in my opinion of great necessity and effect, so that it's a wonder we've lost all knowledge of them. I'll say only this: quite recently, in our fathers' time, the Hungarians used them to great advantage against the Turks. Each one carried a man with a shield and a musketeer, plus a stack of harquebuses ready loaded, the whole thing screened with canvas like a war-galley. They formed the front of their battle line with three thousand such coaches, and after the cannon had spoken, sent the whole volley pouring into the enemy — who had to swallow that taste of things to come, which was no small head start. Then the chariots charged into the enemy squadrons to break them open and clear a way for the rest. Besides that, they could shield the troops on a dangerous flank while marching, or cover a position and fortify it in a hurry. In my own time, a gentleman on one of our frontiers — too bulky a man for any horse to carry — having a quarrel on his hands, rode about the country in a chariot of this sort, and got on very comfortably. But let's leave these chariots of war.

As if the worthlessness of coaches weren't already proven well enough, the last kings of our first dynasty traveled in a chariot drawn by four oxen. Mark Antony was the first man in Rome to have himself drawn in a coach by lions — with a singing girl beside him. Heliogabalus later did the same, calling himself Cybele, the mother of the gods; he was also drawn by tigers, playing the god Bacchus; sometimes he harnessed two stags to his coach, another time four dogs, and another time four naked girls, having himself drawn by them in full pomp, stark naked himself. The Emperor Firmus had his chariot drawn by ostriches of such prodigious size that it seemed to fly rather than roll.

The strangeness of these inventions puts another notion in my head: that it is a kind of small-mindedness in monarchs, and proof that they don't quite understand what they are, when they labor to make themselves honored and to look great through excessive spending. It would be excusable in a foreign country; but among their own subjects, where they hold sovereign power and can do whatever they please, it actually cheapens their dignity — the highest degree of honor they can reach. Just as, it seems to me, it's superfluous for a private gentleman to dress up finely at home: his house, his servants, and his kitchen answer for him well enough.

The advice Isocrates gives his king seems grounded in good sense: be splendid in plate and furniture, since that's a durable expense that passes down to your successors; and avoid all magnificence that will be forgotten in short order.

I loved dressing finely when I was a younger son, having no other ornament, and it suited me. There are some men on whom their rich clothes sit and weep. We have remarkable stories of the frugality of our kings about their own persons and in their gifts — kings who were great in reputation, in valor, and in fortune. Demosthenes fought hard against his city's law assigning public money to the pomp of its plays and festivals; he wanted its greatness shown in numbers of well-equipped ships and well-provided armies. And there's good reason to condemn Theophrastus, who in his book on riches takes the contrary position and maintains that this sort of expense is the true fruit of abundance. Such delights, says Aristotle, please only the basest sort of people; they vanish from memory as soon as the crowd has had its fill, and no serious, judicious man can hold them in any esteem.

The money would, in my opinion, be spent far more royally — being more profitably, justly, and durably spent — on ports, harbors, walls, and fortifications; on handsome buildings, churches, hospitals, colleges, and the improvement of streets and highways. In that line Pope Gregory XIII will leave an honorable memory to future ages, and in that line our Queen Catherine would prove her natural generosity to long posterity, if her means matched her inclination. Fortune did me a real spite when she interrupted the noble structure of the Pont-Neuf of our great city and robbed me of the hope of seeing it finished before I die.

Besides, it seems to subjects watching these triumphs that it's their own wealth being paraded before them — that they're being entertained at their own expense. For the people tend to assume about kings what we assume about our servants: that their job is to provide everything we need in abundance, and to touch none of it themselves. That's why the Emperor Galba, pleased with a musician who played for him at supper, sent for his money-box and gave him a handful of crowns out of it with these words: "This is not the public money, but my own." Still, it usually turns out that the people have reason on their side, and that princes feed the people's eyes with what their bellies need.

Even generosity doesn't show its true luster in a sovereign's hand; private men have the better right to it. For, taking it strictly, a king has nothing that is properly his own; he owes his very self to others. Authority is not given for the benefit of the magistrate but for the benefit of the people. A superior is never made superior for his own profit but for the profit of those beneath him — a doctor exists for the sick, not for himself. Every office, like every art, has its purpose outside itself.

So the tutors of young princes who make it their business to stamp this virtue of open-handedness into them — preaching that they should refuse nothing, and count nothing so well spent as what they give away (a doctrine I have seen in high credit in my time) — either care more for their own profit than their master's, or don't understand whom they're talking to. It's far too easy to preach generosity to someone with unlimited means of practicing it at other people's expense. And since a gift is measured not by its size but by the means of the giver, in such mighty hands it comes to nothing: they find themselves wastrels before they can be counted generous. Besides, it's a minor virtue compared with the other royal ones — and the only one, as the tyrant Dionysius said, that sits comfortably with tyranny itself. I would rather teach a prince the old farmer's verse: that whoever wants a good crop must sow with the hand, not pour from the sack. He must scatter the seed, not dump it in one heap. And since he has to give — or, to put it more accurately, to pay and to restore to so many people according to what they've earned — he ought to be a loyal and discerning steward of it. If a prince's generosity has neither measure nor discrimination, I would rather have him a miser.

Royal virtue seems to consist most of all in justice; and of all the branches of justice, the one that best marks a king is the justice that goes with giving — because that's the part kings have reserved to perform in person, while every other kind they hand off to be administered by others. Bounty without measure is a weak way to win goodwill; it puts off more people than it attracts. As Cicero says, "the more men you spend it on, the fewer you can spend it on later; and what greater folly than to make sure you can no longer do the very thing you love to do?" And if it's handed out without regard to merit, it embarrasses the man who receives it, and is received without grace. Tyrants have been sacrificed to the people's hatred by the hands of the very men they unjustly raised up — buffoons, panders, fiddlers, and ragamuffins of that stamp — who reckon they can secure their ill-gotten gains by showing hatred and contempt for the man they got them from, signing on to the common verdict.

The subjects of a prince who is excessive in gifts grow excessive in asking; they peg their demands not to reason but to precedent. Honestly, we often have cause to blush at our own shamelessness. By any standard of justice we are overpaid the moment the reward equals the service — do we owe our princes no natural obligation at all? If a prince carries our costs entirely, he does too much; it's enough that he contributes to them. The surplus is called a benefit, and a benefit cannot be demanded — the very word "liberality" has liberty in it.

The way we do it, it never ends. What we've already received doesn't count; only future generosity counts. So the more a prince exhausts himself in giving, the poorer he grows in friends. How could he satisfy appetites that grow with every feeding? Whoever has his mind on getting no longer thinks about what he's gotten. Nothing belongs to greed so properly as ingratitude.

The example of Cyrus fits well here, as a touchstone for the kings of our day to test whether their gifts are well or badly placed — and to see how much better that emperor placed his than they do, who are reduced to borrowing from subjects they don't know, and sooner from the people they've wronged than from the people they've showered with benefits, receiving "aid" in which nothing is voluntary but the name. Croesus once needled Cyrus about his open hand, and calculated what his treasury would be worth if he'd been a little tighter-fisted. Cyrus wanted to vindicate his generosity, so he sent letters in every direction to the great men of his realm whom he had particularly advanced, asking each of them to send him as much money as he could for a pressing need, and to send an account of what he could provide. When the answers came in, every one of his friends — judging it not enough to offer merely what they'd received from his bounty, and adding plenty of their own — it turned out the total came to far more than Croesus's reckoning. Whereupon Cyrus said: "I am no less in love with riches than other princes; I am merely a better manager of them. You see at how small a cost I have acquired the priceless treasure of so many friends, and how much more faithful treasurers they are to me than hired men with no obligation and no affection would be — and my money better stored than in chests, which would bring down on me the hatred, envy, and contempt of other princes."

The emperors excused the extravagance of their games and public spectacles on the grounds that their authority depended in some sort — at least in outward show — on the will of the Roman people, who from time immemorial had been used to being courted with such shows and excesses. But it was private citizens who had fed that custom, gratifying their fellow citizens chiefly out of their own purses with that sort of profusion and magnificence. It had quite another taste when the masters came to imitate it. As Cicero says, "the handing of money from its rightful owners to strangers should not be counted generosity."

Philip, seeing that his son was trying to win the affection of the Macedonians with presents, reprimanded him in a letter along these lines: "What! Do you want your subjects to look on you as their cashier and not their king? Do you want to buy their affections? Then buy them with the benefits of your virtue, not the benefits of your money-box."

And yet, no doubt about it, it was a fine thing to bring into the amphitheater and plant there a great number of huge trees, fully branched and fully green, a great shady forest arranged in beautiful order — and on the first day to turn loose into it a thousand ostriches, a thousand stags, a thousand boars, and a thousand fallow deer, to be killed and carried off by the people; on the second day, to have a hundred huge lions, a hundred leopards, and three hundred bears slaughtered before the crowd; and on the third day, three hundred pairs of gladiators fighting to the finish — as the Emperor Probus did. It was a fine thing, too, to see those vast amphitheaters faced all over with marble on the outside, worked with figures and statues, and glittering inside with rare ornament — "a balustrade studded with gems, a portico overlaid with gold," as Calpurnius says — every side of that enormous space filled and ringed, bottom to top, with sixty or eighty rows of seats, marble too, and laid with cushions. "Let him go out," says Juvenal, "if he has any shame, and get up off the equestrian cushion, since his fortune doesn't meet the legal mark." A hundred thousand men could sit there at their ease.

And the floor below, where the games were played: they made it open and split apart by machinery into chasms, like caves vomiting out the beasts for the spectacle. Then they flooded it into a deep sea, full of sea monsters and loaded with warships, to stage a naval battle. Then they drained it dry and level again for the gladiators. And for the fourth act they strewed it with vermilion and fragrant resin instead of sand, and laid out a solemn feast there for that whole infinite crowd of people — the final act of a single day. As Calpurnius describes it: how often have we seen the arena floor sink and split, and wild beasts climb out of the chasm, and from those same depths a grove of gilded trees rise up in enameled blossom; nor were the marvels only of the forest — I have watched sea-calves fighting with bears, and an unlovely sort of cattle that might be called sea-horses.

Sometimes they raised up a high mountain, covered with fruit trees and other green growth, sending streams of water down from its summit as if from a spring's mouth. Sometimes a great ship came rolling in, opened and split apart by itself, disgorged four or five hundred fighting beasts from its hold, closed up again, and vanished without anyone's help. At other times they made jets of perfumed water shoot upward from the floor, high enough to sprinkle that whole vast multitude. To shelter the crowd from the weather, they had that immense space covered now with embroidered purple curtains, now with silk of one color or another, drawn off or on in a moment, just as they pleased — though, as Martial jokes about a notorious thief, "however fiercely the sun scorches the spectators, the awnings are drawn in when Hermogenes arrives."

Even the netting stretched in front of the spectators to protect them from the beasts turned loose in the arena was woven of gold — "the woven nets glitter with gold," as Calpurnius says.

If anything can excuse such excesses, it is when the novelty and invention produce more wonder than the expense. Even in these vanities we can see how fertile those ages were in minds of a kind quite different from ours. This sort of fertility works like everything else nature produces. It is not that she spent her whole force then and there. We do not advance; we scurry up and down, wheel this way and that, double back the way we came. I am afraid our knowledge is feeble in every direction. We see neither far forward nor far backward. Our understanding takes in little and lives only a little while — short in reach of time and short in reach of matter. As Horace says, many brave men lived before Agamemnon, but all of them lie pressed under the long night, unwept and unknown. And Lucretius asks why the poets sang nothing earlier than the Theban war and the fall of Troy.

And Solon's account of what he learned from the Egyptian priests — the long life of their state, and their way of learning and preserving the histories of other nations — is not, I think, a testimony to be brushed aside here. Cicero puts it this way: if we could see the unlimited stretch of regions and of times on every side, a vastness the mind can wander through so far and wide that it never finds an edge to rest its eye on, we would discover in that infinite immensity an infinite multitude of forms.

Even if everything that has come down to us by report about past ages were true, and known by somebody, it would be less than nothing next to what is unknown. And of this very image of the world that glides away while we live on it, how wretched and limited is the knowledge of even the most curious! Not just of particular events, which fortune often makes weighty and exemplary, but of the condition of great governments and nations — a hundred escape us for every one that reaches us. We make a mighty fuss over the invention of artillery and printing, which other men at the other end of the world, in China, had a thousand years ago. If we could see as much of the world as we do not see, we would perceive, we may well believe, a perpetual multiplication and turnover of forms. Nothing is single and rare in nature's eyes — only in the eyes of our knowledge, which is a wretched foundation for our rules, and gives us a thoroughly false picture of things. Just as we nowadays foolishly conclude that the world is declining into decrepitude from the arguments we draw out of our own weakness and decay — "our age is broken, and the earth worn out," says Lucretius — so he just as foolishly concluded that the world was newborn and young, from the vigor he saw in the minds of his own time, teeming with novelties and new arts: the world is fresh and recent, he says, which is why some arts are still being polished and still growing, and why shipping gains new improvements every day.

Our world has just discovered another one — and who can promise us it is the last of its brothers, since the spirits, the Sibyls, and we ourselves were ignorant of this one until now? — a world as large, as populous, as fruitful as the one we live on, and yet so raw and so childish that we are still teaching it its ABCs. Fifty years ago it knew nothing of letters, weights, measures, clothing, wheat, or vines. It was still naked in its mother's lap, living only on what she gave it. If we are right about our own old age, and that poet was right about the youth of his era, then this other world will only be coming into the light as ours makes its exit. The universe will fall into paralysis — one limb withered, the other in full vigor.

I am very much afraid we have hurried along its decline and ruin by our contagion, and sold it our opinions and our arts at a very steep price. It was an infant world, and yet we have not whipped it into our discipline by any superiority of natural worth and strength, nor won it by our justice and goodness, nor subdued it by our greatness of soul. Most of their answers, and most of the dealings we have had with them, show that they were not one inch behind us in clarity and natural good sense. The astonishing magnificence of the cities of Cusco and Mexico; the king's garden, among many other things, where every tree, fruit, and plant stood excellently formed in gold, in the same order and size they would have in a real garden; his cabinet, stocked with golden likenesses of every animal bred in his lands and in his seas; and the beauty of their work in jewels, feathers, cotton, and painting — all of it proves they were no whit beneath us in industry. But as for devotion, obedience to law, goodness, liberality, loyalty, and plain dealing, it served us well that we had less of these than they did. That advantage is what lost them, sold them, and betrayed them.

As for boldness and courage — for firmness, constancy, and resolution against pain, hunger, and death — I would not hesitate to set the examples I find among them against the most famous examples of the ancient world recorded on our side of it. As for the men who subdued them: take away the tricks and swindles they used to gull them. Take away the perfectly natural astonishment of those nations at the sudden, unannounced arrival of bearded men — different in language, religion, shape, and face, from a part of the world so remote they had never heard it was inhabited at all — mounted on great unknown monsters, against people who had never seen a horse, never seen any beast trained to carry a man or a load of any kind. Men shelled in hard, shining skin, with sharp, glittering weapons in their hands, against people so dazzled by the gleam of a mirror or a knife that they would trade great treasures of gold and pearl for them, and who had neither the knowledge nor the material to pierce our steel even at their leisure. Add the lightning and thunder of our cannons and muskets — enough to rattle Caesar himself, caught off guard with as little experience of them — turned on people who were naked except for some quilted cotton where the art had reached, with no weapons beyond bows, stones, sticks, and wooden shields. People taken by surprise under cover of friendship and good faith, drawn in by their own curiosity to see strange and unknown things. Take away that disparity, I say, from the conquerors, and you take away the whole occasion of so many victories.

When I consider that unconquerable ardor with which so many thousands of men, women, and children threw themselves, over and over, into certain danger for the defense of their gods and their liberty; that noble stubbornness in bearing every extremity and hardship, and death itself, rather than submit to the dominion of the men who had so shamefully cheated them — some of them, once captured, choosing to starve themselves to death rather than accept food from the hands of enemies who had won so basely — I am convinced that anyone who had attacked them on equal terms of weapons, experience, and numbers would have had as hard a war on his hands, and maybe harder, than any we have ever seen.

Why didn't so noble a conquest fall to Alexander, or to the ancient Greeks and Romans? Why didn't so vast a revolution, the transformation of so many empires and nations, fall into hands that would have gently polished and cleared away whatever was rough and wild in them, and cherished and encouraged the good seeds nature had planted there — not only blending the arts of our world into their farming and the adornment of their cities, where these were needed, but blending the Greek and Roman virtues with the virtues native to the country? What a repair of their condition that would have been, and what a benefit to the whole world, if our first examples and conduct over there had drawn those peoples into the admiration and imitation of virtue, and built between them and us a brotherly fellowship and understanding! How easy it would have been to make something of souls so innocent, so hungry to learn, and for the most part starting with such fine natural inclinations!

Instead we did the opposite. We used their ignorance and inexperience to bend them all the more easily toward treachery, luxury, greed, and every sort of inhumanity and cruelty, by the model and pattern of our own manners. Whoever drove up the price of trade goods to such a rate? So many cities razed, so many nations exterminated, so many millions of people put to the sword, and the richest and most beautiful part of the world turned upside down — for the traffic of pearls and pepper! Mechanic victories! Never did ambition, never did public hatreds, drive men against one another into such miserable hostilities and such miserable calamities.

Certain Spaniards, coasting along in search of their mines, landed in a fruitful, pleasant, well-peopled country and made the inhabitants their usual declarations: that they were peaceable men, come from a very distant country, sent on behalf of the King of Castile, the greatest prince of the habitable world, to whom the Pope, God's representative on earth, had given the principality of all the Indies; that if the inhabitants would pay him tribute, they would be very gently and courteously treated. Meanwhile they demanded food for themselves, and gold — for some medicine they pretended to need — and preached the belief in one God and the truth of our religion, which they advised them to embrace, throwing in a few threats for good measure.

This was the answer they got. As for being peaceable, they didn't look it, if they were. As for their king, since he was reduced to begging, he must be poor and needy; and the man who had made him this gift must love quarrels, to give away what wasn't his and bring it into dispute against its ancient owners. As for food, they would supply it. Gold they had little of, since it was a thing they held in low esteem, useless for the actual business of living, which they cared only to pass happily and pleasantly; but whatever they could find, except what was used in the service of their gods, the strangers might freely take. As for one single God, the idea pleased them well — but they would not change their religion, having lived in it so long and so happily, and being in the habit of taking advice only from friends and people they knew. As for the threats, it showed poor judgment to threaten men whose nature and strength you knew nothing about. So the strangers had better hurry up and clear off their coast, since they were not accustomed to take the courtesies and professions of armed foreigners in good part — otherwise they would be dealt with like these others. And they pointed to the heads of executed men around the walls of their city.

There's a fair sample of the babbling of these children. And note this: neither in that place nor in several others where the Spaniards did not find the merchandise they were after did they make any stay or any attempt, whatever other attractions the country offered — witness my essay Of Cannibals.

Of the two most powerful monarchs of that world — and perhaps of this one, kings over so many kings — the last two they deposed: the king of Peru was taken in battle and put to a ransom so enormous it passes belief. He paid it faithfully, and showed in his conversation every sign of a frank, generous, steady spirit and a clear, settled understanding. The conquerors, having already squeezed out of him one million, three hundred and twenty-five thousand, five hundred weight of gold, plus silver and other goods worth no less — so much that their horses went shod in solid gold — still wanted to find out, at whatever cost in disloyalty and injustice, what the rest of this king's treasure might amount to, and to get their hands on that too. So they trumped up a false accusation, with false witnesses, charging that he was plotting to raise his provinces in rebellion and free himself. And by the virtuous verdict of the very men who had conspired his ruin by this treachery, he was sentenced to be publicly hanged and strangled — having first been made to buy his way out of being burned alive by accepting the baptism they gave him at the moment of execution. A horrid, unheard-of barbarity, which he nevertheless endured without flinching in word or look, with a bearing truly grave and royal. And afterwards, to soothe a people stunned and shaken by so strange a thing, the conquerors counterfeited great grief at his death and staged a sumptuous funeral.

The other, the king of Mexico, had long defended his besieged city, and showed in that siege everything that endurance and perseverance can do, if ever a prince and people did. When his misfortune delivered him alive into his enemies' hands, on terms that he would be treated like a king, he did nothing in his captivity unworthy of the title. After their victory, the conquerors did not find as much gold as they had promised themselves; so when they had searched and ransacked everything, they set about extracting information by the cruelest tortures they could devise on the prisoners they held. Gaining nothing — the prisoners' courage proving stronger than the torments — they arrived at last at such a pitch of fury that, against their pledged word and against the law of nations, they condemned the king himself, and one of the chief noblemen of his court, to the rack, each in the other's sight. The lord, overcome with pain, ringed around with burning coals, turned his dying eyes pitifully toward his master, as if to beg his pardon for being able to bear no more. The king fixed him with a fierce, stern look, rebuking his cowardice and faintness of heart, and said only this, in a harsh, steady voice: "And what do you think I am suffering? Am I in a bath? Am I more comfortable than you?" The lord, at that, sank under his torment and died on the spot. The king, half roasted, was carried away — not out of pity (what pity ever touched souls so barbarous that, on a doubtful rumor of some golden vessel to be looted, they would broil a man, and not just a man but a king so great in fortune and in merit, before their eyes?), but because his constancy made their cruelty more and more shameful. They hanged him later, for having nobly attempted to free himself by arms from so long a captivity and subjection; and he died with a courage worthy of so great-souled a prince.

Another time they burned alive, in one fire, four hundred and sixty living men — the four hundred common people, the sixty among the chief lords of a province — simply prisoners of war. We have these accounts from the conquerors themselves: they not only admit it, they boast of it and publish it. Could it be as a testimony to their justice? To their zeal for religion? Surely these ways run too contrary to so holy an end. If their aim had been to spread our faith, they would have remembered that faith grows not by the possession of territory but by the winning of souls, and they would have been more than content with the killing that war makes necessary, without adding a massacre as indiscriminate as if it were of wild beasts, as universal as fire and sword could make it — sparing, by deliberate choice, only as many as they meant to keep for miserable slavery in the work and service of their mines. So it was that many of the captains were put to death on the very ground of their conquests, by order of the kings of Castile, justly outraged by the horror of their conduct; and almost all of them died hated and despised. God, in fitting justice, allowed all that great plunder to be swallowed up by the sea in transport, or in the civil wars in which they devoured one another; and most of the men themselves were buried in foreign soil with no fruit of their victory.

As for the revenue from those countries — the fact that even in the hands of so thrifty and careful a prince as the present King of Spain, it falls so far short of what his predecessors were led to expect, and of that first abundance of riches found at the original landing in those newly discovered lands (for though a great deal is brought home, we can see it is nothing next to what should have been expected) — the explanation is that the use of money was wholly unknown there, so that all their gold sat gathered in one place, serving no purpose but ornament and display, a treasure handed down from father to son by many powerful kings who kept draining their mines to build that vast heap of vessels and statues for the decoration of their palaces and temples. Our gold, by contrast, is all in motion and in trade. We cut it into a thousand little pieces, cast it into a thousand forms, scatter and disperse it a thousand ways. Imagine our kings hoarding up all the gold they could lay hands on for several centuries and letting it lie idle.

The people of the kingdom of Mexico were somewhat more civilized and more advanced in the arts than the other nations around them. So they judged, as we do, that the world was nearing its end, and took the desolation we brought among them as a sure sign of it. They believed the world's existence was divided into five ages, the lives of five successive suns, of which four had already run their course; and the sun that gave them light was the fifth. The first perished, with every other creature, in a universal flood. The second perished when the sky fell upon the earth and smothered every living thing — that was the age they assigned to the giants, and they showed the Spaniards bones by whose proportions men would have stood twenty feet tall. The third perished by fire, which burned and consumed everything. The fourth by a churning of air and wind that flattened whole mountains; in that one men did not die but were turned into baboons. (What impressions will the weakness of human belief not accept!) After the death of this fourth sun, the world spent twenty-five years in perpetual darkness. In the fifteenth of those years a man and a woman were created who restored the human race. Ten years later, on a certain day, the sun appeared, newly created; and they count their years from that day. On the third day after its creation the old gods died, and new ones have been born since, day by day. How they think this last sun will perish, my author could not learn. But their count for that fourth change matches the great conjunction of stars which, eight hundred-odd years ago, by the astrologers' reckoning, produced great upheavals and novelties in the world.

As for pomp and magnificence — which is how I got into this discussion in the first place — neither Greece nor Rome nor Egypt can compare any of its works, for usefulness, difficulty, or grandeur, with the highway to be seen in Peru, built by the kings of that country from the city of Quito to the city of Cusco: three hundred leagues, straight, even, twenty-five paces wide, paved, and lined on both sides with fine high walls, and along the inside of the walls two streams that never fail, bordered with the beautiful plants they call moly. Where they met rocks and mountains, they cut through and leveled them, and filled in pits and valleys with lime and stone. At the end of each day's journey stand beautiful palaces stocked with provisions, clothing, and weapons, for travelers and for armies that have to pass that way. In rating this work I have counted the difficulty, which weighs especially heavily in that place. They built with no stones smaller than ten feet square. They had no means of carriage but hauling their loads by the strength of their own arms. They did not even know the art of scaffolding, and knew no other trick for standing to their work than to pile earth against the building as it rose, and take it away again when they were done.

Let us come back to our coaches. In place of coaches, and of every other kind of carriage, they had themselves carried on men's shoulders. That last king of Peru, on the day he was taken, was carried like this — seated on a chair of gold, borne on staves of gold, in the middle of his army. As many of his bearers as were killed to bring him down (for the Spaniards wanted him alive), just as many others — and they competed for it — stepped into the places of the dead, so that no matter what slaughter was made of those people, he could never be toppled, until a horseman seized hold of him and wrestled him to the ground.

Of Cripples

It is now two or three years since they shortened the year by ten days in France. How many changes were supposed to follow that reform! It was moving heaven and earth at once. And yet nothing has budged from its place. My neighbors find their seasons for sowing and reaping, the right moments for their business, the harmful days and the lucky ones, exactly where they had always set them, time out of mind. There was no more error felt in our old usage than there is improvement felt in the new. That is how deep the uncertainty runs everywhere — how coarse, dim, and dull our perception is.

They say the correction could have been managed with less fuss by following the example of Augustus: skip the leap day for a few years — it is a day of nuisance and trouble anyway — until the debt was exactly paid off. (Which this reform did not even accomplish; we are still running a few days in arrears.) And by the same means we could provide for the future, ordering that after every so many years the extra day would be thrown out again, so that from then on our reckoning could never wander more than twenty-four hours off.

Years are the only accounting of time we have. The world has used them for ages, and yet it is a measure we still have not agreed on — we still wonder what shapes other nations gave it and what their true usage was. And what about the claim some make, that the heavens are growing old and sagging down closer toward us, throwing even our hours and days into uncertainty? Or what Plutarch says of the months — that in his time astrology still had not pinned down the motion of the moon? A fine condition we are in, to keep records of things past.

I was just now musing, as I often do, on what a free and roving thing human reason is. I see ordinarily that when something is set before men, they would rather hunt for reasons than find out the truth. They slide past the presuppositions but examine the consequences minutely. They leave the things themselves and run off after the causes. Comical reasoners! The knowledge of causes only concerns whoever runs the world, not us, who merely undergo it — and who get the full and perfect use of things, according to our need, without ever penetrating to their origin and essence. Wine is no more pleasant to the man who knows its first properties. On the contrary, both body and soul interrupt and weaken their right to the use of the world, and of themselves, by mixing in the pretension of learning. Effects concern us; the means, not at all. Determining and distributing belong to mastery and command; accepting belongs to subjection — and to us.

Let me scold a custom of ours. People commonly begin, "How does such a thing happen?" — when they ought to ask, "Does it happen at all?" Our reason is equipped to build a hundred other worlds and discover their beginnings and their architecture. It needs neither material nor foundation. Just let it run: it builds as readily on air as on earth, with emptiness as with substance — able, as the poet says, to give weight to smoke.

I find that in almost every case we ought to say, "There is no such thing." I would use that answer often myself, but I dare not: people cry that it is a dodge born of ignorance and a weak understanding. So most of the time I have to play along for company's sake, and chatter about frivolous subjects and tales I do not believe a word of. Besides, in truth, it is a little rude and quarrelsome to flatly deny a stated fact; and few people — especially with things hard to believe — will fail to insist they saw it themselves, or to name witnesses whose authority stops our mouths. By this method we come to know the foundations and causes of a thousand things that never happened, and the world scuffles over a thousand questions where both the pro and the con are false. The false stands so close to the true that a wise man should not trust himself near the edge.

Truth and lies wear the same face — the same bearing, the same taste, the same manners — and we look on both with the same eye. I find we are not merely slack in defending ourselves against deceit; we seek it out, we volunteer to be fooled. We love to tangle ourselves in vanity, since vanity suits the stuff we are made of.

I have watched the birth of many miracles in my time. Even when they died in the cradle, we can foresee well enough what they would have grown into, had they lived to full age. It is only a matter of finding the end of the thread; then a man can wind off as much as he pleases — and there is a greater distance between nothing and the smallest thing in the world than between the smallest thing and the greatest. Now the first people steeped in a budding marvel, when they go out to spread their tale, can tell from the resistance they meet exactly where the hard-to-believe part lies — and they caulk that seam with some false patch. Besides, men have a natural appetite for feeding rumors on purpose, so we naturally make it a point of conscience never to return what was lent us without some interest and addition of our own. First the private error creates the public error; then, in its turn, the public error creates the private one. And so this whole vast fabric goes on building and piling itself up from hand to hand, until the remotest witness knows more about the thing than the nearest one did, and the last man told is more thoroughly convinced than the first.

It is a natural progression. Whoever believes anything thinks it a work of charity to persuade somebody else of it; and to do that better, he never hesitates to add as much of his own invention as his story seems to need to overcome the resistance, or the dullness, he meets in others. I myself, who make a particular scruple of lying, and who hardly care whether what I say is believed or carries authority — even I notice that when I am heated, whether by someone's opposition or by the warmth of my own telling, I swell and puff up my subject with voice, gesture, vigor, and force of words, and with stretching and amplifying besides, not without some damage to the naked truth. But I do it on this condition: that to the first person who pulls me up short and asks for the plain bare fact, I instantly surrender my passion and hand the matter over without exaggeration, without emphasis, without any coloring of my own. A quick, emphatic way of talking, like mine, is prone to run into hyperbole.

There is nothing men are commonly more eager about than clearing a path for their own opinions; where the ordinary means fail us, we add command, force, fire, and sword. It is a misfortune to have come to the point where the best test of truth is the number of believers — in a crowd where the fools so vastly outnumber the wise. As if anything were so common as ignorance. As if a mob of madmen could vouch for sanity.

It is hard to stiffen your judgment against the common opinions. The first persuasion, drawn from the subject itself, seizes the simple; from them it spreads to the wise, under the authority of the number and the antiquity of the witnesses. For my part, what I would not believe from one man, I would not believe from a hundred and one. And I do not judge opinions by their age.

Not long ago, one of our princes — a man whose gout had spoiled a fine nature and a cheerful temper — let himself be so persuaded by reports of a certain priest who cured every kind of disease with words and gestures, that he made a long journey to find him; and by the sheer force of his imagination he talked his legs, for a few hours, into a service they had long forgotten. If fortune had piled five or six such accidents on top of one another, it would have been enough to install this miracle in nature. Afterward the author of these performances was found to be so simple, and so without art, that he was judged too contemptible to be worth punishing — as would happen with most such things, if they were examined at the source. We admire things that deceive us from a distance. In the same way our sight often shows us strange shapes far off that vanish as we approach. Rumor never survives being brought into focus.

It is astonishing from what idle beginnings and frivolous causes such famous impressions usually spring. That is exactly what blocks the inquiry: while we hunt for causes solid and weighty, worthy of so great a name, we lose the true ones. They escape our sight by being so small. And in truth, these investigations call for a very prudent, patient, and subtle examiner — impartial, with no theory already in his pocket. To this very hour, all these miracles and strange events have kept themselves hidden from me. I have seen no more evident monstrosity and miracle in the world than myself. Time and habit make us familiar with anything strange; but the more I frequent my own company and the better I know myself, the more my own deformity astonishes me, and the less I understand myself.

The chief right of producing and promoting such accidents is reserved to fortune. The day before yesterday, passing through a village two leagues from my house, I found the place still warm from a miracle that had just failed there. The neighborhood had been entertained by it for months; the neighboring provinces were beginning to catch the fever and run there in great troops of every sort of people. A young fellow of the place had one night, for fun, counterfeited the voice of a spirit in his own house — meaning nothing by it but the joke. It succeeded a little better than he expected; so, to extend the farce with more actors, he took in a dull, simple country girl, and in the end there were three of them, all of an age and an understanding. From household performances they advanced to public preaching, hiding under the altar of the church, speaking only at night, and forbidding anyone to bring a light. From words aimed at the conversion of the world and threats of the day of judgment (for these are subjects whose authority and reverence give imposture its safest cover), they moved on to visions and gesticulations so simple and ridiculous that hardly anything in the games of small children is so crude. Yet if fortune had favored the scheme ever so little, who knows how far the act might have gone? The poor devils are in prison now, and will likely pay shortly for the common folly — and I do not know whether some judge won't make them smart for his folly, too. We see clearly into this case, because it has been uncovered; but in many things of the same kind that pass our knowledge, my opinion is that we should suspend judgment — neither rejecting nor accepting.

Great abuses in the world — or, to put it more boldly, all the abuses in the world — are born of our being taught to be afraid of professing our ignorance, and being told we are bound to accept whatever we cannot refute. We talk about everything by precept and pronouncement. The style at Rome was that even what a witness swore he had seen with his own eyes, and even what a judge determined out of his most certain knowledge, was couched in this formula: "It seems to me." They make me hate likely things when people shove them at me as infallible. I love the words that soften and moderate the rashness of our claims: perhaps; in some sort; some; it is said; I think — and the like. And if I had been set to raise children, I would have put this way of answering into their mouths — inquiring, not settling: "What does this mean? I do not understand it. It may be. Is it true?" — so that they would rather have kept the manner of pupils at sixty than gone out as doctors, the way they do, at ten. Whoever wants to be cured of ignorance must confess it.

Iris is the daughter of Thaumas — the rainbow is Wonder's child. Wonder is the foundation of all philosophy; inquiry its progress; ignorance its end. But there is a kind of ignorance, strong and generous, that yields nothing in honor and courage to knowledge — an ignorance that takes no less knowledge to achieve than knowledge itself.

In my younger years I read a trial that Coras, a judge at Toulouse, put into print — the strange case of two men who each presented himself as the other. I remember (and I hardly remember anything else) that Coras made the imposture of the man he judged guilty seem so wonderful, so far beyond our knowledge and beyond his own — and he was the judge — that I thought it a verdict so bold it hanged a man on a guess. Let us accept some form of decree that says, "The court understands nothing of this matter" — more frankly and openly than the judges of the Areopagus, who, finding themselves cornered by a case they could not untangle, ordered the parties to come back in a hundred years.

The witches of my neighborhood are running for their lives every time some new author comes along to give body to their dreams. To fit the examples that Holy Writ offers us of such things — most certain and irrefutable examples — onto our modern events, when we can see neither their causes nor their means, takes a different sort of mind than ours. Perhaps it belongs to that single all-powerful testimony alone to tell us, "This one is, and that one is, and not that other." God ought to be believed in this — that is plainly reasonable. But not, for all that, one of us: a man astonished at his own story (and he must necessarily be astonished, unless he is out of his mind), whether he tells it about somebody else or against himself.

I am plain and heavy, and I hold to the solid and the probable, steering clear of the old reproaches: men give the most faith to what they least understand, and by a craving of the human mind, obscure things are the easiest to believe. I see well enough that people get angry, and that I am forbidden to doubt on pain of unspeakable insults. A new way of persuading! Thank God, I am not to be punched into belief. Let them be angry with those who accuse their opinion of being false; I only accuse it of being difficult and bold, and I condemn the opposite affirmation just as they do — only less imperiously. Whoever establishes his proposition by swagger and command shows that his reasoning is weak. In a verbal, scholastic wrangle, let them appear as plausible as their opponents — let them state probabilities, but not affirm. But in the real consequence drawn from the argument, the doubters have much the better of it. To kill men, you need a light that is clear and strong; and our life is too real and too essential to be staked on these supernatural and fantastic accidents.

As for drugs and poisons, I throw those out of my reckoning: that is homicide of the worst sort. Yet even there, they say, you cannot always rely on the personal confessions of these people, for they have sometimes been known to accuse themselves of murdering persons who later turned up alive and well. In these other extravagant accusations, I am inclined to say this: any man, whatever his reputation, deserves to be believed about human things; but about what is beyond his conception and of supernatural effect, he should be believed only when some supernatural approval has vouched for him. The privilege God has been pleased to grant to certain of our testimonies must not be cheapened and handed around lightly. My ears are battered with a thousand stories like this: "Three people saw him on such a day in the east; three more saw him the next day in the west; at such an hour, in such a place, dressed in such a way." Truly, I would not believe my own self on that evidence. How much more natural and likely do I find it that two men should lie than that one man, in twelve hours, should fly with the wind from east to west! How much more natural that our understanding should be blown out of its place by the whirl of our own unhinged minds than that one of us, flesh and bone as we are, should be carried up a chimney flue on a broomstick by a strange spirit! Let us not go hunting for illusions outside and unknown, we who are perpetually shaken by illusions homegrown and our own. It seems to me a man is pardonable for disbelieving a miracle wherever its proof can be sidestepped by some path that is not miraculous. And I am of St. Augustine's opinion, that it is better to lean toward doubt than toward assurance in things hard to prove and dangerous to believe.

Some years ago I traveled through the territory of a sovereign prince who, as a favor to me, and to beat down my skepticism, did me the honor of letting me see, in his own presence and in a private place, ten or twelve prisoners of this kind — among them an old woman, a genuine witch in ugliness and deformity, long famous in the profession. I saw evidence and free confessions, and some insensible mark or other on the miserable creature. I examined her and talked with her and the rest as much and as long as I pleased, and brought the best and soundest attention I could — and I am not a man who lets prepossession take his judgment captive. In the end, and in all conscience, I would have prescribed them hellebore rather than hemlock — medicine for madness rather than poison. The whole business looked more like minds possessed than minds guilty. Justice has its own corrections for such maladies.

As for the objections and arguments that worthy men have made to me, there and often elsewhere, I have met none that convinced me — none that did not admit a more likely solution than their conclusion. It is true that proofs and reasons founded on experience and fact I do not try to untie; they have no loose end to take hold of. I often cut them, as Alexander cut his knot. After all, it is putting a very high price on our conjectures to roast a man alive on their account.

We are told by various examples — as Praestantius tells of his father — that, sunk in a sleep far deeper than men usually sleep, he dreamed he was a mare serving the soldiers as a pack animal; and what he dreamed himself to be, he actually proved to be. Even if sorcerers dream this materially — even if dreams can sometimes take flesh in fact — still I cannot believe that our will should answer for it to justice. I say this as one who is neither judge nor royal councillor, who thinks himself many degrees unworthy to be either — an ordinary man, born and sworn to the obedience of public reason in both word and deed. Anyone who took my idle talk as an injury to the pettiest law, opinion, or custom of his parish would do himself a great wrong, and me a greater one. In what I say, I warrant no certainty except that it is what I had in my thought at the time — a tumultuous and wavering thought. Everything I say is by way of conversation, nothing by way of advice. And I am not ashamed, as those others are, to confess I do not know what I do not know.

I would not speak so boldly if it were my due to be believed — and that is what I told a great man who complained of the sharpness and contentiousness of my exhortations. Seeing you set and ready on one side, I lay out the other for you with all the diligence and care I can — to clear your judgment, not to compel it. God holds your hearts in His hands and will furnish you the means of choosing. I am not so presumptuous as to wish that my opinions should tip you one way or the other in a matter of such importance; my fortune has not raised them to conclusions so potent and lofty. Truly, I have not only a great many moods but a great many opinions that I would try to teach my son to dislike, if I had a son. What if the truest opinions are not always the most convenient for man, wild as his composition is?

Whether this is to the purpose or not, no great matter: it is a common proverb in Italy that a man does not know Venus in her perfect sweetness who has never slept with a lame mistress. Fortune, or some particular incident, put that saying into people's mouths long ago; and it is said of men as well as of women — for the queen of the Amazons answered the Scythian who courted her, "The lame man does it best." In that republic of women, to escape the dominion of the males, they crippled them in infancy — arms, legs, and whatever other parts gave men an advantage over them — and used them only for the one purpose for which we, in our part of the world, use women.

I would have guessed it was the lame woman's rolling gait that added some new pleasure to the business and some extra spice for those who tried it. But I have lately learned that ancient philosophy itself has ruled on the question. It says that since the legs and thighs of lame women, because of their imperfection, do not receive their proper nourishment, the genital parts above come out fuller, better fed, and more vigorous. Or else that, since the defect prevents exercise, those who have it spend less of their strength and come more entire to the games of Venus — which is also why the Greeks called the weaving-women hotter than other women: a sedentary trade, carried on without much exercise of the body. At this rate, what can we not reason about? Of these weavers I might just as well say that the jiggling of their work as they sit stirs and provokes their desire, the way the swaying and jolting of coaches does for our ladies.

Do not these examples prove what I said at the start — that our reasons often run ahead of the facts, and hold so boundless a jurisdiction that they judge and exercise themselves on emptiness itself, on things that do not exist? Besides the suppleness of our invention in forging reasons for every sort of dream, our imagination is just as ready to take false impressions from the most frivolous appearances. On the sole authority of the ancient and common use of that proverb, I once made myself believe that I had gotten more pleasure from a woman because she was not straight, and counted that crookedness among her charms.

Torquato Tasso, in the comparison he draws between France and Italy, says he has observed that our legs are generally thinner than those of the Italian gentlemen, and credits it to our being constantly on horseback — the very same cause from which Suetonius draws the exactly opposite conclusion, for he says Germanicus thickened his legs by that same continual exercise.

Nothing is so supple and erratic as our understanding. It is the shoe of Theramenes — it fits every foot. It is double and diverse, and the matters it works on are double and diverse too. "Give me a drachma of silver," a Cynic philosopher said to Antigonus. "That is not a present fit for a king," he replied. "Then give me a talent." "That is not a present fit for a Cynic." Heat works on the soil, the poet says, either by opening hidden passages so the sap can rise into the new shoots, or else by hardening and binding the gaping veins against thin rains, the fierce sun, and the piercing cold of the north wind — one cause, argued both ways. Every medal has its reverse, as the Italians say.

This is why Clitomachus said, long ago, that Carneades had outdone the labors of Hercules: he had torn assent out of men — that is, opinion, and the courage of judging. That vigorous fancy of Carneades sprang, I believe, from the impudence of those who professed knowledge, and from their immeasurable self-conceit. Aesop was put up for sale with two other slaves. The buyer asked the first what he could do, and he, to raise his own price, promised mountains and marvels: he could do this and that and I don't know what all. The second said as much of himself, or more. When it came to Aesop's turn and he was asked what he could do — "Nothing," he said. "These two have gotten there ahead of me. They know everything." So it has happened in the school of philosophy. The pride of those who credited the human mind with a capacity for all things bred in others, out of spite and rivalry, the opinion that it is capable of nothing at all. The one party holds the same extreme in ignorance that the other holds in knowledge — making it undeniably plain that man is immoderate in everything, and never stops anywhere except from necessity, and from sheer inability to go further.

Of Experience

There is no desire more natural than the desire to know. We try every road that might lead us there. When reason fails us, we fall back on experience — as Manilius says, "through trial and error, experience created art, with example showing the way" — which is a weaker and cheaper instrument. But truth is so great a thing that we should not turn up our nose at any means of reaching it.

Reason has so many shapes that we don't know which one to grab. Experience has no fewer. And the conclusions we try to draw by comparing one event with another are shaky, because no two events are ever alike. Nothing in the whole catalogue of things is so universal as diversity and variety.

The Greeks, the Romans, and we ourselves all reach for eggs when we want the perfect example of sameness. And yet there have been men — one at Delphi in particular — who could spot the differences between eggs so well that he never mistook one for another. He kept many hens, and he could tell you which one had laid which egg.

Unlikeness forces its way into our works all by itself. No art can achieve perfect similarity. Neither Perrozet nor anyone else can polish and whiten the backs of his playing cards so carefully that some gambler won't tell them apart just from watching them shuffled in another man's hands. Resemblance does not make things one so much as difference makes them other. Nature has bound herself to make nothing that is not unique.

So I am not much impressed by the fellow who thought he could rein in the authority of judges by multiplying laws — cutting their meat into little pieces for them. He didn't realize that there is just as much liberty and elbow room in interpreting laws as in writing them. And people are fooling themselves when they think they can shrink and settle our disputes by sending us back to the exact words of the Bible. The mind finds the field no less spacious when it's quarreling over someone else's meaning than when it's delivering its own — as if there were less spite and bitterness in commentary than in invention.

We can see how wrong he was. France has more laws than all the rest of the world put together — more than would be needed to govern all the worlds of Epicurus. As Tacitus says, "once we suffered from our crimes; now we suffer from our laws." And yet we have left so much to the opinions and discretion of our judges that there was never such liberty, or such license.

What have our lawmakers gained by picking out a hundred thousand particular cases and attaching a hundred thousand laws to them? That number bears no proportion at all to the infinite diversity of human actions. Multiply our inventions as we like, they will never catch up with the variety of real examples. Add a hundred times as many more: it will still never happen that some future event, out of all those millions of selected and recorded cases, will match any one of them so exactly — pair up with it so perfectly — that no detail remains to demand a different judgment. There is very little relation between our actions, which are in perpetual change, and laws, which are fixed and unmoving. The most desirable laws are the rarest, simplest, and most general. And frankly I think we would be better off with no laws at all than with the prodigious heap of them we have.

Nature always gives us better and happier laws than the ones we make for ourselves. Witness the Golden Age the poets paint, and the condition of nations we see living with no other code. Some peoples take as their only judge the first traveler passing through their mountains, and let him settle their case. Others, on market day, pick out somebody on the spot to decide their quarrels. What would be the danger if the wisest among us settled ours the same way — case by case, at sight, without being chained to precedent and consequence? For every foot its own shoe. When King Ferdinand sent colonists to the Indies, he wisely provided that no students of law should go along, for fear lawsuits would take root in that new world — law being by its very nature a breeder of quarrels and division. He judged, with Plato, that lawyers and doctors are a bad provision for a country.

Why is it that our ordinary language, so handy for every other purpose, turns dark and unintelligible in wills and contracts? Why does a man who expresses himself perfectly clearly in everything else he says or writes find no way of declaring himself there that doesn't collapse into doubt and contradiction? Unless it's that the princes of that art, applying themselves with special care to choosing solemn words and constructing artificial clauses, have weighed every syllable and sifted every kind of finicky connection so thoroughly that they are now tangled and lost in an infinity of figures and tiny subdivisions — beyond the reach of any rule, any prescription, any certain understanding. As Seneca says, whatever is chopped down to dust becomes indistinguishable.

Have you watched children trying to divide a blob of quicksilver into a set number of parts? The more they press it and work it and try to force it to their will, the more they provoke the independence of that noble metal: it slips away from them and scatters into more little beads than anyone can count. It's the same here. By subdividing these subtleties, we teach men to multiply their doubts. We set them on the path of stretching and diversifying difficulties, of lengthening them and scattering them. Sowing questions and cutting them up for retail, we make the world ripen and teem with uncertainty and quarrels — the way soil is made fertile by being crumbled and dug deep. Learning begets difficulty, as Quintilian says.

We had our doubts about Ulpian; Bartolus and Baldus have left us more perplexed than ever. We ought to erase the trail of this endless diversity of opinions, not dress ourselves up in it and stuff posterity's head with quibbles. I don't know what to say about it — but experience makes it plain that so many interpretations scatter the truth and break it to pieces. Aristotle wrote to be understood; if he couldn't manage it, a lesser man won't do better, nor a third man removed yet further from Aristotle's own thoughts. We open up the matter and spill it in pouring it out. From one subject we make a thousand, and by multiplying and subdividing we land back in Epicurus's infinity of atoms. No two men ever judged the same thing the same way; and it is impossible to find two opinions exactly alike — not just in two different men, but in the same man at different hours. I often find room for doubt in passages the commentary didn't think worth a note. Like certain horses I've known that stumble most on the smoothest road, I trip most easily on level ground.

Who can deny that glosses multiply doubt and ignorance? You will not find one book, human or divine, of all the books the world busies itself with, whose difficulties have been cleared up by interpretation. The hundredth commentator hands the text on to the next one knottier and thornier than he found it. When have we ever agreed among ourselves, "This book has had enough; there is nothing more to say about it"?

This shows most plainly in the law. We give the force of law to an infinity of doctors, an infinity of rulings, and as many interpretations. Do we find any end to the need for interpreting? Is there any visible progress toward peace? Do we need any fewer advocates and judges than when this great mass of law was still in its infancy? On the contrary — we darken and bury understanding; we can no longer find it except behind a maze of fences and barriers. Men do not recognize the mind's natural disease: it does nothing but ferret and hunt, endlessly circling, contriving, tangling itself in its own work like a silkworm — and then suffocating in it. A mouse in a pitch barrel. It thinks it spots in the distance some glimmer of light, some imaginary truth; but while it runs that way, so many difficulties and obstacles and fresh questions cut across its path that they lose it the road and leave it drunk with the motion. Not unlike Aesop's dogs, who saw something like a dead body floating far out at sea and, since they couldn't reach it, set about drinking the water dry to open a path — and choked themselves. And what Crates said of the writings of Heraclitus fits perfectly here: they needed a reader who was a good swimmer, so that the depth and weight of the learning wouldn't pull him under and drown him.

It is nothing but personal weakness that makes us settle for what others — or we ourselves — have found in this hunt after knowledge. An abler mind will not settle. There is always room for someone to go further — yes, even for ourselves — and always another road. There is no end to our inquiries; our end is in the other world. When the mind is satisfied, that is a sign it has grown shortsighted, or grown tired. No generous mind stops inside itself. It keeps pressing on past its strength; it leaps beyond what it can actually do. If it doesn't advance and push and back up and crash about and turn, it is only half alive. Its pursuits have no limit and no form. Its food is wonder, the chase, ambiguity — which Apollo showed clearly enough by always speaking to us in a double, obscure, and sideways sense: not feeding us, but keeping us busy and guessing. It is an irregular, perpetual motion, without model and without aim. Its inventions kindle one another, chase one another, breed one another. As my friend Étienne de La Boétie wrote: in a running stream you watch one wave roll after another without end, each pursuing the one ahead, each fleeing the one behind — water forever flowing into water, always the same brook, but always different water.

There is more business in interpreting the interpretations than in interpreting the things themselves, and more books about books than about any other subject. We do nothing but write comments on one another. The whole place swarms with commentaries; of authors there is a great scarcity. Isn't the chief and most celebrated learning of our age the learning of how to understand the learned? Isn't that the common and final goal of all study? Our opinions are grafted one upon another. The first serves as rootstock for the second, the second for the third, and so we climb the ladder rung by rung — which is how it happens that the man perched highest often has more honor than merit, since he has climbed only one inch up the shoulders of the man below him.

How often, and perhaps how foolishly, have I stretched my own book to make it talk about itself? Foolishly, if only because it should remind me of what I say about others who do the same thing: that all those loving glances they keep throwing at their work prove their hearts are pounding with self-love, and that even the disdainful severity with which they whip their books is just a mother's fussing and fondling in disguise — like Aristotle, in whom valuing himself and undervaluing himself often spring from the same air of arrogance. My excuse is that I ought to have more liberty here than others, since I write specifically about myself and my writings, just as I do about my other actions — my subject turns back upon itself. But I don't know whether everyone will accept the excuse.

I noticed in Germany that Luther has left behind as many quarrels and disputes over the uncertainties of his opinions as he ever raised about Holy Scripture — and more. Our argument is an argument about words. I ask what nature is, what pleasure is, what a circle is, what substitution is. The question is made of words, and it is paid in the same coin. A stone is a body. But press further — and what is a body? A substance. And what is a substance? — and so on, until you chase the answerer to the back page of his dictionary. We swap one word for another, and often for one less understood. I know better what "man" is than I know what "animal" is, or "mortal," or "rational." To resolve one doubt, they hand me three: it's the Hydra's head. Socrates asked Meno what virtue was. "There is," said Meno, "the virtue of a man and of a woman, of a magistrate and of a private citizen, of an old man and of a child." "Wonderful," cried Socrates. "We went looking for one virtue, and you've brought us a whole swarm." We ask one question and they hand us back a hive.

Just as no event and no face is exactly like another, none is entirely different either: an ingenious mixture on nature's part. If our faces were not alike, we couldn't tell man from beast; if they were not unlike, we couldn't tell one man from another. All things hold together by some resemblance; every example limps; and any rule drawn from experience is always lame and imperfect. Still, comparisons can be fastened together at one corner or another — and that is how the laws are made to serve, stretched to fit each of our affairs by some twisted, biased, forced interpretation.

Since the ethical laws — the ones that concern each person's private duty to himself — are so hard to frame, as we see they are, it's no wonder the laws that govern multitudes of people are harder still. Just look at the form of this justice that rules over us: it is a standing testimony of human weakness, it is so full of error and contradiction. What we find in justice by way of leniency here and harshness there — and we find so much of both that I doubt the middle ground turns up as often — these are diseased and unjust limbs of the very body and essence of justice.

Some peasants have just come running to tell me that a moment ago they left, in a forest of mine, a man with a hundred wounds on him, still breathing, who begged them for water for pity's sake and for help to carry him somewhere. They tell me they didn't dare go near him and ran away, for fear the officers of justice would catch them there — for, as happens to anyone found near a murdered man, they would have been called to account for the incident, to their total ruin, having neither money nor friends to defend their innocence. What could I say to these people? It is certain that this act of humanity would have gotten them into trouble.

How many innocent people have we known to be punished — and through no fault of the judge — and how many that we never heard about? Here is something that happened in my own time. Certain men were condemned to death for a murder; the sentence was, if not pronounced, at least settled and decided. At just that moment, the judges are informed by the officers of a lower court nearby that they are holding some prisoners who have flatly confessed to that murder and shed unmistakable light on every detail of the deed. And yet the court gravely deliberated whether to suspend the execution of the sentence already passed on the first defendants. They weighed the novelty of the precedent, and the consequences of reversing judgments; they noted that the sentence had been passed and the judges were beyond the reach of second thoughts. In the end, these poor devils were sacrificed to the forms of justice. Philip, or some other king, handled a similar bind better. He had condemned a man to pay another man a heavy fine, in a final judgment. The truth came out later, and he found he had judged wrongly. On one side stood the merits of the case; on the other, the claims of judicial form. He satisfied both, more or less: he let the sentence stand and made up the condemned man's loss from his own purse. But he was dealing with a fixable wrong. My men were unfixably hanged. How many condemnations have I seen that were more criminal than the crime?

All this brings to mind the old opinions: that a man is forced to do wrong in the small change if he wants to do right in the large, and to commit injustice in little things if he wants to achieve justice in great ones; that human justice is built on the model of medicine, by which whatever works is also just and honest; that — as the Stoics held — Nature herself acts against justice in most of her own works; and — as the Cyrenaics held — that nothing is just in itself, but customs and laws make justice; and — as the Theodorians had it — that theft, sacrilege, and every kind of debauchery are just in a wise man, if he knows they will profit him.

There's no help for it: I stand where Alcibiades stood. I will never, if I can help it, put myself into the hands of a man who can decide about my head — where my life and honor depend more on the skill and diligence of my attorney than on my own innocence. I'd take my chances with a justice that would take note of my good deeds as well as my bad, where I had as much to hope for as to fear. Not being punished is poor pay for a man who has done better than merely not doing wrong. Our justice offers us only one of her hands, and the left one at that. Whoever you are, you come away with a loss.

In China — a kingdom whose government and arts, without any commerce with ours or knowledge of ours, outdo our best examples in several ways, and whose history teaches me how much bigger and more various the world is than either the ancients or we have managed to grasp — the officers sent by the prince to inspect the state of his provinces punish those who have abused their office, but they also generously reward those who have conducted themselves better than the run of men, beyond the bare requirements of duty. People come before these inspectors not just to be cleared but to gain something; not merely to be paid, but to be given a present.

No judge, thank God, has ever yet addressed me as a judge, in any cause whatever, my own or a third party's, criminal or civil. No prison has ever received me, even for a stroll. Imagination makes the very outside of a jail unpleasant to me. I am so in love with liberty that if someone forbade me access to one corner of the Indies, I would live a little less at ease for it. And as long as I can find earth or open air anywhere else, I will never sit hiding in a place where I have to hide. My God, how badly I would bear the condition I see so many people in — nailed to one corner of the kingdom, barred from the principal cities and the courts and the use of the public roads, for having quarreled with our laws! If the laws I live under so much as wagged a finger at me in threat, I would immediately go find others, wherever they might be. All my little prudence in these civil wars of ours goes into making sure they don't cut off my freedom to come and go.

Now, laws keep up their credit not because they are just, but because they are laws. That is the mystic foundation of their authority; they have no other — and it serves them well enough. They are often made by fools; more often by men who, hating equality, fail in equity; but always by men — vain and wavering authors. Nothing is so grossly, so widely, or so regularly faulty as the laws. Whoever obeys them because they are just does not obey them for the reason he ought. Our French laws, by their irregularity and deformity, rather lend a hand to the disorder and corruption that show up in their application and enforcement. The command is so confused and inconsistent that it goes some way toward excusing both disobedience and the faults of those who interpret, administer, and observe it.

Whatever fruit we may pluck from experience, the kind we draw from foreign examples will do little for our education if we make so poor a use of the experience we have of ourselves — which is more familiar to us, and certainly sufficient to teach us what we need. I study myself more than any other subject. That is my metaphysics; that is my physics. Let others ask what god steers this great house of the world, where the moon goes when it wanes and how its horns fill out again, where the winds get their power over the sea, where the clouds find their endless supply of water, and whether a day is coming that will bring the world's towers down — as Propertius wonders. "Ask that," says Lucan, "you whom the cares of the world torment."

In that great universal scheme, I let myself be carried along, ignorantly and carelessly, by the general law of the world. I will know that law well enough when I feel it. My learning cannot make it change course; it will not alter itself for my sake. It is folly to hope it might, and greater folly to fret about it, since the law is necessarily the same for everyone, public and common. The goodness and capacity of the governor ought to release us, completely, from worrying about the government. Philosophical probings and speculations serve no purpose but to feed our curiosity. The philosophers, quite rightly, refer us back to the rules of nature; but those rules have no use for such exalted knowledge. The philosophers falsify them, showing us nature's face painted in colors too high and too artificial — which is why we get so many different portraits of so uniform a subject.
