As nature has given us feet to walk with, so she has given us prudence to guide us through life: not the clever, muscular, showy prudence of the philosophers' invention, but a prudence that is easy, quiet, and healthy, and that does very well what the other only promises — in anyone lucky enough to know how to use it honestly and steadily, which is to say, naturally. To entrust yourself to nature most simply is to entrust yourself most wisely. Oh, what a soft, easy, wholesome pillow is ignorance and incuriosity, for resting a well-made head!

I would rather understand myself well in myself than in Cicero. In the experience I have of myself I find enough to make me wise — if only I were a good student. Whoever calls to mind the last excess of his anger, and how far that fever carried him, sees the ugliness of the passion better than in Aristotle, and hates it more justly. Whoever remembers the troubles he has been through, and the ones that merely threatened him, and the trivial causes that have moved him from one state to another, prepares himself by it for the changes to come and for knowing his own condition. The life of Caesar holds no greater lesson for us than our own. Emperor's life or common man's, it is still a life subject to every human accident. Let us only listen to it: we tell ourselves everything we chiefly need to hear.

A man who remembers how many, many times he has been wrong in his own judgment — isn't he a fool if he doesn't distrust it forever after? When someone else's reasoning convinces me I've held a false opinion, what I learn isn't so much the new thing he's told me, or my ignorance of that particular point — that would be a small purchase. I learn, in general, my own weakness and the treachery of my understanding — and from that I draw the reformation of the whole mass. With all my other errors I do the same, and I find the rule of great use in life. I don't treat the species or the instance as one stone I happened to trip over; I learn to distrust my footing everywhere, and I take care to plant it well. To learn that you have said or done a foolish thing is nothing; you must learn that you are nothing but a fool — a far broader and more important lesson.

The false steps my memory has so often made, even when it was most sure of itself, are not wasted on me. Let it swear and promise now till it's blue; I shake my ears. The first objection raised against its testimony throws me into doubt, and I wouldn't dare trust it in anything serious, or vouch for it in anyone else's business. And if it weren't that what I do from lack of memory, others do still more often from lack of good faith, I would always, in matters of fact, take the truth from another man's mouth rather than my own.

If everyone would watch closely the workings and the circumstances of the passions that rule him, as I have watched the ones I'm most prone to, he would see them coming and would slow their charge a little. They don't always leap at our throat without warning; there are threats first, and stages — as Virgil says, the sea first whitens under the wind, then swells little by little, lifting its waves higher, until it rises from the bottom of the deep to the sky.

In me, judgment occupies the magistrate's bench — or at least it tries hard to. It lets my appetites run their own course, including hatred and friendship — even the friendship I bear myself — without altering or corrupting them. If it can't reform the other parts to match its own model, at least it doesn't let itself be corrupted by them. It plays its game apart.

The advice to each of us to know ourselves must be important advice, since the god of wisdom and light had it written across the front of his temple at Delphi, as if it contained everything he had to tell us. Plato says, too, that prudence is nothing but the carrying out of that command, and Socrates proves it point by point in Xenophon. In any science, only those who have entered into it can see its difficulties and its dark places, for it takes a certain degree of intelligence to be able to know that you don't know — you must push on a door to find out whether it's bolted against you. Hence the Platonic subtlety: that those who know need not inquire, since they know; and those who don't know cannot inquire, since to inquire you must know what you're inquiring after. So with this matter of knowing yourself. The fact that everyone is seen to be so decided and so satisfied on the point, that everyone thinks he understands himself well enough, shows that no one understands anything about it — as Socrates teaches Euthydemus.

I, who profess nothing else, find in myself a depth and a variety so endless that all the fruit of my learning is to make me feel how much remains to learn. To my weakness, so often confessed, I owe my leaning toward modesty, toward obedience to the beliefs prescribed for me, toward a steady coolness and moderation in my opinions — and my hatred of that obnoxious, quarrelsome arrogance that believes and trusts wholly in itself, the capital enemy of discipline and truth. Just listen to them lay down the law: the first foolishness they utter comes out in the tone of men founding religions and handing down codes:

"Nothing is more shameful," as Cicero says, "than to let assertion and approval run ahead of knowledge and perception."

Aristarchus said that in ancient times you could scarcely find seven wise men in the world, and in his own day scarcely as many fools. Don't we have more reason to say that of our age than he had of his? Assertion and stubbornness are sure signs of stupidity. Here is a fellow who has fallen flat on his nose a hundred times in one day, and there he is back at his therefores, as resolute and sturdy as ever. You'd think some new soul, some fresh vigor of understanding had been poured into him since — that he was like that ancient son of the earth who, as Lucan tells it, recovered fresh strength in his broken limbs every time he touched his mother ground. Does this incorrigible blockhead imagine he picks up a new mind by picking up a new argument?

It's from my own experience that I accuse human ignorance — and that, in my opinion, is the surest lesson in the whole school of the world. Those who won't conclude it in themselves from so empty an example as mine, or their own, let them take it from Socrates, the master of masters. The philosopher Antisthenes used to tell his disciples, "Come, let's go hear Socrates; there I'll be a pupil along with you." And while he held the Stoic doctrine that virtue alone was enough to make a life completely happy, needing nothing else whatever, he added: "except the strength of Socrates."

That long attention I spend studying myself trains me to judge other people tolerably well, too — and there are few things I speak of better, or with a better excuse. I often manage to see my friends' qualities more precisely than they do themselves. I have astonished some of them with the accuracy of my description, and put them on notice about themselves. Because I've been used since childhood to seeing my own life mirrored in other people's, I have acquired a studious bent that way; and when I'm paying attention, I let little around me escape that serves the purpose — faces, moods, talk. I study it all: what I should avoid and what I should follow.

In my friends, then, I discover their inward inclinations by what they produce outwardly. But I don't sort this endless variety of scattered, disconnected actions into species and chapters, distributing my parcels neatly under known headings and classes — for, as Virgil says, there's no counting how many kinds there are, or what their names might be.

The wise deliver their ideas more specifically, piece by piece. I, who can see no further into things than experience shows me, present mine loosely, without rules, by trial — in disjointed installments, as something that cannot be said all at once and in a lump. Coherence and consistency are not to be found in low, common souls like ours. Wisdom is a solid, complete building, every piece in its place, every piece bearing its mark; "wisdom alone is wholly turned in upon itself," as Cicero says. I leave it to the experts — and I'm not sure even they can pull it off, in a business so tangled, so minute, and so governed by chance — to marshal this infinite diversity of faces into distinct bodies, to pin down our inconstancy and set it in order. I find it hard not only to connect our actions one to another, but even to label each one by itself with any single ruling quality — that's how ambiguous and many-colored they look under different lights.

What was remarked as rare in Perseus, king of Macedon — that his mind, fixing itself to no one condition, wandered through every kind of life, displaying manners so wild and erratic that neither he nor anyone else knew what sort of man he was — seems to me to fit nearly the whole world. And I have known one man especially, of his very stamp, to whom the conclusion applies even better: no middle setting anywhere, always careering from one extreme to the other for reasons nobody could guess; no path without crossings and astonishing contradictions; no single quality pure and unmixed. The best guess anyone will ever make about him is that he worked at making himself known by being unknowable.

You need sound ears to hear yourself frankly judged. And since few of us can stand to hear it without being stung, those who risk doing it for us show a rare effect of friendship. It is genuinely loving someone to be willing to wound and offend him for his own good. I find it harsh to judge a man whose bad qualities outnumber his good ones. Plato requires three things in anyone who would examine another man's soul: knowledge, goodwill, boldness.

I used to be asked, sometimes, what I would have thought myself fit for, if anyone had wanted to make use of me while I was still the right age — "while better blood gave me vigor," as Virgil has it, "before envious old age frosted my temples."

"For nothing," I said. And I'm happy to excuse myself from knowing anything that enslaves me to other people. But I would have told my master the truth, and regulated his conduct, if he'd allowed it — not wholesale, by schoolroom lessons, which I don't understand (and from which I see no true reform spring in those who do understand them), but by watching him at leisure, on every occasion, judging him simply and naturally, as an eyewitness, point by point; letting him know plainly how he stood in common opinion, against the chorus of his flatterers. There's not one of us who wouldn't be worse than kings if we were corrupted as constantly as they are by that breed of riffraff. How could it be otherwise, if even Alexander, that great king and philosopher, couldn't defend himself from them?

I would have had fidelity enough for the job, and judgment, and independence. It would have to be an office without a name — otherwise it loses its grace and its effect — and it's not a part just anyone can play. Even truth doesn't have the privilege of being spoken at all hours and in every manner; noble as its use is, it has its limits and discretions. It often happens, the way the world goes, that a man drops truth into a prince's ear not just uselessly but harmfully, even unjustly. No one will convince me that a virtuous rebuke cannot be viciously delivered, or that the interest of the substance should never give way to the interest of the form.

For such work I'd want a man content with his fortune — "pleased with what he is, and wanting nothing more," as Martial puts it — and of middling rank. On the one hand, he wouldn't be afraid to touch his master's heart to the quick for fear of losing his place; on the other, being of no great station, he'd have easier dealings with all sorts of people. And I would confine this office to one man only; to spread the privilege of such liberty and familiarity among many would breed a damaging irreverence. Of that one man, above all, I would require the fidelity of silence.

A king is not to be believed when he brags of his firmness in standing the shock of the enemy for his glory's sake if, for his own profit and improvement, he can't stand the freedom of a friend's advice — which has no power beyond pinching his ear, everything else remaining in his own hands. Now, no class of men needs true and frank advice as badly as kings do. They sustain a public life; they have to satisfy the opinion of a great crowd of spectators; and since everyone around them hides whatever might turn them from their course, they find themselves, without noticing, sunk in the hatred and loathing of their people — often over things they could have avoided at no cost even to their pleasures, if only someone had warned and corrected them in time. Their favorites usually look out for themselves more than for the master; and it pays them to, since in truth most offices of real friendship, when tried on a sovereign, are rough and dangerous experiments. What's needed there is not only great affection and frankness, but courage too.

In short, this whole hodgepodge I'm scribbling here is nothing but a register of the trials of my own life — which, as far as inward health goes, is exemplary enough, provided you take its instruction against the grain. But as for bodily health, no man can offer more useful experience than I can, because I present it pure, not corrupted or doctored by art or theory. In the matter of medicine, experience is on its own dunghill, and reason yields it the whole field. Tiberius said that anyone who had lived twenty years should be answerable to himself for what hurt or helped him, and should know how to manage himself without medicine. He might have learned that from Socrates, who advised his disciples to make their health a chief study, adding that it would be strange if a man of sense, attending to his exercise and his diet, didn't understand better than any doctor what was good or bad for him.

And medicine itself professes that experience is the test of all its operations. So Plato was right to say that to be a true physician, a man should first have passed through all the diseases he means to cure, and all the accidents and conditions he is to judge of. It's only fair they should catch the pox if they want to know how to cure it. I would honestly trust myself to such hands. The others merely guide us, like the man who paints seas and rocks and harbors while sitting at his table, sailing his model ship there in perfect safety — but put him to the real work and he doesn't know which end to begin at. They describe our diseases the way a town crier describes a lost horse or dog: such a coat, such a height, such an ear — but bring the animal to him and he can't pick it out for all that. If medicine ever gives me some real, visible relief, I will cry out in good earnest, with Horace, "show me a science that works, and I'll take its hand."

The arts that promise to keep our bodies and souls in health promise the most of all — and none keep their promise less. And in our time, those who profess these arts among us show their effects less than any other sort of men. The most you can say of them is that they sell medicinal drugs; that they are physicians, you cannot say.

I have lived long enough to give an account of the regimen that has carried me this far. For anyone who wants to try it, I've been his taster: I have run the experiment. Here are a few of the articles, as memory supplies them. I have no habit that hasn't varied with circumstances; I only record the ones I've known best, the ones that have held the most possession of me up to now.

My way of life is the same in sickness as in health. The same bed, the same hours, the same food, even the same drink serve me in both conditions; I add nothing but a little more or less moderation, according to my strength and appetite. Health, for me, means keeping my accustomed state undisturbed. I see that sickness knocks me off it on one side, and that if I let the doctors have their way, they'll knock me off it on the other — so that between fortune and art, I'm forced clean off my road. The thing I believe most certainly is this: that I cannot be hurt by the use of things I have been so long accustomed to.

It's custom's business to give a man's life whatever shape she pleases; in that, she is everything — she is Circe's potion, changing our nature however she likes. How many nations, only three steps from us, think the fear of the evening damp — which so obviously hurts us — a ridiculous fancy; and our own boatmen and peasants laugh at it too. You make a German sick by laying him on a mattress, an Italian by laying him on a featherbed, and a Frenchman by leaving him without curtains and a fire. A Spanish stomach can't hold out at eating the way we do, nor ours at drinking like the Swiss.

A German amused me greatly at Augsburg by attacking our open hearths with the very arguments we always use against their stoves. To tell the truth, that smothered heat, and the smell of the heated stuff that gives it off, do bother people who aren't used to them — though not me. And since their heat is even, constant, and spread everywhere, without flame, without smoke, and without the wind that comes pouring down our chimneys, their stoves stand up to comparison with our fires in plenty of ways. Why don't we imitate the Roman architecture? They say that in the old days fires were built not inside the house but outside, at its foot, and the heat was carried through the whole building by pipes laid inside the walls, winding around the rooms to be warmed — I have seen this plainly described somewhere in Seneca. This German, hearing me praise the conveniences and beauties of his city — which it truly deserves — began pitying me for having to leave; and the first hardship he named was the heaviness of head the chimneys elsewhere would bring on me. He had heard somebody make that complaint, and fastened it on us — custom having robbed him of the means of noticing it at home. Any heat that comes from a fire weakens and dulls me. Evenus said fire is life's best seasoning; I'd rather warm myself any other way.

We are afraid of wine from the bottom of the cask; in Portugal those fumes are counted delicious — it's the drink of princes. In short, every nation has plenty of customs and usages that are not merely unknown to other nations, but savage and miraculous in their sight.

What should we do with people who accept no evidence unless it's in print, who won't believe men unless they're in a book, or truth unless it's of a respectable age? We dignify our foolishness when we send it to the press. "I have read such a thing" carries a great deal more weight than "I have heard such a thing." But I don't distrust a man's mouth any more than his pen; I know men write as carelessly as they speak; I value this age as if it were already a past one; and I quote a friend as readily as Aulus Gellius or Macrobius, and what I've seen as readily as what they wrote. As they hold of virtue that it is not greater for having lasted longer, so I hold of truth that it is none the wiser for being older. I often say it's pure folly that sets us chasing foreign and bookish examples; the supply is just as rich now as it was in the days of Homer and Plato. Isn't the truth that we want honor from the citation rather than truth in the matter at hand — as if proof borrowed from the print shops of Vascosan or Plantin counted for more than what's there to be seen in our own village? Or else we simply lack the wit to sift what's in front of us and judge it clearly enough to make an example of it. For if we say we lack the authority to make our testimony believed, we're missing the point. In my opinion, the most ordinary, common, familiar things — if we could only find their light — would furnish the greatest miracles of nature and the most wonderful examples, especially on the subject of human actions.

Now, on that subject — setting aside what I've gathered from books, and what Aristotle says of Andron the Argive, who crossed the parched sands of Libya without drinking — a gentleman who has acquitted himself well in several offices said, in company where I was present, that he had ridden from Madrid to Lisbon in the heat of summer without drinking at all. He is healthy and vigorous for his age, and there is nothing unusual in his way of living except this: he can go two or three months, even a whole year, so he has told me, without drinking. He feels thirsty sometimes, but he lets it pass; he holds that thirst is an appetite which fades easily on its own, and he drinks more from whim than from need or pleasure.

Here is another. Not long ago I found one of the most learned men in France — a man of considerable fortune, too — studying in a corner of a hall they had partitioned off for him with a tapestry, while a rabble of his servants carried on around him at full license. He told me — and Seneca says nearly the same of himself — that he turned the racket to profit: battered by the noise, he gathered and withdrew into himself all the more for contemplation, and the storm of voices drove his thoughts inward. As a student at Padua he had kept his study so long amid the rattle of coaches and the tumult of the square that he trained himself not just to ignore the noise but to use it, in the service of his studies. Socrates, when Alcibiades marveled at how he could endure his wife's perpetual scolding, answered: "the way men do who are used to the constant creak of the water-wheel." I am just the opposite. I have a tender head, easily ruffled; when it's bent on something, the least buzzing of a fly murders it.

Seneca in his youth, having warmly taken up the example of Sextius and resolved to eat nothing that had been killed, went a whole year without such food — with pleasure, he said — and gave it up only so he wouldn't be suspected of borrowing the rule from certain new religions that prescribed it. He likewise adopted, from the precepts of Attalus, the custom of never lying on bedding that yielded under his weight, and even into old age used the kind that doesn't give. What the usage of his day made men count as hardness, the usage of ours makes us count as softness.

Just look at the difference between my laborers' way of living and my own: the Scythians and the Indians are no more remote from my capacity and my form. I have taken on charity boys as servants, who quit my kitchen and my livery soon after, just to get back to their old life; I found one of them afterwards picking mussels out of the sewer for his dinner, and neither pleading nor threats could pull him away from the sweetness he found in poverty. Beggars have their splendors and their luxuries, just like the rich — and, people say, their ranks and their politics.

These are the effects of custom. She can mold us not only into whatever form she pleases — the sages say we should apply ourselves to the best one, and she will soon make it easy for us — but also into change and variety, which is the noblest and most useful lesson she teaches. The best of my bodily qualities is that I'm flexible and not very stubborn. I have inclinations that are more my own, more usual, and more agreeable to me than others; but it takes very little to turn me from them, and I slip easily into the opposite course. A young man ought to cross his own rules now and then, to wake up his vigor and keep it from going stale and rusty. There is no way of life so weak and silly as one run entirely by rule and discipline — like Juvenal's man, who consults the almanac before letting himself be carried out to the first milestone, and if the corner of his eye itches, won't reach for ointment until he has checked his horoscope.

If he'll take my advice, a young man should even throw himself into excess once in a while; otherwise the smallest indulgence will lay him flat, and he'll make himself tiresome and disagreeable in company. The worst quality in a well-bred man is fussiness — being bound to one particular way of doing things; and it is particular, if it isn't pliable and supple. It's a kind of disgrace not to be able, or not to dare, to do what you see everyone around you doing — people like that should stay home. In any man it's unbecoming; in a soldier it's a vice, and intolerable: a soldier, as Philopoemen said, should accustom himself to every variety and unevenness of life.

Though I was brought up, as far as possible, for liberty and independence, still — growing old, and having settled by sheer indifference into certain forms (my age is past schooling now; it has nothing left to do but hold itself together as best it can) — custom has already stamped her character into me, in certain things, so deeply that I'd call it excess to give them up. Without doing violence to myself, I cannot sleep in the daytime, or eat between meals, or eat breakfast, or go to bed without a long gap — three hours or so — between supper and sleep; cannot make children except before sleep, and never standing; cannot bear my own sweat; cannot quench my thirst with plain water or with wine unmixed; cannot keep my head bare for long, or get my hair cut after dinner. I'd be as uncomfortable without my gloves as without my shirt, or going unwashed when I rise from the table or my bed; and I couldn't sleep without canopy and curtains, as if they were necessities. I could dine without a tablecloth; but without a clean napkin, German style, only with real discomfort — I soil napkins more than the Germans or the Italians do, making little use of either spoon or fork. I'm sorry we never kept up the fashion, begun on the example of kings, of changing napkins with each course the way we change plates. We're told of Marius, that hard-bitten old soldier, that as he aged he grew fussy about his drink and would only use one particular cup of his own. I have let myself develop the same fancy for a certain shape of glass, and I don't like drinking from a common glass any more than from a common hand. All metal offends me next to clear, transparent material: let my eyes have their taste too, as far as they're able.

Several such daintinesses I owe to custom. Nature, for her part, has contributed some of her own: I cannot handle more than two full meals a day without overloading my stomach, nor skip one of them entirely without filling up with wind, drying out my mouth, and deadening my appetite. I take real harm from too much evening air. These last few years, on night marches — and they often run the whole night — after five or six hours my stomach turns queasy, my head starts pounding violently, and I never make it to daybreak without vomiting. When the others go to breakfast, I go to sleep; and when I get up, I'm as brisk and cheerful as before.

I had always been told that the night damp rises only at nightfall; but in recent years the long and familiar company of a lord — a man convinced that the damp is sharpest and most dangerous as the sun declines, an hour or two before it sets, and who carefully avoids that hour while shrugging off the night's — nearly stamped on me not so much his reasoning as his sensations. What — shall mere doubt and inquiry strike our imagination hard enough to change us? Those who give way to these tendencies all at once and headlong bring total ruin on themselves. And I am sorry for the several gentlemen who, through the folly of their doctors, have shut themselves up indoors while still young and healthy. Better to endure a cough than to lose, forever and through disuse, your commerce with common life in things of such great use. Malignant science, that forbids us the pleasantest hours of the day! Let us keep possession to the last. Mostly, a man toughens himself by being stubborn, and corrects his constitution — as Caesar did his falling sickness — by sheer contempt. A man should commit himself to the best rules, but not enslave himself to them, except to the kind (if any such exist) where obligation and servitude actually pay.

Kings and philosophers go to stool, and ladies too. Public lives are bound to ceremony; mine, obscure and private, enjoys every natural dispensation — and soldier and Gascon, besides, are qualities a little prone to indiscretion. So I'll say this about the act of relieving nature: it's good to assign it to certain set, nighttime hours, and to force yourself to that by custom, as I have done. But don't enslave yourself, as I have done in my declining years, to a particular comfort of place and seat for the purpose, making it a burden through long sitting. And yet, in the fouler offices, isn't there some excuse for demanding extra care and cleanliness? "Man is by nature a clean and delicate animal," as Seneca says. Of all the actions of nature, that is the one I am most impatient to have interrupted. I've seen many a soldier troubled by an unruly belly; mine and I never miss our appointment, which is at the leap out of bed — unless some urgent business or some sickness intrudes.

So, as I was saying, I think sick men can put themselves nowhere safer than sitting tight in the course of life they were bred and trained to. Change, whatever it is, unsettles and damages. Do you believe chestnuts can hurt a man of Périgord or of Lucca, or milk and cheese the mountain folk? Yet we prescribe them a way of life that's not just new but contrary — a change the healthy themselves couldn't endure. Order water for a seventy-year-old Breton; shut a sailor in a stove-heated room; forbid a Basque footman to walk. You are stripping them of motion, and in the end of air and of light.

Is life worth that much? To keep ourselves alive we are made to give up the very things we live by — to live, we stop living. As the poet asks, can you call a man alive when the air he breathes and the light he steers by have been turned into burdens?

If the doctors' regimens do no other good, they do at least this much: they get their patients ready for death early, by undermining the use of life and trimming it away little by little.

Sick or well, I have always willingly obeyed the appetites that pressed on me. I give my desires and inclinations a long rein. I don't like curing one disease with another, and I hate remedies that are more trouble than the sickness itself. To suffer from colic and to be forbidden oysters is two evils instead of one: the disease torments you from one side, the diet from the other. Since we're always in danger of guessing wrong anyway, let's at least risk our mistake after we've had the pleasure. The world goes the opposite way and counts nothing as beneficial unless it hurts; ease makes it deeply suspicious.

My appetite, in any number of things, has fit itself to the health of my stomach quite happily on its own. Sharp, spicy sauces pleased me when I was young; when my stomach later turned against them, my taste followed at once. Wine is bad for the sick — and it's the first thing my mouth rejects when I'm ill, with an unconquerable distaste. Whatever I take against my inclination harms me; nothing I eat with appetite and delight ever does. I have never been hurt by any action that gave me real pleasure, and so I've made every medical pronouncement yield, generously, to my enjoyment.

When I was young — back when Cupid fluttered about me, as the poet says, splendid in his saffron tunic — I gave myself the rein as freely and recklessly as any man alive to whatever desire had hold of me. I soldiered in those wars not without glory, as the line goes — though my strength lay more in lasting out the campaign than in the charge: I can scarcely remember six bouts in a night.

It is a misfortune and a marvel at once to confess how young I was when I first came under love's power. It was pure chance — long before the age of choice or knowledge. I can't remember myself back that far. My case might be paired with Quartilla's, who couldn't remember ever having been a maid. Hence, as the poet says, the early hair, and the beard that astonished my mother.

Doctors bend their rules to fit the violent cravings of their patients, and usually with good success; no craving can be so strange and wrong-headed that nature doesn't have some hand in it. And besides, how small a thing it is to satisfy the imagination! In my opinion that faculty matters most of all — at least more than any other. The heaviest and most common evils are the ones imagination loads onto us. There's a Spanish saying I like from several angles: Defienda me Dios de mí — "God defend me from myself."

When I'm sick, I'm sorry not to have some craving I could enjoy satisfying; all the rules of medicine would hardly keep me from it. I do the same when I'm well: I can see little more left to hope or wish for. It would be pitiful to be so weak and faded that even wishing is beyond you.

The art of medicine is not so settled that we can't find authority for whatever we choose to do. It changes with climates and changes with moons; it changes from one famous doctor to the next — Fernel says one thing, Scaliger another. If your physician thinks it bad for you to sleep, to drink wine, or to eat such-and-such, don't worry: I'll find you another who disagrees with him. The variety of medical arguments and opinions covers every shape a thing can take. I saw one poor sick man panting and burning with thirst — denied water in order to be cured — and laughed at afterward for his pains by a second physician, who condemned the first one's advice as harmful. Hadn't he tortured himself to good purpose? A man of that profession died lately of kidney stones, having used extreme fasting to fight his disease; his fellow physicians say the fasting was exactly what dried him up and baked the gravel in his kidneys.

I've noticed that in wounds and in sickness alike, talking unsettles and hurts me as much as any excess I could commit. My voice is loud and effortful, and it pains and tires me — so much so that when I've gone to whisper in some great man's ear about serious business, he has often asked me to lower my voice.

Here's a story worth the detour. Someone in a Greek school was speaking loudly, the way I do, and the master of ceremonies sent word for him to speak more softly. "Then have him send me," the man replied, "the tone he wants me to use." The answer came back: take your tone from the ears of the person you're speaking to. That was well said — if it meant "suit your voice to the business you have with your listener." But if it meant "it's enough that he hears you; let him set your volume," I don't see the sense of it. The tone and movement of my voice carry a great part of my meaning, and I'm the one who has to govern them to make myself understood. There is a voice for teaching, a voice for flattering, a voice for scolding. I want my voice not just to reach a man but, on occasion, to strike him and pierce him. When I'm dressing down my valet in sharp and bitter language, it would be a fine thing for him to say, "Speak lower, master — I hear you perfectly well." There is a voice fitted to the hearing, as the rhetorician says, not by its loudness but by its rightness. Speech belongs half to the speaker and half to the listener. The listener should prepare to receive it the way it comes — as in tennis, where the man receiving the ball shifts and sets himself according to the motions of the server and the character of the stroke.

Experience has also taught me this: we ruin ourselves by impatience. Evils have their life spans and their limits — their sicknesses and their recoveries.

Diseases are built on the pattern of animals: they have their fortunes and their allotted days from birth. Whoever tries to bully one short by force in the middle of its course only lengthens and multiplies it, and provokes it instead of calming it. I'm of Crantor's opinion: we should neither stubbornly stonewall our evils nor collapse under them for lack of spine, but give way to them naturally, according to their condition and ours. We should grant diseases free passage. I find they stay a shorter time with me because I let them alone; I have shed some of the most stubborn and tenacious kinds, the ones with the worst reputations, by their own decay — without help, without art, and against all of art's rules. Let nature have her way for a while; she understands her business better than we do. But so-and-so died of it! So will you — if not of that disease, then of another. And how many people have failed to escape dying with three doctors at their tails? Example is a vague and universal mirror, reflecting in every direction. If a medicine is delicious, take it: that much, at least, is present good. I will never balk at the name or the color if the thing is pleasant going down — pleasure is one of the chief varieties of profit.

I have allowed colds, gouty discharges, looseness, palpitations of the heart, migraines, and other ailments to grow old in me and die a natural death. I lost them just as I was getting half-fond of keeping them. Courtesy persuades them away faster than bluster. We have to bear the laws of our condition patiently. We are born to grow old, to grow weak, and to be sick, despite all medicine. It's the first lesson the Mexicans teach their children: the moment they come out of the womb they greet them with, "Child, you have come into the world to endure. Endure, suffer, and say nothing." It is unjust to complain that what may happen to anyone has happened to you. Be angry, says Seneca, when something is decreed unjustly against you alone.

Look at an old man begging God to keep his health whole and vigorous — that is, asking to be made young again. Fool, why these childish wishes, doomed in advance? His condition no longer allows it. Gout, kidney stones, indigestion — these are the symptoms of long years, the way heat, rain, and wind are the symptoms of long journeys. Plato doesn't believe Aesculapius bothered prescribing regimens to drag out life in a weak and wasted body, useless to its country and its calling, incapable of fathering strong and healthy children. He doesn't think such fussing fits the justice and prudence of God, which directs all things toward use. My good friend, your business is done; nobody can restore you. At most they can patch you and prop you a little, and stretch your misery out an hour or two — like a man who shores up a collapsing house with prop after prop, until the appointed day when house, props, and all come down together.

We must learn to endure what we cannot escape. Our life is composed of contrary things, like the harmony of the world — different tones, sweet and harsh, sharp and flat, lively and solemn. What could a musician do who loved only some of them? He has to know how to use them all and mix them. And so must we mix the goods and evils that are of one substance with our life. Our being cannot survive without that mixture, and the one half is no less necessary to it than the other. Trying to fight natural necessity is to reenact the folly of Ctesiphon, who got into a kicking contest with his mule.

I consult almost nobody about the changes I feel in myself, because these doctors take advantage of you. Once they have you at their mercy, they stuff your ears with prognoses. Catching me once weakened by sickness, they handled me insultingly with their dogmas and their schoolmaster scowls — threatening me now with great pains, now with approaching death. I was shaken by it, I admit, but not beaten, not knocked off my ground; my judgment was neither changed nor confused, but it was disturbed. And that is always agitation and struggle.

Now I handle my imagination as gently as I can, and would relieve it, if I could, of all strain and conflict. You have to help it along, flatter it, fool it if you can. My mind is well suited to the job — it never lacks for plausible arguments. If it could persuade as well as it preaches, it would set me up perfectly.

Would you like an example? My mind tells me that it's for my own good that I have kidney stones. It says that buildings my age must naturally suffer some leakage — it is time they began to loosen and admit a breach. It's a common necessity; there is nothing miraculous or new in it. I am paying old age its due, and I cannot expect a better bargain. It says the company should comfort me, since I've fallen into the most ordinary ailment of men my age: everywhere I look I see men tormented by the same disease, and the fellowship is honorable, since it most often strikes men of quality. It's a noble disease, a dignified one. It says that of the men struck by it, few get off with less pain than I do — and even they pay for it with strict diets and daily doses of disgusting medicines, while I owe my better condition purely to good luck. The few ordinary broths of eryngo and burst-wort I've taken — twice or three times, to oblige certain ladies who, with more kindness than my pain was sharp, insisted on giving me half of theirs — seemed to me equally easy to swallow and useless in effect. Other men must pay a thousand vows to Aesculapius and as many crowns to their physicians to pass a little gravel that I pass often with nature's help alone. Even my composure in company is undisturbed; I can hold my water ten hours, as long as any man in health.

"The fear of this disease," my mind goes on, "used to terrify you, back when you didn't know it — the cries and despairing groans of those who make it worse by their impatience gave you a horror of it. It's an affliction that punishes the very organs you sinned with most. You are a man of conscience; we are only entitled to complain of punishment we haven't earned. Look at this chastisement: it's gentle compared to others, and laid on with a father's tenderness. Notice how late it came — it occupies and inconveniences only that stretch of your life which is sterile and lost anyway, having, as if by contract, left the license and pleasures of your youth their full term.

"The fear and pity people feel for this disease serve your vanity, too — a quality you may have purged from your judgment and half-cured from your reason, but your friends can still spot the tincture in your complexion. There is pleasure in hearing people say of you: now that is strength of mind, that is patience! They see you sweat with pain, turn pale and turn red, tremble, vomit blood, suffer strange contractions and convulsions, drip great tears from your eyes, pass urine thick, black, and dreadful, or have it stopped by some sharp, jagged stone that cruelly pricks and flays the neck of your bladder — and all the while you keep an ordinary face, chatting with your people in the gaps, holding up your end of a conversation, excusing your pain now and then and underselling your suffering. Do you remember the men of the old days who hunted out diseases on purpose, to keep their virtue in training? Suppose nature has driven and pushed you into that glorious school — one you would never have entered on your own.

"If you tell me it's a dangerous, mortal disease — which ones aren't? It's a doctor's trick to exempt any disease at all from the road to death. What does it matter whether they go there directly or slide and slip toward the path that leads us to it? You do not die because you are sick; you die because you are alive. Death kills you perfectly well without sickness's help — and sickness has actually postponed death for some men, who lived longer precisely because they thought they were always dying. Besides, there are diseases, as there are wounds, that are medicinal and good for us.

"Your stones are often as long-lived as you are. There are men who've kept them company from childhood into extreme old age; if the men hadn't quit first, the stones were ready to stay longer. You kill them more often than they kill you. And even if the disease keeps the image of approaching death before you — is it not a kindness, at your age, to be reminded of your end? And here's the worst of it: you no longer have anything left that should make you want to be cured. One way or another, the common necessity will call for you soon. Consider how skillfully and gently this disease puts you out of love with life and weans you from the world — not bullying you with a tyrant's grip, like so many other infirmities you see in old men, which hold them in continual torment and unbroken weakness and pain, but by warnings and instructions spaced out at intervals, with long rests in between, as if to give you the chance to meditate on its lesson and chew it over at your own leisure. And to give you the means to judge sanely, and to summon the resolve of a brave man, it sets your whole condition before you, the good and the bad — a thoroughly cheerful life and an unbearable one, in one and the same day. If you don't embrace death, at least you shake hands with it once a month. So you have all the more reason to hope it will take you one day without warning — that having been led so often down to the water's edge, still assuming the usual terms apply, you and your confidence will some morning be ferried across before you know it. A man cannot reasonably complain of a disease that splits its time fairly with health."

I am grateful to Fortune for attacking me so often with the same weapons. She shapes and trains me by repetition, hardens me, habituates me, so that I know now within a little what it will cost me to be quit of an attack. Lacking a natural memory, I make one out of paper: whenever some new symptom appears in my disease, I write it down. So by now, having run through nearly every kind of episode, whenever something alarming threatens me I leaf through these little loose notes like the Sibyl's leaves — and I never fail to find, somewhere in my past experience, a favorable precedent to comfort me. Habit also makes me hope better of the future: this clearing-out has gone on so long that nature is unlikely to change her course now, and nothing worse is likely to come than what I already feel.

Besides, the temperament of this disease suits my quick and sudden nature. When it attacks me gently, I get frightened — that means a long siege. But its natural style is the brisk, vigorous flare-up: it claws me thoroughly for a day or two. My kidneys held out a whole age without trouble; I have now lived almost another age since they changed their state. Evils have their seasons, like benefits; perhaps this infirmity is drawing to a close. Age cools the heat of my stomach; its digestion, less perfect now, sends crude matter on to my kidneys. Why shouldn't the heat of my kidneys, in their own turn and season, cool as well, so that they can no longer petrify my phlegm, and nature finds some other route of purging? The years have visibly helped me dry up certain discharges. Why not these residues too, that supply the matter for gravel?

And is there any pleasure on earth like that sudden change — when after extreme pain, by the passing of a stone, I recover, fast as a flash of lightning, the beautiful light of health, free and full, the way it happens in our sharpest and most sudden colics? Is there anything in the pain endured that can outweigh the joy of so swift a recovery? How much fairer health looks to me after sickness — sickness so close, so nearly touching it, that I can see the two of them face to face in their full dress, squaring off like rivals to dispute the field! The Stoics say vices were usefully introduced to give value and luster to virtue. We can say, with better reason and a less reckless guess, that nature has given us pain for the honor and the service of pleasure and ease. When Socrates was relieved of his chains and felt the delicious itch in his legs where their weight had been, he rejoiced to consider the close alliance between pain and pleasure — how the two are yoked by a necessary connection, taking turns following and begetting one another — and he called out to good old Aesop that he should have made a fine fable out of exactly that.

The worst thing I see in other diseases is that they are less grievous in their action than in their aftermath. You spend a whole year recovering, full of weakness and fear the entire time. There's so much hazard, so many stages on the way back to safety, that there's no end to it. Before they've unwrapped you of your kerchief, and then of your cap — before they'll let you walk out for air, drink wine, lie with your wife, eat melons — the odds are you've relapsed into some new misery. Kidney stones have this privilege: they carry themselves clean away. Other maladies always leave some imprint and alteration behind, which makes the body prone to the next disease — they lend each other a hand. The excusable ones are content to possess us without spreading or ushering in their followers; but courteous and kind are those whose passing leaves us some profit. Since the stones came, I find myself free of my other ailments — more so, it seems to me, than I was before — and I haven't had a fever since. I reason that the extreme and frequent vomiting I'm subject to scours me out, while my loss of appetite for this and that, and the strange fasts I'm forced to keep, digest away my unhealthy humors, and nature voids in those stones whatever is excess and harmful in me. Don't ever tell me it's medicine bought too dear. What about all the stinking potions, the cauteries, incisions, sweats, drainage-cords, diets — all the apparatus of cure that, because we can't withstand its violence and persistence, so often brings us to the grave instead? So when I have the stone, I count it as medicine; when I'm free of it, as outright deliverance.

And here is another particular favor my disease does me: it pretty much plays its own game by itself and lets me play mine, if I have the courage to take it up. In its greatest fury I have held out ten hours on horseback. Just endure — you need no other regimen. Play, run, dine, do this and that if you can; your indulgence will do you more good than harm. Try saying that to a man with the pox, the gout, or a hernia! Other diseases bind you everywhere — they rack everything you do, throw your whole order into disarray, and drag your entire way of life into their orbit. This one only pinches the skin. It leaves your understanding and your will entirely at your own disposal, along with your tongue, your hands, and your feet. It wakes you up rather than stupefies you. The soul is struck by the burning of a fever, crushed by an epilepsy, knocked off her seat by a fierce migraine — in short, staggered by every disease that injures the whole mass or the noblest parts. This one never touches the soul. If anything goes wrong with her, it's her own fault — she has betrayed, unhorsed, and abandoned herself. Only fools let themselves be persuaded that this hard, solid body baked in our kidneys can be dissolved by drinks. Once it stirs, there is nothing to do but give it passage; and for that matter, it will make one for itself.

I notice one more particular convenience in it: it's a disease that leaves us little to guess at. We're spared the trouble that other diseases throw us into through the uncertainty of their causes, conditions, and course — a trouble that is infinitely painful. There's no need of consultations and doctoral interpretations: the senses tell us perfectly well what it is and where it is.

With arguments like these, strong and weak — like Cicero with the disease of his old age — I try to rock my imagination to sleep and dress its wounds. If they look worse tomorrow, I'll provide new stratagems. And here's proof it's true: lately I've reached the point where the slightest movement forces pure blood out of my kidneys. So what? I move about exactly as before, and ride after my hounds with a young man's insolent ardor. I count it a very good settlement for an affliction of that importance, when all it costs me is a dull heaviness and unease in that region. It's some large stone wasting and consuming the substance of my kidneys — and of my life, which I drain away little by little, not without a certain natural pleasure, as an excretion that is from now on superfluous and a nuisance.

And if I feel something stirring, don't imagine I run to consult my pulse or my urine in order to brace myself with some annoying precaution. I'll feel the pain soon enough, without lengthening it in advance with the disease of fear. He who fears he will suffer already suffers what he fears. Add to that the doubts and ignorance of those who presume to expound nature's designs and inner workings, and the endless false forecasts of their art, and we ought to conclude that her ways are inscrutable and utterly unknown. There is great uncertainty, variety, and obscurity in what she promises and what she threatens. With the exception of old age — which is an unmistakable sign of death's approach — in everything else I see few signs of the future we could ground a prediction on. I judge myself only by actual sensation, not by reasoning. What would be the use, since I'm resolved to bring nothing to it but waiting and patience? Would you like to know what I gain by that? Look at the people who do otherwise, who hang on so many different persuasions and counsels — how often imagination presses on them without any pain in the body at all! Many times, while feeling well and safe and entirely free of these dangerous attacks, I've amused myself by describing them to physicians as just beginning in me. I sat through the verdict of their dreadful conclusions in perfect comfort — and came away that much more indebted to the favor of God, and that much better instructed in the vanity of this art.

There is nothing youth should be urged to more than activity and alertness. Our life is nothing but movement. I get myself moving with difficulty, and I'm slow at everything — rising, going to bed, eating. Seven in the morning is early for me; where I'm in charge, I never dine before eleven or sup before six. I used to blame the fevers and other illnesses I fell into on the sluggishness that too much sleep had laid on me, and I have always regretted falling back asleep in the morning. Plato is angrier at excess of sleep than at excess of drink. I like to sleep on a hard bed, and alone — even without my wife, in the royal style — and fairly well covered. My bed is never warmed for me, though since I've grown old they give me, when I need them, cloths to warm my feet and stomach. People found fault with the great Scipio for being a heavy sleeper — for no other reason, in my opinion, than that it annoyed them that he alone should have nothing in him to find fault with. If I'm particular about anything in my way of living, it's about my bed more than anything else; but in general I give way and adapt to necessity as well as anyone. Sleep has taken up a great part of my life, and even at my present age I still sleep eight or nine hours at a stretch. I am weaning myself, with profit, from this leaning toward sloth, and I'm clearly the better for it. The change is a little hard, true, but in three days it's done. And I know few men who can live on less sleep when they need to, who keep up their exercise more steadily, or who feel long journeys less.

My body is up to steady exertion, but not violent or sudden effort. These days I steer clear of strenuous exercise, the kind that puts me in a sweat: my limbs tire before they warm. I can stay on my feet a whole day, and walking never wearies me; but from my youth I've preferred to ride when the road is paved — on foot you're up to your haunches in mud, and small fellows like me get elbowed and jostled in the streets for want of presence. And I have always loved to rest, sitting or lying, with my heels as high as my seat or higher.

There is no profession as pleasant as the soldier's. It is noble in its practice — for valor is the sturdiest, proudest, most generous of all the virtues — and noble in its cause: no service is more universal or more just than defending the peace and greatness of your country. You enjoy the company of so many noble, young, active men; the daily sight of great and tragic spectacles; the freedom of plain, artless conversation; a masculine, unceremonious way of living; the variety of a thousand different actions; the brave music of war that fires both your ears and your soul; the honor of the work — even its hardships and difficulties, which Plato rates so lightly that in his Republic he gives women and children a share of them. You volunteer for particular exploits and dangers according to your own judgment of their brilliance and importance, and you find that even life itself may be excusably spent there — for, as the poet says, it is a fine thing to die with sword in hand.

To fear dangers shared by such a multitude of men — not to dare what so many kinds of souls dare, what a whole people dares — is for a heart poor and mean beyond all measure. Company gives courage even to children. If other men outdo you in knowledge, in grace, in strength, in fortune, you have outside causes to blame; but if you give way to them in steadiness of soul, you have no one to blame but yourself. Death is more abject, more lingering, more painful in bed than in battle; fevers and catarrhs are as agonizing and as fatal as a musket shot. Whoever has braced himself to bear the accidents of ordinary life has no need to screw up extra courage to be a soldier. To live, my Lucilius, as Seneca says, is to be a soldier.

I don't remember ever having had the itch — and yet scratching is one of nature's sweetest gratifications, and always within reach. But repentance follows too close behind. I practice it mostly on my ears, which itch from time to time on the inside.

I came into the world with all my senses entire, almost to perfection. My stomach is conveniently good, and so are my head and my lungs; they generally hold up even at the height of a fever. I have passed the age that some nations — not without reason — fixed as so proper a limit of life that they allowed no one to exceed it. And yet I still have stretches, short and inconstant as they are, so clean and sound that they fall little short of the health and cheerfulness of my youth. I'm not talking about vigor and sprightliness; it isn't reasonable to expect those to follow me past their boundary. As Horace says, I can no longer stand waiting at a lady's door in the rain.

My face and eyes immediately give my condition away. All my changes begin there, and they look somewhat worse than they really are; my friends often pity me before I feel the cause in myself. My mirror doesn't frighten me, because even in my youth it happened more than once that I wore a sickly complexion of bad omen without any serious consequence — so that the doctors, finding nothing inside to answer for the change outside, put it down to the mind, to some secret passion gnawing at me within. They were wrong. If my body took orders from me as well as my mind does, we would travel a little more comfortably together. My mind at the time was not only free of trouble but full of joy and satisfaction, as it usually is — half by its constitution, half by its own design. As Ovid says, the sickness of the body does not infect the mind.

I am of the opinion that this even temper of my soul has often helped my body up from its lapses. The body is often down; but if my soul is not exactly brisk and gay, it is at least tranquil and at rest. I had a quartan fever for four or five months that made me look completely wretched; my mind the whole time was, if not calm, at least pleasant. If the pain stays outside me, the weakness and languor don't much depress me. I can name various bodily collapses that horrify me even to mention, and yet I fear them less than a thousand passions and agitations of the mind that I see all around me. I have made up my mind that I am done with running; it's enough that I can crawl along. Nor do I complain of the natural decay I feel in myself — who is surprised, asks Juvenal, to find a goiter in the Alps? — any more than I regret that my term will not be as long and entire as an oak's.

I have no complaint against my imagination. Few thoughts in my life have so much as broken my sleep, except thoughts of desire — which woke me without afflicting me. I dream seldom, and then of chimeras and fantastic things, usually spun out of pleasant thoughts, more ridiculous than sad. And I believe it is true that dreams are faithful interpreters of our inclinations — but it takes art to sort them and understand them. It's small wonder, as the old poet says, that the things men practice, think about, care for, see, and do while awake should run through their heads when they sleep.

Plato goes further and says it is the office of wisdom to draw prophetic instruction about the future from dreams. I don't know about that — except that Socrates, Xenophon, and Aristotle, men of unimpeachable authority, relate wonderful instances of it. Historians say the Atlantes never dream; they also never eat anything that has been killed, and I add that fact because it may be the very reason they don't dream — for Pythagoras prescribed a certain diet to produce the right kind of dreams. My own are very gentle, without any thrashing of the body or talking aloud. I have seen plenty of people in my time wonderfully disturbed by theirs. Theon the philosopher walked in his sleep, and so did Pericles' servant — on the tiles, on the very roof of the house.

At table I hardly ever choose my dish; I take the nearest at hand, and I'm reluctant to trade it for another. A confusion of meats and a clatter of dishes displease me as much as any other kind of confusion. I am easily satisfied with few dishes, and I'm an enemy of Favorinus's notion that at a feast they should snatch away the meat you like and set a plate of something else in front of you — that it's a pitiful supper if you haven't stuffed your guests with the rumps of assorted fowl, and that only the songbird deserves to be eaten whole. I usually eat salted meats, yet I prefer my bread without salt, and my baker, against the custom of the region, never sends any other kind to my table. In my childhood, what they mostly had to correct in me was a refusal of the things children usually love best — sugar, sweets, marzipan. My tutor fought this aversion to delicacies as a kind of delicacy itself; and in truth that's all it is: pickiness of taste, whatever it fastens on. Whoever cures a child of a stubborn attachment to brown bread, bacon, or garlic, cures him of pampering his palate. There are people who play at plainness by pining for beef and ham among the partridges. Very fine — that is the daintiness of the dainty. It's the taste of a pampered fortune that loses its relish for ordinary, familiar things; the kind of luxury, as Seneca says, by which wealth amuses itself with the tedium of riches. Refusing to enjoy what another man is enjoying, fussing over what you eat — that is the essence of this vice; as Horace puts it, if you cannot be content with herbs in a small dish for your supper.

There is, granted, this difference: it's better to bind your appetite to the things easiest to come by. But it is always a vice to bind yourself at all. I once called a kinsman of mine overdelicate because his time in our galleys had untaught him the use of a bed and the habit of undressing to sleep.

If I had sons, I would gladly wish them my upbringing. The good father God gave me — who has nothing from me but the acknowledgment of his goodness, though truly it is a hearty one — sent me from my cradle to be raised in a poor village of his, and kept me there through my nursing and beyond, training me to the lowest and most common way of living. A well-disciplined stomach, as Seneca says, is a great part of liberty. Never take the rearing of your children into your own hands — and still less hand it over to your wives. Leave their formation to fortune, under common and natural laws; leave it to custom to train them up to frugality and hardship, so that they will have to climb down from rigor rather than up to it. My father's scheme aimed at something else as well: to make me familiar with the people, and with the condition of those who most need our help. He reckoned I should be made to look toward the men who stretch out their arms to me rather than those who turn their backs on me. And for the same reason he chose people of the humblest fortune to hold me at the baptismal font — to bind and attach me to them.

His plan has not turned out badly. Whether because there is more honor in stooping, or out of a natural compassion that has enormous power over me, I lean toward the humbler sort of people. Whatever faction I would condemn in our wars, I would condemn it more sharply when it was flourishing and successful; it will go some way toward reconciling me to it when I see it miserable and crushed. How willingly I admire the fine spirit of Cheilonis, daughter and wife of kings of Sparta. While her husband Cleombrotus, in the city's upheaval, had the upper hand over her father Leonidas, she played the good daughter — stuck close to her father in all his misery and exile, set against the conqueror. But the moment the luck of war turned, she changed her allegiance with the change of fortune and bravely took her husband's side, following him wherever his ruin carried him. She admitted, it seems to me, no other choice than to join whichever side needed her most, where her compassion could do the most good. I am naturally more inclined to follow the example of Flamininus, who gave his help to those who needed him rather than to those with the power to repay him, than that of Pyrrhus, whose disposition was to grovel before the great and lord it over the poor.

Long sittings at table both annoy me and do me harm — perhaps because I formed the habit as a child, but I keep eating the whole time I sit. So at my own house, though our meals are on the short side, I usually sit down a little after the others, in the manner of Augustus. But I don't imitate him in rising before the rest. On the contrary, I love to sit on a long while after and listen to the talk — provided I take no part in it. Talking on a full stomach tires me and does me harm, though I find it thoroughly wholesome and pleasant to argue and exercise my voice before dinner.

The ancient Greeks and Romans had better sense than we do. Unless some extraordinary business prevented them, they set aside for eating — which is one of the principal actions of life — many hours, and the better part of the night. They ate and drank more deliberately than we do, who rush through everything at a gallop, and they stretched this natural pleasure out into more leisure and better use, seasoning it with profitable conversation.

The people whose business it is to look after me could very easily keep me from anything they think will hurt me, for in such matters I never crave or miss what I don't see. But once a thing is in front of my eyes, persuading me to abstain is a waste of breath. So when I mean to fast, I have to be kept away from the supper table entirely and given just enough for a prescribed light meal; if I sit down at table, I forget my resolution. When I order my cook to dress some dish a different way, my whole household knows what it means: my stomach is out of order, and I won't touch a thing.

I like most meats — those that can stand it — barely boiled or roasted, and very well hung, even, for some, downright gone. Nothing offends me, generally, except toughness; toward every other quality I am as patient and indifferent as any man I've known. So, contrary to the common taste, even fish often strikes me as too fresh and too firm. Not for lack of teeth — mine have always been good, even excellent, and age hasn't yet begun to threaten them; I've always rubbed them with a napkin every morning, and before and after dinner. God is kind to those whom He makes die by degrees. That is the one benefit of old age: the final death will be that much less complete and less painful — it will kill only half a man, or a quarter. One tooth just fell out, without pulling and without pain; it had reached the natural end of its term. That part of my being, and several others, are already dead — others half dead — and they were among the most active, in the front rank during my vigorous years. This is how I melt and slip away from myself. What folly it would be in my understanding to feel the height of this fall, already so far along, as if it were from the very top! I hope I won't. Truly, I draw one principal consolation from thinking on my death: that it will be just and natural, and that from now on I can neither ask nor hope of Destiny any favor in this matter that isn't an unlawful one. Men persuade themselves that in former ages lives were longer, as men were taller. But they deceive themselves: Solon, who belonged to those elder times, sets the limit of life at seventy years. And I — who have so thoroughly and so universally worshipped that golden mean of the time gone by, and concluded that the most moderate measure is the most perfect — shall I now claim an immeasurable, prodigious old age? Whatever runs against the course of nature may be troublesome, but whatever comes according to her should always be pleasant. All things done according to nature, says Cicero, are to be counted good.

And so, says Plato, the death that comes by wounds or disease is violent, but the death that comes upon us with old age leading the way is of all deaths the easiest — in a certain sense, even delicious. Young men, says Cicero, have their lives taken from them by force; old men, by ripeness.

Death mixes and blends itself with life all the way through. Decay runs ahead of its hour and shoulders its way into the very course of our growth. I have portraits of myself at twenty-five and at thirty-five. I compare them with one painted recently: how many times over it is no longer me! How much further is my present image from those than from the image of my dying! It is abusing nature to make her trot so far that she is forced to quit us — to abandon our conduct, our eyes, teeth, legs, and all the rest to the mercy of a foreign, haggard countenance, and hand us over into the keeping of art, being weary of following us herself.

I'm not excessively fond of either salads or fruit — except melons. My father hated every kind of sauce; I love them all. Eating too much hurts me; but as for the quality of what I eat, I don't yet know for certain that any food disagrees with me, nor have I noticed that full moon or new, autumn or spring, has any effect on me. We have movements in us that are fickle and unaccountable. Radishes, for example, I first found agreeable to my stomach, then nauseating, and now agreeable again. In several other things I find my stomach and appetite shifting the same way: I've changed back and forth from white wine to claret, from claret to white.

I am a great lover of fish, and so I make my fast-days feasts and my feast-days fasts. And I believe what some people say, that fish is easier to digest than meat. As I make it a matter of conscience not to eat meat on fish-days, so my taste makes it a matter of conscience not to mix fish and meat: the difference between them seems too wide.

From my youth I have sometimes skipped a meal — either to sharpen my appetite for the next day (for as Epicurus fasted and ate lean to teach his pleasure to do without abundance, I do it the other way round, to train my pleasure to make better and merrier use of abundance); or I fasted to keep my vigor fresh for some work of body or mind, for both of those are cruelly dulled in me by a full stomach. And above all I hate that stupid coupling of so healthy and sprightly a goddess with that little belching god, bloated with the fumes of his liquor. Or I fasted to settle a sick stomach; or for lack of fit company — for I say, as that same Epicurus did, that one should not so much consider what one eats as with whom one eats it. And I commend Chilo for refusing to commit himself to Periander's feast until he learned who the other guests would be. No dish is so sweet to me, no sauce so appetizing, as the one drawn from good company. I think it healthier to eat at more leisure and eat less, and to eat more often. But I would have appetite and hunger consulted: I would take no pleasure in being fed three or four meager, rationed meals a day, on a doctor's schedule. Who will guarantee me that the good appetite I have in the morning will still be there at supper? Let us old fellows especially seize the first opportune hour of eating, and leave the hopes and forecasts to the almanac-makers. The ripest fruit of my health is pleasure; let us hold fast to the present and the known. I avoid rigid consistency in these laws of fasting. Whoever wants a habit to serve him, let him avoid continuing it: we harden in it, our strength falls asleep there; six months later you'll find your stomach so broken in that all you've gained is the loss of your liberty to do otherwise — except at your own cost.

I keep my legs and thighs no warmer in winter than in summer: one simple pair of silk stockings, nothing more. I have allowed myself, for relief of my colds, to keep my head warmer, and my belly on account of my colic. But my ailments got used to these measures in a few days and turned up their noses at my ordinary provisions. From a coif we soon climb to a kerchief over it, from a plain cap to a quilted hat; the padding of the doublet must no longer serve as mere ornament; you must add a hare's skin or a vulture's skin, and a skullcap under the hat. Follow this staircase and you'll go a fine way. I will do nothing of the sort, and would gladly undo the little I've begun. If you fall into some new trouble, all that gear is wasted — you're used to it; go find something else. This is how men ruin themselves who let themselves be pestered with these enforced, superstitious regimens: they must add something more, and then something more after that. There is no end to it.

For the sake of our affairs and our pleasures, it is far more convenient to do as the ancients did — skip dinner and put off good cheer until the hour of retirement and rest, so as not to break up the day; and that's what I used to do. For health, on the other hand, I have since found by experience that it's better to dine, and that digestion goes better while we're awake. I'm seldom thirsty, well or sick; my mouth is apt to go dry, but without thirst. Usually I drink only with the thirst that eating creates, well along in the meal. I drink pretty well for a man of my size: in summer, at a tasty meal, I not only exceed the limit of Augustus, who drank precisely three times and no more, but — so as not to offend the rule of Democritus, who forbade stopping at four as an unlucky number — I proceed at need to a fifth glass, about three half-pints. The little glasses are my favorites, and I like to drain them dry, which other people avoid as bad manners. I dilute my wine, sometimes by half, sometimes by a third, with water; and when I'm at home, by an old custom my father's doctor prescribed both for him and for himself, what is meant for me is mixed in the buttery two or three hours before it's brought in. They say Cranaus, king of Attica, invented this custom of watering wine; whether it does any good or not, I've heard argued both ways. I think it more decent and more wholesome for children to drink no wine until sixteen or eighteen. The most usual and common way of living is the most becoming; all peculiarity, in my opinion, is to be avoided. I would hate a German who put water in his wine as much as a Frenchman who drank it pure. Public usage lays down the law in these matters.

I dread fog, and flee from smoke as from the plague. The first repairs I rushed into at my own house were the chimneys and the privies — the common, insufferable defects of all old buildings. And among the hardships of war I count the choking dust they kept us riding in for whole days together. My breathing is free and easy, and my colds mostly pass off without troubling the lungs and without a cough.

The heat of summer is more my enemy than the cold of winter. Besides the discomfort of heat, which is harder to remedy than cold, and besides the beating of the sun's rays on the head, all glittering light hurts my eyes — so that nowadays I couldn't sit at dinner facing a blazing fire.

To dull the whiteness of the paper, back in the days when I read more, I used to lay a piece of glass over my book, and it relieved my eyes considerably. To this hour — at fifty-four — I am a stranger to spectacles, and I can see as far as I ever did, as far as anyone. It's true that toward evening I begin to feel a little dimness and weakness in my sight when I read — an exercise that has always strained my eyes, especially at night. There's one step backward, and a plain one. I shall retreat another; from the second to the third, from the third to the fourth, so softly that I'll be stone blind before I feel my sight aging and failing. So cunningly do the Fates untwist the thread of our lives. So too I suspect my hearing is starting to thicken; and you'll see that when I've half lost it, I'll still be blaming the voices of those who speak to me. You must wind the soul up to a high pitch to make it feel how it is ebbing away.

My walk is quick and firm, and I couldn't say which of the two, my mind or my body, I've had more trouble holding in one place. The preacher who can fix my attention through a whole sermon is a good friend of mine indeed. In ceremonious places, where every face is starched, where I have seen the ladies hold even their eyes perfectly still, I have never managed to keep some part of me from breaking loose. Seated I may be; settled, never. And as for fidgeting — I am never without a switch in my hand, walking or riding. As the philosopher Chrysippus's maid said of her master, that he was only drunk in his legs — for he had the habit of forever kicking them about wherever he sat, and she said it at a time when wine had made all his companions drunk and he felt no change at all — so it might have been said of me from infancy that I had either folly or quicksilver in my feet, so much fidgeting and restlessness is in them wherever I put them.

It is unmannerly, besides being bad for the health and even for the pleasure of eating, to eat as greedily as I do. In my haste I often bite my tongue, and sometimes my fingers. Diogenes, meeting a boy eating that way, gave the boy's tutor a box on the ear. There were men at Rome who taught people to chew, as well as to walk, with a good grace. Eating this way, I lose the leisure of talking, which gives such relish to the table — provided the talk suits it, that is, pleasant and short.

There is jealousy and envy among our pleasures; they cross and hinder one another. Alcibiades, a man who well understood good cheer, banished even music from the table so that it wouldn't disturb the pleasure of conversation — because, as Plato tells us, it is the custom of ordinary people to call in fiddlers and singers to their feasts for lack of the good talk and pleasant discourse with which men of understanding know how to entertain one another. Varro asks this of a dinner party: people of graceful presence and agreeable conversation, neither mute nor chattering; cleanness and delicacy in the food and in the place; and fair weather.
